150	THE REPUBLIC
150	
150	by Plato
150	
150	(360 B.C.)
150	
150	
150	translated by Benjamin Jowett
150	
150	
150	
150	
150	THE INTRODUCTION
150	
150	THE Republic of Plato is the longest of his works with the exception of
150	the Laws, and is certainly the greatest of them.  There are nearer
150	approaches to modern metaphysics in the Philebus and in the Sophist;
150	the Politicus or Statesman is more ideal; the form and institutions of
150	the State are more clearly drawn out in the Laws; as works of art, the
150	Symposium and the Protagoras are of higher excellence.  But no other
150	Dialogue of Plato has the same largeness of view and the same
150	perfection of style; no other shows an equal knowledge of the world, or
150	contains more of those thoughts which are new as well as old, and not
150	of one age only but of all.  Nowhere in Plato is there a deeper irony
150	or a greater wealth of humor or imagery, or more dramatic power.  Nor
150	in any other of his writings is the attempt made to interweave life and
150	speculation, or to connect politics with philosophy.  The Republic is
150	the centre around which the other Dialogues may be grouped; here
150	philosophy reaches the highest point to which ancient thinkers ever
150	attained.  Plato among the Greeks, like Bacon among the moderns, was
150	the first who conceived a method of knowledge, although neither of them
150	always distinguished the bare outline or form from the substance of
150	truth; and both of them had to be content with an abstraction of
150	science which was not yet realized.  He was the greatest metaphysical
150	genius whom the world has seen; and in him, more than in any other
150	ancient thinker, the germs of future knowledge are contained.  The
150	sciences of logic and psychology, which have supplied so many
150	instruments of thought to after-ages, are based upon the analyses of
150	Socrates and Plato.  The principles of definition, the law of
150	contradiction, the fallacy of arguing in a circle, the distinction
150	between the essence and accidents of a thing or notion, between means
150	and ends, between causes and conditions; also the division of the mind
150	into the rational, concupiscent, and irascible elements, or of
150	pleasures and desires into necessary and unnecessary--these and other
150	great forms of thought are all of them to be found in the Republic, and
150	were probably first invented by Plato.  The greatest of all logical
150	truths, and the one of which writers on philosophy are most apt to lose
150	sight, the difference between words and things, has been most
150	strenuously insisted on by him, although he has not always avoided the
150	confusion of them in his own writings.  But he does not bind up truth
150	in logical formulae,--logic is still veiled in metaphysics; and the
150	"science which he imagines to ""contemplate all truth and all existence"""
150	is very unlike the doctrine of the syllogism which Aristotle claims to
150	have discovered.
150	
150	Neither must we forget that the Republic is but the third part of a
150	still larger design which was to have included an ideal history of
150	Athens, as well as a political and physical philosophy.  The fragment
150	of the Critias has given birth to a world-famous fiction, second only
150	in importance to the tale of Troy and the legend of Arthur; and is said
150	as a fact to have inspired some of the early navigators of the
150	sixteenth century.  This mythical tale, of which the subject was a
150	history of the wars of the Athenians against the Island of Atlantis, is
150	supposed to be founded upon an unfinished poem of Solon, to which it
150	would have stood in the same relation as the writings of the
150	logographers to the poems of Homer.  It would have told of a struggle
150	for Liberty, intended to represent the conflict of Persia and Hellas.
150	We may judge from the noble commencement of the Timaeus, from the
150	fragment of the Critias itself, and from the third book of the Laws, in
150	what manner Plato would have treated this high argument.  We can only
150	guess why the great design was abandoned; perhaps because Plato became
150	sensible of some incongruity in a fictitious history, or because he had
150	lost his interest in it, or because advancing years forbade the
150	completion of it; and we may please ourselves with the fancy that had
150	this imaginary narrative ever been finished, we should have found Plato
150	himself sympathizing with the struggle for Hellenic independence,
150	singing a hymn of triumph over Marathon and Salamis, perhaps making the
150	reflection of Herodotus where he contemplates the growth of the
150	"Athenian empire--""How brave a thing is freedom of speech, which has"
150	made the Athenians so far exceed every other state of Hellas in
150	"greatness!"" or, more probably, attributing the victory to the ancient"
150	good order of Athens and to the favor of Apollo and Athene.
150	
150	"Again, Plato may be regarded as the ""captain"" ('arhchegoz') or leader"
150	of a goodly band of followers; for in the Republic is to be found the
150	original of Cicero's De Republica, of St. Augustine's City of God, of
150	the Utopia of Sir Thomas More, and of the numerous other imaginary
150	States which are framed upon the same model.  The extent to which
150	Aristotle or the Aristotelian school were indebted to him in the
150	Politics has been little recognized, and the recognition is the more
150	necessary because it is not made by Aristotle himself.  The two
150	philosophers had more in common than they were conscious of; and
150	probably some elements of Plato remain still undetected in Aristotle.
150	In English philosophy too, many affinities may be traced, not only in
150	the works of the Cambridge Platonists, but in great original writers
150	like Berkeley or Coleridge, to Plato and his ideas.  That there is a
150	truth higher than experience, of which the mind bears witness to
150	herself, is a conviction which in our own generation has been
150	enthusiastically asserted, and is perhaps gaining ground.  Of the Greek
150	authors who at the Renaissance brought a new life into the world Plato
150	has had the greatest influence.  The Republic of Plato is also the
150	first treatise upon education, of which the writings of Milton and
150	Locke, Rousseau, Jean Paul, and Goethe are the legitimate descendants.
150	Like Dante or Bunyan, he has a revelation of another life; like Bacon,
150	he is profoundly impressed with the unity of knowledge; in the early
150	Church he exercised a real influence on theology, and at the Revival of
150	"Literature on politics.  Even the fragments of his words when ""repeated"
150	"at second-hand"" have in all ages ravished the hearts of men, who have"
150	seen reflected in them their own higher nature.  He is the father of
150	idealism in philosophy, in politics, in literature.  And many of the
150	latest conceptions of modern thinkers and statesmen, such as the unity
150	of knowledge, the reign of law, and the equality of the sexes, have
150	been anticipated in a dream by him.
150	
150	
150	
150	ARGUMENT
150	
150	The argument of the Republic is the search after Justice, the nature of
150	which is first hinted at by Cephalus, the just and blameless old
150	man--then discussed on the basis of proverbial morality by Socrates and
150	Polemarchus--then caricatured by Thrasymachus and partially explained
150	by Socrates--reduced to an abstraction by Glaucon and Adeimantus, and
150	having become invisible in the individual reappears at length in the
150	ideal State which is constructed by Socrates.  The first care of the
150	rulers is to be education, of which an outline is drawn after the old
150	Hellenic model, providing only for an improved religion and morality,
150	and more simplicity in music and gymnastic, a manlier strain of poetry,
150	and greater harmony of the individual and the State.  We are thus led
150	"on to the conception of a higher State, in which ""no man calls anything"
150	"his own,"" and in which there is neither ""marrying nor giving in"
150	"marriage,"" and ""kings are philosophers"" and ""philosophers are kings;"""
150	and there is another and higher education, intellectual as well as
150	moral and religious, of science as well as of art, and not of youth
150	only but of the whole of life.  Such a State is hardly to be realized
150	in this world and would quickly degenerate.  To the perfect ideal
150	succeeds the government of the soldier and the lover of honor, this
150	again declining into democracy, and democracy into tyranny, in an
150	imaginary but regular order having not much resemblance to the actual
150	"facts.  When ""the wheel has come full circle"" we do not begin again"
150	with a new period of human life; but we have passed from the best to
150	the worst, and there we end.  The subject is then changed and the old
150	quarrel of poetry and philosophy which had been more lightly treated in
150	the earlier books of the Republic is now resumed and fought out to a
150	conclusion.  Poetry is discovered to be an imitation thrice removed
150	from the truth, and Homer, as well as the dramatic poets, having been
150	condemned as an imitator, is sent into banishment along with them.  And
150	the idea of the State is supplemented by the revelation of a future
150	life.
150	
150	The division into books, like all similar divisions, is probably later
150	than the age of Plato.  The natural divisions are five in number;--(1)
150	Book I and the first half of Book II down to the paragraph beginning,
150	"""I had always admired the genius of Glaucon and Adeimantus,"" which is"
150	introductory; the first book containing a refutation of the popular and
150	sophistical notions of justice, and concluding, like some of the
150	earlier Dialogues, without arriving at any definite result.  To this is
150	appended a restatement of the nature of justice according to common
150	opinion, and an answer is demanded to the question--What is justice,
150	stripped of appearances?  The second division (2) includes the
150	remainder of the second and the whole of the third and fourth books,
150	which are mainly occupied with the construction of the first State and
150	the first education.  The third division (3) consists of the fifth,
150	sixth, and seventh books, in which philosophy rather than justice is
150	the subject of inquiry, and the second State is constructed on
150	principles of communism and ruled by philosophers, and the
150	contemplation of the idea of good takes the place of the social and
150	political virtues.  In the eighth and ninth books (4) the perversions
150	of States and of the individuals who correspond to them are reviewed in
150	succession; and the nature of pleasure and the principle of tyranny are
150	further analyzed in the individual man.  The tenth book (5) is the
150	conclusion of the whole, in which the relations of philosophy to poetry
150	are finally determined, and the happiness of the citizens in this life,
150	which has now been assured, is crowned by the vision of another.
150	
150	Or a more general division into two parts may be adopted; the first
150	(Books I-IV) containing the description of a State framed generally in
150	accordance with Hellenic notions of religion and morality, while in the
150	second (Books V-X) the Hellenic State is transformed into an ideal
150	kingdom of philosophy, of which all other governments are the
150	perversions.  These two points of view are really opposed, and the
150	opposition is only veiled by the genius of Plato.  The Republic, like
150	the Phaedrus, is an imperfect whole; the higher light of philosophy
150	breaks through the regularity of the Hellenic temple, which at last
150	fades away into the heavens.  Whether this imperfection of structure
150	arises from an enlargement of the plan; or from the imperfect
150	reconcilement in the writer's own mind of the struggling elements of
150	thought which are now first brought together by him; or, perhaps, from
150	the composition of the work at different times--are questions, like the
150	similar question about the Iliad and the Odyssey, which are worth
150	asking, but which cannot have a distinct answer.  In the age of Plato
150	there was no regular mode of publication, and an author would have the
150	less scruple in altering or adding to a work which was known only to a
150	few of his friends.  There is no absurdity in supposing that he may
150	have laid his labors aside for a time, or turned from one work to
150	another; and such interruptions would be more likely to occur in the
150	case of a long than of a short writing.  In all attempts to determine
150	the chronological he order of the Platonic writings on internal
150	evidence, this uncertainty about any single Dialogue being composed at
150	one time is a disturbing element, which must be admitted to affect
150	longer works, such as the Republic and the Laws, more than shorter
150	ones.  But, on the other hand, the seeming discrepancies of the
150	Republic may only arise out of the discordant elements which the
150	philosopher has attempted to unite in a single whole, perhaps without
150	being himself able to recognize the inconsistency which is obvious to
150	us.  For there is a judgment of after ages which few great writers have
150	ever been able to anticipate for themselves.  They do not perceive the
150	want of connection in their own writings, or the gaps in their systems
150	which are visible enough to those who come after them.  In the
150	beginnings of literature and philosophy, amid the first efforts of
150	thought and language, more inconsistencies occur than now, when the
150	paths of speculation are well worn and the meaning of words precisely
150	defined.  For consistency, too, is the growth of time; and some of the
150	greatest creations of the human mind have been wanting in unity.  Tried
150	by this test, several of the Platonic Dialogues, according to our
150	modern ideas, appear to be defective, but the deficiency is no proof
150	that they were composed at different times or by different hands.  And
150	the supposition that the Republic was written uninterruptedly and by a
150	continuous effort is in some degree confirmed by the numerous
150	references from one part of the work to another.
150	
150	"The second title, ""Concerning Justice,"" is not the one by which the"
150	Republic is quoted, either by Aristotle or generally in antiquity, and,
150	like the other second titles of the Platonic Dialogues, may therefore
150	be assumed to be of later date.  Morgenstern and others have asked
150	whether the definition of justice, which is the professed aim, or the
150	construction of the State is the principal argument of the work.  The
150	answer is, that the two blend in one, and are two faces of the same
150	truth; for justice is the order of the State, and the State is the
150	visible embodiment of justice under the conditions of human society.
150	The one is the soul and the other is the body, and the Greek ideal of
150	the State, as of the individual, is a fair mind in a fair body.  In
150	Hegelian phraseology the State is the reality of which justice is the
150	ideal.  Or, described in Christian language, the kingdom of God is
150	"within, and yet develops into a Church or external kingdom; ""the house"
150	"not made with hands, eternal in the heavens,"" is reduced to the"
150	proportions of an earthly building.  Or, to use a Platonic image,
150	justice and the State are the warp and the woof which run through the
150	whole texture.  And when the constitution of the State is completed,
150	the conception of justice is not dismissed, but reappears under the
150	same or different names throughout the work, both as the inner law of
150	the individual soul, and finally as the principle of rewards and
150	punishments in another life.  The virtues are based on justice, of
150	which common honesty in buying and selling is the shadow, and justice
150	is based on the idea of good, which is the harmony of the world, and is
150	reflected both in the institutions of States and in motions of the
150	heavenly bodies.  The Timaeus, which takes up the political rather than
150	the ethical side of the Republic, and is chiefly occupied with
150	hypotheses concerning the outward world, yet contains many indications
150	that the same law is supposed to reign over the State, over nature, and
150	over man.
150	
150	Too much, however, has been made of this question both in ancient and
150	in modern times.  There is a stage of criticism in which all works,
150	whether of nature or of art, are referred to design.  Now in ancient
150	writings, and indeed in literature generally, there remains often a
150	large element which was not comprehended in the original design.  For
150	the plan grows under the author's hand; new thoughts occur to him in
150	the act of writing; he has not worked out the argument to the end
150	before he begins.  The reader who seeks to find some one idea under
150	which the whole may be conceived, must necessarily seize on the vaguest
150	and most general.  Thus Stallbaum, who is dissatisfied with the
150	ordinary explanations of the argument of the Republic, imagines himself
150	"to have found the true argument ""in the representation of human life in"
150	a State perfected by justice and governed according to the idea of
150	"good."" There may be some use in such general descriptions, but they can"
150	hardly be said to express the design of the writer.  The truth is, that
150	we may as well speak of many designs as of one; nor need anything be
150	excluded from the plan of a great work to which the mind is naturally
150	led by the association of ideas, and which does not interfere with the
150	general purpose.  What kind or degree of unity is to be sought after in
150	a building, in the plastic arts, in poetry, in prose, is a problem
150	which has to be determined relatively to the subject-matter. To Plato
150	"himself, the inquiry ""what was the intention of the writer,"" or ""what"
150	"was the principal argument of the Republic"" would have been hardly"
150	intelligible, and therefore had better be at once dismissed.
150	
150	Is not the Republic the vehicle of three or four great truths which, to
150	Plato's own mind, are most naturally represented in the form of the
150	"State?  Just as in the Jewish prophets the reign of Messiah, or ""the"
150	"day of the Lord,"" or the suffering Servant or people of God, or the"
150	"""Sun of righteousness with healing in his wings"" only convey, to us at"
150	least, their great spiritual ideals, so through the Greek State Plato
150	reveals to us his own thoughts about divine perfection, which is the
150	idea of good--like the sun in the visible world;--about human
150	perfection, which is justice--about education beginning in youth and
150	continuing in later years--about poets and sophists and tyrants who are
150	"the false teachers and evil rulers of mankind--about ""the world"" which"
150	is the embodiment of them--about a kingdom which exists nowhere upon
150	earth but is laid up in heaven to be the pattern and rule of human
150	life.  No such inspired creation is at unity with itself, any more than
150	the clouds of heaven when the sun pierces through them.  Every shade of
150	light and dark, of truth, and of fiction which is the veil of truth, is
150	allowable in a work of philosophical imagination.  It is not all on the
150	same plane; it easily passes from ideas to myths and fancies, from
150	facts to figures of speech.  It is not prose but poetry, at least a
150	great part of it, and ought not to be judged by the rules of logic or
150	the probabilities of history.  The writer is not fashioning his ideas
150	into an artistic whole; they take possession of him and are too much
150	for him.  We have no need therefore to discuss whether a State such as
150	Plato has conceived is practicable or not, or whether the outward form
150	or the inward life came first into the mind of the writer.  For the
150	practicability of his ideas has nothing to do with their truth; and the
150	highest thoughts to which he attains may be truly said to bear the
150	"greatest ""marks of design""--justice more than the external frame-work"
150	of the State, the idea of good more than justice.  The great science of
150	dialectic or the organization of ideas has no real content; but is only
150	a type of the method or spirit in which the higher knowledge is to be
150	pursued by the spectator of all time and all existence.  It is in the
150	"fifth, sixth, and seventh books that Plato reaches the ""summit of"
150	"speculation,"" and these, although they fail to satisfy the requirements"
150	of a modern thinker, may therefore be regarded as the most important,
150	as they are also the most original, portions of the work.
150	
150	It is not necessary to discuss at length a minor question which has
150	been raised by Boeckh, respecting the imaginary date at which the
150	conversation was held (the year 411 B. C. which is proposed by him will
150	do as well as any other); for a writer of fiction, and especially a
150	writer who, like Plato, is notoriously careless of chronology, only
150	aims at general probability.  Whether all the persons mentioned in the
150	Republic could ever have met at any one time is not a difficulty which
150	would have occurred to an Athenian reading the work forty years later,
150	or to Plato himself at the time of writing (any more than to
150	Shakespeare respecting one of his own dramas); and need not greatly
150	"trouble us now.  Yet this may be a question having no answer ""which is"
150	"still worth asking,"" because the investigation shows that we can not"
150	argue historically from the dates in Plato; it would be useless
150	therefore to waste time in inventing far-fetched reconcilements of them
150	in order avoid chronological difficulties, such, for example, as the
150	conjecture of C. F. Hermann, that Glaucon and Adeimantus are not the
150	brothers but the uncles of Plato, or the fancy of Stallbaum that Plato
150	intentionally left anachronisms indicating the dates at which some of
150	his Dialogues were written.
150	
150	
150	
150	CHARACTERS
150	
150	The principal characters in the Republic are Cephalus, Polemarchus,
150	Thrasymachus, Socrates, Glaucon, and Adeimantus.  Cephalus appears in
150	the introduction only, Polemarchus drops at the end of the first
150	argument, and Thrasymachus is reduced to silence at the close of the
150	first book.  The main discussion is carried on by Socrates, Glaucon,
150	and Adeimantus.  Among the company are Lysias (the orator) and
150	Euthydemus, the sons of Cephalus and brothers of Polemarchus, an
150	unknown Charmantides--these are mute auditors; also there is
150	Cleitophon, who once interrupts, where, as in the Dialogue which bears
150	his name, he appears as the friend and ally of Thrasymachus.
150	
150	Cephalus, the patriarch of house, has been appropriately engaged in
150	offering a sacrifice.  He is the pattern of an old man who has almost
150	done with life, and is at peace with himself and with all mankind.  He
150	feels that he is drawing nearer to the world below, and seems to linger
150	around the memory of the past.  He is eager that Socrates should come
150	to visit him, fond of the poetry of the last generation, happy in the
150	consciousness of a well-spent life, glad at having escaped from the
150	tyranny of youthful lusts.  His love of conversation, his affection,
150	his indifference to riches, even his garrulity, are interesting traits
150	of character.  He is not one of those who have nothing to say, because
150	their whole mind has been absorbed in making money.  Yet he
150	acknowledges that riches have the advantage of placing men above the
150	temptation to dishonesty or falsehood.  The respectful attention shown
150	to him by Socrates, whose love of conversation, no less than the
150	mission imposed upon him by the Oracle, leads him to ask questions of
150	all men, young and old alike, should also be noted.  Who better suited
150	to raise the question of justice than Cephalus, whose life might seem
150	to be the expression of it?  The moderation with which old age is
150	pictured by Cephalus as a very tolerable portion of existence is
150	characteristic, not only of him, but of Greek feeling generally, and
150	contrasts with the exaggeration of Cicero in the De Senectute.  The
150	evening of life is described by Plato in the most expressive manner,
150	yet with the fewest possible touches.  As Cicero remarks (Ep. ad Attic.
150	iv. 16), the aged Cephalus would have been out of place in the
150	discussion which follows, and which he could neither have understood
150	nor taken part in without a violation of dramatic propriety.
150	
150	"His ""son and heir"" Polemarchus has the frankness and impetuousness of"
150	youth; he is for detaining Socrates by force in the opening scene, and
150	"will not ""let him off"" on the subject of women and children.  Like"
150	Cephalus, he is limited in his point of view, and represents the
150	proverbial stage of morality which has rules of life rather than
150	principles; and he quotes Simonides as his father had quoted Pindar.
150	But after this he has no more to say; the answers which he makes are
150	only elicited from him by the dialectic of Socrates.  He has not yet
150	experienced the influence of the Sophists like Glaucon and Adeimantus,
150	nor is he sensible of the necessity of refuting them; he belongs to the
150	pre-Socratic or pre-dialectical age.  He is incapable of arguing, and
150	is bewildered by Socrates to such a degree that he does not know what
150	he is saying.  He is made to admit that justice is a thief, and that
150	the virtues follow the analogy of the arts.  From his brother Lysias we
150	learn that he fell a victim to the Thirty Tyrants, but no allusion is
150	here made to his fate, nor to the circumstance that Cephalus and his
150	family were of Syracusan origin, and had migrated from Thurii to Athens.
150	
150	"The ""Chalcedonian giant,"" Thrasymachus, of whom we have already heard"
150	in the Phaedrus, is the personification of the Sophists, according to
150	Plato's conception of them, in some of their worst characteristics.  He
150	is vain and blustering, refusing to discourse unless he is paid, fond
150	of making an oration, and hoping thereby to escape the inevitable
150	Socrates; but a mere child in argument, and unable to foresee that the
150	"next ""move"" (to use a Platonic expression) will ""shut him up.""  He has"
150	reached the stage of framing general notions, and in this respect is in
150	advance of Cephalus and Polemarchus.  But he is incapable of defending
150	them in a discussion, and vainly tries to cover his confusion in banter
150	and insolence.  Whether such doctrines as are attributed to him by
150	Plato were really held either by him or by any other Sophist is
150	uncertain; in the infancy of philosophy serious errors about morality
150	might easily grow up--they are certainly put into the mouths of
150	speakers in Thucydides; but we are concerned at present with Plato's
150	description of him, and not with the historical reality.  The
150	inequality of the contest adds greatly to the humor of the scene.  The
150	pompous and empty Sophist is utterly helpless in the hands of the great
150	master of dialectic, who knows how to touch all the springs of vanity
150	and weakness in him.  He is greatly irritated by the irony of Socrates,
150	but his noisy and imbecile rage only lays him more and more open to the
150	thrusts of his assailant.  His determination to cram down their
150	"throats, or put ""bodily into their souls"" his own words, elicits a cry"
150	of horror from Socrates.  The state of his temper is quite as worthy of
150	remark as the process of the argument.  Nothing is more amusing than
150	his complete submission when he has been once thoroughly beaten.  At
150	first he seems to continue the discussion with reluctance, but soon
150	with apparent good-will, and he even testifies his interest at a later
150	stage by one or two occasional remarks.  When attacked by Glaucon he is
150	"humorously protected by Socrates ""as one who has never been his enemy"
150	"and is now his friend."" From Cicero and Quintilian and from Aristotle's"
150	Rhetoric we learn that the Sophist whom Plato has made so ridiculous
150	was a man of note whose writings were preserved in later ages.  The
150	"play on his name which was made by his contemporary Herodicus, ""thou"
150	"wast ever bold in battle,"" seems to show that the description of him is"
150	not devoid of verisimilitude.
150	
150	When Thrasymachus has been silenced, the two principal respondents,
150	Glaucon and Adeimantus, appear on the scene:  here, as in Greek
150	tragedy, three actors are introduced.  At first sight the two sons of
150	Ariston may seem to wear a family likeness, like the two friends
150	Simmias and Cebes in the Phaedo.  But on a nearer examination of them
150	the similarity vanishes, and they are seen to be distinct characters.
150	"Glaucon is the impetuous youth who can ""just never have enough of"
150	"fechting"" (cf. the character of him in Xen. Mem. iii. 6); the man of"
150	"pleasure who is acquainted with the mysteries of love; the ""juvenis qui"
150	"gaudet canibus,"" and who improves the breed of animals; the lover of"
150	art and music who has all the experiences of youthful life.  He is full
150	of quickness and penetration, piercing easily below the clumsy
150	platitudes of Thrasymachus to the real difficulty; he turns out to the
150	light the seamy side of human life, and yet does not lose faith in the
150	just and true.  It is Glaucon who seizes what may be termed the
150	ludicrous relation of the philosopher to the world, to whom a state of
150	"simplicity is ""a city of pigs,"" who is always prepared with a jest when"
150	the argument offers him an opportunity, and who is ever ready to second
150	the humor of Socrates and to appreciate the ridiculous, whether in the
150	connoisseurs of music, or in the lovers of theatricals, or in the
150	fantastic behavior of the citizens of democracy.  His weaknesses are
150	several times alluded to by Socrates, who, however, will not allow him
150	to be attacked by his brother Adeimantus.  He is a soldier, and, like
150	Adeimantus, has been distinguished at the battle of Megara.
150	
150	The character of Adeimantus is deeper and graver, and the profounder
150	objections are commonly put into his mouth.  Glaucon is more
150	demonstrative, and generally opens the game.  Adeimantus pursues the
150	argument further.  Glaucon has more of the liveliness and quick
150	sympathy of youth; Adeimantus has the maturer judgment of a grown-up
150	man of the world.  In the second book, when Glaucon insists that
150	justice and injustice shall be considered without regard to their
150	consequences, Adeimantus remarks that they are regarded by mankind in
150	general only for the sake of their consequences; and in a similar vein
150	of reflection he urges at the beginning of the fourth book that
150	Socrates falls in making his citizens happy, and is answered that
150	happiness is not the first but the second thing, not the direct aim but
150	the indirect consequence of the good government of a State.  In the
150	discussion about religion and mythology, Adeimantus is the respondent,
150	but Glaucon breaks in with a slight jest, and carries on the
150	conversation in a lighter tone about music and gymnastic to the end of
150	the book.  It is Adeimantus again who volunteers the criticism of
150	common sense on the Socratic method of argument, and who refuses to let
150	Socrates pass lightly over the question of women and children.  It is
150	Adeimantus who is the respondent in the more argumentative, as Glaucon
150	in the lighter and more imaginative portions of the Dialogue.  For
150	example, throughout the greater part of the sixth book, the causes of
150	the corruption of philosophy and the conception of the idea of good are
150	discussed with Adeimantus.  Then Glaucon resumes his place of principal
150	respondent; but he has a difficulty in apprehending the higher
150	education of Socrates, and makes some false hits in the course of the
150	discussion.  Once more Adeimantus returns with the allusion to his
150	brother Glaucon whom he compares to the contentious State; in the next
150	book he is again superseded, and Glaucon continues to the end.
150	
150	Thus in a succession of characters Plato represents the successive
150	stages of morality, beginning with the Athenian gentleman of the olden
150	time, who is followed by the practical man of that day regulating his
150	life by proverbs and saws; to him succeeds the wild generalization of
150	the Sophists, and lastly come the young disciples of the great teacher,
150	who know the sophistical arguments but will not be convinced by them,
150	and desire to go deeper into the nature of things.  These too, like
150	Cephalus, Polemarchus, Thrasymachus, are clearly distinguished from one
150	another.  Neither in the Republic, nor in any other Dialogue of Plato,
150	is a single character repeated.
150	
150	The delineation of Socrates in the Republic is not wholly consistent.
150	In the first book we have more of the real Socrates, such as he is
150	depicted in the Memorabilia of Xenophon, in the earliest Dialogues of
150	Plato, and in the Apology.  He is ironical, provoking, questioning, the
150	old enemy of the Sophists, ready to put on the mask of Silenus as well
150	as to argue seriously.  But in the sixth book his enmity towards the
150	Sophists abates; he acknowledges that they are the representatives
150	rather than the corrupters of the world.  He also becomes more dogmatic
150	and constructive, passing beyond the range either of the political or
150	the speculative ideas of the real Socrates.  In one passage Plato
150	himself seems to intimate that the time had now come for Socrates, who
150	had passed his whole life in philosophy, to give his own opinion and
150	not to be always repeating the notions of other men.  There is no
150	evidence that either the idea of good or the conception of a perfect
150	State were comprehended in the Socratic teaching, though he certainly
150	dwelt on the nature of the universal and of final causes (cp. Xen.
150	Mem. i. 4; Phaedo 97); and a deep thinker like him in his thirty or
150	forty years of public teaching, could hardly have falled to touch on
150	the nature of family relations, for which there is also some positive
150	evidence in the Memorabilia (Mem. i. 2, 51 foll.) The Socratic method
150	is nominally retained; and every inference is either put into the mouth
150	of the respondent or represented as the common discovery of him and
150	Socrates.  But any one can see that this is a mere form, of which the
150	affectation grows wearisome as the work advances.  The method of
150	inquiry has passed into a method of teaching in which by the help of
150	interlocutors the same thesis is looked at from various points of view.
150	
150	The nature of the process is truly characterized by Glaucon, when he
150	describes himself as a companion who is not good for much in an
150	investigation, but can see what he is shown, and may, perhaps, give the
150	answer to a question more fluently than another.
150	
150	Neither can we be absolutely certain that, Socrates himself taught the
150	immortality of the soul, which is unknown to his disciple Glaucon in
150	the Republic; nor is there any reason to suppose that he used myths or
150	revelations of another world as a vehicle of instruction, or that he
150	would have banished poetry or have denounced the Greek mythology.  His
150	favorite oath is retained, and a slight mention is made of the
150	daemonium, or internal sign, which is alluded to by Socrates as a
150	phenomenon peculiar to himself.  A real element of Socratic teaching,
150	which is more prominent in the Republic than in any of the other
150	Dialogues of Plato, is the use of example and illustration
150	"('taphorhtika auto prhospherhontez'): ""Let us apply the test of common"
150	"instances."" ""You,"" says Adeimantus, ironically, in the sixth book, ""are"
150	"so unaccustomed to speak in images.""  And this use of examples or"
150	images, though truly Socratic in origin, is enlarged by the genius of
150	Plato into the form of an allegory or parable, which embodies in the
150	concrete what has been already described, or is about to be described,
150	in the abstract.  Thus the figure of the cave in Book VII is a
150	recapitulation of the divisions of knowledge in Book VI.  The composite
150	animal in Book IX is an allegory of the parts of the soul.  The noble
150	captain and the ship and the true pilot in Book VI are a figure of the
150	relation of the people to the philosophers in the State which has been
150	described.  Other figures, such as the dog in the second, third, and
150	fourth books, or the marriage of the portionless maiden in the sixth
150	book, or the drones and wasps in the eighth and ninth books, also form
150	links of connection in long passages, or are used to recall previous
150	discussions.
150	
150	Plato is most true to the character of his master when he describes him
150	"as ""not of this world.""  And with this representation of him the ideal"
150	State and the other paradoxes of the Republic are quite in accordance,
150	though they can not be shown to have been speculations of Socrates.  To
150	him, as to other great teachers both philosophical and religious, when
150	they looked upward, the world seemed to be the embodiment of error and
150	evil.  The common sense of mankind has revolted against this view, or
150	has only partially admitted it.  And even in Socrates himself the
150	sterner judgment of the multitude at times passes into a sort of
150	ironical pity or love.  Men in general are incapable of philosophy, and
150	are therefore at enmity with the philosopher; but their
150	misunderstanding of him is unavoidable: for they have never seen him as
150	he truly is in his own image; they are only acquainted with artificial
150	systems possessing no native force of truth--words which admit of many
150	applications.  Their leaders have nothing to measure with, and are
150	therefore ignorant of their own stature.  But they are to be pitied or
150	laughed at, not to be quarrelled with; they mean well with their
150	nostrums, if they could only learn that they are cutting off a Hydra's
150	head.  This moderation towards those who are in error is one of the
150	most characteristic features of Socrates in the Republic.  In all the
150	different representations of Socrates, whether of Xenophon or Plato,
150	and the differences of the earlier or later Dialogues, he always
150	retains the character of the unwearied and disinterested seeker after
150	truth, without which he would have ceased to be Socrates.
150	
150	Leaving the characters we may now analyze the contents of the Republic,
150	and then proceed to consider (1) The general aspects of this Hellenic
150	ideal of the State, (2) The modern lights in which the thoughts of
150	Plato may be read.
150	
150	
150	
150	
150	
150	BOOK I
150	
150	
150	SOCRATES - GLAUCON
150	
150	I WENT down yesterday to the Piraeus with Glaucon the son of Ariston,
150	that I might offer up my prayers to the goddess; and also because I
150	wanted to see in what manner they would celebrate the festival, which
150	was a new thing.  I was delighted with the procession of the
150	inhabitants; but that of the Thracians was equally, if not more,
150	beautiful.  When we had finished our prayers and viewed the spectacle,
150	we turned in the direction of the city; and at that instant Polemarchus
150	the son of Cephalus chanced to catch sight of us from a distance as we
150	were starting on our way home, and told his servant to run and bid us
150	wait for him.  The servant took hold of me by the cloak behind, and
150	said: Polemarchus desires you to wait.
150	
150	I turned round, and asked him where his master was.
150	
150	There he is, said the youth, coming after you, if you will only wait.
150	
150	Certainly we will, said Glaucon; and in a few minutes Polemarchus
150	appeared, and with him Adeimantus, Glaucon's brother, Niceratus the son
150	of Nicias, and several others who had been at the procession.
150	
150	SOCRATES - POLEMARCHUS - GLAUCON - ADEIMANTUS
150	
150	Polemarchus said to me:  I perceive, Socrates, that you and our
150	companion are already on your way to the city.
150	
150	You are not far wrong, I said.
150	
150	But do you see, he rejoined, how many we are?
150	
150	Of course.
150	
150	And are you stronger than all these? for if not, you will have to
150	remain where you are.
150	
150	May there not be the alternative, I said, that we may persuade you to
150	let us go?
150	
150	But can you persuade us, if we refuse to listen to you? he said.
150	
150	Certainly not, replied Glaucon.
150	
150	Then we are not going to listen; of that you may be assured.
150	
150	Adeimantus added:  Has no one told you of the torch-race on horseback
150	in honour of the goddess which will take place in the evening?
150	
150	With horses!  I replied:  That is a novelty.  Will horsemen carry
150	torches and pass them one to another during the race?
150	
150	Yes, said Polemarchus, and not only so, but a festival will he
150	celebrated at night, which you certainly ought to see.  Let us rise
150	soon after supper and see this festival; there will be a gathering of
150	young men, and we will have a good talk.  Stay then, and do not be
150	perverse.
150	
150	Glaucon said:  I suppose, since you insist, that we must.
150	
150	Very good, I replied.
150	
150	GLAUCON - CEPHALUS - SOCRATES
150	
150	Accordingly we went with Polemarchus to his house; and there we found
150	his brothers Lysias and Euthydemus, and with them Thrasymachus the
150	Chalcedonian, Charmantides the Paeanian, and Cleitophon the son of
150	Aristonymus.  There too was Cephalus the father of Polemarchus, whom I
150	had not seen for a long time, and I thought him very much aged.  He was
150	seated on a cushioned chair, and had a garland on his head, for he had
150	been sacrificing in the court; and there were some other chairs in the
150	room arranged in a semicircle, upon which we sat down by him.  He
150	saluted me eagerly, and then he said:--
150	
150	You don't come to see me, Socrates, as often as you ought: If I were
150	still able to go and see you I would not ask you to come to me.  But at
150	my age I can hardly get to the city, and therefore you should come
150	oftener to the Piraeus.  For let me tell you, that the more the
150	pleasures of the body fade away, the greater to me is the pleasure and
150	charm of conversation.  Do not then deny my request, but make our house
150	your resort and keep company with these young men; we are old friends,
150	and you will be quite at home with us.
150	
150	I replied:  There is nothing which for my part I like better, Cephalus,
150	than conversing with aged men; for I regard them as travellers who have
150	gone a journey which I too may have to go, and of whom I ought to
150	enquire, whether the way is smooth and easy, or rugged and difficult.
150	And this is a question which I should like to ask of you who have
150	arrived at that time which the poets call the 'threshold of old
150	age'--Is life harder towards the end, or what report do you give of it?
150	
150	I will tell you, Socrates, he said, what my own feeling is.  Men of my
150	age flock together; we are birds of a feather, as the old proverb says;
150	and at our meetings the tale of my acquaintance commonly is--I cannot
150	eat, I cannot drink; the pleasures of youth and love are fled away:
150	there was a good time once, but now that is gone, and life is no longer
150	life.  Some complain of the slights which are put upon them by
150	relations, and they will tell you sadly of how many evils their old age
150	is the cause.  But to me, Socrates, these complainers seem to blame
150	that which is not really in fault.  For if old age were the cause, I
150	too being old, and every other old man, would have felt as they do.
150	But this is not my own experience, nor that of others whom I have
150	known.  How well I remember the aged poet Sophocles, when in answer to
150	the question, How does love suit with age, Sophocles,--are you still
150	the man you were?  Peace, he replied; most gladly have I escaped the
150	thing of which you speak; I feel as if I had escaped from a mad and
150	furious master.  His words have often occurred to my mind since, and
150	they seem as good to me now as at the time when he uttered them.  For
150	certainly old age has a great sense of calm and freedom; when the
150	passions relax their hold, then, as Sophocles says, we are freed from
150	the grasp not of one mad master only, but of many.  The truth is,
150	Socrates, that these regrets, and also the complaints about relations,
150	are to be attributed to the same cause, which is not old age, but men's
150	characters and tempers; for he who is of a calm and happy nature will
150	hardly feel the pressure of age, but to him who is of an opposite
150	disposition youth and age are equally a burden.
150	
150	I listened in admiration, and wanting to draw him out, that he might go
150	on--Yes, Cephalus, I said:  but I rather suspect that people in general
150	are not convinced by you when you speak thus; they think that old age
150	sits lightly upon you, not because of your happy disposition, but
150	because you are rich, and wealth is well known to be a great comforter.
150	
150	You are right, he replied; they are not convinced:  and there is
150	something in what they say; not, however, so much as they imagine.  I
150	might answer them as Themistocles answered the Seriphian who was
150	abusing him and saying that he was famous, not for his own merits but
150	because he was an Athenian:  'If you had been a native of my country or
150	I of yours, neither of us would have been famous.' And to those who are
150	not rich and are impatient of old age, the same reply may be made; for
150	to the good poor man old age cannot be a light burden, nor can a bad
150	rich man ever have peace with himself.
150	
150	May I ask, Cephalus, whether your fortune was for the most part
150	inherited or acquired by you?
150	
150	Acquired!  Socrates; do you want to know how much I acquired?  In the
150	art of making money I have been midway between my father and
150	grandfather: for my grandfather, whose name I bear, doubled and trebled
150	the value of his patrimony, that which he inherited being much what I
150	possess now; but my father Lysanias reduced the property below what it
150	is at present: and I shall be satisfied if I leave to these my sons not
150	less but a little more than I received.
150	
150	That was why I asked you the question, I replied, because I see that
150	you are indifferent about money, which is a characteristic rather of
150	those who have inherited their fortunes than of those who have acquired
150	them; the makers of fortunes have a second love of money as a creation
150	of their own, resembling the affection of authors for their own poems,
150	or of parents for their children, besides that natural love of it for
150	the sake of use and profit which is common to them and all men.  And
150	hence they are very bad company, for they can talk about nothing but
150	the praises of wealth.  That is true, he said.
150	
150	Yes, that is very true, but may I ask another question?  What do you
150	consider to be the greatest blessing which you have reaped from your
150	wealth?
150	
150	One, he said, of which I could not expect easily to convince others.
150	For let me tell you, Socrates, that when a man thinks himself to be
150	near death, fears and cares enter into his mind which he never had
150	before; the tales of a world below and the punishment which is exacted
150	there of deeds done here were once a laughing matter to him, but now he
150	is tormented with the thought that they may be true: either from the
150	weakness of age, or because he is now drawing nearer to that other
150	place, he has a clearer view of these things; suspicions and alarms
150	crowd thickly upon him, and he begins to reflect and consider what
150	wrongs he has done to others.  And when he finds that the sum of his
150	transgressions is great he will many a time like a child start up in
150	his sleep for fear, and he is filled with dark forebodings.  But to him
150	who is conscious of no sin, sweet hope, as Pindar charmingly says, is
150	the kind nurse of his age:
150	
150	    Hope, he says, cherishes the soul of him who lives in
150	    justice and holiness and is the nurse of his age and the
150	    companion of his journey;--hope which is mightiest to sway
150	    the restless soul of man.
150	
150	How admirable are his words!  And the great blessing of riches, I do
150	not say to every man, but to a good man, is, that he has had no
150	occasion to deceive or to defraud others, either intentionally or
150	unintentionally; and when he departs to the world below he is not in
150	any apprehension about offerings due to the gods or debts which he owes
150	to men.  Now to this peace of mind the possession of wealth greatly
150	contributes; and therefore I say, that, setting one thing against
150	another, of the many advantages which wealth has to give, to a man of
150	sense this is in my opinion the greatest.
150	
150	Well said, Cephalus, I replied; but as concerning justice, what is
150	it?--to speak the truth and to pay your debts--no more than this?  And
150	even to this are there not exceptions?  Suppose that a friend when in
150	his right mind has deposited arms with me and he asks for them when he
150	is not in his right mind, ought I to give them back to him?  No one
150	would say that I ought or that I should be right in doing so, any more
150	than they would say that I ought always to speak the truth to one who
150	is in his condition.
150	
150	You are quite right, he replied.
150	
150	But then, I said, speaking the truth and paying your debts is not a
150	correct definition of justice.
150	
150	CEPHALUS - SOCRATES - POLEMARCHUS
150	
150	Quite correct, Socrates, if Simonides is to be believed, said
150	Polemarchus interposing.
150	
150	I fear, said Cephalus, that I must go now, for I have to look after the
150	sacrifices, and I hand over the argument to Polemarchus and the company.
150	
150	Is not Polemarchus your heir?  I said.
150	
150	To be sure, he answered, and went away laughing to the sacrifices.
150	
150	SOCRATES - POLEMARCHUS
150	
150	Tell me then, O thou heir of the argument, what did Simonides say, and
150	according to you truly say, about justice?
150	
150	He said that the repayment of a debt is just, and in saying so he
150	appears to me to be right.
150	
150	I should be sorry to doubt the word of such a wise and inspired man,
150	but his meaning, though probably clear to you, is the reverse of clear
150	to me.  For he certainly does not mean, as we were now saying that I
150	ought to return a return a deposit of arms or of anything else to one
150	who asks for it when he is not in his right senses; and yet a deposit
150	cannot be denied to be a debt.
150	
150	True.
150	
150	Then when the person who asks me is not in his right mind I am by no
150	means to make the return?
150	
150	Certainly not.
150	
150	When Simonides said that the repayment of a debt was justice, he did
150	not mean to include that case?
150	
150	Certainly not; for he thinks that a friend ought always to do good to a
150	friend and never evil.
150	
150	You mean that the return of a deposit of gold which is to the injury of
150	the receiver, if the two parties are friends, is not the repayment of a
150	debt,--that is what you would imagine him to say?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And are enemies also to receive what we owe to them?
150	
150	To be sure, he said, they are to receive what we owe them, and an
150	enemy, as I take it, owes to an enemy that which is due or proper to
150	him--that is to say, evil.
150	
150	Simonides, then, after the manner of poets, would seem to have spoken
150	darkly of the nature of justice; for he really meant to say that
150	justice is the giving to each man what is proper to him, and this he
150	termed a debt.
150	
150	That must have been his meaning, he said.
150	
150	By heaven!  I replied; and if we asked him what due or proper thing is
150	given by medicine, and to whom, what answer do you think that he would
150	make to us?
150	
150	He would surely reply that medicine gives drugs and meat and drink to
150	human bodies.
150	
150	And what due or proper thing is given by cookery, and to what?
150	
150	Seasoning to food.
150	
150	And what is that which justice gives, and to whom?
150	
150	If, Socrates, we are to be guided at all by the analogy of the
150	preceding instances, then justice is the art which gives good to
150	friends and evil to enemies.
150	
150	That is his meaning then?
150	
150	I think so.
150	
150	And who is best able to do good to his friends and evil to his enemies
150	in time of sickness?
150	
150	The physician.
150	
150	Or when they are on a voyage, amid the perils of the sea?
150	
150	The pilot.
150	
150	And in what sort of actions or with a view to what result is the just
150	man most able to do harm to his enemy and good to his friends?
150	
150	In going to war against the one and in making alliances with the other.
150	
150	But when a man is well, my dear Polemarchus, there is no need of a
150	physician?
150	
150	No.
150	
150	And he who is not on a voyage has no need of a pilot?
150	
150	No.
150	
150	Then in time of peace justice will be of no use?
150	
150	I am very far from thinking so.
150	
150	You think that justice may be of use in peace as well as in war?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	Like husbandry for the acquisition of corn?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	Or like shoemaking for the acquisition of shoes,--that is what you mean?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And what similar use or power of acquisition has justice in time of
150	peace?
150	
150	In contracts, Socrates, justice is of use.
150	
150	And by contracts you mean partnerships?
150	
150	Exactly.
150	
150	But is the just man or the skilful player a more useful and better
150	partner at a game of draughts?
150	
150	The skilful player.
150	
150	And in the laying of bricks and stones is the just man a more useful or
150	better partner than the builder?
150	
150	Quite the reverse.
150	
150	Then in what sort of partnership is the just man a better partner than
150	the harp-player, as in playing the harp the harp-player is certainly a
150	better partner than the just man?
150	
150	In a money partnership.
150	
150	Yes, Polemarchus, but surely not in the use of money; for you do not
150	want a just man to be your counsellor the purchase or sale of a horse;
150	a man who is knowing about horses would be better for that, would he
150	not?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	And when you want to buy a ship, the shipwright or the pilot would be
150	better?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	Then what is that joint use of silver or gold in which the just man is
150	to be preferred?
150	
150	When you want a deposit to be kept safely.
150	
150	You mean when money is not wanted, but allowed to lie?
150	
150	Precisely.
150	
150	That is to say, justice is useful when money is useless?
150	
150	That is the inference.
150	
150	And when you want to keep a pruning-hook safe, then justice is useful
150	to the individual and to the state; but when you want to use it, then
150	the art of the vine-dresser?
150	
150	Clearly.
150	
150	And when you want to keep a shield or a lyre, and not to use them, you
150	would say that justice is useful; but when you want to use them, then
150	the art of the soldier or of the musician?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	And so of all the other things;--justice is useful when they are
150	useless, and useless when they are useful?
150	
150	That is the inference.
150	
150	Then justice is not good for much.  But let us consider this further
150	point:  Is not he who can best strike a blow in a boxing match or in
150	any kind of fighting best able to ward off a blow?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	And he who is most skilful in preventing or escaping from a disease is
150	best able to create one?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	And he is the best guard of a camp who is best able to steal a march
150	upon the enemy?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	Then he who is a good keeper of anything is also a good thief?
150	
150	That, I suppose, is to be inferred.
150	
150	Then if the just man is good at keeping money, he is good at stealing
150	it.
150	
150	That is implied in the argument.
150	
150	Then after all the just man has turned out to be a thief.  And this is
150	a lesson which I suspect you must have learnt out of Homer; for he,
150	speaking of Autolycus, the maternal grandfather of Odysseus, who is a
150	favourite of his, affirms that
150	
150	    He was excellent above all men in theft and perjury.
150	
150	And so, you and Homer and Simonides are agreed that justice is an art
150	of theft; to be practised however 'for the good of friends and for the
150	harm of enemies,'--that was what you were saying?
150	
150	No, certainly not that, though I do not now know what I did say; but I
150	still stand by the latter words.
150	
150	Well, there is another question:  By friends and enemies do we mean
150	those who are so really, or only in seeming?
150	
150	Surely, he said, a man may be expected to love those whom he thinks
150	good, and to hate those whom he thinks evil.
150	
150	Yes, but do not persons often err about good and evil: many who are not
150	good seem to be so, and conversely?
150	
150	That is true.
150	
150	Then to them the good will be enemies and the evil will be their
150	friends?  True.
150	
150	And in that case they will be right in doing good to the evil and evil
150	to the good?
150	
150	Clearly.
150	
150	But the good are just and would not do an injustice?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	Then according to your argument it is just to injure those who do no
150	wrong?
150	
150	Nay, Socrates; the doctrine is immoral.
150	
150	Then I suppose that we ought to do good to the just and harm to the
150	unjust?
150	
150	I like that better.
150	
150	But see the consequence:--Many a man who is ignorant of human nature
150	has friends who are bad friends, and in that case he ought to do harm
150	to them; and he has good enemies whom he ought to benefit; but, if so,
150	we shall be saying the very opposite of that which we affirmed to be
150	the meaning of Simonides.
150	
150	Very true, he said:  and I think that we had better correct an error
150	into which we seem to have fallen in the use of the words 'friend' and
150	'enemy.'
150	
150	What was the error, Polemarchus?  I asked.
150	
150	We assumed that he is a friend who seems to be or who is thought good.
150	
150	And how is the error to be corrected?
150	
150	We should rather say that he is a friend who is, as well as seems,
150	good; and that he who seems only, and is not good, only seems to be and
150	is not a friend; and of an enemy the same may be said.
150	
150	You would argue that the good are our friends and the bad our enemies?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And instead of saying simply as we did at first, that it is just to do
150	good to our friends and harm to our enemies, we should further say: It
150	is just to do good to our friends when they are good and harm to our
150	enemies when they are evil?
150	
150	Yes, that appears to me to be the truth.
150	
150	But ought the just to injure any one at all?
150	
150	Undoubtedly he ought to injure those who are both wicked and his
150	enemies.
150	
150	When horses are injured, are they improved or deteriorated?
150	
150	The latter.
150	
150	Deteriorated, that is to say, in the good qualities of horses, not of
150	dogs?
150	
150	Yes, of horses.
150	
150	And dogs are deteriorated in the good qualities of dogs, and not of
150	horses?
150	
150	Of course.
150	
150	And will not men who are injured be deteriorated in that which is the
150	proper virtue of man?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	And that human virtue is justice?
150	
150	To be sure.
150	
150	Then men who are injured are of necessity made unjust?
150	
150	That is the result.
150	
150	But can the musician by his art make men unmusical?
150	
150	Certainly not.
150	
150	Or the horseman by his art make them bad horsemen?
150	
150	Impossible.
150	
150	And can the just by justice make men unjust, or speaking general can
150	the good by virtue make them bad?
150	
150	Assuredly not.
150	
150	Any more than heat can produce cold?
150	
150	It cannot.
150	
150	Or drought moisture?
150	
150	Clearly not.
150	
150	Nor can the good harm any one?
150	
150	Impossible.
150	
150	And the just is the good?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	Then to injure a friend or any one else is not the act of a just man,
150	but of the opposite, who is the unjust?
150	
150	I think that what you say is quite true, Socrates.
150	
150	Then if a man says that justice consists in the repayment of debts, and
150	that good is the debt which a man owes to his friends, and evil the
150	debt which he owes to his enemies,--to say this is not wise; for it is
150	not true, if, as has been clearly shown, the injuring of another can be
150	in no case just.
150	
150	I agree with you, said Polemarchus.
150	
150	Then you and I are prepared to take up arms against any one who
150	attributes such a saying to Simonides or Bias or Pittacus, or any other
150	wise man or seer?
150	
150	I am quite ready to do battle at your side, he said.
150	
150	Shall I tell you whose I believe the saying to be?
150	
150	Whose?
150	
150	I believe that Periander or Perdiccas or Xerxes or Ismenias the Theban,
150	or some other rich and mighty man, who had a great opinion of his own
150	power, was the first to say that justice is 'doing good to your friends
150	and harm to your enemies.'
150	
150	Most true, he said.
150	
150	Yes, I said; but if this definition of justice also breaks down, what
150	other can be offered?
150	
150	Several times in the course of the discussion Thrasymachus had made an
150	attempt to get the argument into his own hands, and had been put down
150	by the rest of the company, who wanted to hear the end.  But when
150	Polemarchus and I had done speaking and there was a pause, he could no
150	longer hold his peace; and, gathering himself up, he came at us like a
150	wild beast, seeking to devour us.  We were quite panic-stricken at the
150	sight of him.
150	
150	SOCRATES - POLEMARCHUS - THRASYMACHUS
150	
150	He roared out to the whole company:  What folly.  Socrates, has taken
150	possession of you all?  And why, sillybillies, do you knock under to
150	one another?  I say that if you want really to know what justice is,
150	you should not only ask but answer, and you should not seek honour to
150	yourself from the refutation of an opponent, but have your own answer;
150	for there is many a one who can ask and cannot answer.  And now I will
150	not have you say that justice is duty or advantage or profit or gain or
150	interest, for this sort of nonsense will not do for me; I must have
150	clearness and accuracy.
150	
150	I was panic-stricken at his words, and could not look at him without
150	trembling.  Indeed I believe that if I had not fixed my eye upon him, I
150	should have been struck dumb:  but when I saw his fury rising, I looked
150	at him first, and was therefore able to reply to him.
150	
150	Thrasymachus, I said, with a quiver, don't be hard upon us.
150	Polemarchus and I may have been guilty of a little mistake in the
150	argument, but I can assure you that the error was not intentional.  If
150	we were seeking for a piece of gold, you would not imagine that we were
150	'knocking under to one another,' and so losing our chance of finding
150	it.  And why, when we are seeking for justice, a thing more precious
150	than many pieces of gold, do you say that we are weakly yielding to one
150	another and not doing our utmost to get at the truth?  Nay, my good
150	friend, we are most willing and anxious to do so, but the fact is that
150	we cannot.  And if so, you people who know all things should pity us
150	and not be angry with us.
150	
150	How characteristic of Socrates! he replied, with a bitter
150	laugh;--that's your ironical style!  Did I not foresee--have I not
150	already told you, that whatever he was asked he would refuse to answer,
150	and try irony or any other shuffle, in order that he might avoid
150	answering?
150	
150	You are a philosopher, Thrasymachus, I replied, and well know that if
150	you ask a person what numbers make up twelve, taking care to prohibit
150	him whom you ask from answering twice six, or three times four, or six
150	times two, or four times three, 'for this sort of nonsense will not do
150	for me,'--then obviously, that is your way of putting the question, no
150	one can answer you.  But suppose that he were to retort, 'Thrasymachus,
150	what do you mean?  If one of these numbers which you interdict be the
150	true answer to the question, am I falsely to say some other number
150	which is not the right one?--is that your meaning?'--How would you
150	answer him?
150	
150	Just as if the two cases were at all alike! he said.
150	
150	Why should they not be?  I replied; and even if they are not, but only
150	appear to be so to the person who is asked, ought he not to say what he
150	thinks, whether you and I forbid him or not?
150	
150	I presume then that you are going to make one of the interdicted
150	answers?
150	
150	I dare say that I may, notwithstanding the danger, if upon reflection I
150	approve of any of them.
150	
150	But what if I give you an answer about justice other and better, he
150	said, than any of these?  What do you deserve to have done to you?
150	
150	Done to me!--as becomes the ignorant, I must learn from the wise--that
150	is what I deserve to have done to me.
150	
150	What, and no payment! a pleasant notion!
150	
150	I will pay when I have the money, I replied.
150	
150	SOCRATES - THRASYMACHUS - GLAUCON
150	
150	But you have, Socrates, said Glaucon:  and you, Thrasymachus, need be
150	under no anxiety about money, for we will all make a contribution for
150	Socrates.
150	
150	Yes, he replied, and then Socrates will do as he always does--refuse to
150	answer himself, but take and pull to pieces the answer of some one else.
150	
150	Why, my good friend, I said, how can any one answer who knows, and says
150	that he knows, just nothing; and who, even if he has some faint notions
150	of his own, is told by a man of authority not to utter them?  The
150	natural thing is, that the speaker should be some one like yourself who
150	professes to know and can tell what he knows.  Will you then kindly
150	answer, for the edification of the company and of myself?
150	
150	Glaucon and the rest of the company joined in my request and
150	Thrasymachus, as any one might see, was in reality eager to speak; for
150	he thought that he had an excellent answer, and would distinguish
150	himself.  But at first he to insist on my answering; at length he
150	consented to begin.  Behold, he said, the wisdom of Socrates; he
150	refuses to teach himself, and goes about learning of others, to whom he
150	never even says thank you.
150	
150	That I learn of others, I replied, is quite true; but that I am
150	ungrateful I wholly deny.  Money I have none, and therefore I pay in
150	praise, which is all I have:  and how ready I am to praise any one who
150	appears to me to speak well you will very soon find out when you
150	answer; for I expect that you will answer well.
150	
150	Listen, then, he said; I proclaim that justice is nothing else than the
150	interest of the stronger.  And now why do you not me?  But of course
150	you won't.
150	
150	Let me first understand you, I replied.  Justice, as you say, is the
150	interest of the stronger.  What, Thrasymachus, is the meaning of this?
150	You cannot mean to say that because Polydamas, the pancratiast, is
150	stronger than we are, and finds the eating of beef conducive to his
150	bodily strength, that to eat beef is therefore equally for our good who
150	are weaker than he is, and right and just for us?
150	
150	That's abominable of you, Socrates; you take the words in the sense
150	which is most damaging to the argument.
150	
150	Not at all, my good sir, I said; I am trying to understand them; and I
150	wish that you would be a little clearer.
150	
150	Well, he said, have you never heard that forms of government differ;
150	there are tyrannies, and there are democracies, and there are
150	aristocracies?
150	
150	Yes, I know.
150	
150	And the government is the ruling power in each state?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	And the different forms of government make laws democratical,
150	aristocratical, tyrannical, with a view to their several interests; and
150	these laws, which are made by them for their own interests, are the
150	justice which they deliver to their subjects, and him who transgresses
150	them they punish as a breaker of the law, and unjust.  And that is what
150	I mean when I say that in all states there is the same principle of
150	justice, which is the interest of the government; and as the government
150	must be supposed to have power, the only reasonable conclusion is, that
150	everywhere there is one principle of justice, which is the interest of
150	the stronger.
150	
150	Now I understand you, I said; and whether you are right or not I will
150	try to discover.  But let me remark, that in defining justice you have
150	yourself used the word 'interest' which you forbade me to use.  It is
150	true, however, that in your definition the words 'of the stronger' are
150	added.
150	
150	A small addition, you must allow, he said.
150	
150	Great or small, never mind about that:  we must first enquire whether
150	what you are saying is the truth.  Now we are both agreed that justice
150	is interest of some sort, but you go on to say 'of the stronger'; about
150	this addition I am not so sure, and must therefore consider further.
150	
150	Proceed.
150	
150	I will; and first tell me, Do you admit that it is just or subjects to
150	obey their rulers?
150	
150	I do.
150	
150	But are the rulers of states absolutely infallible, or are they
150	sometimes liable to err?
150	
150	To be sure, he replied, they are liable to err.
150	
150	Then in making their laws they may sometimes make them rightly, and
150	sometimes not?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	When they make them rightly, they make them agreeably to their
150	interest; when they are mistaken, contrary to their interest; you admit
150	that?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And the laws which they make must be obeyed by their subjects,--and
150	that is what you call justice?
150	
150	Doubtless.
150	
150	Then justice, according to your argument, is not only obedience to the
150	interest of the stronger but the reverse?
150	
150	What is that you are saying? he asked.
150	
150	I am only repeating what you are saying, I believe.  But let us
150	consider: Have we not admitted that the rulers may be mistaken about
150	their own interest in what they command, and also that to obey them is
150	justice?  Has not that been admitted?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	Then you must also have acknowledged justice not to be for the interest
150	of the stronger, when the rulers unintentionally command things to be
150	done which are to their own injury.  For if, as you say, justice is the
150	obedience which the subject renders to their commands, in that case, O
150	wisest of men, is there any escape from the conclusion that the weaker
150	are commanded to do, not what is for the interest, but what is for the
150	injury of the stronger?
150	
150	Nothing can be clearer, Socrates, said Polemarchus.
150	
150	SOCRATES - CLEITOPHON - POLEMARCHUS - THRASYMACHUS
150	
150	Yes, said Cleitophon, interposing, if you are allowed to be his witness.
150	
150	But there is no need of any witness, said Polemarchus, for Thrasymachus
150	himself acknowledges that rulers may sometimes command what is not for
150	their own interest, and that for subjects to obey them is justice.
150	
150	Yes, Polemarchus,--Thrasymachus said that for subjects to do what was
150	commanded by their rulers is just.
150	
150	Yes, Cleitophon, but he also said that justice is the interest of the
150	stronger, and, while admitting both these propositions, he further
150	acknowledged that the stronger may command the weaker who are his
150	subjects to do what is not for his own interest; whence follows that
150	justice is the injury quite as much as the interest of the stronger.
150	
150	But, said Cleitophon, he meant by the interest of the stronger what the
150	stronger thought to be his interest,--this was what the weaker had to
150	do; and this was affirmed by him to be justice.
150	
150	Those were not his words, rejoined Polemarchus.
150	
150	SOCRATES - THRASYMACHUS
150	
150	Never mind, I replied, if he now says that they are, let us accept his
150	statement.  Tell me, Thrasymachus, I said, did you mean by justice what
150	the stronger thought to be his interest, whether really so or not?
150	
150	Certainly not, he said.  Do you suppose that I call him who is mistaken
150	the stronger at the time when he is mistaken?
150	
150	Yes, I said, my impression was that you did so, when you admitted that
150	the ruler was not infallible but might be sometimes mistaken.
150	
150	You argue like an informer, Socrates.  Do you mean, for example, that
150	he who is mistaken about the sick is a physician in that he is
150	mistaken? or that he who errs in arithmetic or grammar is an
150	arithmetician or grammarian at the me when he is making the mistake, in
150	respect of the mistake?  True, we say that the physician or
150	arithmetician or grammarian has made a mistake, but this is only a way
150	of speaking; for the fact is that neither the grammarian nor any other
150	person of skill ever makes a mistake in so far as he is what his name
150	implies; they none of them err unless their skill fails them, and then
150	they cease to be skilled artists.  No artist or sage or ruler errs at
150	the time when he is what his name implies; though he is commonly said
150	to err, and I adopted the common mode of speaking.  But to be perfectly
150	accurate, since you are such a lover of accuracy, we should say that
150	the ruler, in so far as he is the ruler, is unerring, and, being
150	unerring, always commands that which is for his own interest; and the
150	subject is required to execute his commands; and therefore, as I said
150	at first and now repeat, justice is the interest of the stronger.
150	
150	Indeed, Thrasymachus, and do I really appear to you to argue like an
150	informer?
150	
150	Certainly, he replied.
150	
150	And you suppose that I ask these questions with any design of injuring
150	you in the argument?
150	
150	Nay, he replied, 'suppose' is not the word--I know it; but you will be
150	found out, and by sheer force of argument you will never prevail.
150	
150	I shall not make the attempt, my dear man; but to avoid any
150	misunderstanding occurring between us in future, let me ask, in what
150	sense do you speak of a ruler or stronger whose interest, as you were
150	saying, he being the superior, it is just that the inferior should
150	execute--is he a ruler in the popular or in the strict sense of the
150	term?
150	
150	In the strictest of all senses, he said.  And now cheat and play the
150	informer if you can; I ask no quarter at your hands.  But you never
150	will be able, never.
150	
150	And do you imagine, I said, that I am such a madman as to try and
150	cheat, Thrasymachus?  I might as well shave a lion.
150	
150	Why, he said, you made the attempt a minute ago, and you failed.
150	
150	Enough, I said, of these civilities.  It will be better that I should
150	ask you a question:  Is the physician, taken in that strict sense of
150	which you are speaking, a healer of the sick or a maker of money?  And
150	remember that I am now speaking of the true physician.
150	
150	A healer of the sick, he replied.
150	
150	And the pilot--that is to say, the true pilot--is he a captain of
150	sailors or a mere sailor?
150	
150	A captain of sailors.
150	
150	The circumstance that he sails in the ship is not to be taken into
150	account; neither is he to be called a sailor; the name pilot by which
150	he is distinguished has nothing to do with sailing, but is significant
150	of his skill and of his authority over the sailors.
150	
150	Very true, he said.
150	
150	Now, I said, every art has an interest?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	For which the art has to consider and provide?
150	
150	Yes, that is the aim of art.
150	
150	And the interest of any art is the perfection of it--this and nothing
150	else?
150	
150	What do you mean?
150	
150	I mean what I may illustrate negatively by the example of the body.
150	Suppose you were to ask me whether the body is self-sufficing or has
150	wants, I should reply:  Certainly the body has wants; for the body may
150	be ill and require to be cured, and has therefore interests to which
150	the art of medicine ministers; and this is the origin and intention of
150	medicine, as you will acknowledge.  Am I not right?
150	
150	Quite right, he replied.
150	
150	But is the art of medicine or any other art faulty or deficient in any
150	quality in the same way that the eye may be deficient in sight or the
150	ear fail of hearing, and therefore requires another art to provide for
150	the interests of seeing and hearing--has art in itself, I say, any
150	similar liability to fault or defect, and does every art require
150	another supplementary art to provide for its interests, and that
150	another and another without end?  Or have the arts to look only after
150	their own interests?  Or have they no need either of themselves or of
150	another?--having no faults or defects, they have no need to correct
150	them, either by the exercise of their own art or of any other; they
150	have only to consider the interest of their subject-matter. For every
150	art remains pure and faultless while remaining true--that is to say,
150	while perfect and unimpaired.  Take the words in your precise sense,
150	"and tell me whether I am not right."""
150	
150	Yes, clearly.
150	
150	Then medicine does not consider the interest of medicine, but the
150	interest of the body?
150	
150	True, he said.
150	
150	Nor does the art of horsemanship consider the interests of the art of
150	horsemanship, but the interests of the horse; neither do any other arts
150	care for themselves, for they have no needs; they care only for that
150	which is the subject of their art?
150	
150	True, he said.
150	
150	But surely, Thrasymachus, the arts are the superiors and rulers of
150	their own subjects?
150	
150	To this he assented with a good deal of reluctance.
150	
150	Then, I said, no science or art considers or enjoins the interest of
150	the stronger or superior, but only the interest of the subject and
150	weaker?
150	
150	He made an attempt to contest this proposition also, but finally
150	acquiesced.
150	
150	Then, I continued, no physician, in so far as he is a physician,
150	considers his own good in what he prescribes, but the good of his
150	patient; for the true physician is also a ruler having the human body
150	as a subject, and is not a mere money-maker; that has been admitted?
150	
150	Yes.
150
