150	And the pilot likewise, in the strict sense of the term, is a ruler of
150	sailors and not a mere sailor?
150	
150	That has been admitted.
150	
150	And such a pilot and ruler will provide and prescribe for the interest
150	of the sailor who is under him, and not for his own or the ruler's
150	interest?
150	
150	He gave a reluctant 'Yes.'
150	
150	Then, I said, Thrasymachus, there is no one in any rule who, in so far
150	as he is a ruler, considers or enjoins what is for his own interest,
150	but always what is for the interest of his subject or suitable to his
150	art; to that he looks, and that alone he considers in everything which
150	he says and does.
150	
150	When we had got to this point in the argument, and every one saw that
150	the definition of justice had been completely upset, Thrasymachus,
150	instead of replying to me, said:  Tell me, Socrates, have you got a
150	nurse?
150	
150	Why do you ask such a question, I said, when you ought rather to be
150	answering?
150	
150	Because she leaves you to snivel, and never wipes your nose: she has
150	not even taught you to know the shepherd from the sheep.
150	
150	What makes you say that?  I replied.
150	
150	Because you fancy that the shepherd or neatherd fattens of tends the
150	sheep or oxen with a view to their own good and not to the good of
150	himself or his master; and you further imagine that the rulers of
150	states, if they are true rulers, never think of their subjects as
150	sheep, and that they are not studying their own advantage day and
150	night.  Oh, no; and so entirely astray are you in your ideas about the
150	just and unjust as not even to know that justice and the just are in
150	reality another's good; that is to say, the interest of the ruler and
150	stronger, and the loss of the subject and servant; and injustice the
150	opposite; for the unjust is lord over the truly simple and just: he is
150	the stronger, and his subjects do what is for his interest, and
150	minister to his happiness, which is very far from being their own.
150	Consider further, most foolish Socrates, that the just is always a
150	loser in comparison with the unjust.  First of all, in private
150	contracts: wherever the unjust is the partner of the just you will find
150	that, when the partnership is dissolved, the unjust man has always more
150	and the just less.  Secondly, in their dealings with the State: when
150	there is an income tax, the just man will pay more and the unjust less
150	on the same amount of income; and when there is anything to be received
150	the one gains nothing and the other much.  Observe also what happens
150	when they take an office; there is the just man neglecting his affairs
150	and perhaps suffering other losses, and getting nothing out of the
150	public, because he is just; moreover he is hated by his friends and
150	acquaintance for refusing to serve them in unlawful ways.  But all this
150	is reversed in the case of the unjust man.  I am speaking, as before,
150	of injustice on a large scale in which the advantage of the unjust is
150	more apparent; and my meaning will be most clearly seen if we turn to
150	that highest form of injustice in which the criminal is the happiest of
150	men, and the sufferers or those who refuse to do injustice are the most
150	miserable--that is to say tyranny, which by fraud and force takes away
150	the property of others, not little by little but wholesale;
150	comprehending in one, things sacred as well as profane, private and
150	public; for which acts of wrong, if he were detected perpetrating any
150	one of them singly, he would be punished and incur great disgrace--they
150	who do such wrong in particular cases are called robbers of temples,
150	and man-stealers and burglars and swindlers and thieves.  But when a
150	man besides taking away the money of the citizens has made slaves of
150	them, then, instead of these names of reproach, he is termed happy and
150	blessed, not only by the citizens but by all who hear of his having
150	achieved the consummation of injustice.  For mankind censure injustice,
150	fearing that they may be the victims of it and not because they shrink
150	from committing it.  And thus, as I have shown, Socrates, injustice,
150	when on a sufficient scale, has more strength and freedom and mastery
150	than justice; and, as I said at first, justice is the interest of the
150	stronger, whereas injustice is a man's own profit and interest.
150	
150	Thrasymachus, when he had thus spoken, having, like a bathman, deluged
150	our ears with his words, had a mind to go away.  But the company would
150	not let him; they insisted that he should remain and defend his
150	position; and I myself added my own humble request that he would not
150	leave us.  Thrasymachus, I said to him, excellent man, how suggestive
150	are your remarks!  And are you going to run away before you have fairly
150	taught or learned whether they are true or not?  Is the attempt to
150	determine the way of man's life so small a matter in your eyes--to
150	determine how life may be passed by each one of us to the greatest
150	advantage?
150	
150	And do I differ from you, he said, as to the importance of the enquiry?
150	
150	You appear rather, I replied, to have no care or thought about us,
150	Thrasymachus--whether we live better or worse from not knowing what you
150	say you know, is to you a matter of indifference.  Prithee, friend, do
150	not keep your knowledge to yourself; we are a large party; and any
150	benefit which you confer upon us will be amply rewarded.  For my own
150	part I openly declare that I am not convinced, and that I do not
150	believe injustice to be more gainful than justice, even if uncontrolled
150	and allowed to have free play.  For, granting that there may be an
150	unjust man who is able to commit injustice either by fraud or force,
150	still this does not convince me of the superior advantage of injustice,
150	and there may be others who are in the same predicament with myself.
150	Perhaps we may be wrong; if so, you in your wisdom should convince us
150	that we are mistaken in preferring justice to injustice.
150	
150	And how am I to convince you, he said, if you are not already convinced
150	by what I have just said; what more can I do for you?  Would you have
150	me put the proof bodily into your souls?
150	
150	Heaven forbid!  I said; I would only ask you to be consistent; or, if
150	you change, change openly and let there be no deception.  For I must
150	remark, Thrasymachus, if you will recall what was previously said, that
150	although you began by defining the true physician in an exact sense,
150	you did not observe a like exactness when speaking of the shepherd; you
150	thought that the shepherd as a shepherd tends the sheep not with a view
150	to their own good, but like a mere diner or banqueter with a view to
150	the pleasures of the table; or, again, as a trader for sale in the
150	market, and not as a shepherd.  Yet surely the art of the shepherd is
150	concerned only with the good of his subjects; he has only to provide
150	the best for them, since the perfection of the art is already ensured
150	whenever all the requirements of it are satisfied.  And that was what I
150	was saying just now about the ruler.  I conceived that the art of the
150	ruler, considered as ruler, whether in a state or in private life,
150	could only regard the good of his flock or subjects; whereas you seem
150	to think that the rulers in states, that is to say, the true rulers,
150	like being in authority.
150	
150	Think!  Nay, I am sure of it.
150	
150	Then why in the case of lesser offices do men never take them willingly
150	without payment, unless under the idea that they govern for the
150	advantage not of themselves but of others?  Let me ask you a question:
150	Are not the several arts different, by reason of their each having a
150	separate function?  And, my dear illustrious friend, do say what you
150	think, that we may make a little progress.
150	
150	Yes, that is the difference, he replied.
150	
150	And each art gives us a particular good and not merely a general
150	one--medicine, for example, gives us health; navigation, safety at sea,
150	and so on?
150	
150	Yes, he said.
150	
150	And the art of payment has the special function of giving pay: but we
150	do not confuse this with other arts, any more than the art of the pilot
150	is to be confused with the art of medicine, because the health of the
150	pilot may be improved by a sea voyage.  You would not be inclined to
150	say, would you, that navigation is the art of medicine, at least if we
150	are to adopt your exact use of language?
150	
150	Certainly not.
150	
150	Or because a man is in good health when he receives pay you would not
150	say that the art of payment is medicine?
150	
150	I should say not.
150	
150	Nor would you say that medicine is the art of receiving pay because a
150	man takes fees when he is engaged in healing?
150	
150	Certainly not.
150	
150	And we have admitted, I said, that the good of each art is specially
150	confined to the art?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	Then, if there be any good which all artists have in common, that is to
150	be attributed to something of which they all have the common use?
150	
150	True, he replied.
150	
150	And when the artist is benefited by receiving pay the advantage is
150	gained by an additional use of the art of pay, which is not the art
150	professed by him?
150	
150	He gave a reluctant assent to this.
150	
150	Then the pay is not derived by the several artists from their
150	respective arts.  But the truth is, that while the art of medicine
150	gives health, and the art of the builder builds a house, another art
150	attends them which is the art of pay.  The various arts may be doing
150	their own business and benefiting that over which they preside, but
150	would the artist receive any benefit from his art unless he were paid
150	as well?
150	
150	I suppose not.
150	
150	But does he therefore confer no benefit when he works for nothing?
150	
150	Certainly, he confers a benefit.
150	
150	Then now, Thrasymachus, there is no longer any doubt that neither arts
150	nor governments provide for their own interests; but, as we were before
150	saying, they rule and provide for the interests of their subjects who
150	are the weaker and not the stronger--to their good they attend and not
150	to the good of the superior.
150	
150	And this is the reason, my dear Thrasymachus, why, as I was just now
150	saying, no one is willing to govern; because no one likes to take in
150	hand the reformation of evils which are not his concern without
150	remuneration.  For, in the execution of his work, and in giving his
150	orders to another, the true artist does not regard his own interest,
150	but always that of his subjects; and therefore in order that rulers may
150	be willing to rule, they must be paid in one of three modes of payment:
150	money, or honour, or a penalty for refusing.
150	
150	SOCRATES - GLAUCON
150	
150	What do you mean, Socrates? said Glaucon.  The first two modes of
150	payment are intelligible enough, but what the penalty is I do not
150	understand, or how a penalty can be a payment.
150	
150	You mean that you do not understand the nature of this payment which to
150	the best men is the great inducement to rule?  Of course you know that
150	ambition and avarice are held to be, as indeed they are, a disgrace?
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	And for this reason, I said, money and honour have no attraction for
150	them; good men do not wish to be openly demanding payment for governing
150	and so to get the name of hirelings, nor by secretly helping themselves
150	out of the public revenues to get the name of thieves.  And not being
150	ambitious they do not care about honour.  Wherefore necessity must be
150	laid upon them, and they must be induced to serve from the fear of
150	punishment.  And this, as I imagine, is the reason why the forwardness
150	to take office, instead of waiting to be compelled, has been deemed
150	dishonourable.  Now the worst part of the punishment is that he who
150	refuses to rule is liable to be ruled by one who is worse than himself.
150	And the fear of this, as I conceive, induces the good to take office,
150	not because they would, but because they cannot help--not under the
150	idea that they are going to have any benefit or enjoyment themselves,
150	but as a necessity, and because they are not able to commit the task of
150	ruling to any one who is better than themselves, or indeed as good.
150	For there is reason to think that if a city were composed entirely of
150	good men, then to avoid office would be as much an object of contention
150	as to obtain office is at present; then we should have plain proof that
150	the true ruler is not meant by nature to regard his own interest, but
150	that of his subjects; and every one who knew this would choose rather
150	to receive a benefit from another than to have the trouble of
150	conferring one.  So far am I from agreeing with Thrasymachus that
150	justice is the interest of the stronger.  This latter question need not
150	be further discussed at present; but when Thrasymachus says that the
150	life of the unjust is more advantageous than that of the just, his new
150	statement appears to me to be of a far more serious character.  Which
150	of us has spoken truly?  And which sort of life, Glaucon, do you prefer?
150	
150	I for my part deem the life of the just to be the more advantageous, he
150	answered.
150	
150	Did you hear all the advantages of the unjust which Thrasymachus was
150	rehearsing?
150	
150	Yes, I heard him, he replied, but he has not convinced me.
150	
150	Then shall we try to find some way of convincing him, if we can, that
150	he is saying what is not true?
150	
150	Most certainly, he replied.
150	
150	If, I said, he makes a set speech and we make another recounting all
150	the advantages of being just, and he answers and we rejoin, there must
150	be a numbering and measuring of the goods which are claimed on either
150	side, and in the end we shall want judges to decide; but if we proceed
150	in our enquiry as we lately did, by making admissions to one another,
150	we shall unite the offices of judge and advocate in our own persons.
150	
150	Very good, he said.
150	
150	And which method do I understand you to prefer?  I said.
150	
150	That which you propose.
150	
150	Well, then, Thrasymachus, I said, suppose you begin at the beginning
150	and answer me.  You say that perfect injustice is more gainful than
150	perfect justice?
150	
150	SOCRATES - GLAUCON - THRASYMACHUS
150	
150	Yes, that is what I say, and I have given you my reasons.
150	
150	And what is your view about them?  Would you call one of them virtue
150	and the other vice?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	I suppose that you would call justice virtue and injustice vice?
150	
150	What a charming notion!  So likely too, seeing that I affirm injustice
150	to be profitable and justice not.
150	
150	What else then would you say?
150	
150	The opposite, he replied.
150	
150	And would you call justice vice?
150	
150	No, I would rather say sublime simplicity.
150	
150	Then would you call injustice malignity?
150	
150	No; I would rather say discretion.
150	
150	And do the unjust appear to you to be wise and good?
150	
150	Yes, he said; at any rate those of them who are able to be perfectly
150	unjust, and who have the power of subduing states and nations; but
150	perhaps you imagine me to be talking of cutpurses.
150	
150	Even this profession if undetected has advantages, though they are not
150	to be compared with those of which I was just now speaking.
150	
150	I do not think that I misapprehend your meaning, Thrasymachus, I
150	replied; but still I cannot hear without amazement that you class
150	injustice with wisdom and virtue, and justice with the opposite.
150	
150	Certainly I do so class them.
150	
150	Now, I said, you are on more substantial and almost unanswerable
150	ground; for if the injustice which you were maintaining to be
150	profitable had been admitted by you as by others to be vice and
150	deformity, an answer might have been given to you on received
150	principles; but now I perceive that you will call injustice honourable
150	and strong, and to the unjust you will attribute all the qualities
150	which were attributed by us before to the just, seeing that you do not
150	hesitate to rank injustice with wisdom and virtue.
150	
150	You have guessed most infallibly, he replied.
150	
150	Then I certainly ought not to shrink from going through with the
150	argument so long as I have reason to think that you, Thrasymachus, are
150	speaking your real mind; for I do believe that you are now in earnest
150	and are not amusing yourself at our expense.
150	
150	I may be in earnest or not, but what is that to you?--to refute the
150	argument is your business.
150	
150	Very true, I said; that is what I have to do:  But will you be so good
150	as answer yet one more question?  Does the just man try to gain any
150	advantage over the just?
150	
150	Far otherwise; if he did would not be the simple, amusing creature
150	which he is.
150	
150	And would he try to go beyond just action?
150	
150	He would not.
150	
150	And how would he regard the attempt to gain an advantage over the
150	unjust; would that be considered by him as just or unjust?
150	
150	He would think it just, and would try to gain the advantage; but he
150	would not be able.
150	
150	Whether he would or would not be able, I said, is not to the point.  My
150	question is only whether the just man, while refusing to have more than
150	another just man, would wish and claim to have more than the unjust?
150	
150	Yes, he would.
150	
150	And what of the unjust--does he claim to have more than the just man
150	and to do more than is just.
150	
150	Of course, he said, for he claims to have more than all men.
150	
150	And the unjust man will strive and struggle to obtain more than the
150	unjust man or action, in order that he may have more than all?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	We may put the matter thus, I said--the just does not desire more than
150	his like but more than his unlike, whereas the unjust desires more than
150	both his like and his unlike?
150	
150	Nothing, he said, can be better than that statement.
150	
150	And the unjust is good and wise, and the just is neither?
150	
150	Good again, he said.
150	
150	And is not the unjust like the wise and good and the just unlike them?
150	
150	Of course, he said, he who is of a certain nature, is like those who
150	are of a certain nature; he who is not, not.
150	
150	Each of them, I said, is such as his like is?
150	
150	Certainly, he replied.
150	
150	Very good, Thrasymachus, I said; and now to take the case of the arts:
150	you would admit that one man is a musician and another not a musician?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And which is wise and which is foolish?
150	
150	Clearly the musician is wise, and he who is not a musician is foolish.
150	
150	And he is good in as far as he is wise, and bad in as far as he is
150	foolish?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And you would say the same sort of thing of the physician?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And do you think, my excellent friend, that a musician when he adjusts
150	the lyre would desire or claim to exceed or go beyond a musician in the
150	tightening and loosening the strings?
150	
150	I do not think that he would.
150	
150	But he would claim to exceed the non-musician?
150	
150	Of course.
150	
150	And what would you say of the physician?  In prescribing meats and
150	drinks would he wish to go beyond another physician or beyond the
150	practice of medicine?
150	
150	He would not.
150	
150	But he would wish to go beyond the non-physician?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And about knowledge and ignorance in general; see whether you think
150	that any man who has knowledge ever would wish to have the choice of
150	saying or doing more than another man who has knowledge.  Would he not
150	rather say or do the same as his like in the same case?
150	
150	That, I suppose, can hardly be denied.
150	
150	And what of the ignorant? would he not desire to have more than either
150	the knowing or the ignorant?
150	
150	I dare say.
150	
150	And the knowing is wise?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And the wise is good?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	Then the wise and good will not desire to gain more than his like, but
150	more than his unlike and opposite?
150	
150	I suppose so.
150	
150	Whereas the bad and ignorant will desire to gain more than both?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	But did we not say, Thrasymachus, that the unjust goes beyond both his
150	like and unlike?  Were not these your words?  They were.
150	
150	They were.
150	
150	And you also said that the lust will not go beyond his like but his
150	unlike?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	Then the just is like the wise and good, and the unjust like the evil
150	and ignorant?
150	
150	That is the inference.
150	
150	And each of them is such as his like is?
150	
150	That was admitted.
150	
150	Then the just has turned out to be wise and good and the unjust evil
150	and ignorant.
150	
150	Thrasymachus made all these admissions, not fluently, as I repeat them,
150	but with extreme reluctance; it was a hot summer's day, and the
150	perspiration poured from him in torrents; and then I saw what I had
150	never seen before, Thrasymachus blushing.  As we were now agreed that
150	justice was virtue and wisdom, and injustice vice and ignorance, I
150	proceeded to another point:
150	
150	Well, I said, Thrasymachus, that matter is now settled; but were we not
150	also saying that injustice had strength; do you remember?
150	
150	Yes, I remember, he said, but do not suppose that I approve of what you
150	are saying or have no answer; if however I were to answer, you would be
150	quite certain to accuse me of haranguing; therefore either permit me to
150	have my say out, or if you would rather ask, do so, and I will answer
150	'Very good,' as they say to story-telling old women, and will nod 'Yes'
150	and 'No.'
150	
150	Certainly not, I said, if contrary to your real opinion.
150	
150	Yes, he said, I will, to please you, since you will not let me speak.
150	What else would you have?
150	
150	Nothing in the world, I said; and if you are so disposed I will ask and
150	you shall answer.
150	
150	Proceed.
150	
150	Then I will repeat the question which I asked before, in order that our
150	examination of the relative nature of justice and injustice may be
150	carried on regularly.  A statement was made that injustice is stronger
150	and more powerful than justice, but now justice, having been identified
150	with wisdom and virtue, is easily shown to be stronger than injustice,
150	if injustice is ignorance; this can no longer be questioned by any one.
150	But I want to view the matter, Thrasymachus, in a different way: You
150	would not deny that a state may be unjust and may be unjustly
150	attempting to enslave other states, or may have already enslaved them,
150	and may be holding many of them in subjection?
150	
150	True, he replied; and I will add the best and perfectly unjust state
150	will be most likely to do so.
150	
150	I know, I said, that such was your position; but what I would further
150	consider is, whether this power which is possessed by the superior
150	state can exist or be exercised without justice.
150	
150	If you are right in you view, and justice is wisdom, then only with
150	justice; but if I am right, then without justice.
150	
150	I am delighted, Thrasymachus, to see you not only nodding assent and
150	dissent, but making answers which are quite excellent.
150	
150	That is out of civility to you, he replied.
150	
150	You are very kind, I said; and would you have the goodness also to
150	inform me, whether you think that a state, or an army, or a band of
150	robbers and thieves, or any other gang of evil-doers could act at all
150	if they injured one another?
150	
150	No indeed, he said, they could not.
150	
150	But if they abstained from injuring one another, then they might act
150	together better?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And this is because injustice creates divisions and hatreds and
150	fighting, and justice imparts harmony and friendship; is not that true,
150	Thrasymachus?
150	
150	I agree, he said, because I do not wish to quarrel with you.
150	
150	How good of you, I said; but I should like to know also whether
150	injustice, having this tendency to arouse hatred, wherever existing,
150	among slaves or among freemen, will not make them hate one another and
150	set them at variance and render them incapable of common action?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	And even if injustice be found in two only, will they not quarrel and
150	fight, and become enemies to one another and to the just.
150	
150	They will.
150	
150	And suppose injustice abiding in a single person, would your wisdom say
150	that she loses or that she retains her natural power?
150	
150	Let us assume that she retains her power.
150	
150	Yet is not the power which injustice exercises of such a nature that
150	wherever she takes up her abode, whether in a city, in an army, in a
150	family, or in any other body, that body is, to begin with, rendered
150	incapable of united action by reason of sedition and distraction; and
150	does it not become its own enemy and at variance with all that opposes
150	it, and with the just?  Is not this the case?
150	
150	Yes, certainly.
150	
150	And is not injustice equally fatal when existing in a single person; in
150	the first place rendering him incapable of action because he is not at
150	unity with himself, and in the second place making him an enemy to
150	himself and the just?  Is not that true, Thrasymachus?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And O my friend, I said, surely the gods are just?
150	
150	Granted that they are.
150	
150	But if so, the unjust will be the enemy of the gods, and the just will
150	be their friend?
150	
150	Feast away in triumph, and take your fill of the argument; I will not
150	oppose you, lest I should displease the company.
150	
150	Well then, proceed with your answers, and let me have the remainder of
150	my repast.  For we have already shown that the just are clearly wiser
150	and better and abler than the unjust, and that the unjust are incapable
150	of common action; nay ing at more, that to speak as we did of men who
150	are evil acting at any time vigorously together, is not strictly true,
150	for if they had been perfectly evil, they would have laid hands upon
150	one another; but it is evident that there must have been some remnant
150	of justice in them, which enabled them to combine; if there had not
150	been they would have injured one another as well as their victims; they
150	were but half--villains in their enterprises; for had they been whole
150	villains, and utterly unjust, they would have been utterly incapable of
150	action.  That, as I believe, is the truth of the matter, and not what
150	you said at first.  But whether the just have a better and happier life
150	than the unjust is a further question which we also proposed to
150	consider.  I think that they have, and for the reasons which to have
150	given; but still I should like to examine further, for no light matter
150	is at stake, nothing less than the rule of human life.
150	
150	Proceed.
150	
150	I will proceed by asking a question:  Would you not say that a horse
150	has some end?
150	
150	I should.
150	
150	And the end or use of a horse or of anything would be that which could
150	not be accomplished, or not so well accomplished, by any other thing?
150	
150	I do not understand, he said.
150	
150	Let me explain:  Can you see, except with the eye?
150	
150	Certainly not.
150	
150	Or hear, except with the ear?
150	
150	No.
150	
150	These then may be truly said to be the ends of these organs?
150	
150	They may.
150	
150	But you can cut off a vine-branch with a dagger or with a chisel, and
150	in many other ways?
150	
150	Of course.
150	
150	And yet not so well as with a pruning-hook made for the purpose?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	May we not say that this is the end of a pruning-hook?
150	
150	We may.
150	
150	Then now I think you will have no difficulty in understanding my
150	meaning when I asked the question whether the end of anything would be
150	that which could not be accomplished, or not so well accomplished, by
150	any other thing?
150	
150	I understand your meaning, he said, and assent.
150	
150	And that to which an end is appointed has also an excellence?  Need I
150	ask again whether the eye has an end?
150	
150	It has.
150	
150	And has not the eye an excellence?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And the ear has an end and an excellence also?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	And the same is true of all other things; they have each of them an end
150	and a special excellence?
150	
150	That is so.
150	
150	Well, and can the eyes fulfil their end if they are wanting in their
150	own proper excellence and have a defect instead?
150	
150	How can they, he said, if they are blind and cannot see?
150	
150	You mean to say, if they have lost their proper excellence, which is
150	sight; but I have not arrived at that point yet.  I would rather ask
150	the question more generally, and only enquire whether the things which
150	fulfil their ends fulfil them by their own proper excellence, and fall
150	of fulfilling them by their own defect?
150	
150	Certainly, he replied.
150	
150	I might say the same of the ears; when deprived of their own proper
150	excellence they cannot fulfil their end?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	And the same observation will apply to all other things?
150	
150	I agree.
150	
150	Well; and has not the soul an end which nothing else can fulfil?  for
150	example, to superintend and command and deliberate and the like.  Are
150	not these functions proper to the soul, and can they rightly be
150	assigned to any other?
150	
150	To no other.
150	
150	And is not life to be reckoned among the ends of the soul?
150	
150	Assuredly, he said.
150	
150	And has not the soul an excellence also?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And can she or can she not fulfil her own ends when deprived of that
150	excellence?
150	
150	She cannot.
150	
150	Then an evil soul must necessarily be an evil ruler and superintendent,
150	and the good soul a good ruler?
150	
150	Yes, necessarily.
150	
150	And we have admitted that justice is the excellence of the soul, and
150	injustice the defect of the soul?
150	
150	That has been admitted.
150	
150	Then the just soul and the just man will live well, and the unjust man
150	will live ill?
150	
150	That is what your argument proves.
150	
150	And he who lives well is blessed and happy, and he who lives ill the
150	reverse of happy?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	Then the just is happy, and the unjust miserable?
150	
150	So be it.
150	
150	But happiness and not misery is profitable.
150	
150	Of course.
150	
150	Then, my blessed Thrasymachus, injustice can never be more profitable
150	than justice.
150	
150	Let this, Socrates, he said, be your entertainment at the Bendidea.
150	
150	For which I am indebted to you, I said, now that you have grown gentle
150	towards me and have left off scolding.  Nevertheless, I have not been
150	well entertained; but that was my own fault and not yours.  As an
150	epicure snatches a taste of every dish which is successively brought to
150	table, he not having allowed himself time to enjoy the one before, so
150	have I gone from one subject to another without having discovered what
150	I sought at first, the nature of justice.  I left that enquiry and
150	turned away to consider whether justice is virtue and wisdom or evil
150	and folly; and when there arose a further question about the
150	comparative advantages of justice and injustice, I could not refrain
150	from passing on to that.  And the result of the whole discussion has
150	been that I know nothing at all.  For I know not what justice is, and
150	therefore I am not likely to know whether it is or is not a virtue, nor
150	can I say whether the just man is happy or unhappy.
150	
150	
150	
150	
150	BOOK II
150	
150	
150	SOCRATES - GLAUCON
150	
150	WITH these words I was thinking that I had made an end of the
150	discussion; but the end, in truth, proved to be only a beginning.  For
150	Glaucon, who is always the most pugnacious of men, was dissatisfied at
150	Thrasymachus' retirement; he wanted to have the battle out.  So he said
150	to me:  Socrates, do you wish really to persuade us, or only to seem to
150	have persuaded us, that to be just is always better than to be unjust?
150	
150	I should wish really to persuade you, I replied, if I could.
150	
150	Then you certainly have not succeeded.  Let me ask you now:--How would
150	you arrange goods--are there not some which we welcome for their own
150	sakes, and independently of their consequences, as, for example,
150	harmless pleasures and enjoyments, which delight us at the time,
150	although nothing follows from them?
150	
150	I agree in thinking that there is such a class, I replied.
150	
150	Is there not also a second class of goods, such as knowledge, sight,
150	health, which are desirable not only in themselves, but also for their
150	results?
150	
150	Certainly, I said.
150	
150	And would you not recognize a third class, such as gymnastic, and the
150	care of the sick, and the physician's art; also the various ways of
150	money-making--these do us good but we regard them as disagreeable; and
150	no one would choose them for their own sakes, but only for the sake of
150	some reward or result which flows from them?
150	
150	There is, I said, this third class also.  But why do you ask?
150	
150	Because I want to know in which of the three classes you would place
150	justice?
150	
150	In the highest class, I replied,--among those goods which he who would
150	be happy desires both for their own sake and for the sake of their
150	results.
150	
150	Then the many are of another mind; they think that justice is to be
150	reckoned in the troublesome class, among goods which are to be pursued
150	for the sake of rewards and of reputation, but in themselves are
150	disagreeable and rather to be avoided.
150	
150	I know, I said, that this is their manner of thinking, and that this
150	was the thesis which Thrasymachus was maintaining just now, when he
150	censured justice and praised injustice.  But I am too stupid to be
150	convinced by him.
150	
150	I wish, he said, that you would hear me as well as him, and then I
150	shall see whether you and I agree.  For Thrasymachus seems to me, like
150	a snake, to have been charmed by your voice sooner than he ought to
150	have been; but to my mind the nature of justice and injustice have not
150	yet been made clear.  Setting aside their rewards and results, I want
150	to know what they are in themselves, and how they inwardly work in the
150	soul.  If you, please, then, I will revive the argument of
150	Thrasymachus.  And first I will speak of the nature and origin of
150	justice according to the common view of them.  Secondly, I will show
150	that all men who practise justice do so against their will, of
150	necessity, but not as a good.  And thirdly, I will argue that there is
150	reason in this view, for the life of the unjust is after all better far
150	than the life of the just--if what they say is true, Socrates, since I
150	myself am not of their opinion.  But still I acknowledge that I am
150	perplexed when I hear the voices of Thrasymachus and myriads of others
150	dinning in my ears; and, on the other hand, I have never yet heard the
150	superiority of justice to injustice maintained by any one in a
150	satisfactory way.  I want to hear justice praised in respect of itself;
150	then I shall be satisfied, and you are the person from whom I think
150	that I am most likely to hear this; and therefore I will praise the
150	unjust life to the utmost of my power, and my manner of speaking will
150	indicate the manner in which I desire to hear you too praising justice
150	and censuring injustice.  Will you say whether you approve of my
150	proposal?
150	
150	Indeed I do; nor can I imagine any theme about which a man of sense
150	would oftener wish to converse.
150	
150	I am delighted, he replied, to hear you say so, and shall begin by
150	speaking, as I proposed, of the nature and origin of justice.
150	
150	GLAUCON
150	
150	They say that to do injustice is, by nature, good; to suffer injustice,
150	evil; but that the evil is greater than the good.  And so when men have
150	both done and suffered injustice and have had experience of both, not
150	being able to avoid the one and obtain the other, they think that they
150	had better agree among themselves to have neither; hence there arise
150	laws and mutual covenants; and that which is ordained by law is termed
150	by them lawful and just.  This they affirm to be the origin and nature
150	of justice;--it is a mean or compromise, between the best of all, which
150	is to do injustice and not be punished, and the worst of all, which is
150	to suffer injustice without the power of retaliation; and justice,
150	being at a middle point between the two, is tolerated not as a good,
150	but as the lesser evil, and honoured by reason of the inability of men
150	to do injustice.  For no man who is worthy to be called a man would
150	ever submit to such an agreement if he were able to resist; he would be
150	mad if he did.  Such is the received account, Socrates, of the nature
150	and origin of justice.
150	
150	Now that those who practise justice do so involuntarily and because
150	they have not the power to be unjust will best appear if we imagine
150	something of this kind:  having given both to the just and the unjust
150	power to do what they will, let us watch and see whither desire will
150	lead them; then we shall discover in the very act the just and unjust
150	man to be proceeding along the same road, following their interest,
150	which all natures deem to be their good, and are only diverted into the
150	path of justice by the force of law.  The liberty which we are
150	supposing may be most completely given to them in the form of such a
150	power as is said to have been possessed by Gyges the ancestor of
150	Croesus the Lydian.  According to the tradition, Gyges was a shepherd
150	in the service of the king of Lydia; there was a great storm, and an
150	earthquake made an opening in the earth at the place where he was
150	feeding his flock.  Amazed at the sight, he descended into the opening,
150	where, among other marvels, he beheld a hollow brazen horse, having
150	doors, at which he stooping and looking in saw a dead body of stature,
150	as appeared to him, more than human, and having nothing on but a gold
150	ring; this he took from the finger of the dead and reascended.  Now the
150	shepherds met together, according to custom, that they might send their
150	monthly report about the flocks to the king; into their assembly he
150	came having the ring on his finger, and as he was sitting among them he
150	chanced to turn the collet of the ring inside his hand, when instantly
150	he became invisible to the rest of the company and they began to speak
150	of him as if he were no longer present.  He was astonished at this, and
150	again touching the ring he turned the collet outwards and reappeared;
150	he made several trials of the ring, and always with the same
150	result-when he turned the collet inwards he became invisible, when
150	outwards he reappeared.  Whereupon he contrived to be chosen one of the
150	messengers who were sent to the court; where as soon as he arrived he
150	seduced the queen, and with her help conspired against the king and
150	slew him, and took the kingdom.  Suppose now that there were two such
150	magic rings, and the just put on one of them and the unjust the other;
150	no man can be imagined to be of such an iron nature that he would stand
150	fast in justice.  No man would keep his hands off what was not his own
150	when he could safely take what he liked out of the market, or go into
150	houses and lie with any one at his pleasure, or kill or release from
150	prison whom he would, and in all respects be like a God among men.
150	Then the actions of the just would be as the actions of the unjust;
150	they would both come at last to the same point.  And this we may truly
150	affirm to be a great proof that a man is just, not willingly or because
150	he thinks that justice is any good to him individually, but of
150	necessity, for wherever any one thinks that he can safely be unjust,
150	there he is unjust.  For all men believe in their hearts that injustice
150	is far more profitable to the individual than justice, and he who
150	argues as I have been supposing, will say that they are right.  If you
150	could imagine any one obtaining this power of becoming invisible, and
150	never doing any wrong or touching what was another's, he would be
150	thought by the lookers-on to be a most wretched idiot, although they
150	would praise him to one another's faces, and keep up appearances with
150	one another from a fear that they too might suffer injustice.  Enough
150	of this.
150	
150	Now, if we are to form a real judgment of the life of the just and
150	unjust, we must isolate them; there is no other way; and how is the
150	isolation to be effected?  I answer:  Let the unjust man be entirely
150	unjust, and the just man entirely just; nothing is to be taken away
150	from either of them, and both are to be perfectly furnished for the
150	work of their respective lives.  First, let the unjust be like other
150	distinguished masters of craft; like the skilful pilot or physician,
150	who knows intuitively his own powers and keeps within their limits, and
150	who, if he fails at any point, is able to recover himself.  So let the
150	unjust make his unjust attempts in the right way, and lie hidden if he
150	means to be great in his injustice (he who is found out is nobody): for
150	the highest reach of injustice is: to be deemed just when you are not.
150	Therefore I say that in the perfectly unjust man we must assume the
150	most perfect injustice; there is to be no deduction, but we must allow
150	him, while doing the most unjust acts, to have acquired the greatest
150	reputation for justice.  If he have taken a false step he must be able
150	to recover himself; he must be one who can speak with effect, if any of
150	his deeds come to light, and who can force his way where force is
150	required his courage and strength, and command of money and friends.
150	And at his side let us place the just man in his nobleness and
150	simplicity, wishing, as Aeschylus says, to be and not to seem good.
150	There must be no seeming, for if he seem to be just he will be honoured
150	and rewarded, and then we shall not know whether he is just for the
150	sake of justice or for the sake of honours and rewards; therefore, let
150	him be clothed in justice only, and have no other covering; and he must
150	be imagined in a state of life the opposite of the former.  Let him be
150	the best of men, and let him be thought the worst; then he will have
150	been put to the proof; and we shall see whether he will be affected by
150	the fear of infamy and its consequences.  And let him continue thus to
150	the hour of death; being just and seeming to be unjust.  When both have
150	reached the uttermost extreme, the one of justice and the other of
150	injustice, let judgment be given which of them is the happier of the
150	two.
150	
150	SOCRATES - GLAUCON
150	
150	Heavens! my dear Glaucon, I said, how energetically you polish them up
150	for the decision, first one and then the other, as if they were two
150	statues.
150	
150	I do my best, he said.  And now that we know what they are like there
150	is no difficulty in tracing out the sort of life which awaits either of
150	them.  This I will proceed to describe; but as you may think the
150	description a little too coarse, I ask you to suppose, Socrates, that
150	the words which follow are not mine.-- Let me put them into the mouths
150	of the eulogists of injustice: They will tell you that the just man who
150	is thought unjust will be scourged, racked, bound--will have his eyes
150	burnt out; and, at last, after suffering every kind of evil, he will be
150	impaled:  Then he will understand that he ought to seem only, and not
150	to be, just; the words of Aeschylus may be more truly spoken of the
150	unjust than of the just.  For the unjust is pursuing a reality; he does
150	not live with a view to appearances--he wants to be really unjust and
150	not to seem only:--
150	
150	    His mind has a soil deep and fertile,
150	    Out of which spring his prudent counsels.
150	
150	In the first place, he is thought just, and therefore bears rule in the
150	city; he can marry whom he will, and give in marriage to whom he will;
150	also he can trade and deal where he likes, and always to his own
150	advantage, because he has no misgivings about injustice and at every
150	contest, whether in public or private, he gets the better of his
150	antagonists, and gains at their expense, and is rich, and out of his
150	gains he can benefit his friends, and harm his enemies; moreover, he
150	can offer sacrifices, and dedicate gifts to the gods abundantly and
150	magnificently, and can honour the gods or any man whom he wants to
150	honour in a far better style than the just, and therefore he is likely
150	to be dearer than they are to the gods.  And thus, Socrates, gods and
150	men are said to unite in making the life of the unjust better than the
150	life of the just.
150	
150	ADEIMANTUS - SOCRATES
150	
150	I was going to say something in answer to Glaucon, when Adeimantus, his
150	brother, interposed:  Socrates, he said, you do not suppose that there
150	is nothing more to be urged?
150	
150	Why, what else is there?  I answered.
150	
150	The strongest point of all has not been even mentioned, he replied.
150	
150	Well, then, according to the proverb, 'Let brother help brother'--if he
150	fails in any part do you assist him; although I must confess that
150	Glaucon has already said quite enough to lay me in the dust, and take
150	from me the power of helping justice.
150	
150	ADEIMANTUS
150	
150	Nonsense, he replied.  But let me add something more:  There is another
150	side to Glaucon's argument about the praise and censure of justice and
150	injustice, which is equally required in order to bring out what I
150	believe to be his meaning.  Parents and tutors are always telling their
150	sons and their wards that they are to be just; but why? not for the
150	sake of justice, but for the sake of character and reputation; in the
150	hope of obtaining for him who is reputed just some of those offices,
150	marriages, and the like which Glaucon has enumerated among the
150	advantages accruing to the unjust from the reputation of justice.
150	More, however, is made of appearances by this class of persons than by
150	the others; for they throw in the good opinion of the gods, and will
150	tell you of a shower of benefits which the heavens, as they say, rain
150	upon the pious; and this accords with the testimony of the noble Hesiod
150	and Homer, the first of whom says, that the gods make the oaks of the
150	just--
150	
150	    To hear acorns at their summit, and bees I the middle;
150	    And the sheep the bowed down bowed the with the their fleeces.
150	
150	and many other blessings of a like kind are provided for them.  And
150	Homer has a very similar strain; for he speaks of one whose fame is--
150	
150	    As the fame of some blameless king who, like a god,
150	    Maintains justice to whom the black earth brings forth
150	    Wheat and barley, whose trees are bowed with fruit,
150	    And his sheep never fail to bear, and the sea gives him fish.
150	
150	Still grander are the gifts of heaven which Musaeus and his son
150	vouchsafe to the just; they take them down into the world below, where
150	they have the saints lying on couches at a feast, everlastingly drunk,
150	crowned with garlands; their idea seems to be that an immortality of
150	drunkenness is the highest meed of virtue.  Some extend their rewards
150	yet further; the posterity, as they say, of the faithful and just shall
150	survive to the third and fourth generation.  This is the style in which
150	they praise justice.  But about the wicked there is another strain;
150	they bury them in a slough in Hades, and make them carry water in a
150	sieve; also while they are yet living they bring them to infamy, and
150	inflict upon them the punishments which Glaucon described as the
150	portion of the just who are reputed to be unjust; nothing else does
150	their invention supply.  Such is their manner of praising the one and
150	censuring the other.
150	
150	Once more, Socrates, I will ask you to consider another way of speaking
150	about justice and injustice, which is not confined to the poets, but is
150	found in prose writers.  The universal voice of mankind is always
150	declaring that justice and virtue are honourable, but grievous and
150	toilsome; and that the pleasures of vice and injustice are easy of
150	attainment, and are only censured by law and opinion.  They say also
150	that honesty is for the most part less profitable than dishonesty; and
150	they are quite ready to call wicked men happy, and to honour them both
150	in public and private when they are rich or in any other way
150	influential, while they despise and overlook those who may be weak and
150	poor, even though acknowledging them to be better than the others.  But
150	most extraordinary of all is their mode of speaking about virtue and
150	the gods: they say that the gods apportion calamity and misery to many
150	good men, and good and happiness to the wicked.  And mendicant prophets
150	go to rich men's doors and persuade them that they have a power
150	committed to them by the gods of making an atonement for a man's own or
150	his ancestor's sins by sacrifices or charms, with rejoicings and
150	feasts; and they promise to harm an enemy, whether just or unjust, at a
150	small cost; with magic arts and incantations binding heaven, as they
150	say, to execute their will.  And the poets are the authorities to whom
150	they appeal, now smoothing the path of vice with the words of Hesiod;--
150	
150	    Vice may be had in abundance without trouble; the way is smooth
150	    and her dwelling-place is near. But before virtue the gods have
150	    set toil,
150	
150	and a tedious and uphill road:  then citing Homer as a witness that the
150	gods may be influenced by men; for he also says:
150	
150	    The gods, too, may he turned from their purpose; and men pray to
150	    them and avert their wrath by sacrifices and soothing entreaties,
150	    and by libations and the odour of fat, when they have sinned and
150	    transgressed.
150	
150	And they produce a host of books written by Musaeus and Orpheus, who
150	were children of the Moon and the Muses--that is what they
150	say--according to which they perform their ritual, and persuade not
150	only individuals, but whole cities, that expiations and atonements for
150	sin may be made by sacrifices and amusements which fill a vacant hour,
150	and are equally at the service of the living and the dead; the latter
150	sort they call mysteries, and they redeem us from the pains of hell,
150	but if we neglect them no one knows what awaits us.
150	
150	He proceeded:  And now when the young hear all this said about virtue
150	and vice, and the way in which gods and men regard them, how are their
150	minds likely to be affected, my dear Socrates,--those of them, I mean,
150	who are quickwitted, and, like bees on the wing, light on every flower,
150	and from all that they hear are prone to draw conclusions as to what
150	manner of persons they should be and in what way they should walk if
150	they would make the best of life?  Probably the youth will say to
150	himself in the words of Pindar--
150	
150	    Can I by justice or by crooked ways of deceit ascend a loftier
150	    tower which may he a fortress to me all my days?
150	
150	For what men say is that, if I am really just and am not also thought
150	just profit there is none, but the pain and loss on the other hand are
150	unmistakable.  But if, though unjust, I acquire the reputation of
150	justice, a heavenly life is promised to me.  Since then, as
150	philosophers prove, appearance tyrannizes over truth and is lord of
150	happiness, to appearance I must devote myself.  I will describe around
150	me a picture and shadow of virtue to be the vestibule and exterior of
150	my house; behind I will trail the subtle and crafty fox, as
150	Archilochus, greatest of sages, recommends.  But I hear some one
150	exclaiming that the concealment of wickedness is often difficult; to
150	which I answer, Nothing great is easy.  Nevertheless, the argument
150	indicates this, if we would be happy, to be the path along which we
150	should proceed.  With a view to concealment we will establish secret
150	brotherhoods and political clubs.  And there are professors of rhetoric
150	who teach the art of persuading courts and assemblies; and so, partly
150	by persuasion and partly by force, I shall make unlawful gains and not
150	be punished.  Still I hear a voice saying that the gods cannot be
150	deceived, neither can they be compelled.  But what if there are no
150	gods? or, suppose them to have no care of human things--why in either
150	case should we mind about concealment?  And even if there are gods, and
150	they do care about us, yet we know of them only from tradition and the
150	genealogies of the poets; and these are the very persons who say that
150	they may be influenced and turned by 'sacrifices and soothing
150	entreaties and by offerings.' Let us be consistent then, and believe
150	both or neither.  If the poets speak truly, why then we had better be
150	unjust, and offer of the fruits of injustice; for if we are just,
150	although we may escape the vengeance of heaven, we shall lose the gains
150	of injustice; but, if we are unjust, we shall keep the gains, and by
150	our sinning and praying, and praying and sinning, the gods will be
150	propitiated, and we shall not be punished.  'But there is a world below
150	in which either we or our posterity will suffer for our unjust deeds.'
150	Yes, my friend, will be the reflection, but there are mysteries and
150	atoning deities, and these have great power.  That is what mighty
150	cities declare; and the children of the gods, who were their poets and
150	prophets, bear a like testimony.
150	
150	On what principle, then, shall we any longer choose justice rather than
150	the worst injustice? when, if we only unite the latter with a deceitful
150	regard to appearances, we shall fare to our mind both with gods and
150	men, in life and after death, as the most numerous and the highest
150	authorities tell us.  Knowing all this, Socrates, how can a man who has
150	any superiority of mind or person or rank or wealth, be willing to
150	honour justice; or indeed to refrain from laughing when he hears
150	justice praised?  And even if there should be some one who is able to
150	disprove the truth of my words, and who is satisfied that justice is
150	best, still he is not angry with the unjust, but is very ready to
150	forgive them, because he also knows that men are not just of their own
150	free will; unless, peradventure, there be some one whom the divinity
150	within him may have inspired with a hatred of injustice, or who has
150	attained knowledge of the truth--but no other man.  He only blames
150	injustice who, owing to cowardice or age or some weakness, has not the
150	power of being unjust.  And this is proved by the fact that when he
150	obtains the power, he immediately becomes unjust as far as he can be.
150	
150	The cause of all this, Socrates, was indicated by us at the beginning
150	of the argument, when my brother and I told you how astonished we were
150	to find that of all the professing panegyrists of justice--beginning
150	with the ancient heroes of whom any memorial has been preserved to us,
150	and ending with the men of our own time--no one has ever blamed
150	injustice or praised justice except with a view to the glories,
150	honours, and benefits which flow from them.  No one has ever adequately
150	described either in verse or prose the true essential nature of either
150	of them abiding in the soul, and invisible to any human or divine eye;
150	or shown that of all the things of a man's soul which he has within
150	him, justice is the greatest good, and injustice the greatest evil.
150	Had this been the universal strain, had you sought to persuade us of
150	this from our youth upwards, we should not have been on the watch to
150	keep one another from doing wrong, but every one would have been his
150	own watchman, because afraid, if he did wrong, of harbouring in himself
150	the greatest of evils.  I dare say that Thrasymachus and others would
150	seriously hold the language which I have been merely repeating, and
150	words even stronger than these about justice and injustice, grossly, as
150	I conceive, perverting their true nature.  But I speak in this vehement
150	manner, as I must frankly confess to you, because I want to hear from
150	you the opposite side; and I would ask you to show not only the
150	superiority which justice has over injustice, but what effect they have
150	on the possessor of them which makes the one to be a good and the other
150	an evil to him.  And please, as Glaucon requested of you, to exclude
150	reputations; for unless you take away from each of them his true
150	reputation and add on the false, we shall say that you do not praise
150	justice, but the appearance of it; we shall think that you are only
150	exhorting us to keep injustice dark, and that you really agree with
150	Thrasymachus in thinking that justice is another's good and the
150	interest of the stronger, and that injustice is a man's own profit and
150	interest, though injurious to the weaker.  Now as you have admitted
150	that justice is one of that highest class of goods which are desired
150	indeed for their results, but in a far greater degree for their own
150	sakes--like sight or hearing or knowledge or health, or any other real
150	and natural and not merely conventional good--I would ask you in your
150	praise of justice to regard one point only: I mean the essential good
150	and evil which justice and injustice work in the possessors of them.
150	Let others praise justice and censure injustice, magnifying the rewards
150	and honours of the one and abusing the other; that is a manner of
150	arguing which, coming from them, I am ready to tolerate, but from you
150	who have spent your whole life in the consideration of this question,
150	unless I hear the contrary from your own lips, I expect something
150	better.  And therefore, I say, not only prove to us that justice is
150	better than injustice, but show what they either of them do to the
150	possessor of them, which makes the one to be a good and the other an
150	evil, whether seen or unseen by gods and men.
150	
150	SOCRATES - ADEIMANTUS
150	
150	I had always admired the genius of Glaucon and Adeimantus, but on
150	hearing these words I was quite delighted, and said: Sons of an
150	illustrious father, that was not a bad beginning of the Elegiac verses
150	which the admirer of Glaucon made in honour of you after you had
150	distinguished yourselves at the battle of Megara:--
150	
150	    'Sons of Ariston,' he sang, 'divine offspring of an
150	    illustrious hero.'
150	
150	The epithet is very appropriate, for there is something truly divine in
150	being able to argue as you have done for the superiority of injustice,
150	and remaining unconvinced by your own arguments.  And I do believe that
150	you are not convinced--this I infer from your general character, for
150	had I judged only from your speeches I should have mistrusted you.  But
150	now, the greater my confidence in you, the greater is my difficulty in
150	knowing what to say.  For I am in a strait between two; on the one hand
150	I feel that I am unequal to the task; and my inability is brought home
150	to me by the fact that you were not satisfied with the answer which I
150	made to Thrasymachus, proving, as I thought, the superiority which
150	justice has over injustice.  And yet I cannot refuse to help, while
150	breath and speech remain to me; I am afraid that there would be an
150	impiety in being present when justice is evil spoken of and not lifting
150	up a hand in her defence.  And therefore I had best give such help as I
150	can.
150	
150	Glaucon and the rest entreated me by all means not to let the question
150	drop, but to proceed in the investigation.  They wanted to arrive at
150	the truth, first, about the nature of justice and injustice, and
150	secondly, about their relative advantages.  I told them, what I really
150	thought, that the enquiry would be of a serious nature, and would
150	require very good eyes.  Seeing then, I said, that we are no great
150	wits, I think that we had better adopt a method which I may illustrate
150	thus; suppose that a short-sighted person had been asked by some one to
150	read small letters from a distance; and it occurred to some one else
150	that they might be found in another place which was larger and in which
150	the letters were larger--if they were the same and he could read the
150	larger letters first, and then proceed to the lesser--this would have
150	been thought a rare piece of good fortune.
150	
150	Very true, said Adeimantus; but how does the illustration apply to our
150	enquiry?
150	
150	I will tell you, I replied; justice, which is the subject of our
150	enquiry, is, as you know, sometimes spoken of as the virtue of an
150	individual, and sometimes as the virtue of a State.
150	
150	True, he replied.
150	
150	And is not a State larger than an individual?
150	
150	It is.
150	
150	Then in the larger the quantity of justice is likely to be larger and
150	more easily discernible.  I propose therefore that we enquire into the
150	nature of justice and injustice, first as they appear in the State, and
150	secondly in the individual, proceeding from the greater to the lesser
150	and comparing them.
150	
150	That, he said, is an excellent proposal.
150	
150	And if we imagine the State in process of creation, we shall see the
150	justice and injustice of the State in process of creation also.
150	
150	I dare say.
150	
150	When the State is completed there may be a hope that the object of our
150	search will be more easily discovered.
150	
150	Yes, far more easily.
150	
150	But ought we to attempt to construct one?  I said; for to do so, as I
150	am inclined to think, will be a very serious task.  Reflect therefore.
150	
150	I have reflected, said Adeimantus, and am anxious that you should
150	proceed.
150	
150	A State, I said, arises, as I conceive, out of the needs of mankind; no
150	one is self-sufficing, but all of us have many wants.  Can any other
150	origin of a State be imagined?
150	
150	There can I be no other.
150	
150	Then, as we have many wants, and many persons are needed to supply
150	them, one takes a helper for one purpose and another for another; and
150	when these partners and helpers are gathered together in one habitation
150	the body of inhabitants is termed a State.
150	
150	True, he said.
150	
150	And they exchange with one another, and one gives, and another
150	receives, under the idea that the exchange will be for their good.
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	Then, I said, let us begin and create in idea a State; and yet the true
150	creator is necessity, who is the mother of our invention.
150	
150	Of course, he replied.
150	
150	Now the first and greatest of necessities is food, which is the
150	condition of life and existence.
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	The second is a dwelling, and the third clothing and the like.
150	
150	True.
150	
150	And now let us see how our city will be able to supply this great
150	demand: We may suppose that one man is a husbandman, another a builder,
150	some one else a weaver--shall we add to them a shoemaker, or perhaps
150	some other purveyor to our bodily wants?
150	
150	Quite right.
150	
150	The barest notion of a State must include four or five men.
150	
150	Clearly.
150	
150	And how will they proceed?  Will each bring the result of his labours
150	into a common stock?--the individual husbandman, for example, producing
150	for four, and labouring four times as long and as much as he need in
150	the provision of food with which he supplies others as well as himself;
150	or will he have nothing to do with others and not be at the trouble of
150	producing for them, but provide for himself alone a fourth of the food
150	in a fourth of the time, and in the remaining three-fourths of his time
150	be employed in making a house or a coat or a pair of shoes, having no
150	partnership with others, but supplying himself all his own wants?
150	
150	Adeimantus thought that he should aim at producing food only and not at
150	producing everything.
150	
150	Probably, I replied, that would be the better way; and when I hear you
150	say this, I am myself reminded that we are not all alike; there are
150	diversities of natures among us which are adapted to different
150	occupations.
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	And will you have a work better done when the workman has many
150	occupations, or when he has only one?
150	
150	When he has only one.
150	
150	Further, there can be no doubt that a work is spoilt when not done at
150	the right time?
150	
150	No doubt.
150	
150	For business is not disposed to wait until the doer of the business is
150	at leisure; but the doer must follow up what he is doing, and make the
150	business his first object.
150	
150	He must.
150	
150	And if so, we must infer that all things are produced more plentifully
150	and easily and of a better quality when one man does one thing which is
150	natural to him and does it at the right time, and leaves other things.
150	
150	Undoubtedly..
150	
150	Then more than four citizens will be required; for the husbandman will
150	not make his own plough or mattock, or other implements of agriculture,
150	if they are to be good for anything.  Neither will the builder make his
150	tools--and he too needs many; and in like manner the weaver and
150	shoemaker.
150	
150	True.
150	
150	Then carpenters, and smiths, and many other artisans, will be sharers
150	in our little State, which is already beginning to grow?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	Yet even if we add neatherds, shepherds, and other herdsmen, in order
150	that our husbandmen may have oxen to plough with, and builders as well
150	as husbandmen may have draught cattle, and curriers and weavers fleeces
150	and hides,--still our State will not be very large.
150	
150	That is true; yet neither will it be a very small State which contains
150	all these.
150	
150	Then, again, there is the situation of the city--to find a place where
150	nothing need be imported is well-nigh impossible.
150	
150	Impossible.
150	
150	Then there must be another class of citizens who will bring the
150	required supply from another city?
150	
150	There must.
150	
150	But if the trader goes empty-handed, having nothing which they require
150	who would supply his need, he will come back empty-handed.
150	
150	That is certain.
150	
150	And therefore what they produce at home must be not only enough for
150	themselves, but such both in quantity and quality as to accommodate
150	those from whom their wants are supplied.
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	Then more husbandmen and more artisans will be required?
150	
150	They will.
150	
150	Not to mention the importers and exporters, who are called merchants?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	Then we shall want merchants?
150	
150	We shall.
150	
150	And if merchandise is to be carried over the sea, skilful sailors will
150	also be needed, and in considerable numbers?
150	
150	Yes, in considerable numbers.
150	
150	Then, again, within the city, how will they exchange their productions?
150	To secure such an exchange was, as you will remember, one of our
150	principal objects when we formed them into a society and constituted a
150	State.
150	
150	Clearly they will buy and sell.
150	
150	Then they will need a market-place, and a money-token for purposes of
150	exchange.
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	Suppose now that a husbandman, or an artisan, brings some production to
150	market, and he comes at a time when there is no one to exchange with
150	him,--is he to leave his calling and sit idle in the market-place?
150	
150	Not at all; he will find people there who, seeing the want, undertake
150	the office of salesmen.  In well-ordered States they are commonly those
150	who are the weakest in bodily strength, and therefore of little use for
150	any other purpose; their duty is to be in the market, and to give money
150	in exchange for goods to those who desire to sell and to take money
150	from those who desire to buy.
150	
150	This want, then, creates a class of retail-traders in our State.  Is
150	not 'retailer' the term which is applied to those who sit in the
150	market-place engaged in buying and selling, while those who wander from
150	one city to another are called merchants?
150	
150	Yes, he said.
150	
150	And there is another class of servants, who are intellectually hardly
150	on the level of companionship; still they have plenty of bodily
150	strength for labour, which accordingly they sell, and are called, if I
150	do not mistake, hirelings, hire being the name which is given to the
150	price of their labour.
150	
150	True.
150	
150	Then hirelings will help to make up our population?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And now, Adeimantus, is our State matured and perfected?
150	
150	I think so.
150	
150	Where, then, is justice, and where is injustice, and in what part of
150	the State did they spring up?
150	
150	Probably in the dealings of these citizens with one another.  cannot
150	imagine that they are more likely to be found anywhere else.
150	
150	I dare say that you are right in your suggestion, I said; we had better
150	think the matter out, and not shrink from the enquiry.
150	
150	Let us then consider, first of all, what will be their way of life, now
150	that we have thus established them.  Will they not produce corn, and
150	wine, and clothes, and shoes, and build houses for themselves?  And
150	when they are housed, they will work, in summer, commonly, stripped and
150	barefoot, but in winter substantially clothed and shod.  They will feed
150	on barley-meal and flour of wheat, baking and kneading them, making
150	noble cakes and loaves; these they will serve up on a mat of reeds or
150	on clean leaves, themselves reclining the while upon beds strewn with
150	yew or myrtle.  And they and their children will feast, drinking of the
150	wine which they have made, wearing garlands on their heads, and hymning
150	the praises of the gods, in happy converse with one another.  And they
150	will take care that their families do not exceed their means; having an
150	eye to poverty or war.
150	
150	SOCRATES - GLAUCON
150	
150	But, said Glaucon, interposing, you have not given them a relish to
150	their meal.
150	
150	True, I replied, I had forgotten; of course they must have a
150	relish-salt, and olives, and cheese, and they will boil roots and herbs
150	such as country people prepare; for a dessert we shall give them figs,
150	and peas, and beans; and they will roast myrtle-berries and acorns at
150	the fire, drinking in moderation.  And with such a diet they may be
150	expected to live in peace and health to a good old age, and bequeath a
150	similar life to their children after them.
150	
150	Yes, Socrates, he said, and if you were providing for a city of pigs,
150	how else would you feed the beasts?
150	
150	But what would you have, Glaucon?  I replied.
150	
150	Why, he said, you should give them the ordinary conveniences of life.
150	People who are to be comfortable are accustomed to lie on sofas, and
150	dine off tables, and they should have sauces and sweets in the modern
150	style.
150	
150	Yes, I said, now I understand:  the question which you would have me
150	consider is, not only how a State, but how a luxurious State is
150	created; and possibly there is no harm in this, for in such a State we
150	shall be more likely to see how justice and injustice originate.  In my
150	opinion the true and healthy constitution of the State is the one which
150	I have described.  But if you wish also to see a State at fever heat, I
150	have no objection.  For I suspect that many will not be satisfied with
150	the simpler way of way They will be for adding sofas, and tables, and
150	other furniture; also dainties, and perfumes, and incense, and
150	courtesans, and cakes, all these not of one sort only, but in every
150	variety; we must go beyond the necessaries of which I was at first
150	speaking, such as houses, and clothes, and shoes: the arts of the
150	painter and the embroiderer will have to be set in motion, and gold and
150	ivory and all sorts of materials must be procured.
150	
150	True, he said.
150	
150	Then we must enlarge our borders; for the original healthy State is no
150	longer sufficient.  Now will the city have to fill and swell with a
150	multitude of callings which are not required by any natural want; such
150	as the whole tribe of hunters and actors, of whom one large class have
150	to do with forms and colours; another will be the votaries of
150	music--poets and their attendant train of rhapsodists, players,
150	dancers, contractors; also makers of divers kinds of articles,
150	including women's dresses.  And we shall want more servants.  Will not
150	tutors be also in request, and nurses wet and dry, tirewomen and
150	barbers, as well as confectioners and cooks; and swineherds, too, who
150	were not needed and therefore had no place in the former edition of our
150	State, but are needed now?  They must not be forgotten:  and there will
150	be animals of many other kinds, if people eat them.
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	And living in this way we shall have much greater need of physicians
150	than before?
150	
150	Much greater.
150	
150	And the country which was enough to support the original inhabitants
150	will be too small now, and not enough?
150	
150	Quite true.
150	
150	Then a slice of our neighbours' land will be wanted by us for pasture
150	and tillage, and they will want a slice of ours, if, like ourselves,
150	they exceed the limit of necessity, and give themselves up to the
150	unlimited accumulation of wealth?
150	
150	That, Socrates, will be inevitable.
150	
150	And so we shall go to war, Glaucon.  Shall we not?
150	
150	Most certainly, he replied.
150	
150	Then without determining as yet whether war does good or harm, thus
150	much we may affirm, that now we have discovered war to be derived from
150	causes which are also the causes of almost all the evils in States,
150	private as well as public.
150	
150	Undoubtedly.
150	
150	And our State must once more enlarge; and this time the will be nothing
150	short of a whole army, which will have to go out and fight with the
150	invaders for all that we have, as well as for the things and persons
150	whom we were describing above.
150	
150	Why? he said; are they not capable of defending themselves?
150	
150	No, I said; not if we were right in the principle which was
150	acknowledged by all of us when we were framing the State: the
150	principle, as you will remember, was that one man cannot practise many
150	arts with success.
150	
150	Very true, he said.
150	
150	But is not war an art?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	And an art requiring as much attention as shoemaking?
150	
150	Quite true.
150	
150	And the shoemaker was not allowed by us to be husbandman, or a weaver,
150	a builder--in order that we might have our shoes well made; but to him
150	and to every other worker was assigned one work for which he was by
150	nature fitted, and at that he was to continue working all his life long
150	and at no other; he was not to let opportunities slip, and then he
150	would become a good workman.  Now nothing can be more important than
150	that the work of a soldier should be well done.  But is war an art so
150	easily acquired that a man may be a warrior who is also a husbandman,
150	or shoemaker, or other artisan; although no one in the world would be a
150	good dice or draught player who merely took up the game as a
150	recreation, and had not from his earliest years devoted himself to this
150	and nothing else?
150	
150	No tools will make a man a skilled workman, or master of defence, nor
150	be of any use to him who has not learned how to handle them, and has
150	never bestowed any attention upon them.  How then will he who takes up
150	a shield or other implement of war become a good fighter all in a day,
150	whether with heavy-armed or any other kind of troops?
150	
150	Yes, he said, the tools which would teach men their own use would be
150	beyond price.
150	
150	And the higher the duties of the guardian, I said, the more time, and
150	skill, and art, and application will be needed by him?
150	
150	No doubt, he replied.
150	
150	Will he not also require natural aptitude for his calling?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	Then it will be our duty to select, if we can, natures which are fitted
150	for the task of guarding the city?
150	
150	It will.
150	
150	And the selection will be no easy matter, I said; but we must be brave
150	and do our best.
150	
150	We must.
150	
150	Is not the noble youth very like a well-bred dog in respect of guarding
150	and watching?
150	
150	What do you mean?
150	
150	I mean that both of them ought to be quick to see, and swift to
150	overtake the enemy when they see him; and strong too if, when they have
150	caught him, they have to fight with him.
150	
150	All these qualities, he replied, will certainly be required by them.
150	
150	Well, and your guardian must be brave if he is to fight well?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	And is he likely to be brave who has no spirit, whether horse or dog or
150	any other animal?  Have you never observed how invincible and
150	unconquerable is spirit and how the presence of it makes the soul of
150	any creature to be absolutely fearless and indomitable?
150	
150	I have.
150	
150	Then now we have a clear notion of the bodily qualities which are
150	required in the guardian.
150	
150	True.
150	
150	And also of the mental ones; his soul is to be full of spirit?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	But are not these spirited natures apt to be savage with one another,
150	and with everybody else?
150	
150	A difficulty by no means easy to overcome, he replied.
150	
150	Whereas, I said, they ought to be dangerous to their enemies, and
150	gentle to their friends; if not, they will destroy themselves without
150	waiting for their enemies to destroy them.
150	
150	True, he said.
150	
150	What is to be done then?  I said; how shall we find a gentle nature
150	which has also a great spirit, for the one is the contradiction of the
150	other?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	He will not be a good guardian who is wanting in either of these two
150	qualities; and yet the combination of them appears to be impossible;
150	and hence we must infer that to be a good guardian is impossible.
150	
150	I am afraid that what you say is true, he replied.
150	
150	Here feeling perplexed I began to think over what had preceded.  My
