150	friend, I said, no wonder that we are in a perplexity; for we have lost
150	sight of the image which we had before us.
150	
150	What do you mean? he said.
150	
150	I mean to say that there do exist natures gifted with those opposite
150	qualities.
150	
150	And where do you find them?
150	
150	Many animals, I replied, furnish examples of them; our friend the dog
150	is a very good one:  you know that well-bred dogs are perfectly gentle
150	to their familiars and acquaintances, and the reverse to strangers.
150	
150	Yes, I know.
150	
150	Then there is nothing impossible or out of the order of nature in our
150	finding a guardian who has a similar combination of qualities?
150	
150	Certainly not.
150	
150	Would not he who is fitted to be a guardian, besides the spirited
150	nature, need to have the qualities of a philosopher?
150	
150	I do not apprehend your meaning.
150	
150	The trait of which I am speaking, I replied, may be also seen in the
150	dog, and is remarkable in the animal.
150	
150	What trait?
150	
150	Why, a dog, whenever he sees a stranger, is angry; when an
150	acquaintance, he welcomes him, although the one has never done him any
150	harm, nor the other any good.  Did this never strike you as curious?
150	
150	The matter never struck me before; but I quite recognise the truth of
150	your remark.
150	
150	And surely this instinct of the dog is very charming;--your dog is a
150	true philosopher.
150	
150	Why?
150	
150	Why, because he distinguishes the face of a friend and of an enemy only
150	by the criterion of knowing and not knowing.  And must not an animal be
150	a lover of learning who determines what he likes and dislikes by the
150	test of knowledge and ignorance?
150	
150	Most assuredly.
150	
150	And is not the love of learning the love of wisdom, which is philosophy?
150	
150	They are the same, he replied.
150	
150	And may we not say confidently of man also, that he who is likely to be
150	gentle to his friends and acquaintances, must by nature be a lover of
150	wisdom and knowledge?
150	
150	That we may safely affirm.
150	
150	Then he who is to be a really good and noble guardian of the State will
150	require to unite in himself philosophy and spirit and swiftness and
150	strength?
150	
150	Undoubtedly.
150	
150	Then we have found the desired natures; and now that we have found
150	them, how are they to be reared and educated?  Is not this enquiry
150	which may be expected to throw light on the greater enquiry which is
150	our final end--How do justice and injustice grow up in States? for we
150	do not want either to omit what is to the point or to draw out the
150	argument to an inconvenient length.
150	
150	SOCRATES - ADEIMANTUS
150	
150	Adeimantus thought that the enquiry would be of great service to us.
150	
150	Then, I said, my dear friend, the task must not be given up, even if
150	somewhat long.
150	
150	Certainly not.
150	
150	Come then, and let us pass a leisure hour in story-telling, and our
150	story shall be the education of our heroes.
150	
150	By all means.
150	
150	And what shall be their education?  Can we find a better than the
150	traditional sort?--and this has two divisions, gymnastic for the body,
150	and music for the soul.
150	
150	True.
150	
150	Shall we begin education with music, and go on to gymnastic afterwards?
150	
150	By all means.
150	
150	And when you speak of music, do you include literature or not?
150	
150	I do.
150	
150	And literature may be either true or false?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And the young should be trained in both kinds, and we begin with the
150	false?
150	
150	I do not understand your meaning, he said.
150	
150	You know, I said, that we begin by telling children stories which,
150	though not wholly destitute of truth, are in the main fictitious; and
150	these stories are told them when they are not of an age to learn
150	gymnastics.
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	That was my meaning when I said that we must teach music before
150	gymnastics.
150	
150	Quite right, he said.
150	
150	You know also that the beginning is the most important part of any
150	work, especially in the case of a young and tender thing; for that is
150	the time at which the character is being formed and the desired
150	impression is more readily taken.
150	
150	Quite true.
150	
150	And shall we just carelessly allow children to hear any casual tales
150	which may be devised by casual persons, and to receive into their minds
150	ideas for the most part the very opposite of those which we should wish
150	them to have when they are grown up?
150	
150	We cannot.
150	
150	Then the first thing will be to establish a censorship of the writers
150	of fiction, and let the censors receive any tale of fiction which is
150	good, and reject the bad; and we will desire mothers and nurses to tell
150	their children the authorised ones only.  Let them fashion the mind
150	with such tales, even more fondly than they mould the body with their
150	hands; but most of those which are now in use must be discarded.
150	
150	Of what tales are you speaking? he said.
150	
150	You may find a model of the lesser in the greater, I said; for they are
150	necessarily of the same type, and there is the same spirit in both of
150	them.
150	
150	Very likely, he replied; but I do not as yet know what you would term
150	the greater.
150	
150	Those, I said, which are narrated by Homer and Hesiod, and the rest of
150	the poets, who have ever been the great story-tellers of mankind.
150	
150	But which stories do you mean, he said; and what fault do you find with
150	them?
150	
150	A fault which is most serious, I said; the fault of telling a lie, and,
150	what is more, a bad lie.
150	
150	But when is this fault committed?
150	
150	Whenever an erroneous representation is made of the nature of gods and
150	heroes,--as when a painter paints a portrait not having the shadow of a
150	likeness to the original.
150	
150	Yes, he said, that sort of thing is certainly very blamable; but what
150	are the stories which you mean?
150	
150	First of all, I said, there was that greatest of all lies, in high
150	places, which the poet told about Uranus, and which was a bad lie
150	too,--I mean what Hesiod says that Uranus did, and how Cronus
150	retaliated on him.  The doings of Cronus, and the sufferings which in
150	turn his son inflicted upon him, even if they were true, ought
150	certainly not to be lightly told to young and thoughtless persons; if
150	possible, they had better be buried in silence.  But if there is an
150	absolute necessity for their mention, a chosen few might hear them in a
150	mystery, and they should sacrifice not a common [Eleusinian] pig, but
150	some huge and unprocurable victim; and then the number of the hearers
150	will be very few indeed.
150	
150	Why, yes, said he, those stories are extremely objectionable.
150	
150	Yes, Adeimantus, they are stories not to be repeated in our State; the
150	young man should not be told that in committing the worst of crimes he
150	is far from doing anything outrageous; and that even if he chastises
150	his father when does wrong, in whatever manner, he will only be
150	following the example of the first and greatest among the gods.
150	
150	I entirely agree with you, he said; in my opinion those stories are
150	quite unfit to be repeated.
150	
150	Neither, if we mean our future guardians to regard the habit of
150	quarrelling among themselves as of all things the basest, should any
150	word be said to them of the wars in heaven, and of the plots and
150	fightings of the gods against one another, for they are not true.  No,
150	we shall never mention the battles of the giants, or let them be
150	embroidered on garments; and we shall be silent about the innumerable
150	other quarrels of gods and heroes with their friends and relatives.  If
150	they would only believe us we would tell them that quarrelling is
150	unholy, and that never up to this time has there been any, quarrel
150	between citizens; this is what old men and old women should begin by
150	telling children; and when they grow up, the poets also should be told
150	to compose for them in a similar spirit.  But the narrative of
150	Hephaestus binding Here his mother, or how on another occasion Zeus
150	sent him flying for taking her part when she was being beaten, and all
150	the battles of the gods in Homer--these tales must not be admitted into
150	our State, whether they are supposed to have an allegorical meaning or
150	not.  For a young person cannot judge what is allegorical and what is
150	literal; anything that he receives into his mind at that age is likely
150	to become indelible and unalterable; and therefore it is most important
150	that the tales which the young first hear should be models of virtuous
150	thoughts.
150	
150	There you are right, he replied; but if any one asks where are such
150	models to be found and of what tales are you speaking--how shall we
150	answer him?
150	
150	I said to him, You and I, Adeimantus, at this moment are not poets, but
150	founders of a State:  now the founders of a State ought to know the
150	general forms in which poets should cast their tales, and the limits
150	which must be observed by them, but to make the tales is not their
150	business.
150	
150	Very true, he said; but what are these forms of theology which you mean?
150	
150	Something of this kind, I replied:--God is always to be represented as
150	he truly is, whatever be the sort of poetry, epic, lyric or tragic, in
150	which the representation is given.
150	
150	Right.
150	
150	And is he not truly good? and must he not be represented as such?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	And no good thing is hurtful?
150	
150	No, indeed.
150	
150	And that which is not hurtful hurts not?
150	
150	Certainly not.
150	
150	And that which hurts not does no evil?
150	
150	No.
150	
150	And can that which does no evil be a cause of evil?
150	
150	Impossible.
150	
150	And the good is advantageous?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And therefore the cause of well-being?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	It follows therefore that the good is not the cause of all things, but
150	of the good only?
150	
150	Assuredly.
150	
150	Then God, if he be good, is not the author of all things, as the many
150	assert, but he is the cause of a few things only, and not of most
150	things that occur to men.  For few are the goods of human life, and
150	many are the evils, and the good is to be attributed to God alone; of
150	the evils the causes are to be sought elsewhere, and not in him.
150	
150	That appears to me to be most true, he said.
150	
150	Then we must not listen to Homer or to any other poet who is guilty of
150	the folly of saying that two casks
150	
150	    Lie at the threshold of Zeus, full of lots, one of good,
150	    the other of evil lots,
150	
150	and that he to whom Zeus gives a mixture of the two
150	
150	    Sometimes meets with evil fortune, at other times with good;
150	
150	but that he to whom is given the cup of unmingled ill,
150	
150	    Him wild hunger drives o'er the beauteous earth.
150	
150	And again
150	
150	    Zeus, who is the dispenser of good and evil to us.
150	
150	And if any one asserts that the violation of oaths and treaties, which
150	was really the work of Pandarus, was brought about by Athene and Zeus,
150	or that the strife and contention of the gods was instigated by Themis
150	and Zeus, he shall not have our approval; neither will we allow our
150	young men to hear the words of Aeschylus, that
150	
150	    God plants guilt among men when he desires utterly to
150	    destroy a house.
150	
150	And if a poet writes of the sufferings of Niobe--the subject of the
150	tragedy in which these iambic verses occur--or of the house of Pelops,
150	or of the Trojan war or on any similar theme, either we must not permit
150	him to say that these are the works of God, or if they are of God, he
150	must devise some explanation of them such as we are seeking; he must
150	say that God did what was just and right, and they were the better for
150	being punished; but that those who are punished are miserable, and that
150	God is the author of their misery--the poet is not to be permitted to
150	say; though he may say that the wicked are miserable because they
150	require to be punished, and are benefited by receiving punishment from
150	God; but that God being good is the author of evil to any one is to be
150	strenuously denied, and not to be said or sung or heard in verse or
150	prose by any one whether old or young in any well-ordered commonwealth.
150	Such a fiction is suicidal, ruinous, impious.
150	
150	I agree with you, he replied, and am ready to give my assent to the law.
150	
150	Let this then be one of our rules and principles concerning the gods,
150	to which our poets and reciters will be expected to conform--that God
150	is not the author of all things, but of good only.
150	
150	That will do, he said.
150	
150	And what do you think of a second principle?  Shall I ask you whether
150	God is a magician, and of a nature to appear insidiously now in one
150	shape, and now in another--sometimes himself changing and passing into
150	many forms, sometimes deceiving us with the semblance of such
150	transformations; or is he one and the same immutably fixed in his own
150	proper image?
150	
150	I cannot answer you, he said, without more thought.
150	
150	Well, I said; but if we suppose a change in anything, that change must
150	be effected either by the thing itself, or by some other thing?
150	
150	Most certainly.
150	
150	And things which are at their best are also least liable to be altered
150	or discomposed; for example, when healthiest and strongest, the human
150	frame is least liable to be affected by meats and drinks, and the plant
150	which is in the fullest vigour also suffers least from winds or the
150	heat of the sun or any similar causes.
150	
150	Of course.
150	
150	And will not the bravest and wisest soul be least confused or deranged
150	by any external influence?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	And the same principle, as I should suppose, applies to all composite
150	things--furniture, houses, garments; when good and well made, they are
150	least altered by time and circumstances.
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	Then everything which is good, whether made by art or nature, or both,
150	is least liable to suffer change from without?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	But surely God and the things of God are in every way perfect?
150	
150	Of course they are.
150	
150	Then he can hardly be compelled by external influence to take many
150	shapes?
150	
150	He cannot.
150	
150	But may he not change and transform himself?
150	
150	Clearly, he said, that must be the case if he is changed at all.
150	
150	And will he then change himself for the better and fairer, or for the
150	worse and more unsightly?
150	
150	If he change at all he can only change for the worse, for we cannot
150	suppose him to be deficient either in virtue or beauty.
150	
150	Very true, Adeimantus; but then, would any one, whether God or man,
150	desire to make himself worse?
150	
150	Impossible.
150	
150	Then it is impossible that God should ever be willing to change; being,
150	as is supposed, the fairest and best that is conceivable, every god
150	remains absolutely and for ever in his own form.
150	
150	That necessarily follows, he said, in my judgment.
150	
150	Then, I said, my dear friend, let none of the poets tell us that
150	
150	    The gods, taking the disguise of strangers from other lands,
150	    walk up and down cities in all sorts of forms;
150	
150	and let no one slander Proteus and Thetis, neither let any one, either
150	in tragedy or in any other kind of poetry, introduce Here disguised in
150	the likeness of a priestess asking an alms
150	
150	    For the life-giving daughters of Inachus the river of Argos;
150	
150	--let us have no more lies of that sort.  Neither must we have mothers
150	under the influence of the poets scaring their children with a bad
150	version of these myths--telling how certain gods, as they say, 'Go
150	about by night in the likeness of so many strangers and in divers
150	forms'; but let them take heed lest they make cowards of their
150	children, and at the same time speak blasphemy against the gods.
150	
150	Heaven forbid, he said.
150	
150	But although the gods are themselves unchangeable, still by witchcraft
150	and deception they may make us think that they appear in various forms?
150	
150	Perhaps, he replied.
150	
150	Well, but can you imagine that God will be willing to lie, whether in
150	word or deed, or to put forth a phantom of himself?
150	
150	I cannot say, he replied.
150	
150	Do you not know, I said, that the true lie, if such an expression may
150	be allowed, is hated of gods and men?
150	
150	What do you mean? he said.
150	
150	I mean that no one is willingly deceived in that which is the truest
150	and highest part of himself, or about the truest and highest matters;
150	there, above all, he is most afraid of a lie having possession of him.
150	
150	Still, he said, I do not comprehend you.
150	
150	The reason is, I replied, that you attribute some profound meaning to
150	my words; but I am only saying that deception, or being deceived or
150	uninformed about the highest realities in the highest part of
150	themselves, which is the soul, and in that part of them to have and to
150	hold the lie, is what mankind least like;--that, I say, is what they
150	utterly detest.
150	
150	There is nothing more hateful to them.
150	
150	And, as I was just now remarking, this ignorance in the soul of him who
150	is deceived may be called the true lie; for the lie in words is only a
150	kind of imitation and shadowy image of a previous affection of the
150	soul, not pure unadulterated falsehood.  Am I not right?
150	
150	Perfectly right.
150	
150	The true lie is hated not only by the gods, but also by men?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	Whereas the lie in words is in certain cases useful and not hateful; in
150	dealing with enemies--that would be an instance; or again, when those
150	whom we call our friends in a fit of madness or illusion are going to
150	do some harm, then it is useful and is a sort of medicine or
150	preventive; also in the tales of mythology, of which we were just now
150	speaking--because we do not know the truth about ancient times, we make
150	falsehood as much like truth as we can, and so turn it to account.
150	
150	Very true, he said.
150	
150	But can any of these reasons apply to God?  Can we suppose that he is
150	ignorant of antiquity, and therefore has recourse to invention?
150	
150	That would be ridiculous, he said.
150	
150	Then the lying poet has no place in our idea of God?
150	
150	I should say not.
150	
150	Or perhaps he may tell a lie because he is afraid of enemies?
150	
150	That is inconceivable.
150	
150	But he may have friends who are senseless or mad?
150	
150	But no mad or senseless person can be a friend of God.
150	
150	Then no motive can be imagined why God should lie?
150	
150	None whatever.
150	
150	Then the superhuman and divine is absolutely incapable of falsehood?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	Then is God perfectly simple and true both in word and deed; he changes
150	not; he deceives not, either by sign or word, by dream or waking vision.
150	
150	Your thoughts, he said, are the reflection of my own.
150	
150	You agree with me then, I said, that this is the second type or form in
150	which we should write and speak about divine things.  The gods are not
150	magicians who transform themselves, neither do they deceive mankind in
150	any way.
150	
150	I grant that.
150	
150	Then, although we are admirers of Homer, we do not admire the lying
150	dream which Zeus sends to Agamemnon; neither will we praise the verses
150	of Aeschylus in which Thetis says that Apollo at her nuptials
150	
150	    Was celebrating in song her fair progeny whose days were
150	    to be long, and to know no sickness. And when he had
150	    spoken of my lot as in all things blessed of heaven he
150	    raised a note of triumph and cheered my soul. And I
150	    thought that the word of Phoebus being divine and full
150	    of prophecy, would not fail. And now he himself who
150	    uttered the strain, he who was present at the banquet,
150	    and who said this--he it is who has slain my son.
150	
150	These are the kind of sentiments about the gods which will arouse our
150	anger; and he who utters them shall be refused a chorus; neither shall
150	we allow teachers to make use of them in the instruction of the young,
150	meaning, as we do, that our guardians, as far as men can be, should be
150	true worshippers of the gods and like them.
150	
150	I entirely agree, be said, in these principles, and promise to make
150	them my laws.
150	
150	
150	
150	
150	BOOK III
150	
150	
150	SOCRATES - ADEIMANTUS
150	
150	SUCH then, I said, are our principles of theology--some tales are to be
150	told, and others are not to be told to our disciples from their youth
150	upwards, if we mean them to honour the gods and their parents, and to
150	value friendship with one another.
150	
150	Yes; and I think that our principles are right, he said.
150	
150	But if they are to be courageous, must they not learn other lessons
150	besides these, and lessons of such a kind as will take away the fear of
150	death?  Can any man be courageous who has the fear of death in him?
150	
150	Certainly not, he said.
150	
150	And can he be fearless of death, or will he choose death in battle
150	rather than defeat and slavery, who believes the world below to be real
150	and terrible?
150	
150	Impossible.
150	
150	Then we must assume a control over the narrators of this class of tales
150	as well as over the others, and beg them not simply to but rather to
150	commend the world below, intimating to them that their descriptions are
150	untrue, and will do harm to our future warriors.
150	
150	That will be our duty, he said.
150	
150	Then, I said, we shall have to obliterate many obnoxious passages,
150	beginning with the verses,
150	
150	    I would rather he a serf on the land of a poor and portionless
150	    man than rule over all the dead who have come to nought.
150	
150	We must also expunge the verse, which tells us how Pluto feared,
150	
150	    Lest the mansions grim and squalid which the gods abhor should
150	    be seen both of mortals and immortals.
150	
150	And again:
150	
150	    O heavens! verily in the house of Hades there is soul and
150	    ghostly form but no mind at all!
150	
150	Again of Tiresias:--
150	
150	    [To him even after death did Persephone grant mind,] that he
150	    alone should be wise; but the other souls are flitting shades.
150	
150	Again:--
150	
150	    The soul flying from the limbs had gone to Hades, lamentng her
150	    fate, leaving manhood and youth.
150	
150	Again:--
150	
150	    And the soul, with shrilling cry, passed like smoke beneath the
150	    earth.
150	
150	And,--
150	
150	    As bats in hollow of mystic cavern, whenever any of the has
150	    dropped out of the string and falls from the rock, fly shrilling
150	    and cling to one another, so did they with shrilling cry hold
150	    together as they moved.
150	
150	And we must beg Homer and the other poets not to be angry if we strike
150	out these and similar passages, not because they are unpoetical, or
150	unattractive to the popular ear, but because the greater the poetical
150	charm of them, the less are they meet for the ears of boys and men who
150	are meant to be free, and who should fear slavery more than death.
150	
150	Undoubtedly.
150	
150	Also we shall have to reject all the terrible and appalling names
150	describe the world below--Cocytus and Styx, ghosts under the earth, and
150	sapless shades, and any similar words of which the very mention causes
150	a shudder to pass through the inmost soul of him who hears them.  I do
150	not say that these horrible stories may not have a use of some kind;
150	but there is a danger that the nerves of our guardians may be rendered
150	too excitable and effeminate by them.
150	
150	There is a real danger, he said.
150	
150	Then we must have no more of them.
150	
150	True.
150	
150	Another and a nobler strain must be composed and sung by us.
150	
150	Clearly.
150	
150	And shall we proceed to get rid of the weepings and wailings of famous
150	men?
150	
150	They will go with the rest.
150	
150	But shall we be right in getting rid of them?  Reflect:  our principle
150	is that the good man will not consider death terrible to any other good
150	man who is his comrade.
150	
150	Yes; that is our principle.
150	
150	And therefore he will not sorrow for his departed friend as though he
150	had suffered anything terrible?
150	
150	He will not.
150	
150	Such an one, as we further maintain, is sufficient for himself and his
150	own happiness, and therefore is least in need of other men.
150	
150	True, he said.
150	
150	And for this reason the loss of a son or brother, or the deprivation of
150	fortune, is to him of all men least terrible.
150	
150	Assuredly.
150	
150	And therefore he will be least likely to lament, and will bear with the
150	greatest equanimity any misfortune of this sort which may befall him.
150	
150	Yes, he will feel such a misfortune far less than another.
150	
150	Then we shall be right in getting rid of the lamentations of famous
150	men, and making them over to women (and not even to women who are good
150	for anything), or to men of a baser sort, that those who are being
150	educated by us to be the defenders of their country may scorn to do the
150	like.
150	
150	That will be very right.
150	
150	Then we will once more entreat Homer and the other poets not to depict
150	Achilles, who is the son of a goddess, first lying on his side, then on
150	his back, and then on his face; then starting up and sailing in a
150	frenzy along the shores of the barren sea; now taking the sooty ashes
150	in both his hands and pouring them over his head, or weeping and
150	wailing in the various modes which Homer has delineated.  Nor should he
150	describe Priam the kinsman of the gods as praying and beseeching,
150	
150	    Rolling in the dirt, calling each man loudly by his name.
150	
150	Still more earnestly will we beg of him at all events not to introduce
150	the gods lamenting and saying,
150	
150	    Alas! my misery! Alas! that I bore the harvest to my sorrow.
150	
150	But if he must introduce the gods, at any rate let him not dare so
150	completely to misrepresent the greatest of the gods, as to make him
150	say--
150	
150	    O heavens! with my eyes verily I behold a dear friend of mine
150	    chased round and round the city, and my heart is sorrowful.
150	
150	Or again:--
150	
150	    Woe is me that I am fated to have Sarpedon, dearest of men to
150	    me, subdued at the hands of Patroclus the son of Menoetius.
150	
150	For if, my sweet Adeimantus, our youth seriously listen to such
150	unworthy representations of the gods, instead of laughing at them as
150	they ought, hardly will any of them deem that he himself, being but a
150	man, can be dishonoured by similar actions; neither will he rebuke any
150	inclination which may arise in his mind to say and do the like.  And
150	instead of having any shame or self-control, he will be always whining
150	and lamenting on slight occasions.
150	
150	Yes, he said, that is most true.
150	
150	Yes, I replied; but that surely is what ought not to be, as the
150	argument has just proved to us; and by that proof we must abide until
150	it is disproved by a better.
150	
150	It ought not to be.
150	
150	Neither ought our guardians to be given to laughter.  For a fit of
150	laughter which has been indulged to excess almost always produces a
150	violent reaction.
150	
150	So I believe.
150	
150	Then persons of worth, even if only mortal men, must not be represented
150	as overcome by laughter, and still less must such a representation of
150	the gods be allowed.
150	
150	Still less of the gods, as you say, he replied.
150	
150	Then we shall not suffer such an expression to be used about the gods
150	as that of Homer when he describes how
150	
150	    Inextinguishable laughter arose among the blessed gods, when
150	    they saw Hephaestus bustling about the mansion.
150	
150	On your views, we must not admit them.
150	
150	On my views, if you like to father them on me; that we must not admit
150	them is certain.
150	
150	Again, truth should be highly valued; if, as we were saying, a lie is
150	useless to the gods, and useful only as a medicine to men, then the use
150	of such medicines should be restricted to physicians; private
150	individuals have no business with them.
150	
150	Clearly not, he said.
150	
150	Then if any one at all is to have the privilege of lying, the rulers of
150	the State should be the persons; and they, in their dealings either
150	with enemies or with their own citizens, may be allowed to lie for the
150	public good.  But nobody else should meddle with anything of the kind;
150	and although the rulers have this privilege, for a private man to lie
150	to them in return is to be deemed a more heinous fault than for the
150	patient or the pupil of a gymnasium not to speak the truth about his
150	own bodily illnesses to the physician or to the trainer, or for a
150	sailor not to tell the captain what is happening about the ship and the
150	rest of the crew, and how things are going with himself or his fellow
150	sailors.
150	
150	Most true, he said.
150	
150	If, then, the ruler catches anybody beside himself lying in the State,
150	
150	    Any of the craftsmen, whether he priest or physician or
150	    carpenter.
150	
150	he will punish him for introducing a practice which is equally
150	subversive and destructive of ship or State.
150	
150	Most certainly, he said, if our idea of the State is ever carried out.
150	
150	In the next place our youth must be temperate?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	Are not the chief elements of temperance, speaking generally, obedience
150	to commanders and self-control in sensual pleasures?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	Then we shall approve such language as that of Diomede in Homer,
150	
150	    Friend, sit still and obey my word,
150	
150	and the verses which follow,
150	
150	    The Greeks marched breathing prowess,
150	    ...in silent awe of their leaders,
150	
150	and other sentiments of the same kind.
150	
150	We shall.
150	
150	What of this line,
150	
150	    O heavy with wine, who hast the eyes of a dog and the heart of a
150	    stag,
150	
150	and of the words which follow?  Would you say that these, or any
150	similar impertinences which private individuals are supposed to address
150	to their rulers, whether in verse or prose, are well or ill spoken?
150	
150	They are ill spoken.
150	
150	They may very possibly afford some amusement, but they do not conduce
150	to temperance.  And therefore they are likely to do harm to our young
150	men--you would agree with me there?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And then, again, to make the wisest of men say that nothing in his
150	opinion is more glorious than
150	
150	    When the tables are full of bread and meat, and the cup-bearer
150	    carries round wine which he draws from the bowl and pours into
150	    the cups,
150	
150	is it fit or conducive to temperance for a young man to hear such
150	words?  Or the verse
150	
150	    The saddest of fates is to die and meet destiny from hunger?
150	
150	What would you say again to the tale of Zeus, who, while other gods and
150	men were asleep and he the only person awake, lay devising plans, but
150	forgot them all in a moment through his lust, and was so completely
150	overcome at the sight of Here that he would not even go into the hut,
150	but wanted to lie with her on the ground, declaring that he had never
150	been in such a state of rapture before, even when they first met one
150	another
150	
150	    Without the knowledge of their parents;
150	
150	or that other tale of how Hephaestus, because of similar goings on,
150	cast a chain around Ares and Aphrodite?
150	
150	Indeed, he said, I am strongly of opinion that they ought not to hear
150	that sort of thing.
150	
150	But any deeds of endurance which are done or told by famous men, these
150	they ought to see and hear; as, for example, what is said in the verses,
150	
150	    He smote his breast, and thus reproached his heart,
150	    Endure, my heart; far worse hast thou endured!
150	
150	Certainly, he said.
150	
150	In the next place, we must not let them be receivers of gifts or lovers
150	of money.
150	
150	Certainly not.
150	
150	Neither must we sing to them of
150	
150	    Gifts persuading gods, and persuading reverend kings.
150	
150	Neither is Phoenix, the tutor of Achilles, to be approved or deemed to
150	have given his pupil good counsel when he told him that he should take
150	the gifts of the Greeks and assist them; but that without a gift he
150	should not lay aside his anger.  Neither will we believe or acknowledge
150	Achilles himself to have been such a lover of money that he took
150	Agamemnon's or that when he had received payment he restored the dead
150	body of Hector, but that without payment he was unwilling to do so.
150	
150	Undoubtedly, he said, these are not sentiments which can be approved.
150	
150	Loving Homer as I do, I hardly like to say that in attributing these
150	feelings to Achilles, or in believing that they are truly to him, he is
150	guilty of downright impiety.  As little can I believe the narrative of
150	his insolence to Apollo, where he says,
150	
150	    Thou hast wronged me, O far-darter, most abominable of deities.
150	    Verily I would he even with thee, if I had only the power,
150	
150	or his insubordination to the river-god, on whose divinity he is ready
150	to lay hands; or his offering to the dead Patroclus of his own hair,
150	which had been previously dedicated to the other river-god Spercheius,
150	and that he actually performed this vow; or that he dragged Hector
150	round the tomb of Patroclus, and slaughtered the captives at the pyre;
150	of all this I cannot believe that he was guilty, any more than I can
150	allow our citizens to believe that he, the wise Cheiron's pupil, the
150	son of a goddess and of Peleus who was the gentlest of men and third in
150	descent from Zeus, was so disordered in his wits as to be at one time
150	the slave of two seemingly inconsistent passions, meanness, not
150	untainted by avarice, combined with overweening contempt of gods and
150	men.
150	
150	You are quite right, he replied.
150	
150	And let us equally refuse to believe, or allow to be repeated, the tale
150	of Theseus son of Poseidon, or of Peirithous son of Zeus, going forth
150	as they did to perpetrate a horrid rape; or of any other hero or son of
150	a god daring to do such impious and dreadful things as they falsely
150	ascribe to them in our day: and let us further compel the poets to
150	declare either that these acts were not done by them, or that they were
150	not the sons of gods;--both in the same breath they shall not be
150	permitted to affirm.  We will not have them trying to persuade our
150	youth that the gods are the authors of evil, and that heroes are no
150	better than men-sentiments which, as we were saying, are neither pious
150	nor true, for we have already proved that evil cannot come from the
150	gods.
150	
150	Assuredly not.
150	
150	And further they are likely to have a bad effect on those who hear
150	them; for everybody will begin to excuse his own vices when he is
150	convinced that similar wickednesses are always being perpetrated by--
150	
150	    The kindred of the gods, the relatives of Zeus, whose ancestral
150	    altar, the attar of Zeus, is aloft in air on the peak of Ida,
150	
150	and who have
150	
150	    the blood of deities yet flowing in their veins.
150	
150	And therefore let us put an end to such tales, lest they engender
150	laxity of morals among the young.
150	
150	By all means, he replied.
150	
150	But now that we are determining what classes of subjects are or are not
150	to be spoken of, let us see whether any have been omitted by us.  The
150	manner in which gods and demigods and heroes and the world below should
150	be treated has been already laid down.
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	And what shall we say about men?  That is clearly the remaining portion
150	of our subject.
150	
150	Clearly so.
150	
150	But we are not in a condition to answer this question at present, my
150	friend.
150	
150	Why not?
150	
150	Because, if I am not mistaken, we shall have to say that about men
150	poets and story-tellers are guilty of making the gravest misstatements
150	when they tell us that wicked men are often happy, and the good
150	miserable; and that injustice is profitable when undetected, but that
150	justice is a man's own loss and another's gain--these things we shall
150	forbid them to utter, and command them to sing and say the opposite.
150	
150	To be sure we shall, he replied.
150	
150	But if you admit that I am right in this, then I shall maintain that
150	you have implied the principle for which we have been all along
150	contending.
150	
150	I grant the truth of your inference.
150	
150	That such things are or are not to be said about men is a question
150	which we cannot determine until we have discovered what justice is, and
150	how naturally advantageous to the possessor, whether he seems to be
150	just or not.
150	
150	Most true, he said.
150	
150	Enough of the subjects of poetry:  let us now speak of the style; and
150	when this has been considered, both matter and manner will have been
150	completely treated.
150	
150	I do not understand what you mean, said Adeimantus.
150	
150	Then I must make you understand; and perhaps I may be more intelligible
150	if I put the matter in this way.  You are aware, I suppose, that all
150	mythology and poetry is a narration of events, either past, present, or
150	to come?
150	
150	Certainly, he replied.
150	
150	And narration may be either simple narration, or imitation, or a union
150	of the two?
150	
150	That again, he said, I do not quite understand.
150	
150	I fear that I must be a ridiculous teacher when I have so much
150	difficulty in making myself apprehended.  Like a bad speaker,
150	therefore, I will not take the whole of the subject, but will break a
150	piece off in illustration of my meaning.  You know the first lines of
150	the Iliad, in which the poet says that Chryses prayed Agamemnon to
150	release his daughter, and that Agamemnon flew into a passion with him;
150	whereupon Chryses, failing of his object, invoked the anger of the God
150	against the Achaeans.  Now as far as these lines,
150	
150	    And he prayed all the Greeks, but especially the two sons of Atreus,
150	    the chiefs of the people,
150	
150	the poet is speaking in his own person; he never leads us to suppose
150	that he is any one else.  But in what follows he takes the person of
150	Chryses, and then he does all that he can to make us believe that the
150	speaker is not Homer, but the aged priest himself.  And in this double
150	form he has cast the entire narrative of the events which occurred at
150	Troy and in Ithaca and throughout the Odyssey.
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And a narrative it remains both in the speeches which the poet recites
150	from time to time and in the intermediate passages?
150	
150	Quite true.
150	
150	But when the poet speaks in the person of another, may we not say that
150	he assimilates his style to that of the person who, as he informs you,
150	is going to speak?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	And this assimilation of himself to another, either by the use of voice
150	or gesture, is the imitation of the person whose character he assumes?
150	
150	Of course.
150	
150	Then in this case the narrative of the poet may be said to proceed by
150	way of imitation?
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	Or, if the poet everywhere appears and never conceals himself, then
150	again the imitation is dropped, and his poetry becomes simple
150	narration.  However, in order that I may make my meaning quite clear,
150	and that you may no more say, I don't understand,' I will show how the
150	change might be effected.  If Homer had said, 'The priest came, having
150	his daughter's ransom in his hands, supplicating the Achaeans, and
150	above all the kings;' and then if, instead of speaking in the person of
150	Chryses, he had continued in his own person, the words would have been,
150	not imitation, but simple narration.  The passage would have run as
150	follows (I am no poet, and therefore I drop the metre), 'The priest
150	came and prayed the gods on behalf of the Greeks that they might
150	capture Troy and return safely home, but begged that they would give
150	him back his daughter, and take the ransom which he brought, and
150	respect the God.  Thus he spoke, and the other Greeks revered the
150	priest and assented.  But Agamemnon was wroth, and bade him depart and
150	not come again, lest the staff and chaplets of the God should be of no
150	avail to him--the daughter of Chryses should not be released, he
150	said--she should grow old with him in Argos.  And then he told him to
150	go away and not to provoke him, if he intended to get home unscathed.
150	And the old man went away in fear and silence, and, when he had left
150	the camp, he called upon Apollo by his many names, reminding him of
150	everything which he had done pleasing to him, whether in building his
150	temples, or in offering sacrifice, and praying that his good deeds
150	might be returned to him, and that the Achaeans might expiate his tears
150	by the arrows of the god,'--and so on.  In this way the whole becomes
150	simple narrative.
150	
150	I understand, he said.
150	
150	Or you may suppose the opposite case--that the intermediate passages
150	are omitted, and the dialogue only left.
150	
150	That also, he said, I understand; you mean, for example, as in tragedy.
150	
150	You have conceived my meaning perfectly; and if I mistake not, what you
150	failed to apprehend before is now made clear to you, that poetry and
150	mythology are, in some cases, wholly imitative--instances of this are
150	supplied by tragedy and comedy; there is likewise the opposite style,
150	in which the my poet is the only speaker--of this the dithyramb affords
150	the best example; and the combination of both is found in epic, and in
150	several other styles of poetry.  Do I take you with me?
150	
150	Yes, he said; I see now what you meant.
150	
150	I will ask you to remember also what I began by saying, that we had
150	done with the subject and might proceed to the style.
150	
150	Yes, I remember.
150	
150	In saying this, I intended to imply that we must come to an
150	understanding about the mimetic art,--whether the poets, in narrating
150	their stories, are to be allowed by us to imitate, and if so, whether
150	in whole or in part, and if the latter, in what parts; or should all
150	imitation be prohibited?
150	
150	You mean, I suspect, to ask whether tragedy and comedy shall be
150	admitted into our State?
150	
150	Yes, I said; but there may be more than this in question: I really do
150	not know as yet, but whither the argument may blow, thither we go.
150	
150	And go we will, he said.
150	
150	Then, Adeimantus, let me ask you whether our guardians ought to be
150	imitators; or rather, has not this question been decided by the rule
150	already laid down that one man can only do one thing well, and not
150	many; and that if he attempt many, he will altogether fall of gaining
150	much reputation in any?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	And this is equally true of imitation; no one man can imitate many
150	things as well as he would imitate a single one?
150	
150	He cannot.
150	
150	Then the same person will hardly be able to play a serious part in
150	life, and at the same time to be an imitator and imitate many other
150	parts as well; for even when two species of imitation are nearly
150	allied, the same persons cannot succeed in both, as, for example, the
150	writers of tragedy and comedy--did you not just now call them
150	imitations?
150	
150	Yes, I did; and you are right in thinking that the same persons cannot
150	succeed in both.
150	
150	Any more than they can be rhapsodists and actors at once?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	Neither are comic and tragic actors the same; yet all these things are
150	but imitations.
150	
150	They are so.
150	
150	And human nature, Adeimantus, appears to have been coined into yet
150	smaller pieces, and to be as incapable of imitating many things well,
150	as of performing well the actions of which the imitations are copies.
150	
150	Quite true, he replied.
150	
150	If then we adhere to our original notion and bear in mind that our
150	guardians, setting aside every other business, are to dedicate
150	themselves wholly to the maintenance of freedom in the State, making
150	this their craft, and engaging in no work which does not bear on this
150	end, they ought not to practise or imitate anything else; if they
150	imitate at all, they should imitate from youth upward only those
150	characters which are suitable to their profession--the courageous,
150	temperate, holy, free, and the like; but they should not depict or be
150	skilful at imitating any kind of illiberality or baseness, lest from
150	imitation they should come to be what they imitate.  Did you never
150	observe how imitations, beginning in early youth and continuing far
150	into life, at length grow into habits and become a second nature,
150	affecting body, voice, and mind?
150	
150	Yes, certainly, he said.
150	
150	Then, I said, we will not allow those for whom we profess a care and of
150	whom we say that they ought to be good men, to imitate a woman, whether
150	young or old, quarrelling with her husband, or striving and vaunting
150	against the gods in conceit of her happiness, or when she is in
150	affliction, or sorrow, or weeping; and certainly not one who is in
150	sickness, love, or labour.
150	
150	Very right, he said.
150	
150	Neither must they represent slaves, male or female, performing the
150	offices of slaves?
150	
150	They must not.
150	
150	And surely not bad men, whether cowards or any others, who do the
150	reverse of what we have just been prescribing, who scold or mock or
150	revile one another in drink or out of in drink or, or who in any other
150	manner sin against themselves and their neighbours in word or deed, as
150	the manner of such is.  Neither should they be trained to imitate the
150	action or speech of men or women who are mad or bad; for madness, like
150	vice, is to be known but not to be practised or imitated.
150	
150	Very true, he replied.
150	
150	Neither may they imitate smiths or other artificers, or oarsmen, or
150	boatswains, or the like?
150	
150	How can they, he said, when they are not allowed to apply their minds
150	to the callings of any of these?
150	
150	Nor may they imitate the neighing of horses, the bellowing of bulls,
150	the murmur of rivers and roll of the ocean, thunder, and all that sort
150	of thing?
150	
150	Nay, he said, if madness be forbidden, neither may they copy the
150	behaviour of madmen.
150	
150	You mean, I said, if I understand you aright, that there is one sort of
150	narrative style which may be employed by a truly good man when he has
150	anything to say, and that another sort will be used by a man of an
150	opposite character and education.
150	
150	And which are these two sorts? he asked.
150	
150	Suppose, I answered, that a just and good man in the course of a
150	narration comes on some saying or action of another good man,--I should
150	imagine that he will like to personate him, and will not be ashamed of
150	this sort of imitation:  he will be most ready to play the part of the
150	good man when he is acting firmly and wisely; in a less degree when he
150	is overtaken by illness or love or drink, or has met with any other
150	disaster.  But when he comes to a character which is unworthy of him,
150	he will not make a study of that; he will disdain such a person, and
150	will assume his likeness, if at all, for a moment only when he is
150	performing some good action; at other times he will be ashamed to play
150	a part which he has never practised, nor will he like to fashion and
150	frame himself after the baser models; he feels the employment of such
150	an art, unless in jest, to be beneath him, and his mind revolts at it.
150	
150	So I should expect, he replied.
150	
150	Then he will adopt a mode of narration such as we have illustrated out
150	of Homer, that is to say, his style will be both imitative and
150	narrative; but there will be very little of the former, and a great
150	deal of the latter.  Do you agree?
150	
150	Certainly, he said; that is the model which such a speaker must
150	necessarily take.
150	
150	But there is another sort of character who will narrate anything, and,
150	the worse lie is, the more unscrupulous he will be; nothing will be too
150	bad for him:  and he will be ready to imitate anything, not as a joke,
150	but in right good earnest, and before a large company.  As I was just
150	now saying, he will attempt to represent the roll of thunder, the noise
150	of wind and hall, or the creaking of wheels, and pulleys, and the
150	various sounds of flutes; pipes, trumpets, and all sorts of
150	instruments:  he will bark like a dog, bleat like a sheep, or crow like
150	a cock; his entire art will consist in imitation of voice and gesture,
150	and there will be very little narration.
150	
150	That, he said, will be his mode of speaking.
150	
150	These, then, are the two kinds of style?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And you would agree with me in saying that one of them is simple and
150	has but slight changes; and if the harmony and rhythm are also chosen
150	for their simplicity, the result is that the speaker, if he speaks
150	correctly, is always pretty much the same in style, and he will keep
150	within the limits of a single harmony (for the changes are not great),
150	and in like manner he will make use of nearly the same rhythm?
150	
150	That is quite true, he said.
150	
150	Whereas the other requires all sorts of harmonies and all sorts of
150	rhythms, if the music and the style are to correspond, because the
150	style has all sorts of changes.
150	
150	That is also perfectly true, he replied.
150	
150	And do not the two styles, or the mixture of the two, comprehend all
150	poetry, and every form of expression in words?  No one can say anything
150	except in one or other of them or in both together.
150	
150	They include all, he said.
150	
150	And shall we receive into our State all the three styles, or one only
150	of the two unmixed styles? or would you include the mixed?
150	
150	I should prefer only to admit the pure imitator of virtue.
150	
150	Yes, I said, Adeimantus, but the mixed style is also very charming: and
150	indeed the pantomimic, which is the opposite of the one chosen by you,
150	is the most popular style with children and their attendants, and with
150	the world in general.
150	
150	I do not deny it.
150	
150	But I suppose you would argue that such a style is unsuitable to our
150	State, in which human nature is not twofold or manifold, for one man
150	plays one part only?
150	
150	Yes; quite unsuitable.
150	
150	And this is the reason why in our State, and in our State only, we
150	shall find a shoemaker to be a shoemaker and not a pilot also, and a
150	husbandman to be a husbandman and not a dicast also, and a soldier a
150	soldier and not a trader also, and the same throughout?
150	
150	True, he said.
150	
150	And therefore when any one of these pantomimic gentlemen, who are so
150	clever that they can imitate anything, comes to us, and makes a
150	proposal to exhibit himself and his poetry, we will fall down and
150	worship him as a sweet and holy and wonderful being; but we must also
150	inform him that in our State such as he are not permitted to exist; the
150	law will not allow them.  And so when we have anointed him with myrrh,
150	and set a garland of wool upon his head, we shall send him away to
150	another city.  For we mean to employ for our souls' health the rougher
150	and severer poet or story-teller, who will imitate the style of the
150	virtuous only, and will follow those models which we prescribed at
150	first when we began the education of our soldiers.
150	
150	We certainly will, he said, if we have the power.
150	
150	Then now, my friend, I said, that part of music or literary education
150	which relates to the story or myth may be considered to be finished;
150	for the matter and manner have both been discussed.
150	
150	I think so too, he said.
150	
150	Next in order will follow melody and song.
150	
150	That is obvious.
150	
150	Every one can see already what we ought to say about them, if we are to
150	be consistent with ourselves.
150	
150	SOCRATES - GLAUCON
150	
150	I fear, said Glaucon, laughing, that the words 'every one' hardly
150	includes me, for I cannot at the moment say what they should be; though
150	I may guess.
150	
150	At any rate you can tell that a song or ode has three parts--the words,
150	the melody, and the rhythm; that degree of knowledge I may presuppose?
150	
150	Yes, he said; so much as that you may.
150	
150	And as for the words, there surely be no difference words between words
150	which are and which are not set to music; both will conform to the same
150	laws, and these have been already determined by us?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And the melody and rhythm will depend upon the words?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	We were saying, when we spoke of the subject-matter, that we had no
150	need of lamentations and strains of sorrow?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	And which are the harmonies expressive of sorrow?  You are musical, and
150	can tell me.
150	
150	The harmonies which you mean are the mixed or tenor Lydian, and the
150	full-toned or bass Lydian, and such like.
150	
150	These then, I said, must be banished; even to women who have a
150	character to maintain they are of no use, and much less to men.
150	Certainly.
150	
150	In the next place, drunkenness and softness and indolence are utterly
150	unbecoming the character of our guardians.
150	
150	Utterly unbecoming.
150	
150	And which are the soft or drinking harmonies?
150	
150	The Ionian, he replied, and the Lydian; they are termed 'relaxed.'
150	
150	Well, and are these of any military use?
150	
150	Quite the reverse, he replied; and if so the Dorian and the Phrygian
150	are the only ones which you have left.
150	
150	I answered:  Of the harmonies I know nothing, but I want to have one
150	warlike, to sound the note or accent which a brave man utters in the
150	hour of danger and stern resolve, or when his cause is failing, and he
150	is going to wounds or death or is overtaken by some other evil, and at
150	every such crisis meets the blows of fortune with firm step and a
150	determination to endure; and another to be used by him in times of
150	peace and freedom of action, when there is no pressure of necessity,
150	and he is seeking to persuade God by prayer, or man by instruction and
150	admonition, or on the other hand, when he is expressing his willingness
150	to yield to persuasion or entreaty or admonition, and which represents
150	him when by prudent conduct he has attained his end, not carried away
150	by his success, but acting moderately and wisely under the
150	circumstances, and acquiescing in the event.  These two harmonies I ask
150	you to leave; the strain of necessity and the strain of freedom, the
150	strain of the unfortunate and the strain of the fortunate, the strain
150	of courage, and the strain of temperance; these, I say, leave.
150	
150	And these, he replied, are the Dorian and Phrygian harmonies of which I
150	was just now speaking.
150	
150	Then, I said, if these and these only are to be used in our songs and
150	melodies, we shall not want multiplicity of notes or a panharmonic
150	scale?
150	
150	I suppose not.
150	
150	Then we shall not maintain the artificers of lyres with three corners
150	and complex scales, or the makers of any other many-stringed
150	curiously-harmonised instruments?
150	
150	Certainly not.
150	
150	But what do you say to flute-makers and flute-players? Would you admit
150	them into our State when you reflect that in this composite use of
150	harmony the flute is worse than all the stringed instruments put
150	together; even the panharmonic music is only an imitation of the flute?
150	
150	Clearly not.
150	
150	There remain then only the lyre and the harp for use in the city, and
150	the shepherds may have a pipe in the country.
150	
150	That is surely the conclusion to be drawn from the argument.
150	
150	The preferring of Apollo and his instruments to Marsyas and his
150	instruments is not at all strange, I said.
150	
150	Not at all, he replied.
150	
150	And so, by the dog of Egypt, we have been unconsciously purging the
150	State, which not long ago we termed luxurious.
150	
150	And we have done wisely, he replied.
150	
150	Then let us now finish the purgation, I said.  Next in order to
150	harmonies, rhythms will naturally follow, and they should be subject to
150	the same rules, for we ought not to seek out complex systems of metre,
150	or metres of every kind, but rather to discover what rhythms are the
150	expressions of a courageous and harmonious life; and when we have found
150	them, we shall adapt the foot and the melody to words having a like
150	spirit, not the words to the foot and melody.  To say what these
150	rhythms are will be your duty--you must teach me them, as you have
150	already taught me the harmonies.
150	
150	But, indeed, he replied, I cannot tell you.  I only know that there are
150	some three principles of rhythm out of which metrical systems are
150	framed, just as in sounds there are four notes out of which all the
150	harmonies are composed; that is an observation which I have made.  But
150	of what sort of lives they are severally the imitations I am unable to
150	say.
150	
150	Then, I said, we must take Damon into our counsels; and he will tell us
150	what rhythms are expressive of meanness, or insolence, or fury, or
150	other unworthiness, and what are to be reserved for the expression of
150	opposite feelings.  And I think that I have an indistinct recollection
150	of his mentioning a complex Cretic rhythm; also a dactylic or heroic,
150	and he arranged them in some manner which I do not quite understand,
150	making the rhythms equal in the rise and fall of the foot, long and
150	short alternating; and, unless I am mistaken, he spoke of an iambic as
150	well as of a trochaic rhythm, and assigned to them short and long
150	quantities.  Also in some cases he appeared to praise or censure the
150	movement of the foot quite as much as the rhythm; or perhaps a
150	combination of the two; for I am not certain what he meant.  These
150	matters, however, as I was saying, had better be referred to Damon
150	himself, for the analysis of the subject would be difficult, you know.
150	
150	Rather so, I should say.
150	
150	But there is no difficulty in seeing that grace or the absence of grace
150	is an effect of good or bad rhythm.
150	
150	None at all.
150	
150	And also that good and bad rhythm naturally assimilate to a good and
150	bad style; and that harmony and discord in like manner follow style;
150	for our principle is that rhythm and harmony are regulated by the
150	words, and not the words by them.
150	
150	Just so, he said, they should follow the words.
150	
150	And will not the words and the character of the style depend on the
150	temper of the soul?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And everything else on the style?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	Then beauty of style and harmony and grace and good rhythm depend on
150	simplicity,--I mean the true simplicity of a rightly and nobly ordered
150	mind and character, not that other simplicity which is only an
150	euphemism for folly?
150	
150	Very true, he replied.
150	
150	And if our youth are to do their work in life, must they not make these
150	graces and harmonies their perpetual aim?
150	
150	They must.
150	
150	And surely the art of the painter and every other creative and
150	constructive art are full of them,--weaving, embroidery, architecture,
150	and every kind of manufacture; also nature, animal and vegetable,--in
150	all of them there is grace or the absence of grace.  And ugliness and
150	discord and inharmonious motion are nearly allied to ill words and ill
150	nature, as grace and harmony are the twin sisters of goodness and
150	virtue and bear their likeness.
150	
150	That is quite true, he said.
150	
150	But shall our superintendence go no further, and are the poets only to
150	be required by us to express the image of the good in their works, on
150	pain, if they do anything else, of expulsion from our State?  Or is the
150	same control to be extended to other artists, and are they also to be
150	prohibited from exhibiting the opposite forms of vice and intemperance
150	and meanness and indecency in sculpture and building and the other
150	creative arts; and is he who cannot conform to this rule of ours to be
150	prevented from practising his art in our State, lest the taste of our
150	citizens be corrupted by him?  We would not have our guardians grow up
150	amid images of moral deformity, as in some noxious pasture, and there
150	browse and feed upon many a baneful herb and flower day by day, little
150	by little, until they silently gather a festering mass of corruption in
150	their own soul.  Let our artists rather be those who are gifted to
150	discern the true nature of the beautiful and graceful; then will our
150	youth dwell in a land of health, amid fair sights and sounds, and
150	receive the good in everything; and beauty, the effluence of fair
150	works, shall flow into the eye and ear, like a health-giving breeze
150	from a purer region, and insensibly draw the soul from earliest years
150	into likeness and sympathy with the beauty of reason.
150	
150	There can be no nobler training than that, he replied.
150	
150	And therefore, I said, Glaucon, musical training is a more potent
150	instrument than any other, because rhythm and harmony find their way
150	into the inward places of the soul, on which they mightily fasten,
150	imparting grace, and making the soul of him who is rightly educated
150	graceful, or of him who is ill-educated ungraceful; and also because he
150	who has received this true education of the inner being will most
150	shrewdly perceive omissions or faults in art and nature, and with a
150	true taste, while he praises and rejoices over and receives into his
150	soul the good, and becomes noble and good, he will justly blame and
150	hate the bad, now in the days of his youth, even before he is able to
150	know the reason why; and when reason comes he will recognise and salute
150	the friend with whom his education has made him long familiar.
150	
150	Yes, he said, I quite agree with you in thinking that our youth should
150	be trained in music and on the grounds which you mention.
150	
150	Just as in learning to read, I said, we were satisfied when we knew the
150	letters of the alphabet, which are very few, in all their recurring
150	sizes and combinations; not slighting them as unimportant whether they
150	occupy a space large or small, but everywhere eager to make them out;
150	and not thinking ourselves perfect in the art of reading until we
150	recognise them wherever they are found:
150	
150	True--
150	
150	Or, as we recognise the reflection of letters in the water, or in a
150	mirror, only when we know the letters themselves; the same art and
150	study giving us the knowledge of both:
150	
150	Exactly--
150	
150	Even so, as I maintain, neither we nor our guardians, whom we have to
150	educate, can ever become musical until we and they know the essential
150	forms, in all their combinations, and can recognise them and their
150	images wherever they are found, not slighting them either in small
150	things or great, but believing them all to be within the sphere of one
150	art and study.
150	
150	Most assuredly.
150	
150	And when a beautiful soul harmonises with a beautiful form, and the two
150	are cast in one mould, that will be the fairest of sights to him who
150	has an eye to see it?
150	
150	The fairest indeed.
150	
150	And the fairest is also the loveliest?
150	
150	That may be assumed.
150	
150	And the man who has the spirit of harmony will be most in love with the
150	loveliest; but he will not love him who is of an inharmonious soul?
150	
150	That is true, he replied, if the deficiency be in his soul; but if
150	there be any merely bodily defect in another he will be patient of it,
150	and will love all the same.
150	
150	I perceive, I said, that you have or have had experiences of this sort,
150	and I agree.  But let me ask you another question: Has excess of
150	pleasure any affinity to temperance?
150	
150	How can that be? he replied; pleasure deprives a man of the use of his
150	faculties quite as much as pain.
150	
150	Or any affinity to virtue in general?
150	
150	None whatever.
150	
150	Any affinity to wantonness and intemperance?
150	
150	Yes, the greatest.
150	
150	And is there any greater or keener pleasure than that of sensual love?
150	
150	No, nor a madder.
150	
150	Whereas true love is a love of beauty and order--temperate and
150	harmonious?
150	
150	Quite true, he said.
150	
150	Then no intemperance or madness should be allowed to approach true love?
150	
150	Certainly not.
150	
150	Then mad or intemperate pleasure must never be allowed to come near the
150	lover and his beloved; neither of them can have any part in it if their
150	love is of the right sort?
150	
150	No, indeed, Socrates, it must never come near them.
150	
150	Then I suppose that in the city which we are founding you would make a
150	law to the effect that a friend should use no other familiarity to his
150	love than a father would use to his son, and then only for a noble
150	purpose, and he must first have the other's consent; and this rule is
150	to limit him in all his intercourse, and he is never to be seen going
150	further, or, if he exceeds, he is to be deemed guilty of coarseness and
150	bad taste.
150	
150	I quite agree, he said.
150	
150	Thus much of music, which makes a fair ending; for what should be the
150	end of music if not the love of beauty?
150	
150	I agree, he said.
150	
150	After music comes gymnastic, in which our youth are next to be trained.
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	Gymnastic as well as music should begin in early years; the training in
150	it should be careful and should continue through life.  Now my belief
150	is,--and this is a matter upon which I should like to have your opinion
150	in confirmation of my own, but my own belief is,--not that the good
150	body by any bodily excellence improves the soul, but, on the contrary,
150	that the good soul, by her own excellence, improves the body as far as
150	this may be possible.  What do you say?
150	
150	Yes, I agree.
150	
150	Then, to the mind when adequately trained, we shall be right in handing
150	over the more particular care of the body; and in order to avoid
150	prolixity we will now only give the general outlines of the subject.
150	
150	Very good.
150	
150	That they must abstain from intoxication has been already remarked by
150	us; for of all persons a guardian should be the last to get drunk and
150	not know where in the world he is.
150	
150	Yes, he said; that a guardian should require another guardian to take
150	care of him is ridiculous indeed.
150	
150	But next, what shall we say of their food; for the men are in training
150	for the great contest of all--are they not?
150	
150	Yes, he said.
150	
150	And will the habit of body of our ordinary athletes be suited to them?
150	
150	Why not?
150	
150	I am afraid, I said, that a habit of body such as they have is but a
150	sleepy sort of thing, and rather perilous to health.  Do you not
150	observe that these athletes sleep away their lives, and are liable to
150	most dangerous illnesses if they depart, in ever so slight a degree,
150	from their customary regimen?
150	
150	Yes, I do.
150	
150	Then, I said, a finer sort of training will be required for our warrior
150	athletes, who are to be like wakeful dogs, and to see and hear with the
150	utmost keenness; amid the many changes of water and also of food, of
150	summer heat and winter cold, which they will have to endure when on a
150	campaign, they must not be liable to break down in health.
150	
150	That is my view.
150	
150	The really excellent gymnastic is twin sister of that simple music
150	which we were just now describing.
150	
150	How so?
150	
150	Why, I conceive that there is a gymnastic which, like our music, is
150	simple and good; and especially the military gymnastic.
150	
150	What do you mean?
150	
150	My meaning may be learned from Homer; he, you know, feeds his heroes at
150	their feasts, when they are campaigning, on soldiers' fare; they have
150	no fish, although they are on the shores of the Hellespont, and they
150	are not allowed boiled meats but only roast, which is the food most
150	convenient for soldiers, requiring only that they should light a fire,
150	and not involving the trouble of carrying about pots and pans.
150	
150	True.
150	
150	And I can hardly be mistaken in saying that sweet sauces are nowhere
150	mentioned in Homer.  In proscribing them, however, he is not singular;
150	all professional athletes are well aware that a man who is to be in
150	good condition should take nothing of the kind.
150	
150	Yes, he said; and knowing this, they are quite right in not taking them.
150	
150	Then you would not approve of Syracusan dinners, and the refinements of
150	Sicilian cookery?
150	
150	I think not.
150	
150	Nor, if a man is to be in condition, would you allow him to have a
150	Corinthian girl as his fair friend?
150	
150	Certainly not.
150	
150	Neither would you approve of the delicacies, as they are thought, of
150	Athenian confectionery?
150	
150	Certainly not.
150	
150	All such feeding and living may be rightly compared by us to melody and
150	song composed in the panharmonic style, and in all the rhythms.
150	Exactly.
150	
150	There complexity engendered license, and here disease; whereas
150	simplicity in music was the parent of temperance in the soul; and
150	simplicity in gymnastic of health in the body.
150	
150	Most true, he said.
150	
150	But when intemperance and disease multiply in a State, halls of justice
150	and medicine are always being opened; and the arts of the doctor and
150	the lawyer give themselves airs, finding how keen is the interest which
150	not only the slaves but the freemen of a city take about them.
150	
150	Of course.
150	
150	And yet what greater proof can there be of a bad and disgraceful state
150	of education than this, that not only artisans and the meaner sort of
150	people need the skill of first-rate physicians and judges, but also
150	those who would profess to have had a liberal education?  Is it not
150	disgraceful, and a great sign of want of good-breeding, that a man
150	should have to go abroad for his law and physic because he has none of
150	his own at home, and must therefore surrender himself into the hands of
150	other men whom he makes lords and judges over him?
150	
150	Of all things, he said, the most disgraceful.
150	
150	Would you say 'most,' I replied, when you consider that there is a
150	further stage of the evil in which a man is not only a life-long
150	litigant, passing all his days in the courts, either as plaintiff or
150	defendant, but is actually led by his bad taste to pride himself on his
150	litigiousness; he imagines that he is a master in dishonesty; able to
150	take every crooked turn, and wriggle into and out of every hole,
150	bending like a withy and getting out of the way of justice: and all for
150	what?--in order to gain small points not worth mentioning, he not
150	knowing that so to order his life as to be able to do without a napping
150	judge is a far higher and nobler sort of thing.  Is not that still more
150	disgraceful?
150	
150	Yes, he said, that is still more disgraceful.
150	
150	Well, I said, and to require the help of medicine, not when a wound has
150	to be cured, or on occasion of an epidemic, but just because, by
150	indolence and a habit of life such as we have been describing, men fill
150	themselves with waters and winds, as if their bodies were a marsh,
150	compelling the ingenious sons of Asclepius to find more names for
150	diseases, such as flatulence and catarrh; is not this, too, a disgrace?
150	
150	Yes, he said, they do certainly give very strange and newfangled names
150	to diseases.
150	
150	Yes, I said, and I do not believe that there were any such diseases in
150	the days of Asclepius; and this I infer from the circumstance that the
150	hero Eurypylus, after he has been wounded in Homer, drinks a posset of
150	Pramnian wine well besprinkled with barley-meal and grated cheese,
150	which are certainly inflammatory, and yet the sons of Asclepius who
150	were at the Trojan war do not blame the damsel who gives him the drink,
150	or rebuke Patroclus, who is treating his case.
150	
150	Well, he said, that was surely an extraordinary drink to be given to a
150	person in his condition.
150	
150	Not so extraordinary, I replied, if you bear in mind that in former
150	days, as is commonly said, before the time of Herodicus, the guild of
150	Asclepius did not practise our present system of medicine, which may be
150	said to educate diseases.  But Herodicus, being a trainer, and himself
150	of a sickly constitution, by a combination of training and doctoring
150	found out a way of torturing first and chiefly himself, and secondly
150	the rest of the world.
150	
150	How was that? he said.
150	
150	By the invention of lingering death; for he had a mortal disease which
150	he perpetually tended, and as recovery was out of the question, he
150	passed his entire life as a valetudinarian; he could do nothing but
150	attend upon himself, and he was in constant torment whenever he
150	departed in anything from his usual regimen, and so dying hard, by the
150	help of science he struggled on to old age.
150	
150	A rare reward of his skill!
150	
150	Yes, I said; a reward which a man might fairly expect who never
150	understood that, if Asclepius did not instruct his descendants in
150	valetudinarian arts, the omission arose, not from ignorance or
150	inexperience of such a branch of medicine, but because he knew that in
150	all well-ordered states every individual has an occupation to which he
150	must attend, and has therefore no leisure to spend in continually being
150	ill.  This we remark in the case of the artisan, but, ludicrously
150	enough, do not apply the same rule to people of the richer sort.
150	
150	How do you mean? he said.
150	
150	I mean this:  When a carpenter is ill he asks the physician for a rough
150	and ready cure; an emetic or a purge or a cautery or the knife,--these
150	are his remedies.  And if some one prescribes for him a course of
150	dietetics, and tells him that he must swathe and swaddle his head, and
150	all that sort of thing, he replies at once that he has no time to be
150	ill, and that he sees no good in a life which is spent in nursing his
150	disease to the neglect of his customary employment; and therefore
150	bidding good-bye to this sort of physician, he resumes his ordinary
150	habits, and either gets well and lives and does his business, or, if
150	his constitution falls, he dies and has no more trouble.
150	
150	Yes, he said, and a man in his condition of life ought to use the art
150	of medicine thus far only.
150	
150	Has he not, I said, an occupation; and what profit would there be in
150	his life if he were deprived of his occupation?
150	
150	Quite true, he said.
150	
150	But with the rich man this is otherwise; of him we do not say that he
150	has any specially appointed work which he must perform, if he would
150	live.
150	
150	He is generally supposed to have nothing to do.
150	
150	Then you never heard of the saying of Phocylides, that as soon as a man
150	has a livelihood he should practise virtue?
150	
150	Nay, he said, I think that he had better begin somewhat sooner.
150	
150	Let us not have a dispute with him about this, I said; but rather ask
150	ourselves:  Is the practice of virtue obligatory on the rich man, or
150	can he live without it?  And if obligatory on him, then let us raise a
150	further question, whether this dieting of disorders which is an
150	impediment to the application of the mind t in carpentering and the
150	mechanical arts, does not equally stand in the way of the sentiment of
150	Phocylides?
150	
150	Of that, he replied, there can be no doubt; such excessive care of the
150	body, when carried beyond the rules of gymnastic, is most inimical to
150	the practice of virtue.
150	
150	Yes, indeed, I replied, and equally incompatible with the management of
150	a house, an army, or an office of state; and, what is most important of
150	all, irreconcilable with any kind of study or thought or
150	self-reflection--there is a constant suspicion that headache and
150	giddiness are to be ascribed to philosophy, and hence all practising or
150	making trial of virtue in the higher sense is absolutely stopped; for a
150	man is always fancying that he is being made ill, and is in constant
150	anxiety about the state of his body.
150	
150	Yes, likely enough.
150	
150	And therefore our politic Asclepius may be supposed to have exhibited
150	the power of his art only to persons who, being generally of healthy
150	constitution and habits of life, had a definite ailment; such as these
150	he cured by purges and operations, and bade them live as usual, herein
150	consulting the interests of the State; but bodies which disease had
150	penetrated through and through he would not have attempted to cure by
150	gradual processes of evacuation and infusion:  he did not want to
150	lengthen out good-for-nothing lives, or to have weak fathers begetting
150	weaker sons;--if a man was not able to live in the ordinary way he had
150	no business to cure him; for such a cure would have been of no use
150	either to himself, or to the State.
150	
150	Then, he said, you regard Asclepius as a statesman.
150	
150	Clearly; and his character is further illustrated by his sons.  Note
150	that they were heroes in the days of old and practised the medicines of
150	which I am speaking at the siege of Troy:  You will remember how, when
150	Pandarus wounded Menelaus, they
150	
150	    Sucked the blood out of the wound, and sprinkled soothing remedies,
150	
150	but they never prescribed what the patient was afterwards to eat or
150	drink in the case of Menelaus, any more than in the case of Eurypylus;
150	the remedies, as they conceived, were enough to heal any man who before
150	he was wounded was healthy and regular in habits; and even though he
150	did happen to drink a posset of Pramnian wine, he might get well all
150	the same.  But they would have nothing to do with unhealthy and
150	intemperate subjects, whose lives were of no use either to themselves
150	or others; the art of medicine was not designed for their good, and
150	though they were as rich as Midas, the sons of Asclepius would have
150	declined to attend them.
150	
150	They were very acute persons, those sons of Asclepius.
150	
150	Naturally so, I replied.  Nevertheless, the tragedians and Pindar
