150	disobeying our behests, although they acknowledge that Asclepius was
150	the son of Apollo, say also that he was bribed into healing a rich man
150	who was at the point of death, and for this reason he was struck by
150	lightning.  But we, in accordance with the principle already affirmed
150	by us, will not believe them when they tell us both;--if he was the son
150	of a god, we maintain that he was not avaricious; or, if he was
150	avaricious he was not the son of a god.
150	
150	All that, Socrates, is excellent; but I should like to put a question
150	to you:  Ought there not to be good physicians in a State, and are not
150	the best those who have treated the greatest number of constitutions
150	good and bad? and are not the best judges in like manner those who are
150	acquainted with all sorts of moral natures?
150	
150	Yes, I said, I too would have good judges and good physicians.  But do
150	you know whom I think good?
150	
150	Will you tell me?
150	
150	I will, if I can.  Let me however note that in the same question you
150	join two things which are not the same.
150	
150	How so? he asked.
150	
150	Why, I said, you join physicians and judges.  Now the most skilful
150	physicians are those who, from their youth upwards, have combined with
150	the knowledge of their art the greatest experience of disease; they had
150	better not be robust in health, and should have had all manner of
150	diseases in their own persons.  For the body, as I conceive, is not the
150	instrument with which they cure the body; in that case we could not
150	allow them ever to be or to have been sickly; but they cure the body
150	with the mind, and the mind which has become and is sick can cure
150	nothing.
150	
150	That is very true, he said.
150	
150	But with the judge it is otherwise; since he governs mind by mind; he
150	ought not therefore to have been trained among vicious minds, and to
150	have associated with them from youth upwards, and to have gone through
150	the whole calendar of crime, only in order that he may quickly infer
150	the crimes of others as he might their bodily diseases from his own
150	self-consciousness; the honourable mind which is to form a healthy
150	judgment should have had no experience or contamination of evil habits
150	when young.  And this is the reason why in youth good men often appear
150	to be simple, and are easily practised upon by the dishonest, because
150	they have no examples of what evil is in their own souls.
150	
150	Yes, he said, they are far too apt to be deceived.
150	
150	Therefore, I said, the judge should not be young; he should have
150	learned to know evil, not from his own soul, but from late and long
150	observation of the nature of evil in others: knowledge should be his
150	guide, not personal experience.
150	
150	Yes, he said, that is the ideal of a judge.
150	
150	Yes, I replied, and he will be a good man (which is my answer to your
150	question); for he is good who has a good soul.  But the cunning and
150	suspicious nature of which we spoke,--he who has committed many crimes,
150	and fancies himself to be a master in wickedness, when he is amongst
150	his fellows, is wonderful in the precautions which he takes, because he
150	judges of them by himself:  but when he gets into the company of men of
150	virtue, who have the experience of age, he appears to be a fool again,
150	owing to his unseasonable suspicions; he cannot recognise an honest
150	man, because he has no pattern of honesty in himself; at the same time,
150	as the bad are more numerous than the good, and he meets with them
150	oftener, he thinks himself, and is by others thought to be, rather wise
150	than foolish.
150	
150	Most true, he said.
150	
150	Then the good and wise judge whom we are seeking is not this man, but
150	the other; for vice cannot know virtue too, but a virtuous nature,
150	educated by time, will acquire a knowledge both of virtue and vice: the
150	virtuous, and not the vicious, man has wisdom--in my opinion.
150	
150	And in mine also.
150	
150	This is the sort of medicine, and this is the sort of law, which you
150	sanction in your State.  They will minister to better natures, giving
150	health both of soul and of body; but those who are diseased in their
150	bodies they will leave to die, and the corrupt and incurable souls they
150	will put an end to themselves.
150	
150	That is clearly the best thing both for the patients and for the State.
150	
150	And thus our youth, having been educated only in that simple music
150	which, as we said, inspires temperance, will be reluctant to go to law.
150	
150	Clearly.
150	
150	And the musician, who, keeping to the same track, is content to
150	practise the simple gymnastic, will have nothing to do with medicine
150	unless in some extreme case.
150	
150	That I quite believe.
150	
150	The very exercises and tolls which he undergoes are intended to
150	stimulate the spirited element of his nature, and not to increase his
150	strength; he will not, like common athletes, use exercise and regimen
150	to develop his muscles.
150	
150	Very right, he said.
150	
150	Neither are the two arts of music and gymnastic really designed, as is
150	often supposed, the one for the training of the soul, the other fir the
150	training of the body.
150	
150	What then is the real object of them?
150	
150	I believe, I said, that the teachers of both have in view chiefly the
150	improvement of the soul.
150	
150	How can that be? he asked.
150	
150	Did you never observe, I said, the effect on the mind itself of
150	exclusive devotion to gymnastic, or the opposite effect of an exclusive
150	devotion to music?
150	
150	In what way shown? he said.
150	
150	The one producing a temper of hardness and ferocity, the other of
150	softness and effeminacy, I replied.
150	
150	Yes, he said, I am quite aware that the mere athlete becomes too much
150	of a savage, and that the mere musician is melted and softened beyond
150	what is good for him.
150	
150	Yet surely, I said, this ferocity only comes from spirit, which, if
150	rightly educated, would give courage, but, if too much intensified, is
150	liable to become hard and brutal.
150	
150	That I quite think.
150	
150	On the other hand the philosopher will have the quality of gentleness.
150	And this also, when too much indulged, will turn to softness, but, if
150	educated rightly, will be gentle and moderate.
150	
150	True.
150	
150	And in our opinion the guardians ought to have both these qualities?
150	
150	Assuredly.
150	
150	And both should be in harmony?
150	
150	Beyond question.
150	
150	And the harmonious soul is both temperate and courageous?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And the inharmonious is cowardly and boorish?
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	And, when a man allows music to play upon him and to pour into his soul
150	through the funnel of his ears those sweet and soft and melancholy airs
150	of which we were just now speaking, and his whole life is passed in
150	warbling and the delights of song; in the first stage of the process
150	the passion or spirit which is in him is tempered like iron, and made
150	useful, instead of brittle and useless.  But, if he carries on the
150	softening and soothing process, in the next stage he begins to melt and
150	waste, until he has wasted away his spirit and cut out the sinews of
150	his soul; and he becomes a feeble warrior.
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	If the element of spirit is naturally weak in him the change is
150	speedily accomplished, but if he have a good deal, then the power of
150	music weakening the spirit renders him excitable;--on the least
150	provocation he flames up at once, and is speedily extinguished; instead
150	of having spirit he grows irritable and passionate and is quite
150	impracticable.
150	
150	Exactly.
150	
150	And so in gymnastics, if a man takes violent exercise and is a great
150	feeder, and the reverse of a great student of music and philosophy, at
150	first the high condition of his body fills him with pride and spirit,
150	and lie becomes twice the man that he was.
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	And what happens? if he do nothing else, and holds no con-a verse with
150	the Muses, does not even that intelligence which there may be in him,
150	having no taste of any sort of learning or enquiry or thought or
150	culture, grow feeble and dull and blind, his mind never waking up or
150	receiving nourishment, and his senses not being purged of their mists?
150	
150	True, he said.
150	
150	And he ends by becoming a hater of philosophy, uncivilized, never using
150	the weapon of persuasion,--he is like a wild beast, all violence and
150	fierceness, and knows no other way of dealing; and he lives in all
150	ignorance and evil conditions, and has no sense of propriety and grace.
150	
150	That is quite true, he said.
150	
150	And as there are two principles of human nature, one the spirited and
150	the other the philosophical, some God, as I should say, has given
150	mankind two arts answering to them (and only indirectly to the soul and
150	body), in order that these two principles (like the strings of an
150	instrument) may be relaxed or drawn tighter until they are duly
150	harmonised.
150	
150	That appears to be the intention.
150	
150	And he who mingles music with gymnastic in the fairest proportions, and
150	best attempers them to the soul, may be rightly called the true
150	musician and harmonist in a far higher sense than the tuner of the
150	strings.
150	
150	You are quite right, Socrates.
150	
150	And such a presiding genius will be always required in our State if the
150	government is to last.
150	
150	Yes, he will be absolutely necessary.
150	
150	Such, then, are our principles of nurture and education: Where would be
150	the use of going into further details about the dances of our citizens,
150	or about their hunting and coursing, their gymnastic and equestrian
150	contests?  For these all follow the general principle, and having found
150	that, we shall have no difficulty in discovering them.
150	
150	I dare say that there will be no difficulty.
150	
150	Very good, I said; then what is the next question?  Must we not ask who
150	are to be rulers and who subjects?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	There can be no doubt that the elder must rule the younger.
150	
150	Clearly.
150	
150	And that the best of these must rule.
150	
150	That is also clear.
150	
150	Now, are not the best husbandmen those who are most devoted to
150	husbandry?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And as we are to have the best of guardians for our city, must they not
150	be those who have most the character of guardians?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And to this end they ought to be wise and efficient, and to have a
150	special care of the State?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	And a man will be most likely to care about that which he loves?
150	
150	To be sure.
150	
150	And he will be most likely to love that which he regards as having the
150	same interests with himself, and that of which the good or evil fortune
150	is supposed by him at any time most to affect his own?
150	
150	Very true, he replied.
150	
150	Then there must be a selection.  Let us note among the guardians those
150	who in their whole life show the greatest eagerness to do what is for
150	the good of their country, and the greatest repugnance to do what is
150	against her interests.
150	
150	Those are the right men.
150	
150	And they will have to be watched at every age, in order that we may see
150	whether they preserve their resolution, and never, under the influence
150	either of force or enchantment, forget or cast off their sense of duty
150	to the State.
150	
150	How cast off? he said.
150	
150	I will explain to you, I replied.  A resolution may go out of a man's
150	mind either with his will or against his will; with his will when he
150	gets rid of a falsehood and learns better, against his will whenever he
150	is deprived of a truth.
150	
150	I understand, he said, the willing loss of a resolution; the meaning of
150	the unwilling I have yet to learn.
150	
150	Why, I said, do you not see that men are unwillingly deprived of good,
150	and willingly of evil?  Is not to have lost the truth an evil, and to
150	possess the truth a good? and you would agree that to conceive things
150	as they are is to possess the truth?
150	
150	Yes, he replied; I agree with you in thinking that mankind are deprived
150	of truth against their will.
150	
150	And is not this involuntary deprivation caused either by theft, or
150	force, or enchantment?
150	
150	Still, he replied, I do not understand you.
150	
150	I fear that I must have been talking darkly, like the tragedians.  I
150	only mean that some men are changed by persuasion and that others
150	forget; argument steals away the hearts of one class, and time of the
150	other; and this I call theft.  Now you understand me?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	Those again who are forced are those whom the violence of some pain or
150	grief compels to change their opinion.
150	
150	I understand, he said, and you are quite right.
150	
150	And you would also acknowledge that the enchanted are those who change
150	their minds either under the softer influence of pleasure, or the
150	sterner influence of fear?
150	
150	Yes, he said; everything that deceives may be said to enchant.
150	
150	Therefore, as I was just now saying, we must enquire who are the best
150	guardians of their own conviction that what they think the interest of
150	the State is to be the rule of their lives.  We must watch them from
150	their youth upwards, and make them perform actions in which they are
150	most likely to forget or to be deceived, and he who remembers and is
150	not deceived is to be selected, and he who falls in the trial is to be
150	rejected.  That will be the way?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And there should also be toils and pains and conflicts prescribed for
150	them, in which they will be made to give further proof of the same
150	qualities.
150	
150	Very right, he replied.
150	
150	And then, I said, we must try them with enchantments that is the third
150	sort of test--and see what will be their behaviour: like those who take
150	colts amid noise and tumult to see if they are of a timid nature, so
150	must we take our youth amid terrors of some kind, and again pass them
150	into pleasures, and prove them more thoroughly than gold is proved in
150	the furnace, that we may discover whether they are armed against all
150	enchantments, and of a noble bearing always, good guardians of
150	themselves and of the music which they have learned, and retaining
150	under all circumstances a rhythmical and harmonious nature, such as
150	will be most serviceable to the individual and to the State.  And he
150	who at every age, as boy and youth and in mature life, has come out of
150	the trial victorious and pure, shall be appointed a ruler and guardian
150	of the State; he shall be honoured in life and death, and shall receive
150	sepulture and other memorials of honour, the greatest that we have to
150	give.  But him who fails, we must reject.  I am inclined to think that
150	this is the sort of way in which our rulers and guardians should be
150	chosen and appointed.  I speak generally, and not with any pretension
150	to exactness.
150	
150	And, speaking generally, I agree with you, he said.
150	
150	And perhaps the word 'guardian' in the fullest sense ought to be
150	applied to this higher class only who preserve us against foreign
150	enemies and maintain peace among our citizens at home, that the one may
150	not have the will, or the others the power, to harm us.  The young men
150	whom we before called guardians may be more properly designated
150	auxiliaries and supporters of the principles of the rulers.
150	
150	I agree with you, he said.
150	
150	How then may we devise one of those needful falsehoods of which we
150	lately spoke--just one royal lie which may deceive the rulers, if that
150	be possible, and at any rate the rest of the city?
150	
150	What sort of lie? he said.
150	
150	Nothing new, I replied; only an old Phoenician tale of what has often
150	occurred before now in other places, (as the poets say, and have made
150	the world believe,) though not in our time, and I do not know whether
150	such an event could ever happen again, or could now even be made
150	probable, if it did.
150	
150	How your words seem to hesitate on your lips!
150	
150	You will not wonder, I replied, at my hesitation when you have heard.
150	
150	Speak, he said, and fear not.
150	
150	Well then, I will speak, although I really know not how to look you in
150	the face, or in what words to utter the audacious fiction, which I
150	propose to communicate gradually, first to the rulers, then to the
150	soldiers, and lastly to the people.  They are to be told that their
150	youth was a dream, and the education and training which they received
150	from us, an appearance only; in reality during all that time they were
150	being formed and fed in the womb of the earth, where they themselves
150	and their arms and appurtenances were manufactured; when they were
150	completed, the earth, their mother, sent them up; and so, their country
150	being their mother and also their nurse, they are bound to advise for
150	her good, and to defend her against attacks, and her citizens they are
150	to regard as children of the earth and their own brothers.
150	
150	You had good reason, he said, to be ashamed of the lie which you were
150	going to tell.
150	
150	True, I replied, but there is more coming; I have only told you half.
150	Citizens, we shall say to them in our tale, you are brothers, yet God
150	has framed you differently.  Some of you have the power of command, and
150	in the composition of these he has mingled gold, wherefore also they
150	have the greatest honour; others he has made of silver, to be
150	auxillaries; others again who are to be husbandmen and craftsmen he has
150	composed of brass and iron; and the species will generally be preserved
150	in the children.  But as all are of the same original stock, a golden
150	parent will sometimes have a silver son, or a silver parent a golden
150	son.  And God proclaims as a first principle to the rulers, and above
150	all else, that there is nothing which should so anxiously guard, or of
150	which they are to be such good guardians, as of the purity of the race.
150	They should observe what elements mingle in their off spring; for if
150	the son of a golden or silver parent has an admixture of brass and
150	iron, then nature orders a transposition of ranks, and the eye of the
150	ruler must not be pitiful towards the child because he has to descend
150	in the scale and become a husbandman or artisan, just as there may be
150	sons of artisans who having an admixture of gold or silver in them are
150	raised to honour, and become guardians or auxiliaries.  For an oracle
150	says that when a man of brass or iron guards the State, it will be
150	destroyed.  Such is the tale; is there any possibility of making our
150	citizens believe in it?
150	
150	Not in the present generation, he replied; there is no way of
150	accomplishing this; but their sons may be made to believe in the tale,
150	and their sons' sons, and posterity after them.
150	
150	I see the difficulty, I replied; yet the fostering of such a belief
150	will make them care more for the city and for one another.  Enough,
150	however, of the fiction, which may now fly abroad upon the wings of
150	rumour, while we arm our earth-born heroes, and lead them forth under
150	the command of their rulers.  Let them look round and select a spot
150	whence they can best suppress insurrection, if any prove refractory
150	within, and also defend themselves against enemies, who like wolves may
150	come down on the fold from without; there let them encamp, and when
150	they have encamped, let them sacrifice to the proper Gods and prepare
150	their dwellings.
150	
150	Just so, he said.
150	
150	And their dwellings must be such as will shield them against the cold
150	of winter and the heat of summer.
150	
150	I suppose that you mean houses, he replied.
150	
150	Yes, I said; but they must be the houses of soldiers, and not of
150	shop-keepers.
150	
150	What is the difference? he said.
150	
150	That I will endeavour to explain, I replied.  To keep watchdogs, who,
150	from want of discipline or hunger, or some evil habit, or evil habit or
150	other, would turn upon the sheep and worry them, and behave not like
150	dogs but wolves, would be a foul and monstrous thing in a shepherd?
150	
150	Truly monstrous, he said.
150	
150	And therefore every care must be taken that our auxiliaries, being
150	stronger than our citizens, may not grow to be too much for them and
150	become savage tyrants instead of friends and allies?
150	
150	Yes, great care should be taken.
150	
150	And would not a really good education furnish the best safeguard?
150	
150	But they are well-educated already, he replied.
150	
150	I cannot be so confident, my dear Glaucon, I said; I am much certain
150	that they ought to be, and that true education, whatever that may be,
150	will have the greatest tendency to civilize and humanize them in their
150	relations to one another, and to those who are under their protection.
150	
150	Very true, he replied.
150	
150	And not only their education, but their habitations, and all that
150	belongs to them, should be such as will neither impair their virtue as
150	guardians, nor tempt them to prey upon the other citizens.  Any man of
150	sense must acknowledge that.
150	
150	He must.
150	
150	Then let us consider what will be their way of life, if they are to
150	realize our idea of them.  In the first place, none of them should have
150	any property of his own beyond what is absolutely necessary; neither
150	should they have a private house or store closed against any one who
150	has a mind to enter; their provisions should be only such as are
150	required by trained warriors, who are men of temperance and courage;
150	they should agree to receive from the citizens a fixed rate of pay,
150	enough to meet the expenses of the year and no more; and they will go
150	and live together like soldiers in a camp.  Gold and silver we will
150	tell them that they have from God; the diviner metal is within them,
150	and they have therefore no need of the dross which is current among
150	men, and ought not to pollute the divine by any such earthly admixture;
150	for that commoner metal has been the source of many unholy deeds, but
150	their own is undefiled.  And they alone of all the citizens may not
150	touch or handle silver or gold, or be under the same roof with them, or
150	wear them, or drink from them.  And this will be their salvation, and
150	they will be the saviours of the State.  But should they ever acquire
150	homes or lands or moneys of their own, they will become housekeepers
150	and husbandmen instead of guardians, enemies and tyrants instead of
150	allies of the other citizens; hating and being hated, plotting and
150	being plotted against, they will pass their whole life in much greater
150	terror of internal than of external enemies, and the hour of ruin, both
150	to themselves and to the rest of the State, will be at hand.  For all
150	which reasons may we not say that thus shall our State be ordered, and
150	that these shall be the regulations appointed by us for guardians
150	concerning their houses and all other matters?  other
150	
150	Yes, said Glaucon.
150	
150	
150	
150	
150	BOOK IV
150	
150	
150	ADEIMANTUS - SOCRATES
150	
150	HERE Adeimantus interposed a question:  How would you answer, Socrates,
150	said he, if a person were to say that you are making these people
150	miserable, and that they are the cause of their own unhappiness; the
150	city in fact belongs to them, but they are none the better for it;
150	whereas other men acquire lands, and build large and handsome houses,
150	and have everything handsome about them, offering sacrifices to the
150	gods on their own account, and practising hospitality; moreover, as you
150	were saying just now, they have gold and silver, and all that is usual
150	among the favourites of fortune; but our poor citizens are no better
150	than mercenaries who are quartered in the city and are always mounting
150	guard?
150	
150	Yes, I said; and you may add that they are only fed, and not paid in
150	addition to their food, like other men; and therefore they cannot, if
150	they would, take a journey of pleasure; they have no money to spend on
150	a mistress or any other luxurious fancy, which, as the world goes, is
150	thought to be happiness; and many other accusations of the same nature
150	might be added.
150	
150	But, said he, let us suppose all this to be included in the charge.
150	
150	You mean to ask, I said, what will be our answer?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	If we proceed along the old path, my belief, I said, is that we shall
150	find the answer.  And our answer will be that, even as they are, our
150	guardians may very likely be the happiest of men; but that our aim in
150	founding the State was not the disproportionate happiness of any one
150	class, but the greatest happiness of the whole; we thought that in a
150	State which is ordered with a view to the good of the whole we should
150	be most likely to find Justice, and in the ill-ordered State injustice:
150	and, having found them, we might then decide which of the two is the
150	happier.  At present, I take it, we are fashioning the happy State, not
150	piecemeal, or with a view of making a few happy citizens, but as a
150	whole; and by-and-by we will proceed to view the opposite kind of
150	State.  Suppose that we were painting a statue, and some one came up to
150	us and said, Why do you not put the most beautiful colours on the most
150	beautiful parts of the body--the eyes ought to be purple, but you have
150	made them black--to him we might fairly answer, Sir, you would not
150	surely have us beautify the eyes to such a degree that they are no
150	longer eyes; consider rather whether, by giving this and the other
150	features their due proportion, we make the whole beautiful.  And so I
150	say to you, do not compel us to assign to the guardians a sort of
150	happiness which will make them anything but guardians; for we too can
150	clothe our husbandmen in royal apparel, and set crowns of gold on their
150	heads, and bid them till the ground as much as they like, and no more.
150	Our potters also might be allowed to repose on couches, and feast by
150	the fireside, passing round the winecup, while their wheel is
150	conveniently at hand, and working at pottery only as much as they like;
150	in this way we might make every class happy-and then, as you imagine,
150	the whole State would be happy.  But do not put this idea into our
150	heads; for, if we listen to you, the husbandman will be no longer a
150	husbandman, the potter will cease to be a potter, and no one will have
150	the character of any distinct class in the State.  Now this is not of
150	much consequence where the corruption of society, and pretension to be
150	what you are not, is confined to cobblers; but when the guardians of
150	the laws and of the government are only seemingly and not real
150	guardians, then see how they turn the State upside down; and on the
150	other hand they alone have the power of giving order and happiness to
150	the State.  We mean our guardians to be true saviours and not the
150	destroyers of the State, whereas our opponent is thinking of peasants
150	at a festival, who are enjoying a life of revelry, not of citizens who
150	are doing their duty to the State.  But, if so, we mean different
150	things, and he is speaking of something which is not a State.  And
150	therefore we must consider whether in appointing our guardians we would
150	look to their greatest happiness individually, or whether this
150	principle of happiness does not rather reside in the State as a whole.
150	But the latter be the truth, then the guardians and auxillaries, and
150	all others equally with them, must be compelled or induced to do their
150	own work in the best way.  And thus the whole State will grow up in a
150	noble order, and the several classes will receive the proportion of
150	happiness which nature assigns to them.
150	
150	I think that you are quite right.
150	
150	I wonder whether you will agree with another remark which occurs to me.
150	
150	What may that be?
150	
150	There seem to be two causes of the deterioration of the arts.
150	
150	What are they?
150	
150	Wealth, I said, and poverty.
150	
150	How do they act?
150	
150	The process is as follows:  When a potter becomes rich, will he, think
150	you, any longer take the same pains with his art?
150	
150	Certainly not.
150	
150	He will grow more and more indolent and careless?
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	And the result will be that he becomes a worse potter?
150	
150	Yes; he greatly deteriorates.
150	
150	But, on the other hand, if he has no money, and cannot provide himself
150	tools or instruments, he will not work equally well himself, nor will
150	he teach his sons or apprentices to work equally well.
150	
150	Certainly not.
150	
150	Then, under the influence either of poverty or of wealth, workmen and
150	their work are equally liable to degenerate?
150	
150	That is evident.
150	
150	Here, then, is a discovery of new evils, I said, against which the
150	guardians will have to watch, or they will creep into the city
150	unobserved.
150	
150	What evils?
150	
150	Wealth, I said, and poverty; the one is the parent of luxury and
150	indolence, and the other of meanness and viciousness, and both of
150	discontent.
150	
150	That is very true, he replied; but still I should like to know,
150	Socrates, how our city will be able to go to war, especially against an
150	enemy who is rich and powerful, if deprived of the sinews of war.
150	
150	There would certainly be a difficulty, I replied, in going to war with
150	one such enemy; but there is no difficulty where there are two of them.
150	
150	How so? he asked.
150	
150	In the first place, I said, if we have to fight, our side will be
150	trained warriors fighting against an army of rich men.
150	
150	That is true, he said.
150	
150	And do you not suppose, Adeimantus, that a single boxer who was perfect
150	in his art would easily be a match for two stout and well-to-do
150	gentlemen who were not boxers?
150	
150	Hardly, if they came upon him at once.
150	
150	What, not, I said, if he were able to run away and then turn and strike
150	at the one who first came up?  And supposing he were to do this several
150	times under the heat of a scorching sun, might he not, being an expert,
150	overturn more than one stout personage?
150	
150	Certainly, he said, there would be nothing wonderful in that.
150	
150	And yet rich men probably have a greater superiority in the science and
150	practice of boxing than they have in military qualities.
150	
150	Likely enough.
150	
150	Then we may assume that our athletes will be able to fight with two or
150	three times their own number?
150	
150	I agree with you, for I think you right.
150	
150	And suppose that, before engaging, our citizens send an embassy to one
150	of the two cities, telling them what is the truth: Silver and gold we
150	neither have nor are permitted to have, but you may; do you therefore
150	come and help us in war, of and take the spoils of the other city:
150	Who, on hearing these words, would choose to fight against lean wiry
150	dogs, rather than, with the dogs on their side, against fat and tender
150	sheep?
150	
150	That is not likely; and yet there might be a danger to the poor State
150	if the wealth of many States were to be gathered into one.
150	
150	But how simple of you to use the term State at all of any but our own!
150	
150	Why so?
150	
150	You ought to speak of other States in the plural number; not one of
150	them is a city, but many cities, as they say in the game.  For indeed
150	any city, however small, is in fact divided into two, one the city of
150	the poor, the other of the rich; these are at war with one another; and
150	in either there are many smaller divisions, and you would be altogether
150	beside the mark if you treated them all as a single State.  But if you
150	deal with them as many, and give the wealth or power or persons of the
150	one to the others, you will always have a great many friends and not
150	many enemies.  And your State, while the wise order which has now been
150	prescribed continues to prevail in her, will be the greatest of States,
150	I do not mean to say in reputation or appearance, but in deed and
150	truth, though she number not more than a thousand defenders.  A single
150	State which is her equal you will hardly find, either among Hellenes or
150	barbarians, though many that appear to be as great and many times
150	greater.
150	
150	That is most true, he said.
150	
150	And what, I said, will be the best limit for our rulers to fix when
150	they are considering the size of the State and the amount of territory
150	which they are to include, and beyond which they will not go?
150	
150	What limit would you propose?
150	
150	I would allow the State to increase so far as is consistent with unity;
150	that, I think, is the proper limit.
150	
150	Very good, he said.
150	
150	Here then, I said, is another order which will have to be conveyed to
150	our guardians:  Let our city be accounted neither large nor small, but
150	one and self-sufficing.
150	
150	And surely, said he, this is not a very severe order which we impose
150	upon them.
150	
150	And the other, said I, of which we were speaking before is lighter
150	still, I mean the duty of degrading the offspring of the guardians
150	when inferior, and of elevating into the rank of guardians the
150	offspring of the lower classes, when naturally superior.  The intention
150	was, that, in the case of the citizens generally, each individual
150	should be put to the use for which nature which nature intended him,
150	one to one work, and then every man would do his own business, and be
150	one and not many; and so the whole city would be one and not many.
150	
150	Yes, he said; that is not so difficult.
150	
150	The regulations which we are prescribing, my good Adeimantus, are not,
150	as might be supposed, a number of great principles, but trifles all, if
150	care be taken, as the saying is, of the one great thing,--a thing,
150	however, which I would rather call, not great, but sufficient for our
150	purpose.
150	
150	What may that be? he asked.
150	
150	Education, I said, and nurture:  If our citizens are well educated, and
150	grow into sensible men, they will easily see their way through all
150	these, as well as other matters which I omit; such, for example, as
150	marriage, the possession of women and the procreation of children,
150	which will all follow the general principle that friends have all
150	things in common, as the proverb says.
150	
150	That will be the best way of settling them.
150	
150	Also, I said, the State, if once started well, moves with accumulating
150	force like a wheel.  For good nurture and education implant good
150	constitutions, and these good constitutions taking root in a good
150	education improve more and more, and this improvement affects the breed
150	in man as in other animals.
150	
150	Very possibly, he said.
150	
150	Then to sum up:  This is the point to which, above all, the attention
150	of our rulers should be directed,--that music and gymnastic be
150	preserved in their original form, and no innovation made.  They must do
150	their utmost to maintain them intact.  And when any one says that
150	mankind most regard
150	
150	    The newest song which the singers have,
150	
150	they will be afraid that he may be praising, not new songs, but a new
150	kind of song; and this ought not to be praised, or conceived to be the
150	meaning of the poet; for any musical innovation is full of danger to
150	the whole State, and ought to be prohibited.  So Damon tells me, and I
150	can quite believe him;-he says that when modes of music change, of the
150	State always change with them.
150	
150	Yes, said Adeimantus; and you may add my suffrage to Damon's and your
150	own.
150	
150	Then, I said, our guardians must lay the foundations of their fortress
150	in music?
150	
150	Yes, he said; the lawlessness of which you speak too easily steals in.
150	
150	Yes, I replied, in the form of amusement; and at first sight it appears
150	harmless.
150	
150	Why, yes, he said, and there is no harm; were it not that little by
150	little this spirit of licence, finding a home, imperceptibly penetrates
150	into manners and customs; whence, issuing with greater force, it
150	invades contracts between man and man, and from contracts goes on to
150	laws and constitutions, in utter recklessness, ending at last,
150	Socrates, by an overthrow of all rights, private as well as public.
150	
150	Is that true?  I said.
150	
150	That is my belief, he replied.
150	
150	Then, as I was saying, our youth should be trained from the first in a
150	stricter system, for if amusements become lawless, and the youths
150	themselves become lawless, they can never grow up into well-conducted
150	and virtuous citizens.
150	
150	Very true, he said.
150	
150	And when they have made a good beginning in play, and by the help of
150	music have gained the habit of good order, then this habit of order, in
150	a manner how unlike the lawless play of the others! will accompany them
150	in all their actions and be a principle of growth to them, and if there
150	be any fallen places a principle in the State will raise them up again.
150	
150	Very true, he said.
150	
150	Thus educated, they will invent for themselves any lesser rules which
150	their predecessors have altogether neglected.
150	
150	What do you mean?
150	
150	I mean such things as these:--when the young are to be silent before
150	their elders; how they are to show respect to them by standing and
150	making them sit; what honour is due to parents; what garments or shoes
150	are to be worn; the mode of dressing the hair; deportment and manners
150	in general.  You would agree with me?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	But there is, I think, small wisdom in legislating about such
150	matters,--I doubt if it is ever done; nor are any precise written
150	enactments about them likely to be lasting.
150	
150	Impossible.
150	
150	It would seem, Adeimantus, that the direction in which education starts
150	a man, will determine his future life.  Does not like always attract
150	like?
150	
150	To be sure.
150	
150	Until some one rare and grand result is reached which may be good, and
150	may be the reverse of good?
150	
150	That is not to be denied.
150	
150	And for this reason, I said, I shall not attempt to legislate further
150	about them.
150	
150	Naturally enough, he replied.
150	
150	Well, and about the business of the agora, dealings and the ordinary
150	dealings between man and man, or again about agreements with the
150	commencement with artisans; about insult and injury, of the
150	commencement of actions, and the appointment of juries, what would you
150	say? there may also arise questions about any impositions and
150	extractions of market and harbour dues which may be required, and in
150	general about the regulations of markets, police, harbours, and the
150	like.  But, oh heavens! shall we condescend to legislate on any of
150	these particulars?
150	
150	I think, he said, that there is no need to impose laws about them on
150	good men; what regulations are necessary they will find out soon enough
150	for themselves.
150	
150	Yes, I said, my friend, if God will only preserve to them the laws
150	which we have given them.
150	
150	And without divine help, said Adeimantus, they will go on for ever
150	making and mending their laws and their lives in the hope of attaining
150	perfection.
150	
150	You would compare them, I said, to those invalids who, having no
150	self-restraint, will not leave off their habits of intemperance?
150	
150	Exactly.
150	
150	Yes, I said; and what a delightful life they lead! they are always
150	doctoring and increasing and complicating their disorders, and always
150	fancying that they will be cured by any nostrum which anybody advises
150	them to try.
150	
150	Such cases are very common, he said, with invalids of this sort.
150	
150	Yes, I replied; and the charming thing is that they deem him their
150	worst enemy who tells them the truth, which is simply that, unless they
150	give up eating and drinking and wenching and idling, neither drug nor
150	cautery nor spell nor amulet nor any other remedy will avail.
150	
150	Charming! he replied.  I see nothing charming in going into a passion
150	with a man who tells you what is right.
150	
150	These gentlemen, I said, do not seem to be in your good graces.
150	
150	Assuredly not.
150	
150	Nor would you praise the behaviour of States which act like the men
150	whom I was just now describing.  For are there not ill-ordered States
150	in which the citizens are forbidden under pain of death to alter the
150	constitution; and yet he who most sweetly courts those who live under
150	this regime and indulges them and fawns upon them and is skilful in
150	anticipating and gratifying their humours is held to be a great and
150	good statesman--do not these States resemble the persons whom I was
150	describing?
150	
150	Yes, he said; the States are as bad as the men; and I am very far from
150	praising them.
150	
150	But do you not admire, I said, the coolness and dexterity of these
150	ready ministers of political corruption?
150	
150	Yes, he said, I do; but not of all of them, for there are some whom the
150	applause of the multitude has deluded into the belief that they are
150	really statesmen, and these are not much to be admired.
150	
150	What do you mean?  I said; you should have more feeling for them.  When
150	a man cannot measure, and a great many others who cannot measure
150	declare that he is four cubits high, can he help believing what they
150	say?
150	
150	Nay, he said, certainly not in that case.
150	
150	Well, then, do not be angry with them; for are they not as good as a
150	play, trying their hand at paltry reforms such as I was describing;
150	they are always fancying that by legislation they will make an end of
150	frauds in contracts, and the other rascalities which I was mentioning,
150	not knowing that they are in reality cutting off the heads of a hydra?
150	
150	Yes, he said; that is just what they are doing.
150	
150	I conceive, I said, that the true legislator will not trouble himself
150	with this class of enactments whether concerning laws or the
150	constitution either in an ill-ordered or in a well-ordered State; for
150	in the former they are quite useless, and in the latter there will be
150	no difficulty in devising them; and many of them will naturally flow
150	out of our previous regulations.
150	
150	What, then, he said, is still remaining to us of the work of
150	legislation?
150	
150	Nothing to us, I replied; but to Apollo, the God of Delphi, there
150	remains the ordering of the greatest and noblest and chiefest things of
150	all.
150	
150	Which are they? he said.
150	
150	The institution of temples and sacrifices, and the entire service of
150	gods, demigods, and heroes; also the ordering of the repositories of
150	the dead, and the rites which have to be observed by him who would
150	propitiate the inhabitants of the world below.  These are matters of
150	which we are ignorant ourselves, and as founders of a city we should be
150	unwise in trusting them to any interpreter but our ancestral deity.  He
150	is the god who sits in the center, on the navel of the earth, and he is
150	the interpreter of religion to all mankind.
150	
150	You are right, and we will do as you propose.
150	
150	But where, amid all this, is justice? son of Ariston, tell me where.
150	Now that our city has been made habitable, light a candle and search,
150	and get your brother and Polemarchus and the rest of our friends to
150	help, and let us see where in it we can discover justice and where
150	injustice, and in what they differ from one another, and which of them
150	the man who would be happy should have for his portion, whether seen or
150	unseen by gods and men.
150	
150	SOCRATES - GLAUCON
150	
150	Nonsense, said Glaucon:  did you not promise to search yourself, saying
150	that for you not to help justice in her need would be an impiety?
150	
150	I do not deny that I said so, and as you remind me, I will be as good
150	as my word; but you must join.
150	
150	We will, he replied.
150	
150	Well, then, I hope to make the discovery in this way:  I mean to begin
150	with the assumption that our State, if rightly ordered, is perfect.
150	
150	That is most certain.
150	
150	And being perfect, is therefore wise and valiant and temperate and just.
150	
150	That is likewise clear.
150	
150	And whichever of these qualities we find in the State, the one which is
150	not found will be the residue?
150	
150	Very good.
150	
150	If there were four things, and we were searching for one of them,
150	wherever it might be, the one sought for might be known to us from the
150	first, and there would be no further trouble; or we might know the
150	other three first, and then the fourth would clearly be the one left.
150	
150	Very true, he said.
150	
150	And is not a similar method to be pursued about the virtues, which are
150	also four in number?
150	
150	Clearly.
150	
150	First among the virtues found in the State, wisdom comes into view, and
150	in this I detect a certain peculiarity.
150	
150	What is that?
150	
150	The State which we have been describing is said to be wise as being
150	good in counsel?
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	And good counsel is clearly a kind of knowledge, for not by ignorance,
150	but by knowledge, do men counsel well?
150	
150	Clearly.
150	
150	And the kinds of knowledge in a State are many and diverse?
150	
150	Of course.
150	
150	There is the knowledge of the carpenter; but is that the sort of
150	knowledge which gives a city the title of wise and good in counsel?
150	
150	Certainly not; that would only give a city the reputation of skill in
150	carpentering.
150	
150	Then a city is not to be called wise because possessing a knowledge
150	which counsels for the best about wooden implements?
150	
150	Certainly not.
150	
150	Nor by reason of a knowledge which advises about brazen pots, I said,
150	nor as possessing any other similar knowledge?
150	
150	Not by reason of any of them, he said.
150	
150	Nor yet by reason of a knowledge which cultivates the earth; that would
150	give the city the name of agricultural?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	Well, I said, and is there any knowledge in our recently founded State
150	among any of the citizens which advises, not about any particular thing
150	in the State, but about the whole, and considers how a State can best
150	deal with itself and with other States?
150	
150	There certainly is.
150	
150	And what is knowledge, and among whom is it found?  I asked.
150	
150	It is the knowledge of the guardians, he replied, and found among those
150	whom we were just now describing as perfect guardians.
150	
150	And what is the name which the city derives from the possession of this
150	sort of knowledge?
150	
150	The name of good in counsel and truly wise.
150	
150	And will there be in our city more of these true guardians or more
150	smiths?
150	
150	The smiths, he replied, will be far more numerous.
150	
150	Will not the guardians be the smallest of all the classes who receive a
150	name from the profession of some kind of knowledge?
150	
150	Much the smallest.
150	
150	And so by reason of the smallest part or class, and of the knowledge
150	which resides in this presiding and ruling part of itself, the whole
150	State, being thus constituted according to nature, will be wise; and
150	this, which has the only knowledge worthy to be called wisdom, has been
150	ordained by nature to be of all classes the least.
150	
150	Most true.
150	
150	Thus, then, I said, the nature and place in the State of one of the
150	four virtues has somehow or other been discovered.
150	
150	And, in my humble opinion, very satisfactorily discovered, he replied.
150	
150	Again, I said, there is no difficulty in seeing the nature of courage;
150	and in what part that quality resides which gives the name of
150	courageous to the State.
150	
150	How do you mean?
150	
150	Why, I said, every one who calls any State courageous or cowardly, will
150	be thinking of the part which fights and goes out to war on the State's
150	behalf.
150	
150	No one, he replied, would ever think of any other.
150	
150	Certainly not.
150	
150	The rest of the citizens may be courageous or may be cowardly but their
150	courage or cowardice will not, as I conceive, have the effect of making
150	the city either the one or the other.
150	
150	The city will be courageous in virtue of a portion of herself which
150	preserves under all circumstances that opinion about the nature of
150	things to be feared and not to be feared in which our legislator
150	educated them; and this is what you term courage.
150	
150	I should like to hear what you are saying once more, for I do not think
150	that I perfectly understand you.
150	
150	I mean that courage is a kind of salvation.
150	
150	Salvation of what?
150	
150	Of the opinion respecting things to be feared, what they are and of
150	what nature, which the law implants through education; and I mean by
150	the words 'under all circumstances' to intimate that in pleasure or in
150	pain, or under the influence of desire or fear, a man preserves, and
150	does not lose this opinion.  Shall I give you an illustration?
150	
150	If you please.
150	
150	You know, I said, that dyers, when they want to dye wool for making the
150	true sea-purple, begin by selecting their white colour first; this they
150	prepare and dress with much care and pains, in order that the white
150	ground may take the purple hue in full perfection.  The dyeing then
150	proceeds; and whatever is dyed in this manner becomes a fast colour,
150	and no washing either with lyes or without them can take away the
150	bloom.  But, when the ground has not been duly prepared, you will have
150	noticed how poor is the look either of purple or of any other colour.
150	
150	Yes, he said; I know that they have a washed-out and ridiculous
150	appearance.
150	
150	Then now, I said, you will understand what our object was in selecting
150	our soldiers, and educating them in music and gymnastic; we were
150	contriving influences which would prepare them to take the dye of the
150	laws in perfection, and the colour of their opinion about dangers and
150	of every other opinion was to be indelibly fixed by their nurture and
150	training, not to be washed away by such potent lyes as
150	pleasure--mightier agent far in washing the soul than any soda or lye;
150	or by sorrow, fear, and desire, the mightiest of all other solvents.
150	And this sort of universal saving power of true opinion in conformity
150	with law about real and false dangers I call and maintain to be
150	courage, unless you disagree.
150	
150	But I agree, he replied; for I suppose that you mean to exclude mere
150	uninstructed courage, such as that of a wild beast or of a slave--this,
150	in your opinion, is not the courage which the law ordains, and ought to
150	have another name.
150	
150	Most certainly.
150	
150	Then I may infer courage to be such as you describe?
150	
150	Why, yes, said I, you may, and if you add the words 'of a citizen,' you
150	will not be far wrong;--hereafter, if you like, we will carry the
150	examination further, but at present we are we w seeking not for courage
150	but justice; and for the purpose of our enquiry we have said enough.
150	
150	You are right, he replied.
150	
150	Two virtues remain to be discovered in the State-first temperance, and
150	then justice which is the end of our search.
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	Now, can we find justice without troubling ourselves about temperance?
150	
150	I do not know how that can be accomplished, he said, nor do I desire
150	that justice should be brought to light and temperance lost sight of;
150	and therefore I wish that you would do me the favour of considering
150	temperance first.
150	
150	Certainly, I replied, I should not be justified in refusing your
150	request.
150	
150	Then consider, he said.
150	
150	Yes, I replied; I will; and as far as I can at present see, the virtue
150	of temperance has more of the nature of harmony and symphony than the
150	preceding.
150	
150	How so? he asked.
150	
150	Temperance, I replied, is the ordering or controlling of certain
150	pleasures and desires; this is curiously enough implied in the saying
150	of 'a man being his own master' and other traces of the same notion may
150	be found in language.
150	
150	No doubt, he said.
150	
150	There is something ridiculous in the expression 'master of himself';
150	for the master is also the servant and the servant the master; and in
150	all these modes of speaking the same person is denoted.
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	The meaning is, I believe, that in the human soul there is a better and
150	also a worse principle; and when the better has the worse under
150	control, then a man is said to be master of himself; and this is a term
150	of praise:  but when, owing to evil education or association, the
150	better principle, which is also the smaller, is overwhelmed by the
150	greater mass of the worse--in this case he is blamed and is called the
150	slave of self and unprincipled.
150	
150	Yes, there is reason in that.
150	
150	And now, I said, look at our newly created State, and there you will
150	find one of these two conditions realised; for the State, as you will
150	acknowledge, may be justly called master of itself, if the words
150	'temperance' and 'self-mastery' truly express the rule of the better
150	part over the worse.
150	
150	Yes, he said, I see that what you say is true.
150	
150	Let me further note that the manifold and complex pleasures and desires
150	and pains are generally found in children and women and servants, and
150	in the freemen so called who are of the lowest and more numerous class.
150	
150	Certainly, he said.
150	
150	Whereas the simple and moderate desires which follow reason, and are
150	under the guidance of mind and true opinion, are to be found only in a
150	few, and those the best born and best educated.
150	
150	Very true.  These two, as you may perceive, have a place in our State;
150	and the meaner desires of the are held down by the virtuous desires and
150	wisdom of the few.
150	
150	That I perceive, he said.
150	
150	Then if there be any city which may be described as master of its own
150	pleasures and desires, and master of itself, ours may claim such a
150	designation?
150	
150	Certainly, he replied.
150	
150	It may also be called temperate, and for the same reasons?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And if there be any State in which rulers and subjects will be agreed
150	as to the question who are to rule, that again will be our State?
150	
150	Undoubtedly.
150	
150	And the citizens being thus agreed among themselves, in which class
150	will temperance be found--in the rulers or in the subjects?
150	
150	In both, as I should imagine, he replied.
150	
150	Do you observe that we were not far wrong in our guess that temperance
150	was a sort of harmony?
150	
150	Why so?
150	
150	Why, because temperance is unlike courage and wisdom, each of which
150	resides in a part only, the one making the State wise and the other
150	valiant; not so temperance, which extends to the whole, and runs
150	through all the notes of the scale, and produces a harmony of the
150	weaker and the stronger and the middle class, whether you suppose them
150	to be stronger or weaker in wisdom or power or numbers or wealth, or
150	anything else.  Most truly then may we deem temperance to be the
150	agreement of the naturally superior and inferior, as to the right to
150	rule of either, both in states and individuals.
150	
150	I entirely agree with you.
150	
150	And so, I said, we may consider three out of the four virtues to have
150	been discovered in our State.  The last of those qualities which make a
150	state virtuous must be justice, if we only knew what that was.
150	
150	The inference is obvious.
150	
150	The time then has arrived, Glaucon, when, like huntsmen, we should
150	surround the cover, and look sharp that justice does not steal away,
150	and pass out of sight and escape us; for beyond a doubt she is
150	somewhere in this country:  watch therefore and strive to catch a sight
150	of her, and if you see her first, let me know.
150	
150	Would that I could! but you should regard me rather as a follower who
150	has just eyes enough to, see what you show him--that is about as much
150	as I am good for.
150	
150	Offer up a prayer with me and follow.
150	
150	I will, but you must show me the way.
150	
150	Here is no path, I said, and the wood is dark and perplexing; still we
150	must push on.
150	
150	Let us push on.
150	
150	Here I saw something:  Halloo!  I said, I begin to perceive a track,
150	and I believe that the quarry will not escape.
150	
150	Good news, he said.
150	
150	Truly, I said, we are stupid fellows.
150	
150	Why so?
150	
150	Why, my good sir, at the beginning of our enquiry, ages ago, there was
150	justice tumbling out at our feet, and we never saw her; nothing could
150	be more ridiculous.  Like people who go about looking for what they
150	have in their hands--that was the way with us--we looked not at what we
150	were seeking, but at what was far off in the distance; and therefore, I
150	suppose, we missed her.
150	
150	What do you mean?
150	
150	I mean to say that in reality for a long time past we have been talking
150	of justice, and have failed to recognise her.
150	
150	I grow impatient at the length of your exordium.
150	
150	Well then, tell me, I said, whether I am right or not: You remember the
150	original principle which we were always laying down at the foundation
150	of the State, that one man should practise one thing only, the thing to
150	which his nature was best adapted;--now justice is this principle or a
150	part of it.
150	
150	Yes, we often said that one man should do one thing only.
150	
150	Further, we affirmed that justice was doing one's own business, and not
150	being a busybody; we said so again and again, and many others have said
150	the same to us.
150	
150	Yes, we said so.
150	
150	Then to do one's own business in a certain way may be assumed to be
150	justice.  Can you tell me whence I derive this inference?
150	
150	I cannot, but I should like to be told.
150	
150	Because I think that this is the only virtue which remains in the State
150	when the other virtues of temperance and courage and wisdom are
150	abstracted; and, that this is the ultimate cause and condition of the
150	existence of all of them, and while remaining in them is also their
150	preservative; and we were saying that if the three were discovered by
150	us, justice would be the fourth or remaining one.
150	
150	That follows of necessity.
150	
150	If we are asked to determine which of these four qualities by its
150	presence contributes most to the excellence of the State, whether the
150	agreement of rulers and subjects, or the preservation in the soldiers
150	of the opinion which the law ordains about the true nature of dangers,
150	or wisdom and watchfulness in the rulers, or whether this other which I
150	am mentioning, and which is found in children and women, slave and
150	freeman, artisan, ruler, subject,--the quality, I mean, of every one
150	doing his own work, and not being a busybody, would claim the palm--the
150	question is not so easily answered.
150	
150	Certainly, he replied, there would be a difficulty in saying which.
150	
150	Then the power of each individual in the State to do his own work
150	appears to compete with the other political virtues, wisdom,
150	temperance, courage.
150	
150	Yes, he said.
150	
150	And the virtue which enters into this competition is justice?
150	
150	Exactly.
150	
150	Let us look at the question from another point of view: Are not the
150	rulers in a State those to whom you would entrust the office of
150	determining suits at law?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	And are suits decided on any other ground but that a man may neither
150	take what is another's, nor be deprived of what is his own?
150	
150	Yes; that is their principle.
150	
150	Which is a just principle?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	Then on this view also justice will be admitted to be the having and
150	doing what is a man's own, and belongs to him?
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	Think, now, and say whether you agree with me or not.  Suppose a
150	carpenter to be doing the business of a cobbler, or a cobbler of a
150	carpenter; and suppose them to exchange their implements or their
150	duties, or the same person to be doing the work of both, or whatever be
150	the change; do you think that any great harm would result to the State?
150	
150	Not much.
150	
150	But when the cobbler or any other man whom nature designed to be a
150	trader, having his heart lifted up by wealth or strength or the number
150	of his followers, or any like advantage, attempts to force his way into
150	the class of warriors, or a warrior into that of legislators and
150	guardians, for which he is unfitted, and either to take the implements
150	or the duties of the other; or when one man is trader, legislator, and
150	warrior all in one, then I think you will agree with me in saying that
150	this interchange and this meddling of one with another is the ruin of
150	the State.
150	
150	Most true.
150	
150	Seeing then, I said, that there are three distinct classes, any
150	meddling of one with another, or the change of one into another, is the
150	greatest harm to the State, and may be most justly termed evil-doing?
150	
150	Precisely.
150	
150	And the greatest degree of evil-doing to one's own city would be termed
150	by you injustice?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	This then is injustice; and on the other hand when the trader, the
150	auxiliary, and the guardian each do their own business, that is
150	justice, and will make the city just.
150	
150	I agree with you.
150	
150	We will not, I said, be over-positive as yet; but if, on trial, this
150	conception of justice be verified in the individual as well as in the
150	State, there will be no longer any room for doubt; if it be not
150	verified, we must have a fresh enquiry.  First let us complete the old
150	investigation, which we began, as you remember, under the impression
150	that, if we could previously examine justice on the larger scale, there
150	would be less difficulty in discerning her in the individual.  That
150	larger example appeared to be the State, and accordingly we constructed
150	as good a one as we could, knowing well that in the good State justice
150	would be found.  Let the discovery which we made be now applied to the
150	individual--if they agree, we shall be satisfied; or, if there be a
150	difference in the individual, we will come back to the State and have
150	another trial of the theory.  The friction of the two when rubbed
150	together may possibly strike a light in which justice will shine forth,
150	and the vision which is then revealed we will fix in our souls.
150	
150	That will be in regular course; let us do as you say.
150	
150	I proceeded to ask:  When two things, a greater and less, are called by
150	the same name, are they like or unlike in so far as they are called the
150	same?
150	
150	Like, he replied.
150	
150	The just man then, if we regard the idea of justice only, will be like
150	the just State?
150	
150	He will.
150	
150	And a State was thought by us to be just when the three classes in the
150	State severally did their own business; and also thought to be
150	temperate and valiant and wise by reason of certain other affections
150	and qualities of these same classes?
150	
150	True, he said.
150	
150	And so of the individual; we may assume that he has the same three
150	principles in his own soul which are found in the State; and he may be
150	rightly described in the same terms, because he is affected in the same
150	manner?
150	
150	Certainly, he said.
150	
150	Once more then, O my friend, we have alighted upon an easy
150	question--whether the soul has these three principles or not?
150	
150	An easy question!  Nay, rather, Socrates, the proverb holds that hard
150	is the good.
150	
150	Very true, I said; and I do not think that the method which we are
150	employing is at all adequate to the accurate solution of this question;
150	the true method is another and a longer one.  Still we may arrive at a
150	solution not below the level of the previous enquiry.
150	
150	May we not be satisfied with that? he said;--under the circumstances, I
150	am quite content.
150	
150	I too, I replied, shall be extremely well satisfied.
150	
150	Then faint not in pursuing the speculation, he said.
150	
150	Must we not acknowledge, I said, that in each of us there are the same
150	principles and habits which there are in the State; and that from the
150	individual they pass into the State?--how else can they come there?
150	Take the quality of passion or spirit;--it would be ridiculous to
150	imagine that this quality, when found in States, is not derived from
150	the individuals who are supposed to possess it, e.g. the Thracians,
150	Scythians, and in general the northern nations; and the same may be
150	said of the love of knowledge, which is the special characteristic of
150	our part of the world, or of the love of money, which may, with equal
150	truth, be attributed to the Phoenicians and Egyptians.
150	
150	Exactly so, he said.
150	
150	There is no difficulty in understanding this.
150	
150	None whatever.
150	
150	But the question is not quite so easy when we proceed to ask whether
150	these principles are three or one; whether, that is to say, we learn
150	with one part of our nature, are angry with another, and with a third
150	part desire the satisfaction of our natural appetites; or whether the
150	whole soul comes into play in each sort of action--to determine that is
150	the difficulty.
150	
150	Yes, he said; there lies the difficulty.
150	
150	Then let us now try and determine whether they are the same or
150	different.
150	
150	How can we? he asked.
150	
150	I replied as follows:  The same thing clearly cannot act or be acted
150	upon in the same part or in relation to the same thing at the same
150	time, in contrary ways; and therefore whenever this contradiction
150	occurs in things apparently the same, we know that they are really not
150	the same, but different.
150	
150	Good.
150	
150	For example, I said, can the same thing be at rest and in motion at the
150	same time in the same part?
150	
150	Impossible.
150	
150	Still, I said, let us have a more precise statement of terms, lest we
150	should hereafter fall out by the way.  Imagine the case of a man who is
150	standing and also moving his hands and his head, and suppose a person
150	to say that one and the same person is in motion and at rest at the
150	same moment-to such a mode of speech we should object, and should
150	rather say that one part of him is in motion while another is at rest.
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	And suppose the objector to refine still further, and to draw the nice
150	distinction that not only parts of tops, but whole tops, when they spin
150	round with their pegs fixed on the spot, are at rest and in motion at
150	the same time (and he may say the same of anything which revolves in
150	the same spot), his objection would not be admitted by us, because in
150	such cases things are not at rest and in motion in the same parts of
150	themselves; we should rather say that they have both an axis and a
150	circumference, and that the axis stands still, for there is no
150	deviation from the perpendicular; and that the circumference goes
150	round.  But if, while revolving, the axis inclines either to the right
150	or left, forwards or backwards, then in no point of view can they be at
150	rest.
150	
150	That is the correct mode of describing them, he replied.
150	
150	Then none of these objections will confuse us, or incline us to believe
150	that the same thing at the same time, in the same part or in relation
150	to the same thing, can act or be acted upon in contrary ways.
150	
150	Certainly not, according to my way of thinking.
150	
150	Yet, I said, that we may not be compelled to examine all such
150	objections, and prove at length that they are untrue, let us assume
150	their absurdity, and go forward on the understanding that hereafter, if
150	this assumption turn out to be untrue, all the consequences which
150	follow shall be withdrawn.
150	
150	Yes, he said, that will be the best way.
150	
150	Well, I said, would you not allow that assent and dissent, desire and
150	aversion, attraction and repulsion, are all of them opposites, whether
150	they are regarded as active or passive (for that makes no difference in
150	the fact of their opposition)?
150	
150	Yes, he said, they are opposites.
150	
150	Well, I said, and hunger and thirst, and the desires in general, and
150	again willing and wishing,--all these you would refer to the classes
150	already mentioned.  You would say--would you not?--that the soul of him
150	who desires is seeking after the object of his desires; or that he is
150	drawing to himself the thing which he wishes to possess: or again, when
150	a person wants anything to be given him, his mind, longing for the
150	realisation of his desires, intimates his wish to have it by a nod of
150	assent, as if he had been asked a question?
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	And what would you say of unwillingness and dislike and the absence of
150	desire; should not these be referred to the opposite class of repulsion
150	and rejection?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	Admitting this to be true of desire generally, let us suppose a
150	particular class of desires, and out of these we will select hunger and
150	thirst, as they are termed, which are the most obvious of them?
150	
150	Let us take that class, he said.
150	
150	The object of one is food, and of the other drink?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And here comes the point:  is not thirst the desire which the soul has
150	of drink, and of drink only; not of drink qualified by anything else;
150	for example, warm or cold, or much or little, or, in a word, drink of
150	any particular sort:  but if the thirst be accompanied by heat, then
150	the desire is of cold drink; or, if accompanied by cold, then of warm
150	drink; or, if the thirst be excessive, then the drink which is desired
150	will be excessive; or, if not great, the quantity of drink will also be
150	small:  but thirst pure and simple will desire drink pure and simple,
150	which is the natural satisfaction of thirst, as food is of hunger?
150	
150	Yes, he said; the simple desire is, as you say, in every case of the
150	simple object, and the qualified desire of the qualified object.
150	
150	But here a confusion may arise; and I should wish to guard against an
150	opponent starting up and saying that no man desires drink only, but
150	good drink, or food only, but good food; for good is the universal
150	object of desire, and thirst being a desire, will necessarily be thirst
150	after good drink; and the same is true of every other desire.
150	
150	Yes, he replied, the opponent might have something to say.
150	
150	Nevertheless I should still maintain, that of relatives some have a
150	quality attached to either term of the relation; others are simple and
150	have their correlatives simple.
150	
150	I do not know what you mean.
150	
150	Well, you know of course that the greater is relative to the less?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	And the much greater to the much less?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And the sometime greater to the sometime less, and the greater that is
150	to be to the less that is to be?
150	
150	Certainly, he said.
150	
150	And so of more and less, and of other correlative terms, such as the
150	double and the half, or again, the heavier and the lighter, the swifter
150	and the slower; and of hot and cold, and of any other relatives;--is
150	not this true of all of them?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And does not the same principle hold in the sciences?  The object of
150	science is knowledge (assuming that to be the true definition), but the
150	object of a particular science is a particular kind of knowledge; I
150	mean, for example, that the science of house-building is a kind of
150	knowledge which is defined and distinguished from other kinds and is
150	therefore termed architecture.
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	Because it has a particular quality which no other has?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And it has this particular quality because it has an object of a
150	particular kind; and this is true of the other arts and sciences?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	Now, then, if I have made myself clear, you will understand my original
150	meaning in what I said about relatives.  My meaning was, that if one
150	term of a relation is taken alone, the other is taken alone; if one
150	term is qualified, the other is also qualified.  I do not mean to say
150	that relatives may not be disparate, or that the science of health is
150	healthy, or of disease necessarily diseased, or that the sciences of
150	good and evil are therefore good and evil; but only that, when the term
150	science is no longer used absolutely, but has a qualified object which
150	in this case is the nature of health and disease, it becomes defined,
150	and is hence called not merely science, but the science of medicine.
150	
150	I quite understand, and I think as you do.
150	
150	Would you not say that thirst is one of these essentially relative
150	terms, having clearly a relation--
150	
150	Yes, thirst is relative to drink.
150	
150	And a certain kind of thirst is relative to a certain kind of drink;
150	but thirst taken alone is neither of much nor little, nor of good nor
150	bad, nor of any particular kind of drink, but of drink only?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	Then the soul of the thirsty one, in so far as he is thirsty, desires
150	only drink; for this he yearns and tries to obtain it?
150	
150	That is plain.
150	
150	And if you suppose something which pulls a thirsty soul away from
150	drink, that must be different from the thirsty principle which draws
150	him like a beast to drink; for, as we were saying, the same thing
150	cannot at the same time with the same part of itself act in contrary
150	ways about the same.
150	
150	Impossible.
150	
150	No more than you can say that the hands of the archer push and pull the
150	bow at the same time, but what you say is that one hand pushes and the
150	other pulls.
150	
150	Exactly so, he replied.
150	
150	And might a man be thirsty, and yet unwilling to drink?
150	
150	Yes, he said, it constantly happens.
150	
150	And in such a case what is one to say?  Would you not say that there
150	was something in the soul bidding a man to drink, and something else
150	forbidding him, which is other and stronger than the principle which
150	bids him?
150	
150	I should say so.
150	
150	And the forbidding principle is derived from reason, and that which
150	bids and attracts proceeds from passion and disease?
150	
150	Clearly.
150	
150	Then we may fairly assume that they are two, and that they differ from
150	one another; the one with which man reasons, we may call the rational
150	principle of the soul, the other, with which he loves and hungers and
150	thirsts and feels the flutterings of any other desire, may be termed
150	the irrational or appetitive, the ally of sundry pleasures and
150	satisfactions?
150	
150	Yes, he said, we may fairly assume them to be different.
150	
150	Then let us finally determine that there are two principles existing in
150	the soul.  And what of passion, or spirit?  Is it a third, or akin to
150	one of the preceding?
150	
150	I should be inclined to say--akin to desire.
150	
150	Well, I said, there is a story which I remember to have heard, and in
150	which I put faith.  The story is, that Leontius, the son of Aglaion,
150	coming up one day from the Piraeus, under the north wall on the
150	outside, observed some dead bodies lying on the ground at the place of
150	execution.  He felt a desire to see them, and also a dread and
150	abhorrence of them; for a time he struggled and covered his eyes, but
150	at length the desire got the better of him; and forcing them open, he
150	ran up to the dead bodies, saying, Look, ye wretches, take your fill of
150	the fair sight.
150	
150	I have heard the story myself, he said.
150	
150	The moral of the tale is, that anger at times goes to war with desire,
150	as though they were two distinct things.
150	
150	Yes; that is the meaning, he said.
150	
150	And are there not many other cases in which we observe that when a
150	man's desires violently prevail over his reason, he reviles himself,
150	and is angry at the violence within him, and that in this struggle,
150	which is like the struggle of factions in a State, his spirit is on the
150	side of his reason;--but for the passionate or spirited element to take
150	part with the desires when reason that she should not be opposed, is a
150	sort of thing which thing which I believe that you never observed
150	occurring in yourself, nor, as I should imagine, in any one else?
150	
150	Certainly not.
150	
150	Suppose that a man thinks he has done a wrong to another, the nobler he
150	is the less able is he to feel indignant at any suffering, such as
150	hunger, or cold, or any other pain which the injured person may inflict
150	upon him--these he deems to be just, and, as I say, his anger refuses
150	to be excited by them.
150	
150	True, he said.
150	
150	But when he thinks that he is the sufferer of the wrong, then he boils
150	and chafes, and is on the side of what he believes to be justice; and
150	because he suffers hunger or cold or other pain he is only the more
150	determined to persevere and conquer.  His noble spirit will not be
150	quelled until he either slays or is slain; or until he hears the voice
150	of the shepherd, that is, reason, bidding his dog bark no more.
150	
150	The illustration is perfect, he replied; and in our State, as we were
150	saying, the auxiliaries were to be dogs, and to hear the voice of the
150	rulers, who are their shepherds.
150	
150	I perceive, I said, that you quite understand me; there is, however, a
150	further point which I wish you to consider.
150	
150	What point?
150	
150	You remember that passion or spirit appeared at first sight to be a
150	kind of desire, but now we should say quite the contrary; for in the
150	conflict of the soul spirit is arrayed on the side of the rational
150	principle.
150	
150	Most assuredly.
150	
150	But a further question arises:  Is passion different from reason also,
150	or only a kind of reason; in which latter case, instead of three
150	principles in the soul, there will only be two, the rational and the
150	concupiscent; or rather, as the State was composed of three classes,
150	traders, auxiliaries, counsellors, so may there not be in the
150	individual soul a third element which is passion or spirit, and when
150	not corrupted by bad education is the natural auxiliary of reason
150	
150	Yes, he said, there must be a third.
150
