150	Yes, I replied, if passion, which has already been shown to be
150	different from desire, turn out also to be different from reason.
150	
150	But that is easily proved:--We may observe even in young children that
150	they are full of spirit almost as soon as they are born, whereas some
150	of them never seem to attain to the use of reason, and most of them
150	late enough.
150	
150	Excellent, I said, and you may see passion equally in brute animals,
150	which is a further proof of the truth of what you are saying.  And we
150	may once more appeal to the words of Homer, which have been already
150	quoted by us,
150	
150	    He smote his breast, and thus rebuked his soul,
150	
150	for in this verse Homer has clearly supposed the power which reasons
150	about the better and worse to be different from the unreasoning anger
150	which is rebuked by it.
150	
150	Very true, he said.
150	
150	And so, after much tossing, we have reached land, and are fairly agreed
150	that the same principles which exist in the State exist also in the
150	individual, and that they are three in number.
150	
150	Exactly.
150	
150	Must we not then infer that the individual is wise in the same way, and
150	in virtue of the same quality which makes the State wise?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	Also that the same quality which constitutes courage in the State
150	constitutes courage in the individual, and that both the State and the
150	individual bear the same relation to all the other virtues?
150	
150	Assuredly.
150	
150	And the individual will be acknowledged by us to be just in the same
150	way in which the State is just?
150	
150	That follows, of course.
150	
150	We cannot but remember that the justice of the State consisted in each
150	of the three classes doing the work of its own class?
150	
150	We are not very likely to have forgotten, he said.
150	
150	We must recollect that the individual in whom the several qualities of
150	his nature do their own work will be just, and will do his own work?
150	
150	Yes, he said, we must remember that too.
150	
150	And ought not the rational principle, which is wise, and has the care
150	of the whole soul, to rule, and the passionate or spirited principle to
150	be the subject and ally?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	And, as we were saying, the united influence of music and gymnastic
150	will bring them into accord, nerving and sustaining the reason with
150	noble words and lessons, and moderating and soothing and civilizing the
150	wildness of passion by harmony and rhythm?
150	
150	Quite true, he said.
150	
150	And these two, thus nurtured and educated, and having learned truly to
150	know their own functions, will rule over the concupiscent, which in
150	each of us is the largest part of the soul and by nature most
150	insatiable of gain; over this they will keep guard, lest, waxing great
150	and strong with the fulness of bodily pleasures, as they are termed,
150	the concupiscent soul, no longer confined to her own sphere, should
150	attempt to enslave and rule those who are not her natural-born
150	subjects, and overturn the whole life of man?
150	
150	Very true, he said.
150	
150	Both together will they not be the best defenders of the whole soul and
150	the whole body against attacks from without; the one counselling, and
150	the other fighting under his leader, and courageously executing his
150	commands and counsels?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	And he is to be deemed courageous whose spirit retains in pleasure and
150	in pain the commands of reason about what he ought or ought not to fear?
150	
150	Right, he replied.
150	
150	And him we call wise who has in him that little part which rules, and
150	which proclaims these commands; that part too being supposed to have a
150	knowledge of what is for the interest of each of the three parts and of
150	the whole?
150	
150	Assuredly.
150	
150	And would you not say that he is temperate who has these same elements
150	in friendly harmony, in whom the one ruling principle of reason, and
150	the two subject ones of spirit and desire are equally agreed that
150	reason ought to rule, and do not rebel?
150	
150	Certainly, he said, that is the true account of temperance whether in
150	the State or individual.
150	
150	And surely, I said, we have explained again and again how and by virtue
150	of what quality a man will be just.
150	
150	That is very certain.
150	
150	And is justice dimmer in the individual, and is her form different, or
150	is she the same which we found her to be in the State?
150	
150	There is no difference in my opinion, he said.
150	
150	Because, if any doubt is still lingering in our minds, a few
150	commonplace instances will satisfy us of the truth of what I am saying.
150	
150	What sort of instances do you mean?
150	
150	If the case is put to us, must we not admit that the just State, or the
150	man who is trained in the principles of such a State, will be less
150	likely than the unjust to make away with a deposit of gold or silver?
150	Would any one deny this?
150	
150	No one, he replied.
150	
150	Will the just man or citizen ever be guilty of sacrilege or theft, or
150	treachery either to his friends or to his country?
150	
150	Never.
150	
150	Neither will he ever break faith where there have been oaths or
150	agreements?
150	
150	Impossible.
150	
150	No one will be less likely to commit adultery, or to dishonour his
150	father and mother, or to fall in his religious duties?
150	
150	No one.
150	
150	And the reason is that each part of him is doing its own business,
150	whether in ruling or being ruled?
150	
150	Exactly so.
150	
150	Are you satisfied then that the quality which makes such men and such
150	states is justice, or do you hope to discover some other?
150	
150	Not I, indeed.
150	
150	Then our dream has been realised; and the suspicion which we
150	entertained at the beginning of our work of construction, that some
150	divine power must have conducted us to a primary form of justice, has
150	now been verified?
150	
150	Yes, certainly.
150	
150	And the division of labour which required the carpenter and the
150	shoemaker and the rest of the citizens to be doing each his own
150	business, and not another's, was a shadow of justice, and for that
150	reason it was of use?
150	
150	Clearly.
150	
150	But in reality justice was such as we were describing, being concerned
150	however, not with the outward man, but with the inward, which is the
150	true self and concernment of man:  for the just man does not permit the
150	several elements within him to interfere with one another, or any of
150	them to do the work of others,--he sets in order his own inner life,
150	and is his own master and his own law, and at peace with himself; and
150	when he has bound together the three principles within him, which may
150	be compared to the higher, lower, and middle notes of the scale, and
150	the intermediate intervals--when he has bound all these together, and
150	is no longer many, but has become one entirely temperate and perfectly
150	adjusted nature, then he proceeds to act, if he has to act, whether in
150	a matter of property, or in the treatment of the body, or in some
150	affair of politics or private business; always thinking and calling
150	that which preserves and co-operates with this harmonious condition,
150	just and good action, and the knowledge which presides over it, wisdom,
150	and that which at any time impairs this condition, he will call unjust
150	action, and the opinion which presides over it ignorance.
150	
150	You have said the exact truth, Socrates.
150	
150	Very good; and if we were to affirm that we had discovered the just man
150	and the just State, and the nature of justice in each of them, we
150	should not be telling a falsehood?
150	
150	Most certainly not.
150	
150	May we say so, then?
150	
150	Let us say so.
150	
150	And now, I said, injustice has to be considered.
150	
150	Clearly.
150	
150	Must not injustice be a strife which arises among the three
150	principles--a meddlesomeness, and interference, and rising up of a part
150	of the soul against the whole, an assertion of unlawful authority,
150	which is made by a rebellious subject against a true prince, of whom he
150	is the natural vassal,--what is all this confusion and delusion but
150	injustice, and intemperance and cowardice and ignorance, and every form
150	of vice?
150	
150	Exactly so.
150	
150	And if the nature of justice and injustice be known, then the meaning
150	of acting unjustly and being unjust, or, again, of acting justly, will
150	also be perfectly clear?
150	
150	What do you mean? he said.
150	
150	Why, I said, they are like disease and health; being in the soul just
150	what disease and health are in the body.
150	
150	How so? he said.
150	
150	Why, I said, that which is healthy causes health, and that which is
150	unhealthy causes disease.
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And just actions cause justice, and unjust actions cause injustice?
150	
150	That is certain.
150	
150	And the creation of health is the institution of a natural order and
150	government of one by another in the parts of the body; and the creation
150	of disease is the production of a state of things at variance with this
150	natural order?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	And is not the creation of justice the institution of a natural order
150	and government of one by another in the parts of the soul, and the
150	creation of injustice the production of a state of things at variance
150	with the natural order?
150	
150	Exactly so, he said.
150	
150	Then virtue is the health and beauty and well-being of the soul, and
150	vice the disease and weakness and deformity of the same?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	And do not good practices lead to virtue, and evil practices to vice?
150	
150	Assuredly.
150	
150	Still our old question of the comparative advantage of justice and
150	injustice has not been answered:  Which is the more profitable, to be
150	just and act justly and practise virtue, whether seen or unseen of gods
150	and men, or to be unjust and act unjustly, if only unpunished and
150	unreformed?
150	
150	In my judgment, Socrates, the question has now become ridiculous.  We
150	know that, when the bodily constitution is gone, life is no longer
150	endurable, though pampered with all kinds of meats and drinks, and
150	having all wealth and all power; and shall we be told that when the
150	very essence of the vital principle is undermined and corrupted, life
150	is still worth having to a man, if only he be allowed to do whatever he
150	likes with the single exception that he is not to acquire justice and
150	virtue, or to escape from injustice and vice; assuming them both to be
150	such as we have described?
150	
150	Yes, I said, the question is, as you say, ridiculous.  Still, as we are
150	near the spot at which we may see the truth in the clearest manner with
150	our own eyes, let us not faint by the way.
150	
150	Certainly not, he replied.
150	
150	Come up hither, I said, and behold the various forms of vice, those of
150	them, I mean, which are worth looking at.
150	
150	I am following you, he replied:  proceed.
150	
150	I said, The argument seems to have reached a height from which, as from
150	some tower of speculation, a man may look down and see that virtue is
150	one, but that the forms of vice are innumerable; there being four
150	special ones which are deserving of note.
150	
150	What do you mean? he said.
150	
150	I mean, I replied, that there appear to be as many forms of the soul as
150	there are distinct forms of the State.
150	
150	How many?
150	
150	There are five of the State, and five of the soul, I said.
150	
150	What are they?
150	
150	The first, I said, is that which we have been describing, and which may
150	be said to have two names, monarchy and aristocracy, accordingly as
150	rule is exercised by one distinguished man or by many.
150	
150	True, he replied.
150	
150	But I regard the two names as describing one form only; for whether the
150	government is in the hands of one or many, if the governors have been
150	trained in the manner which we have supposed, the fundamental laws of
150	the State will be maintained.
150	
150	That is true, he replied.
150	
150	
150	
150	
150	BOOK V
150	
150	
150	SOCRATES - GLAUCON - ADEIMANTUS
150	
150	SUCH is the good and true City or State, and the good and man is of the
150	same pattern; and if this is right every other is wrong; and the evil
150	is one which affects not only the ordering of the State, but also the
150	regulation of the individual soul, and is exhibited in four forms.
150	
150	What are they? he said.
150	
150	I was proceeding to tell the order in which the four evil forms
150	appeared to me to succeed one another, when Pole marchus, who was
150	sitting a little way off, just beyond Adeimantus, began to whisper to
150	him: stretching forth his hand, he took hold of the upper part of his
150	coat by the shoulder, and drew him towards him, leaning forward himself
150	so as to be quite close and saying something in his ear, of which I
150	only caught the words, 'Shall we let him off, or what shall we do?'
150	
150	Certainly not, said Adeimantus, raising his voice.
150	
150	Who is it, I said, whom you are refusing to let off?
150	
150	You, he said.
150	
150	I repeated, Why am I especially not to be let off?
150	
150	Why, he said, we think that you are lazy, and mean to cheat us out of a
150	whole chapter which is a very important part of the story; and you
150	fancy that we shall not notice your airy way of proceeding; as if it
150	were self-evident to everybody, that in the matter of women and
150	children 'friends have all things in common.'
150	
150	And was I not right, Adeimantus?
150	
150	Yes, he said; but what is right in this particular case, like
150	everything else, requires to be explained; for community may be of many
150	kinds.  Please, therefore, to say what sort of community you mean.  We
150	have been long expecting that you would tell us something about the
150	family life of your citizens--how they will bring children into the
150	world, and rear them when they have arrived, and, in general, what is
150	the nature of this community of women and children-for we are of
150	opinion that the right or wrong management of such matters will have a
150	great and paramount influence on the State for good or for evil.  And
150	now, since the question is still undetermined, and you are taking in
150	hand another State, we have resolved, as you heard, not to let you go
150	until you give an account of all this.
150	
150	To that resolution, said Glaucon, you may regard me as saying Agreed.
150	
150	SOCRATES - ADEIMANTUS - GLAUCON - THRASYMACHUS
150	
150	And without more ado, said Thrasymachus, you may consider us all to be
150	equally agreed.
150	
150	I said, You know not what you are doing in thus assailing me: What an
150	argument are you raising about the State!  Just as I thought that I had
150	finished, and was only too glad that I had laid this question to sleep,
150	and was reflecting how fortunate I was in your acceptance of what I
150	then said, you ask me to begin again at the very foundation, ignorant
150	of what a hornet's nest of words you are stirring.  Now I foresaw this
150	gathering trouble, and avoided it.
150	
150	For what purpose do you conceive that we have come here, said
150	Thrasymachus,--to look for gold, or to hear discourse?
150	
150	Yes, but discourse should have a limit.
150	
150	Yes, Socrates, said Glaucon, and the whole of life is the only limit
150	which wise men assign to the hearing of such discourses.  But never
150	mind about us; take heart yourself and answer the question in your own
150	way: What sort of community of women and children is this which is to
150	prevail among our guardians? and how shall we manage the period between
150	birth and education, which seems to require the greatest care?  Tell us
150	how these things will be.
150	
150	Yes, my simple friend, but the answer is the reverse of easy; many more
150	doubts arise about this than about our previous conclusions.  For the
150	practicability of what is said may be doubted; and looked at in another
150	point of view, whether the scheme, if ever so practicable, would be for
150	the best, is also doubtful.  Hence I feel a reluctance to approach the
150	subject, lest our aspiration, my dear friend, should turn out to be a
150	dream only.
150	
150	Fear not, he replied, for your audience will not be hard upon you; they
150	are not sceptical or hostile.
150	
150	I said:  My good friend, I suppose that you mean to encourage me by
150	these words.
150	
150	Yes, he said.
150	
150	Then let me tell you that you are doing just the reverse; the
150	encouragement which you offer would have been all very well had I
150	myself believed that I knew what I was talking about: to declare the
150	truth about matters of high interest which a man honours and loves
150	among wise men who love him need occasion no fear or faltering in his
150	mind; but to carry on an argument when you are yourself only a
150	hesitating enquirer, which is my condition, is a dangerous and slippery
150	thing; and the danger is not that I shall be laughed at (of which the
150	fear would be childish), but that I shall miss the truth where I have
150	most need to be sure of my footing, and drag my friends after me in my
150	fall.  And I pray Nemesis not to visit upon me the words which I am
150	going to utter.  For I do indeed believe that to be an involuntary
150	homicide is a less crime than to be a deceiver about beauty or goodness
150	or justice in the matter of laws.  And that is a risk which I would
150	rather run among enemies than among friends, and therefore you do well
150	to encourage me.
150	
150	Glaucon laughed and said:  Well then, Socrates, in case you and your
150	argument do us any serious injury you shall be acquitted beforehand of
150	the and shall not be held to be a deceiver; take courage then and speak.
150	
150	Well, I said, the law says that when a man is acquitted he is free from
150	guilt, and what holds at law may hold in argument.
150	
150	Then why should you mind?
150	
150	Well, I replied, I suppose that I must retrace my steps and say what I
150	perhaps ought to have said before in the proper place.  The part of the
150	men has been played out, and now properly enough comes the turn of the
150	women.  Of them I will proceed to speak, and the more readily since I
150	am invited by you.
150	
150	For men born and educated like our citizens, the only way, in my
150	opinion, of arriving at a right conclusion about the possession and use
150	of women and children is to follow the path on which we originally
150	started, when we said that the men were to be the guardians and
150	watchdogs of the herd.
150	
150	True.
150	
150	Let us further suppose the birth and education of our women to be
150	subject to similar or nearly similar regulations; then we shall see
150	whether the result accords with our design.
150	
150	What do you mean?
150	
150	What I mean may be put into the form of a question, I said: Are dogs
150	divided into hes and shes, or do they both share equally in hunting and
150	in keeping watch and in the other duties of dogs? or do we entrust to
150	the males the entire and exclusive care of the flocks, while we leave
150	the females at home, under the idea that the bearing and suckling their
150	puppies is labour enough for them?
150	
150	No, he said, they share alike; the only difference between them is that
150	the males are stronger and the females weaker.
150	
150	But can you use different animals for the same purpose, unless they are
150	bred and fed in the same way?
150	
150	You cannot.
150	
150	Then, if women are to have the same duties as men, they must have the
150	same nurture and education?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	The education which was assigned to the men was music and gymnastic.
150	Yes.
150	
150	Then women must be taught music and gymnastic and also the art of war,
150	which they must practise like the men?
150	
150	That is the inference, I suppose.
150	
150	I should rather expect, I said, that several of our proposals, if they
150	are carried out, being unusual, may appear ridiculous.
150	
150	No doubt of it.
150	
150	Yes, and the most ridiculous thing of all will be the sight of women
150	naked in the palaestra, exercising with the men, especially when they
150	are no longer young; they certainly will not be a vision of beauty, any
150	more than the enthusiastic old men who in spite of wrinkles and
150	ugliness continue to frequent the gymnasia.
150	
150	Yes, indeed, he said:  according to present notions the proposal would
150	be thought ridiculous.
150	
150	But then, I said, as we have determined to speak our minds, we must not
150	fear the jests of the wits which will be directed against this sort of
150	innovation; how they will talk of women's attainments both in music and
150	gymnastic, and above all about their wearing armour and riding upon
150	horseback!
150	
150	Very true, he replied.
150	
150	Yet having begun we must go forward to the rough places of the law; at
150	the same time begging of these gentlemen for once in their life to be
150	serious.  Not long ago, as we shall remind them, the Hellenes were of
150	the opinion, which is still generally received among the barbarians,
150	that the sight of a naked man was ridiculous and improper; and when
150	first the Cretans and then the Lacedaemonians introduced the custom,
150	the wits of that day might equally have ridiculed the innovation.
150	
150	No doubt.
150	
150	But when experience showed that to let all things be uncovered was far
150	better than to cover them up, and the ludicrous effect to the outward
150	eye vanished before the better principle which reason asserted, then
150	the man was perceived to be a fool who directs the shafts of his
150	ridicule at any other sight but that of folly and vice, or seriously
150	inclines to weigh the beautiful by any other standard but that of the
150	good.
150	
150	Very true, he replied.
150	
150	First, then, whether the question is to be put in jest or in earnest,
150	let us come to an understanding about the nature of woman:  Is she
150	capable of sharing either wholly or partially in the actions of men, or
150	not at all?  And is the art of war one of those arts in which she can
150	or can not share?  That will be the best way of commencing the enquiry,
150	and will probably lead to the fairest conclusion.
150	
150	That will be much the best way.
150	
150	Shall we take the other side first and begin by arguing against
150	ourselves; in this manner the adversary's position will not be
150	undefended.
150	
150	Why not? he said.
150	
150	Then let us put a speech into the mouths of our opponents.  They will
150	say: 'Socrates and Glaucon, no adversary need convict you, for you
150	yourselves, at the first foundation of the State, admitted the
150	principle that everybody was to do the one work suited to his own
150	nature.' And certainly, if I am not mistaken, such an admission was
150	made by us.  'And do not the natures of men and women differ very much
150	indeed?' And we shall reply:  Of course they do.  Then we shall be
150	asked, 'Whether the tasks assigned to men and to women should not be
150	different, and such as are agreeable to their different natures?'
150	Certainly they should.  'But if so, have you not fallen into a serious
150	inconsistency in saying that men and women, whose natures are so
150	entirely different, ought to perform the same actions?'-- What defence
150	will you make for us, my good Sir, against any one who offers these
150	objections?
150	
150	That is not an easy question to answer when asked suddenly; and I shall
150	and I do beg of you to draw out the case on our side.
150	
150	These are the objections, Glaucon, and there are many others of a like
150	kind, which I foresaw long ago; they made me afraid and reluctant to
150	take in hand any law about the possession and nurture of women and
150	children.
150	
150	By Zeus, he said, the problem to be solved is anything but easy.
150	
150	Why yes, I said, but the fact is that when a man is out of his depth,
150	whether he has fallen into a little swimming bath or into mid-ocean, he
150	has to swim all the same.
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	And must not we swim and try to reach the shore:  we will hope that
150	Arion's dolphin or some other miraculous help may save us?
150	
150	I suppose so, he said.
150	
150	Well then, let us see if any way of escape can be found.  We
150	acknowledged--did we not? that different natures ought to have
150	different pursuits, and that men's and women's natures are different.
150	And now what are we saying?--that different natures ought to have the
150	same pursuits,--this is the inconsistency which is charged upon us.
150	
150	Precisely.
150	
150	Verily, Glaucon, I said, glorious is the power of the art of
150	contradiction!
150	
150	Why do you say so?
150	
150	Because I think that many a man falls into the practice against his
150	will.  When he thinks that he is reasoning he is really disputing, just
150	because he cannot define and divide, and so know that of which he is
150	speaking; and he will pursue a merely verbal opposition in the spirit
150	of contention and not of fair discussion.
150	
150	Yes, he replied, such is very often the case; but what has that to do
150	with us and our argument?
150	
150	A great deal; for there is certainly a danger of our getting
150	unintentionally into a verbal opposition.
150	
150	In what way?
150	
150	Why, we valiantly and pugnaciously insist upon the verbal truth, that
150	different natures ought to have different pursuits, but we never
150	considered at all what was the meaning of sameness or difference of
150	nature, or why we distinguished them when we assigned different
150	pursuits to different natures and the same to the same natures.
150	
150	Why, no, he said, that was never considered by us.
150	
150	I said:  Suppose that by way of illustration we were to ask the
150	question whether there is not an opposition in nature between bald men
150	and hairy men; and if this is admitted by us, then, if bald men are
150	cobblers, we should forbid the hairy men to be cobblers, and conversely?
150	
150	That would be a jest, he said.
150	
150	Yes, I said, a jest; and why? because we never meant when we
150	constructed the State, that the opposition of natures should extend to
150	every difference, but only to those differences which affected the
150	pursuit in which the individual is engaged; we should have argued, for
150	example, that a physician and one who is in mind a physician may be
150	said to have the same nature.
150	
150	True.
150	
150	Whereas the physician and the carpenter have different natures?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	And if, I said, the male and female sex appear to differ in their
150	fitness for any art or pursuit, we should say that such pursuit or art
150	ought to be assigned to one or the other of them; but if the difference
150	consists only in women bearing and men begetting children, this does
150	not amount to a proof that a woman differs from a man in respect of the
150	sort of education she should receive; and we shall therefore continue
150	to maintain that our guardians and their wives ought to have the same
150	pursuits.
150	
150	Very true, he said.
150	
150	Next, we shall ask our opponent how, in reference to any of the
150	pursuits or arts of civic life, the nature of a woman differs from that
150	of a man?
150	
150	That will be quite fair.
150	
150	And perhaps he, like yourself, will reply that to give a sufficient
150	answer on the instant is not easy; but after a little reflection there
150	is no difficulty.
150	
150	Yes, perhaps.
150	
150	Suppose then that we invite him to accompany us in the argument, and
150	then we may hope to show him that there is nothing peculiar in the
150	constitution of women which would affect them in the administration of
150	the State.
150	
150	By all means.
150	
150	Let us say to him:  Come now, and we will ask you a question:--when you
150	spoke of a nature gifted or not gifted in any respect, did you mean to
150	say that one man will acquire a thing easily, another with difficulty;
150	a little learning will lead the one to discover a great deal; whereas
150	the other, after much study and application, no sooner learns than he
150	forgets; or again, did you mean, that the one has a body which is a
150	good servant to his mind, while the body of the other is a hindrance to
150	him?-would not these be the sort of differences which distinguish the
150	man gifted by nature from the one who is ungifted?
150	
150	No one will deny that.
150	
150	And can you mention any pursuit of mankind in which the male sex has
150	not all these gifts and qualities in a higher degree than the female?
150	Need I waste time in speaking of the art of weaving, and the management
150	of pancakes and preserves, in which womankind does really appear to be
150	great, and in which for her to be beaten by a man is of all things the
150	most absurd?
150	
150	You are quite right, he replied, in maintaining the general inferiority
150	of the female sex:  although many women are in many things superior to
150	many men, yet on the whole what you say is true.
150	
150	And if so, my friend, I said, there is no special faculty of
150	administration in a state which a woman has because she is a woman, or
150	which a man has by virtue of his sex, but the gifts of nature are alike
150	diffused in both; all the pursuits of men are the pursuits of women
150	also, but in all of them a woman is inferior to a man.
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	Then are we to impose all our enactments on men and none of them on
150	women?
150	
150	That will never do.
150	
150	One woman has a gift of healing, another not; one is a musician, and
150	another has no music in her nature?
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	And one woman has a turn for gymnastic and military exercises, and
150	another is unwarlike and hates gymnastics?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	And one woman is a philosopher, and another is an enemy of philosophy;
150	one has spirit, and another is without spirit?
150	
150	That is also true.
150	
150	Then one woman will have the temper of a guardian, and another not.
150	Was not the selection of the male guardians determined by differences
150	of this sort?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	Men and women alike possess the qualities which make a guardian; they
150	differ only in their comparative strength or weakness.
150	
150	Obviously.
150	
150	And those women who have such qualities are to be selected as the
150	companions and colleagues of men who have similar qualities and whom
150	they resemble in capacity and in character?
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	And ought not the same natures to have the same pursuits?
150	
150	They ought.
150	
150	Then, as we were saying before, there is nothing unnatural in assigning
150	music and gymnastic to the wives of the guardians--to that point we
150	come round again.
150	
150	Certainly not.
150	
150	The law which we then enacted was agreeable to nature, and therefore
150	not an impossibility or mere aspiration; and the contrary practice,
150	which prevails at present, is in reality a violation of nature.
150	
150	That appears to be true.
150	
150	We had to consider, first, whether our proposals were possible, and
150	secondly whether they were the most beneficial?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And the possibility has been acknowledged?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	The very great benefit has next to be established?
150	
150	Quite so.
150	
150	You will admit that the same education which makes a man a good
150	guardian will make a woman a good guardian; for their original nature
150	is the same?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	I should like to ask you a question.
150	
150	What is it?
150	
150	Would you say that all men are equal in excellence, or is one man
150	better than another?
150	
150	The latter.
150	
150	And in the commonwealth which we were founding do you conceive the
150	guardians who have been brought up on our model system to be more
150	perfect men, or the cobblers whose education has been cobbling?
150	
150	What a ridiculous question!
150	
150	You have answered me, I replied:  Well, and may we not further say that
150	our guardians are the best of our citizens?
150	
150	By far the best.
150	
150	And will not their wives be the best women?
150	
150	Yes, by far the best.
150	
150	And can there be anything better for the interests of the State than
150	that the men and women of a State should be as good as possible?
150	
150	There can be nothing better.
150	
150	And this is what the arts of music and gymnastic, when present in such
150	manner as we have described, will accomplish?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	Then we have made an enactment not only possible but in the highest
150	degree beneficial to the State?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	Then let the wives of our guardians strip, for their virtue will be
150	their robe, and let them share in the toils of war and the defence of
150	their country; only in the distribution of labours the lighter are to
150	be assigned to the women, who are the weaker natures, but in other
150	respects their duties are to be the same.  And as for the man who
150	laughs at naked women exercising their bodies from the best of motives,
150	in his laughter he is plucking
150	
150	    A fruit of unripe wisdom,
150	
150	and he himself is ignorant of what he is laughing at, or what he is
150	about;--for that is, and ever will be, the best of sayings, That the
150	useful is the noble and the hurtful is the base.
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	Here, then, is one difficulty in our law about women, which we may say
150	that we have now escaped; the wave has not swallowed us up alive for
150	enacting that the guardians of either sex should have all their
150	pursuits in common; to the utility and also to the possibility of this
150	arrangement the consistency of the argument with itself bears witness.
150	
150	Yes, that was a mighty wave which you have escaped.
150	
150	Yes, I said, but a greater is coming; you will of this when you see the
150	next.
150	
150	Go on; let me see.
150	
150	The law, I said, which is the sequel of this and of all that has
150	preceded, is to the following effect,--'that the wives of our guardians
150	are to be common, and their children are to be common, and no parent is
150	to know his own child, nor any child his parent.'
150	
150	Yes, he said, that is a much greater wave than the other; and the
150	possibility as well as the utility of such a law are far more
150	questionable.
150	
150	I do not think, I said, that there can be any dispute about the very
150	great utility of having wives and children in common; the possibility
150	is quite another matter, and will be very much disputed.
150	
150	I think that a good many doubts may be raised about both.
150	
150	You imply that the two questions must be combined, I replied.  Now I
150	meant that you should admit the utility; and in this way, as I thought;
150	I should escape from one of them, and then there would remain only the
150	possibility.
150	
150	But that little attempt is detected, and therefore you will please to
150	give a defence of both.
150	
150	Well, I said, I submit to my fate.  Yet grant me a little favour: let
150	me feast my mind with the dream as day dreamers are in the habit of
150	feasting themselves when they are walking alone; for before they have
150	discovered any means of effecting their wishes--that is a matter which
150	never troubles them--they would rather not tire themselves by thinking
150	about possibilities; but assuming that what they desire is already
150	granted to them, they proceed with their plan, and delight in detailing
150	what they mean to do when their wish has come true--that is a way which
150	they have of not doing much good to a capacity which was never good for
150	much.  Now I myself am beginning to lose heart, and I should like, with
150	your permission, to pass over the question of possibility at present.
150	Assuming therefore the possibility of the proposal, I shall now proceed
150	to enquire how the rulers will carry out these arrangements, and I
150	shall demonstrate that our plan, if executed, will be of the greatest
150	benefit to the State and to the guardians.  First of all, then, if you
150	have no objection, I will endeavour with your help to consider the
150	advantages of the measure; and hereafter the question of possibility.
150	
150	I have no objection; proceed.
150	
150	First, I think that if our rulers and their auxiliaries are to be
150	worthy of the name which they bear, there must be willingness to obey
150	in the one and the power of command in the other; the guardians must
150	themselves obey the laws, and they must also imitate the spirit of them
150	in any details which are entrusted to their care.
150	
150	That is right, he said.
150	
150	You, I said, who are their legislator, having selected the men, will
150	now select the women and give them to them;--they must be as far as
150	possible of like natures with them; and they must live in common houses
150	and meet at common meals, None of them will have anything specially his
150	or her own; they will be together, and will be brought up together, and
150	will associate at gymnastic exercises.  And so they will be drawn by a
150	necessity of their natures to have intercourse with each
150	other--necessity is not too strong a word, I think?
150	
150	Yes, he said;--necessity, not geometrical, but another sort of
150	necessity which lovers know, and which is far more convincing and
150	constraining to the mass of mankind.
150	
150	True, I said; and this, Glaucon, like all the rest, must proceed after
150	an orderly fashion; in a city of the blessed, licentiousness is an
150	unholy thing which the rulers will forbid.
150	
150	Yes, he said, and it ought not to be permitted.
150	
150	Then clearly the next thing will be to make matrimony sacred in the
150	highest degree, and what is most beneficial will be deemed sacred?
150	
150	Exactly.
150	
150	And how can marriages be made most beneficial?--that is a question
150	which I put to you, because I see in your house dogs for hunting, and
150	of the nobler sort of birds not a few.  Now, I beseech you, do tell me,
150	have you ever attended to their pairing and breeding?
150	
150	In what particulars?
150	
150	Why, in the first place, although they are all of a good sort, are not
150	some better than others?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	And do you breed from them all indifferently, or do you take care to
150	breed from the best only?
150	
150	From the best.
150	
150	And do you take the oldest or the youngest, or only those of ripe age?
150	
150	I choose only those of ripe age.
150	
150	And if care was not taken in the breeding, your dogs and birds would
150	greatly deteriorate?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	And the same of horses and animals in general?
150	
150	Undoubtedly.
150	
150	Good heavens! my dear friend, I said, what consummate skill will our
150	rulers need if the same principle holds of the human species!
150	
150	Certainly, the same principle holds; but why does this involve any
150	particular skill?
150	
150	Because, I said, our rulers will often have to practise upon the body
150	corporate with medicines.  Now you know that when patients do not
150	require medicines, but have only to be put under a regimen, the
150	inferior sort of practitioner is deemed to be good enough; but when
150	medicine has to be given, then the doctor should be more of a man.
150	
150	That is quite true, he said; but to what are you alluding?
150	
150	I mean, I replied, that our rulers will find a considerable dose of
150	falsehood and deceit necessary for the good of their subjects: we were
150	saying that the use of all these things regarded as medicines might be
150	of advantage.
150	
150	And we were very right.
150	
150	And this lawful use of them seems likely to be often needed in the
150	regulations of marriages and births.
150	
150	How so?
150	
150	Why, I said, the principle has been already laid down that the best of
150	either sex should be united with the best as often, and the inferior
150	with the inferior, as seldom as possible; and that they should rear the
150	offspring of the one sort of union, but not of the other, if the flock
150	is to be maintained in first-rate condition.  Now these goings on must
150	be a secret which the rulers only know, or there will be a further
150	danger of our herd, as the guardians may be termed, breaking out into
150	rebellion.
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	Had we not better appoint certain festivals at which we will bring
150	together the brides and bridegrooms, and sacrifices will be offered and
150	suitable hymeneal songs composed by our poets:  the number of weddings
150	is a matter which must be left to the discretion of the rulers, whose
150	aim will be to preserve the average of population?  There are many
150	other things which they will have to consider, such as the effects of
150	wars and diseases and any similar agencies, in order as far as this is
150	possible to prevent the State from becoming either too large or too
150	small.
150	
150	Certainly, he replied.
150	
150	We shall have to invent some ingenious kind of lots which the less
150	worthy may draw on each occasion of our bringing them together, and
150	then they will accuse their own ill-luck and not the rulers.
150	
150	To be sure, he said.
150	
150	And I think that our braver and better youth, besides their other
150	honours and rewards, might have greater facilities of intercourse with
150	women given them; their bravery will be a reason, and such fathers
150	ought to have as many sons as possible.
150	
150	True.
150	
150	And the proper officers, whether male or female or both, for offices
150	are to be held by women as well as by men--
150	
150	Yes--
150	
150	The proper officers will take the offspring of the good parents to the
150	pen or fold, and there they will deposit them with certain nurses who
150	dwell in a separate quarter; but the offspring of the inferior, or of
150	the better when they chance to be deformed, will be put away in some
150	mysterious, unknown place, as they should be.
150	
150	Yes, he said, that must be done if the breed of the guardians is to be
150	kept pure.
150	
150	They will provide for their nurture, and will bring the mothers to the
150	fold when they are full of milk, taking the greatest possible care that
150	no mother recognizes her own child; and other wet-nurses may be engaged
150	if more are required.  Care will also be taken that the process of
150	suckling shall not be protracted too long; and the mothers will have no
150	getting up at night or other trouble, but will hand over all this sort
150	of thing to the nurses and attendants.
150	
150	You suppose the wives of our guardians to have a fine easy time of it
150	when they are having children.
150	
150	Why, said I, and so they ought.  Let us, however, proceed with our
150	scheme.  We were saying that the parents should be in the prime of life?
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	And what is the prime of life?  May it not be defined as a period of
150	about twenty years in a woman's life, and thirty in a man's?
150	
150	Which years do you mean to include?
150	
150	A woman, I said, at twenty years of age may begin to bear children to
150	the State, and continue to bear them until forty; a man may begin at
150	five-and-twenty, when he has passed the point at which the pulse of
150	life beats quickest, and continue to beget children until he be
150	fifty-five.
150	
150	Certainly, he said, both in men and women those years are the prime of
150	physical as well as of intellectual vigour.
150	
150	Any one above or below the prescribed ages who takes part in the public
150	hymeneals shall be said to have done an unholy and unrighteous thing;
150	the child of which he is the father, if it steals into life, will have
150	been conceived under auspices very unlike the sacrifices and prayers,
150	which at each hymeneal priestesses and priest and the whole city will
150	offer, that the new generation may be better and more useful than their
150	good and useful parents, whereas his child will be the offspring of
150	darkness and strange lust.
150	
150	Very true, he replied.
150	
150	And the same law will apply to any one of those within the prescribed
150	age who forms a connection with any woman in the prime of life without
150	the sanction of the rulers; for we shall say that he is raising up a
150	bastard to the State, uncertified and unconsecrated.
150	
150	Very true, he replied.
150	
150	This applies, however, only to those who are within the specified age:
150	after that we allow them to range at will, except that a man may not
150	marry his daughter or his daughter's daughter, or his mother or his
150	mother's mother; and women, on the other hand, are prohibited from
150	marrying their sons or fathers, or son's son or father's father, and so
150	on in either direction.  And we grant all this, accompanying the
150	permission with strict orders to prevent any embryo which may come into
150	being from seeing the light; and if any force a way to the birth, the
150	parents must understand that the offspring of such an union cannot be
150	maintained, and arrange accordingly.
150	
150	That also, he said, is a reasonable proposition.  But how will they
150	know who are fathers and daughters, and so on?
150	
150	They will never know.  The way will be this:--dating from the day of
150	the hymeneal, the bridegroom who was then married will call all the
150	male children who are born in the seventh and tenth month afterwards
150	his sons, and the female children his daughters, and they will call him
150	father, and he will call their children his grandchildren, and they
150	will call the elder generation grandfathers and grandmothers.  All who
150	were begotten at the time when their fathers and mothers came together
150	will be called their brothers and sisters, and these, as I was saying,
150	will be forbidden to inter-marry. This, however, is not to be
150	understood as an absolute prohibition of the marriage of brothers and
150	sisters; if the lot favours them, and they receive the sanction of the
150	Pythian oracle, the law will allow them.
150	
150	Quite right, he replied.
150	
150	Such is the scheme, Glaucon, according to which the guardians of our
150	State are to have their wives and families in common.  And now you
150	would have the argument show that this community is consistent with the
150	rest of our polity, and also that nothing can be better--would you not?
150	
150	Yes, certainly.
150	
150	Shall we try to find a common basis by asking of ourselves what ought
150	to be the chief aim of the legislator in making laws and in the
150	organization of a State,--what is the greatest I good, and what is the
150	greatest evil, and then consider whether our previous description has
150	the stamp of the good or of the evil?
150	
150	By all means.
150	
150	Can there be any greater evil than discord and distraction and
150	plurality where unity ought to reign? or any greater good than the bond
150	of unity?
150	
150	There cannot.
150	
150	And there is unity where there is community of pleasures and
150	pains--where all the citizens are glad or grieved on the same occasions
150	of joy and sorrow?
150	
150	No doubt.
150	
150	Yes; and where there is no common but only private feeling a State is
150	disorganized--when you have one half of the world triumphing and the
150	other plunged in grief at the same events happening to the city or the
150	citizens?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	Such differences commonly originate in a disagreement about the use of
150	the terms 'mine' and 'not mine,' 'his' and 'not his.'
150	
150	Exactly so.
150	
150	And is not that the best-ordered State in which the greatest number of
150	persons apply the terms 'mine' and 'not mine' in the same way to the
150	same thing?
150	
150	Quite true.
150	
150	Or that again which most nearly approaches to the condition of the
150	individual--as in the body, when but a finger of one of us is hurt, the
150	whole frame, drawn towards the soul as a center and forming one kingdom
150	under the ruling power therein, feels the hurt and sympathizes all
150	together with the part affected, and we say that the man has a pain in
150	his finger; and the same expression is used about any other part of the
150	body, which has a sensation of pain at suffering or of pleasure at the
150	alleviation of suffering.
150	
150	Very true, he replied; and I agree with you that in the best-ordered
150	State there is the nearest approach to this common feeling which you
150	describe.
150	
150	Then when any one of the citizens experiences any good or evil, the
150	whole State will make his case their own, and will either rejoice or
150	sorrow with him?
150	
150	Yes, he said, that is what will happen in a well-ordered State.
150	
150	It will now be time, I said, for us to return to our State and see
150	whether this or some other form is most in accordance with these
150	fundamental principles.
150	
150	Very good.
150	
150	Our State like every other has rulers and subjects?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	All of whom will call one another citizens?
150	
150	Of course.
150	
150	But is there not another name which people give to their rulers in
150	other States?
150	
150	Generally they call them masters, but in democratic States they simply
150	call them rulers.
150	
150	And in our State what other name besides that of citizens do the people
150	give the rulers?
150	
150	They are called saviours and helpers, he replied.
150	
150	And what do the rulers call the people?
150	
150	Their maintainers and foster-fathers.
150	
150	And what do they call them in other States?
150	
150	Slaves.
150	
150	And what do the rulers call one another in other States?
150	
150	Fellow-rulers.
150	
150	And what in ours?
150	
150	Fellow-guardians.
150	
150	Did you ever know an example in any other State of a ruler who would
150	speak of one of his colleagues as his friend and of another as not
150	being his friend?
150	
150	Yes, very often.
150	
150	And the friend he regards and describes as one in whom he has an
150	interest, and the other as a stranger in whom he has no interest?
150	
150	Exactly.
150	
150	But would any of your guardians think or speak of any other guardian as
150	a stranger?
150	
150	Certainly he would not; for every one whom they meet will be regarded
150	by them either as a brother or sister, or father or mother, or son or
150	daughter, or as the child or parent of those who are thus connected
150	with him.
150	
150	Capital, I said; but let me ask you once more:  Shall they be a family
150	in name only; or shall they in all their actions be true to the name?
150	For example, in the use of the word 'father,' would the care of a
150	father be implied and the filial reverence and duty and obedience to
150	him which the law commands; and is the violator of these duties to be
150	regarded as an impious and unrighteous person who is not likely to
150	receive much good either at the hands of God or of man?  Are these to
150	be or not to be the strains which the children will hear repeated in
150	their ears by all the citizens about those who are intimated to them to
150	be their parents and the rest of their kinsfolk?
150	
150	These, he said, and none other; for what can be more ridiculous than
150	for them to utter the names of family ties with the lips only and not
150	to act in the spirit of them?
150	
150	Then in our city the language of harmony and concord will be more often
150	beard than in any other.  As I was describing before, when any one is
150	well or ill, the universal word will be with me 'it is well' or 'it is
150	ill.'
150	
150	Most true.
150	
150	And agreeably to this mode of thinking and speaking, were we not saying
150	that they will have their pleasures and pains in common?
150	
150	Yes, and so they will.
150	
150	And they will have a common interest in the same thing which they will
150	alike call 'my own,' and having this common interest they will have a
150	common feeling of pleasure and pain?
150	
150	Yes, far more so than in other States.
150	
150	And the reason of this, over and above the general constitution of the
150	State, will be that the guardians will have a community of women and
150	children?
150	
150	That will be the chief reason.
150	
150	And this unity of feeling we admitted to be the greatest good, as was
150	implied in our own comparison of a well-ordered State to the relation
150	of the body and the members, when affected by pleasure or pain?
150	
150	That we acknowledged, and very rightly.
150	
150	Then the community of wives and children among our citizens is clearly
150	the source of the greatest good to the State?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	And this agrees with the other principle which we were affirming,--that
150	the guardians were not to have houses or lands or any other property;
150	their pay was to be their food, which they were to receive from the
150	other citizens, and they were to have no private expenses; for we
150	intended them to preserve their true character of guardians.
150	
150	Right, he replied.
150	
150	Both the community of property and the community of families, as I am
150	saying, tend to make them more truly guardians; they will not tear the
150	city in pieces by differing about 'mine' and 'not mine;' each man
150	dragging any acquisition which he has made into a separate house of his
150	own, where he has a separate wife and children and private pleasures
150	and pains; but all will be affected as far as may be by the same
150	pleasures and pains because they are all of one opinion about what is
150	near and dear to them, and therefore they all tend towards a common end.
150	
150	Certainly, he replied.
150	
150	And as they have nothing but their persons which they can call their
150	own, suits and complaints will have no existence among them; they will
150	be delivered from all those quarrels of which money or children or
150	relations are the occasion.
150	
150	Of course they will.
150	
150	Neither will trials for assault or insult ever be likely to occur among
150	them.  For that equals should defend themselves against equals we shall
150	maintain to be honourable and right; we shall make the protection of
150	the person a matter of necessity.
150	
150	That is good, he said.
150	
150	Yes; and there is a further good in the law; viz.  that if a man has a
150	quarrel with another he will satisfy his resentment then and there, and
150	not proceed to more dangerous lengths.
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	To the elder shall be assigned the duty of ruling and chastising the
150	younger.
150	
150	Clearly.
150	
150	Nor can there be a doubt that the younger will not strike or do any
150	other violence to an elder, unless the magistrates command him; nor
150	will he slight him in any way.  For there are two guardians, shame and
150	fear, mighty to prevent him:  shame, which makes men refrain from
150	laying hands on those who are to them in the relation of parents; fear,
150	that the injured one will be succoured by the others who are his
150	brothers, sons, one wi fathers.
150	
150	That is true, he replied.
150	
150	Then in every way the laws will help the citizens to keep the peace
150	with one another?
150	
150	Yes, there will be no want of peace.
150	
150	And as the guardians will never quarrel among themselves there will be
150	no danger of the rest of the city being divided either against them or
150	against one another.
150	
150	None whatever.
150	
150	I hardly like even to mention the little meannesses of which they will
150	be rid, for they are beneath notice:  such, for example, as the
150	flattery of the rich by the poor, and all the pains and pangs which men
150	experience in bringing up a family, and in finding money to buy
150	necessaries for their household, borrowing and then repudiating,
150	getting how they can, and giving the money into the hands of women and
150	slaves to keep--the many evils of so many kinds which people suffer in
150	this way are mean enough and obvious enough, and not worth speaking of.
150	
150	Yes, he said, a man has no need of eyes in order to perceive that.
150	
150	And from all these evils they will be delivered, and their life will be
150	blessed as the life of Olympic victors and yet more blessed.
150	
150	How so?
150	
150	The Olympic victor, I said, is deemed happy in receiving a part only of
150	the blessedness which is secured to our citizens, who have won a more
150	glorious victory and have a more complete maintenance at the public
150	cost.  For the victory which they have won is the salvation of the
150	whole State; and the crown with which they and their children are
150	crowned is the fulness of all that life needs; they receive rewards
150	from the hands of their country while living, and after death have an
150	honourable burial.
150	
150	Yes, he said, and glorious rewards they are.
150	
150	Do you remember, I said, how in the course of the previous discussion
150	some one who shall be nameless accused us of making our guardians
150	unhappy--they had nothing and might have possessed all things-to whom
150	we replied that, if an occasion offered, we might perhaps hereafter
150	consider this question, but that, as at present advised, we would make
150	our guardians truly guardians, and that we were fashioning the State
150	with a view to the greatest happiness, not of any particular class, but
150	of the whole?
150	
150	Yes, I remember.
150	
150	And what do you say, now that the life of our protectors is made out to
150	be far better and nobler than that of Olympic victors--is the life of
150	shoemakers, or any other artisans, or of husbandmen, to be compared
150	with it?
150	
150	Certainly not.
150	
150	At the same time I ought here to repeat what I have said elsewhere,
150	that if any of our guardians shall try to be happy in such a manner
150	that he will cease to be a guardian, and is not content with this safe
150	and harmonious life, which, in our judgment, is of all lives the best,
150	but infatuated by some youthful conceit of happiness which gets up into
150	his head shall seek to appropriate the whole State to himself, then he
150	will have to learn how wisely Hesiod spoke, when he said, 'half is more
150	than the whole.'
150	
150	If he were to consult me, I should say to him:  Stay where you are,
150	when you have the offer of such a life.
150	
150	You agree then, I said, that men and women are to have a common way of
150	life such as we have described--common education, common children; and
150	they are to watch over the citizens in common whether abiding in the
150	city or going out to war; they are to keep watch together, and to hunt
150	together like dogs; and always and in all things, as far as they are
150	able, women are to share with the men?  And in so doing they will do
150	what is best, and will not violate, but preserve the natural relation
150	of the sexes.
150	
150	I agree with you, he replied.
150	
150	The enquiry, I said, has yet to be made, whether such a community be
150	found possible--as among other animals, so also among men--and if
150	possible, in what way possible?
150	
150	You have anticipated the question which I was about to suggest.
150	
150	There is no difficulty, I said, in seeing how war will be carried on by
150	them.
150	
150	How?
150	
150	Why, of course they will go on expeditions together; and will take with
150	them any of their children who are strong enough, that, after the
150	manner of the artisan's child, they may look on at the work which they
150	will have to do when they are grown up; and besides looking on they
150	will have to help and be of use in war, and to wait upon their fathers
150	and mothers.  Did you never observe in the arts how the potters' boys
150	look on and help, long before they touch the wheel?
150	
150	Yes, I have.
150	
150	And shall potters be more careful in educating their children and in
150	giving them the opportunity of seeing and practising their duties than
150	our guardians will be?
150	
150	The idea is ridiculous, he said.
150	
150	There is also the effect on the parents, with whom, as with other
150	animals, the presence of their young ones will be the greatest
150	incentive to valour.
150	
150	That is quite true, Socrates; and yet if they are defeated, which may
150	often happen in war, how great the danger is! the children will be lost
150	as well as their parents, and the State will never recover.
150	
150	True, I said; but would you never allow them to run any risk?
150	
150	I am far from saying that.
150	
150	Well, but if they are ever to run a risk should they not do so on some
150	occasion when, if they escape disaster, they will be the better for it?
150	
150	Clearly.
150	
150	Whether the future soldiers do or do not see war in the days of their
150	youth is a very important matter, for the sake of which some risk may
150	fairly be incurred.
150	
150	Yes, very important.
150	
150	This then must be our first step,--to make our children spectators of
150	war; but we must also contrive that they shall be secured against
150	danger; then all will be well.
150	
150	True.
150	
150	Their parents may be supposed not to be blind to the risks of war, but
150	to know, as far as human foresight can, what expeditions are safe and
150	what dangerous?
150	
150	That may be assumed.
150	
150	And they will take them on the safe expeditions and be cautious about
150	the dangerous ones?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	And they will place them under the command of experienced veterans who
150	will be their leaders and teachers?
150	
150	Very properly.
150	
150	Still, the dangers of war cannot be always foreseen; there is a good
150	deal of chance about them?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	Then against such chances the children must be at once furnished with
150	wings, in order that in the hour of need they may fly away and escape.
150	
150	What do you mean? he said.
150	
150	I mean that we must mount them on horses in their earliest youth, and
150	when they have learnt to ride, take them on horseback to see war: the
150	horses must be spirited and warlike, but the most tractable and yet the
150	swiftest that can be had.  In this way they will get an excellent view
150	of what is hereafter to be their own business; and if there is danger
150	they have only to follow their elder leaders and escape.
150	
150	I believe that you are right, he said.
150	
150	Next, as to war; what are to be the relations of your soldiers to one
150	another and to their enemies?  I should be inclined to propose that the
150	soldier who leaves his rank or throws away his arms, or is guilty of
150	any other act of cowardice, should be degraded into the rank of a
150	husbandman or artisan.  What do you think?
150	
150	By all means, I should say.
150	
150	And he who allows himself to be taken prisoner may as well be made a
150	present of to his enemies; he is their lawful prey, and let them do
150	what they like with him.
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	But the hero who has distinguished himself, what shall be done to him?
150	In the first place, he shall receive honour in the army from his
150	youthful comrades; every one of them in succession shall crown him.
150	What do you say?
150	
150	I approve.
150	
150	And what do you say to his receiving the right hand of fellowship?
150	
150	To that too, I agree.
150	
150	But you will hardly agree to my next proposal.
150	
150	What is your proposal?
150	
150	That he should kiss and be kissed by them.
150	
150	Most certainly, and I should be disposed to go further, and say: Let no
150	one whom he has a mind to kiss refuse to be kissed by him while the
150	expedition lasts.  So that if there be a lover in the army, whether his
150	love be youth or maiden, he may be more eager to win the prize of
150	valour.
150	
150	Capital, I said.  That the brave man is to have more wives than others
150	has been already determined:  and he is to have first choices in such
150	matters more than others, in order that he may have as many children as
150	possible?
150	
150	Agreed.
150	
150	Again, there is another manner in which, according to Homer, brave
150	youths should be honoured; for he tells how Ajax, after he had
150	distinguished himself in battle, was rewarded with long chines, which
150	seems to be a compliment appropriate to a hero in the flower of his
150	age, being not only a tribute of honour but also a very strengthening
150	thing.
150	
150	Most true, he said.
150	
150	Then in this, I said, Homer shall be our teacher; and we too, at
150	sacrifices and on the like occasions, will honour the brave according
150	to the measure of their valour, whether men or women, with hymns and
150	those other distinctions which we were mentioning; also with
150	
150	    seats of precedence, and meats and full cups;
150	
150	and in honouring them, we shall be at the same time training them.
150	
150	That, he replied, is excellent.
150	
150	Yes, I said; and when a man dies gloriously in war shall we not say, in
150	the first place, that he is of the golden race?
150	
150	To be sure.
150	
150	Nay, have we not the authority of Hesiod for affirming that when they
150	are dead
150	
150	    They are holy angels upon the earth, authors of good,
150	    averters of evil, the guardians of speech-gifted men?
150	
150	Yes; and we accept his authority.
150	
150	We must learn of the god how we are to order the sepulture of divine
150	and heroic personages, and what is to be their special distinction and
150	we must do as he bids?
150	
150	By all means.
150	
150	And in ages to come we will reverence them and kneel before their
150	sepulchres as at the graves of heroes.  And not only they but any who
150	are deemed pre-eminently good, whether they die from age, or in any
150	other way, shall be admitted to the same honours.
150	
150	That is very right, he said.
150	
150	Next, how shall our soldiers treat their enemies?  What about this?
150	
150	In what respect do you mean?
150	
150	First of all, in regard to slavery?  Do you think it right that
150	Hellenes should enslave Hellenic States, or allow others to enslave
150	them, if they can help?  Should not their custom be to spare them,
150	considering the danger which there is that the whole race may one day
150	fall under the yoke of the barbarians?
150	
150	To spare them is infinitely better.
150	
150	Then no Hellene should be owned by them as a slave; that is a rule
150	which they will observe and advise the other Hellenes to observe.
150	
150	Certainly, he said; they will in this way be united against the
150	barbarians and will keep their hands off one another.
150	
150	Next as to the slain; ought the conquerors, I said, to take anything
150	but their armour?  Does not the practice of despoiling an enemy afford
150	an excuse for not facing the battle?  Cowards skulk about the dead,
150	pretending that they are fulfilling a duty, and many an army before now
150	has been lost from this love of plunder.
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	And is there not illiberality and avarice in robbing a corpse, and also
150	a degree of meanness and womanishness in making an enemy of the dead
150	body when the real enemy has flown away and left only his fighting gear
150	behind him,--is not this rather like a dog who cannot get at his
150	assailant, quarrelling with the stones which strike him instead?
150	
150	Very like a dog, he said.
150	
150	Then we must abstain from spoiling the dead or hindering their burial?
150	
150	Yes, he replied, we most certainly must.
150	
150	Neither shall we offer up arms at the temples of the gods, least of all
150	the arms of Hellenes, if we care to maintain good feeling with other
150	Hellenes; and, indeed, we have reason to fear that the offering of
150	spoils taken from kinsmen may be a pollution unless commanded by the
150	god himself?
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	Again, as to the devastation of Hellenic territory or the burning of
150	houses, what is to be the practice?
150	
150	May I have the pleasure, he said, of hearing your opinion?
150	
150	Both should be forbidden, in my judgment; I would take the annual
150	produce and no more.  Shall I tell you why?
150	
150	Pray do.
150	
150	Why, you see, there is a difference in the names 'discord' and 'war,'
150	and I imagine that there is also a difference in their natures; the one
150	is expressive of what is internal and domestic, the other of what is
150	external and foreign; and the first of the two is termed discord, and
150	only the second, war.
150	
150	That is a very proper distinction, he replied.
150	
150	And may I not observe with equal propriety that the Hellenic race is
150	all united together by ties of blood and friendship, and alien and
150	strange to the barbarians?
150	
150	Very good, he said.
150	
150	And therefore when Hellenes fight with barbarians and barbarians with
150	Hellenes, they will be described by us as being at war when they fight,
150	and by nature enemies, and this kind of antagonism should be called
150	war; but when Hellenes fight with one another we shall say that Hellas
150	is then in a state of disorder and discord, they being by nature
150	friends and such enmity is to be called discord.
150	
150	I agree.
150	
150	Consider then, I said, when that which we have acknowledged to be
150	discord occurs, and a city is divided, if both parties destroy the
150	lands and burn the houses of one another, how wicked does the strife
150	appear!  No true lover of his country would bring himself to tear in
150	pieces his own nurse and mother:  There might be reason in the
150	conqueror depriving the conquered of their harvest, but still they
150	would have the idea of peace in their hearts and would not mean to go
150	on fighting for ever.
150	
150	Yes, he said, that is a better temper than the other.
150	
150	And will not the city, which you are founding, be an Hellenic city?
150	
150	It ought to be, he replied.
150	
150	Then will not the citizens be good and civilized?
150	
150	Yes, very civilized.
150	
150	And will they not be lovers of Hellas, and think of Hellas as their own
150	land, and share in the common temples?
150	
150	Most certainly.
150	
150	And any difference which arises among them will be regarded by them as
150	discord only--a quarrel among friends, which is not to be called a war?
150	
150	Certainly not.
150	
150	Then they will quarrel as those who intend some day to be reconciled?
150	Certainly.
150	
150	They will use friendly correction, but will not enslave or destroy
150	their opponents; they will be correctors, not enemies?
150	
150	Just so.
150	
150	And as they are Hellenes themselves they will not devastate Hellas, nor
150	will they burn houses, not even suppose that the whole population of a
150	city--men, women, and children--are equally their enemies, for they
150	know that the guilt of war is always confined to a few persons and that
150	the many are their friends.  And for all these reasons they will be
150	unwilling to waste their lands and raze their houses; their enmity to
150	them will only last until the many innocent sufferers have compelled
150	the guilty few to give satisfaction?
150	
150	I agree, he said, that our citizens should thus deal with their
150	Hellenic enemies; and with barbarians as the Hellenes now deal with one
150	another.
150	
150	Then let us enact this law also for our guardians:-that they are
150	neither to devastate the lands of Hellenes nor to burn their houses.
150	
150	Agreed; and we may agree also in thinking that these, all our previous
150	enactments, are very good.
150	
150	But still I must say, Socrates, that if you are allowed to go on in
150	this way you will entirely forget the other question which at the
150	commencement of this discussion you thrust aside:--Is such an order of
150	things possible, and how, if at all?  For I am quite ready to
150	acknowledge that the plan which you propose, if only feasible, would do
150	all sorts of good to the State.  I will add, what you have omitted,
150	that your citizens will be the bravest of warriors, and will never
150	leave their ranks, for they will all know one another, and each will
150	call the other father, brother, son; and if you suppose the women to
150	join their armies, whether in the same rank or in the rear, either as a
150	terror to the enemy, or as auxiliaries in case of need, I know that
150	they will then be absolutely invincible; and there are many domestic
150	tic advantages which might also be mentioned and which I also fully
150	acknowledge: but, as I admit all these advantages and as many more as
150	you please, if only this State of yours were to come into existence, we
150	need say no more about them; assuming then the existence of the State,
150	let us now turn to the question of possibility and ways and means--the
150	rest may be left.
150	
150	If I loiter for a moment, you instantly make a raid upon me, I said,
150	and have no mercy; I have hardly escaped the first and second waves,
150	and you seem not to be aware that you are now bringing upon me the
150	third, which is the greatest and heaviest.  When you have seen and
150	heard the third wave, I think you be more considerate and will
150	acknowledge that some fear and hesitation was natural respecting a
150	proposal so extraordinary as that which I have now to state and
150	investigate.
150	
150	The more appeals of this sort which you make, he said, the more
150	determined are we that you shall tell us how such a State is possible:
150	speak out and at once.
150	
150	Let me begin by reminding you that we found our way hither in the
150	search after justice and injustice.
150	
150	True, he replied; but what of that?
150	
150	I was only going to ask whether, if we have discovered them, we are to
150	require that the just man should in nothing fail of absolute justice;
150	or may we be satisfied with an approximation, and the attainment in him
150	of a higher degree of justice than is to be found in other men?
150	
150	The approximation will be enough.
150	
150	We are enquiring into the nature of absolute justice and into the
150	character of the perfectly just, and into injustice and the perfectly
150	unjust, that we might have an ideal.  We were to look at these in order
150	that we might judge of our own happiness and unhappiness according to
150	the standard which they exhibited and the degree in which we resembled
150	them, but not with any view of showing that they could exist in fact.
150	
150	True, he said.
150	
150	Would a painter be any the worse because, after having delineated with
150	consummate art an ideal of a perfectly beautiful man, he was unable to
150	show that any such man could ever have existed?
150	
150	He would be none the worse.
150	
150	Well, and were we not creating an ideal of a perfect State?
150	
150	To be sure.
150	
150	And is our theory a worse theory because we are unable to prove the
150	possibility of a city being ordered in the manner described?
150	
150	Surely not, he replied.
150	
150	That is the truth, I said.  But if, at your request, I am to try and
150	show how and under what conditions the possibility is highest, I must
150	ask you, having this in view, to repeat your former admissions.
150	
150	What admissions?
150	
150	I want to know whether ideals are ever fully realised in language?
150	Does not the word express more than the fact, and must not the actual,
150	whatever a man may think, always, in the nature of things, fall short
150	of the truth?  What do you say?
150	
150	I agree.
150	
150	Then you must not insist on my proving that the actual State will in
150	every respect coincide with the ideal:  if we are only able to discover
150	how a city may be governed nearly as we proposed, you will admit that
150	we have discovered the possibility which you demand; and will be
150	contented.  I am sure that I should be contented--will not you?
150	
150	Yes, I will.
150	
150	Let me next endeavour to show what is that fault in States which is the
150	cause of their present maladministration, and what is the least change
150	which will enable a State to pass into the truer form; and let the
150	change, if possible, be of one thing only, or if not, of two; at any
150	rate, let the changes be as few and slight as possible.
150	
150	Certainly, he replied.
150	
150	I think, I said, that there might be a reform of the State if only one
150	change were made, which is not a slight or easy though still a possible
150	one.
150	
150	What is it? he said.
150	
150	Now then, I said, I go to meet that which I liken to the greatest of
150	the waves; yet shall the word be spoken, even though the wave break and
150	drown me in laughter and dishonour; and do you mark my words.
150	
150	Proceed.
150	
150	I said:  Until philosophers are kings, or the kings and princes of this
150	world have the spirit and power of philosophy, and political greatness
150	and wisdom meet in one, and those commoner natures who pursue either to
150	the exclusion of the other are compelled to stand aside, cities will
150	never have rest from their evils,--nor the human race, as I
150	believe,--and then only will this our State have a possibility of life
150	and behold the light of day.  Such was the thought, my dear Glaucon,
150	which I would fain have uttered if it had not seemed too extravagant;
150	for to be convinced that in no other State can there be happiness
150	private or public is indeed a hard thing.
150	
150	Socrates, what do you mean?  I would have you consider that the word
150	which you have uttered is one at which numerous persons, and very
150	respectable persons too, in a figure pulling off their coats all in a
150	moment, and seizing any weapon that comes to hand, will run at you
150	might and main, before you know where you are, intending to do heaven
150	knows what; and if you don't prepare an answer, and put yourself in
150	motion, you will be prepared by their fine wits,' and no mistake.
150	
150	You got me into the scrape, I said.
150	
150	And I was quite right; however, I will do all I can to get you out of
150	it; but I can only give you good-will and good advice, and, perhaps, I
150	may be able to fit answers to your questions better than another--that
150	is all.  And now, having such an auxiliary, you must do your best to
150	show the unbelievers that you are right.
150	
150	I ought to try, I said, since you offer me such invaluable assistance.
150	And I think that, if there is to be a chance of our escaping, we must
150	explain to them whom we mean when we say that philosophers are to rule
150	in the State; then we shall be able to defend ourselves: There will be
150	discovered to be some natures who ought to study philosophy and to be
150	leaders in the State; and others who are not born to be philosophers,
150	and are meant to be followers rather than leaders.
150	
150	Then now for a definition, he said.
150	
150	Follow me, I said, and I hope that I may in some way or other be able
150	to give you a satisfactory explanation.
150	
150	Proceed.
150	
150	I dare say that you remember, and therefore I need not remind you, that
150	a lover, if lie is worthy of the name, ought to show his love, not to
150	some one part of that which he loves, but to the whole.
150	
150	I really do not understand, and therefore beg of you to assist my
150	memory.
150	
150	Another person, I said, might fairly reply as you do; but a man of
150	pleasure like yourself ought to know that all who are in the flower of
