150	youth do somehow or other raise a pang or emotion in a lover's breast,
150	and are thought by him to be worthy of his affectionate regards.  Is
150	not this a way which you have with the fair:  one has a snub nose, and
150	you praise his charming face; the hook-nose of another has, you say, a
150	royal look; while he who is neither snub nor hooked has the grace of
150	regularity:  the dark visage is manly, the fair are children of the
150	gods; and as to the sweet 'honey pale,' as they are called, what is the
150	very name but the invention of a lover who talks in diminutives, and is
150	not adverse to paleness if appearing on the cheek of youth?  In a word,
150	there is no excuse which you will not make, and nothing which you will
150	not say, in order not to lose a single flower that blooms in the
150	spring-time of youth.
150	
150	If you make me an authority in matters of love, for the sake of the
150	argument, I assent.
150	
150	And what do you say of lovers of wine?  Do you not see them doing the
150	same?  They are glad of any pretext of drinking any wine.
150	
150	Very good.
150	
150	And the same is true of ambitious men; if they cannot command an army,
150	they are willing to command a file; and if they cannot be honoured by
150	really great and important persons, they are glad to be honoured by
150	lesser and meaner people, but honour of some kind they must have.
150	
150	Exactly.
150	
150	Once more let me ask:  Does he who desires any class of goods, desire
150	the whole class or a part only?
150	
150	The whole.
150	
150	And may we not say of the philosopher that he is a lover, not of a part
150	of wisdom only, but of the whole?
150	
150	Yes, of the whole.
150	
150	And he who dislikes learnings, especially in youth, when he has no
150	power of judging what is good and what is not, such an one we maintain
150	not to be a philosopher or a lover of knowledge, just as he who refuses
150	his food is not hungry, and may be said to have a bad appetite and not
150	a good one?
150	
150	Very true, he said.
150	
150	Whereas he who has a taste for every sort of knowledge and who is
150	curious to learn and is never satisfied, may be justly termed a
150	philosopher?  Am I not right?
150	
150	Glaucon said:  If curiosity makes a philosopher, you will find many a
150	strange being will have a title to the name.  All the lovers of sights
150	have a delight in learning, and must therefore be included.  Musical
150	amateurs, too, are a folk strangely out of place among philosophers,
150	for they are the last persons in the world who would come to anything
150	like a philosophical discussion, if they could help, while they run
150	about at the Dionysiac festivals as if they had let out their ears to
150	hear every chorus; whether the performance is in town or country--that
150	makes no difference--they are there.  Now are we to maintain that all
150	these and any who have similar tastes, as well as the professors of
150	quite minor arts, are philosophers?
150	
150	Certainly not, I replied; they are only an imitation.
150	
150	He said:  Who then are the true philosophers?
150	
150	Those, I said, who are lovers of the vision of truth.
150	
150	That is also good, he said; but I should like to know what you mean?
150	
150	To another, I replied, I might have a difficulty in explaining; but I
150	am sure that you will admit a proposition which I am about to make.
150	
150	What is the proposition?
150	
150	That since beauty is the opposite of ugliness, they are two?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	And inasmuch as they are two, each of them is one?
150	
150	True again.
150	
150	And of just and unjust, good and evil, and of every other class, the
150	same remark holds:  taken singly, each of them one; but from the
150	various combinations of them with actions and things and with one
150	another, they are seen in all sorts of lights and appear many?  Very
150	true.
150	
150	And this is the distinction which I draw between the sight-loving,
150	art-loving, practical class and those of whom I am speaking, and who
150	are alone worthy of the name of philosophers.
150	
150	How do you distinguish them? he said.
150	
150	The lovers of sounds and sights, I replied, are, as I conceive, fond of
150	fine tones and colours and forms and all the artificial products that
150	are made out of them, but their mind is incapable of seeing or loving
150	absolute beauty.
150	
150	True, he replied.
150	
150	Few are they who are able to attain to the sight of this.
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	And he who, having a sense of beautiful things has no sense of absolute
150	beauty, or who, if another lead him to a knowledge of that beauty is
150	unable to follow--of such an one I ask, Is he awake or in a dream only?
150	Reflect:  is not the dreamer, sleeping or waking, one who likens
150	dissimilar things, who puts the copy in the place of the real object?
150	
150	I should certainly say that such an one was dreaming.
150	
150	But take the case of the other, who recognises the existence of
150	absolute beauty and is able to distinguish the idea from the objects
150	which participate in the idea, neither putting the objects in the place
150	of the idea nor the idea in the place of the objects--is he a dreamer,
150	or is he awake?
150	
150	He is wide awake.
150	
150	And may we not say that the mind of the one who knows has knowledge,
150	and that the mind of the other, who opines only, has opinion
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	But suppose that the latter should quarrel with us and dispute our
150	statement, can we administer any soothing cordial or advice to him,
150	without revealing to him that there is sad disorder in his wits?
150	
150	We must certainly offer him some good advice, he replied.
150	
150	Come, then, and let us think of something to say to him.  Shall we
150	begin by assuring him that he is welcome to any knowledge which he may
150	have, and that we are rejoiced at his having it?  But we should like to
150	ask him a question:  Does he who has knowledge know something or
150	nothing?  (You must answer for him.)
150	
150	I answer that he knows something.
150	
150	Something that is or is not?
150	
150	Something that is; for how can that which is not ever be known?
150	
150	And are we assured, after looking at the matter from many points of
150	view, that absolute being is or may be absolutely known, but that the
150	utterly non-existent is utterly unknown?
150	
150	Nothing can be more certain.
150	
150	Good.  But if there be anything which is of such a nature as to be and
150	not to be, that will have a place intermediate between pure being and
150	the absolute negation of being?
150	
150	Yes, between them.
150	
150	And, as knowledge corresponded to being and ignorance of necessity to
150	not-being, for that intermediate between being and not-being there has
150	to be discovered a corresponding intermediate between ignorance and
150	knowledge, if there be such?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	Do we admit the existence of opinion?
150	
150	Undoubtedly.
150	
150	As being the same with knowledge, or another faculty?
150	
150	Another faculty.
150	
150	Then opinion and knowledge have to do with different kinds of matter
150	corresponding to this difference of faculties?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And knowledge is relative to being and knows being.  But before I
150	proceed further I will make a division.
150	
150	What division?
150	
150	I will begin by placing faculties in a class by themselves: they are
150	powers in us, and in all other things, by which we do as we do.  Sight
150	and hearing, for example, I should call faculties.  Have I clearly
150	explained the class which I mean?
150	
150	Yes, I quite understand.
150	
150	Then let me tell you my view about them.  I do not see them, and
150	therefore the distinctions of fire, colour, and the like, which enable
150	me to discern the differences of some things, do not apply to them.  In
150	speaking of a faculty I think only of its sphere and its result; and
150	that which has the same sphere and the same result I call the same
150	faculty, but that which has another sphere and another result I call
150	different.  Would that be your way of speaking?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And will you be so very good as to answer one more question?  Would you
150	say that knowledge is a faculty, or in what class would you place it?
150	
150	Certainly knowledge is a faculty, and the mightiest of all faculties.
150	
150	And is opinion also a faculty?
150	
150	Certainly, he said; for opinion is that with which we are able to form
150	an opinion.
150	
150	And yet you were acknowledging a little while ago that knowledge is not
150	the same as opinion?
150	
150	Why, yes, he said:  how can any reasonable being ever identify that
150	which is infallible with that which errs?
150	
150	An excellent answer, proving, I said, that we are quite conscious of a
150	distinction between them.
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	Then knowledge and opinion having distinct powers have also distinct
150	spheres or subject-matters?
150	
150	That is certain.
150	
150	Being is the sphere or subject-matter of knowledge, and knowledge is to
150	know the nature of being?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And opinion is to have an opinion?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And do we know what we opine? or is the subject-matter of opinion the
150	same as the subject-matter of knowledge?
150	
150	Nay, he replied, that has been already disproven; if difference in
150	faculty implies difference in the sphere or subject matter, and if, as
150	we were saying, opinion and knowledge are distinct faculties, then the
150	sphere of knowledge and of opinion cannot be the same.
150	
150	Then if being is the subject-matter of knowledge, something else must
150	be the subject-matter of opinion?
150	
150	Yes, something else.
150	
150	Well then, is not-being the subject-matter of opinion? or, rather, how
150	can there be an opinion at all about not-being? Reflect: when a man has
150	an opinion, has he not an opinion about something?  Can he have an
150	opinion which is an opinion about nothing?
150	
150	Impossible.
150	
150	He who has an opinion has an opinion about some one thing?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And not-being is not one thing but, properly speaking, nothing?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	Of not-being, ignorance was assumed to be the necessary correlative; of
150	being, knowledge?
150	
150	True, he said.
150	
150	Then opinion is not concerned either with being or with not-being?
150	
150	Not with either.
150	
150	And can therefore neither be ignorance nor knowledge?
150	
150	That seems to be true.
150	
150	But is opinion to be sought without and beyond either of them, in a
150	greater clearness than knowledge, or in a greater darkness than
150	ignorance?
150	
150	In neither.
150	
150	Then I suppose that opinion appears to you to be darker than knowledge,
150	but lighter than ignorance?
150	
150	Both; and in no small degree.
150	
150	And also to be within and between them?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	Then you would infer that opinion is intermediate?
150	
150	No question.
150	
150	But were we not saying before, that if anything appeared to be of a
150	sort which is and is not at the same time, that sort of thing would
150	appear also to lie in the interval between pure being and absolute
150	not-being; and that the corresponding faculty is neither knowledge nor
150	ignorance, but will be found in the interval between them?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	And in that interval there has now been discovered something which we
150	call opinion?
150	
150	There has.
150	
150	Then what remains to be discovered is the object which partakes equally
150	of the nature of being and not-being, and cannot rightly be termed
150	either, pure and simple; this unknown term, when discovered, we may
150	truly call the subject of opinion, and assign each to its proper
150	faculty, the extremes to the faculties of the extremes and the mean to
150	the faculty of the mean.
150	
150	True.
150	
150	This being premised, I would ask the gentleman who is of opinion that
150	there is no absolute or unchangeable idea of beauty--in whose opinion
150	the beautiful is the manifold--he, I say, your lover of beautiful
150	sights, who cannot bear to be told that the beautiful is one, and the
150	just is one, or that anything is one--to him I would appeal, saying,
150	Will you be so very kind, sir, as to tell us whether, of all these
150	beautiful things, there is one which will not be found ugly; or of the
150	just, which will not be found unjust; or of the holy, which will not
150	also be unholy?
150	
150	No, he replied; the beautiful will in some point of view be found ugly;
150	and the same is true of the rest.
150	
150	And may not the many which are doubles be also halves?--doubles, that
150	is, of one thing, and halves of another?
150	
150	Quite true.
150	
150	And things great and small, heavy and light, as they are termed, will
150	not be denoted by these any more than by the opposite names?
150	
150	True; both these and the opposite names will always attach to all of
150	them.
150	
150	And can any one of those many things which are called by particular
150	names be said to be this rather than not to be this?
150	
150	He replied:  They are like the punning riddles which are asked at
150	feasts or the children's puzzle about the eunuch aiming at the bat,
150	with what he hit him, as they say in the puzzle, and upon what the bat
150	was sitting.  The individual objects of which I am speaking are also a
150	riddle, and have a double sense: nor can you fix them in your mind,
150	either as being or not-being, or both, or neither.
150	
150	Then what will you do with them?  I said.  Can they have a better place
150	than between being and not-being? For they are clearly not in greater
150	darkness or negation than not-being, or more full of light and
150	existence than being.
150	
150	That is quite true, he said.
150	
150	Thus then we seem to have discovered that the many ideas which the
150	multitude entertain about the beautiful and about all other things are
150	tossing about in some region which is halfway between pure being and
150	pure not-being?
150	
150	We have.
150	
150	Yes; and we had before agreed that anything of this kind which we might
150	find was to be described as matter of opinion, and not as matter of
150	knowledge; being the intermediate flux which is caught and detained by
150	the intermediate faculty.
150	
150	Quite true.
150	
150	Then those who see the many beautiful, and who yet neither see absolute
150	beauty, nor can follow any guide who points the way thither; who see
150	the many just, and not absolute justice, and the like,--such persons
150	may be said to have opinion but not knowledge?
150	
150	That is certain.
150	
150	But those who see the absolute and eternal and immutable may be said to
150	know, and not to have opinion only?
150	
150	Neither can that be denied.
150	
150	The one loves and embraces the subjects of knowledge, the other those
150	of opinion?  The latter are the same, as I dare say will remember, who
150	listened to sweet sounds and gazed upon fair colours, but would not
150	tolerate the existence of absolute beauty.
150	
150	Yes, I remember.
150	
150	Shall we then be guilty of any impropriety in calling them lovers of
150	opinion rather than lovers of wisdom, and will they be very angry with
150	us for thus describing them?
150	
150	I shall tell them not to be angry; no man should be angry at what is
150	true.
150	
150	But those who love the truth in each thing are to be called lovers of
150	wisdom and not lovers of opinion.
150	
150	Assuredly.
150	
150	
150	
150	
150	BOOK VI
150	
150	
150	SOCRATES - GLAUCON
150	
150	AND thus, Glaucon, after the argument has gone a weary way, the true
150	and the false philosophers have at length appeared in view.
150	
150	I do not think, he said, that the way could have been shortened.
150	
150	I suppose not, I said; and yet I believe that we might have had a
150	better view of both of them if the discussion could have been confined
150	to this one subject and if there were not many other questions awaiting
150	us, which he who desires to see in what respect the life of the just
150	differs from that of the unjust must consider.
150	
150	And what is the next question? he asked.
150	
150	Surely, I said, the one which follows next in order.  Inasmuch as
150	philosophers only are able to grasp the eternal and unchangeable, and
150	those who wander in the region of the many and variable are not
150	philosophers, I must ask you which of the two classes should be the
150	rulers of our State?
150	
150	And how can we rightly answer that question?
150	
150	Whichever of the two are best able to guard the laws and institutions
150	of our State--let them be our guardians.
150	
150	Very good.
150	
150	Neither, I said, can there be any question that the guardian who is to
150	keep anything should have eyes rather than no eyes?
150	
150	There can be no question of that.
150	
150	And are not those who are verily and indeed wanting in the knowledge of
150	the true being of each thing, and who have in their souls no clear
150	pattern, and are unable as with a painter's eye to look at the absolute
150	truth and to that original to repair, and having perfect vision of the
150	other world to order the laws about beauty, goodness, justice in this,
150	if not already ordered, and to guard and preserve the order of
150	them--are not such persons, I ask, simply blind?
150	
150	Truly, he replied, they are much in that condition.
150	
150	And shall they be our guardians when there are others who, besides
150	being their equals in experience and falling short of them in no
150	particular of virtue, also know the very truth of each thing?
150	
150	There can be no reason, he said, for rejecting those who have this
150	greatest of all great qualities; they must always have the first place
150	unless they fail in some other respect.
150	
150	Suppose then, I said, that we determine how far they can unite this and
150	the other excellences.
150	
150	By all means.
150	
150	In the first place, as we began by observing, the nature of the
150	philosopher has to be ascertained.  We must come to an understanding
150	about him, and, when we have done so, then, if I am not mistaken, we
150	shall also acknowledge that such an union of qualities is possible, and
150	that those in whom they are united, and those only, should be rulers in
150	the State.
150	
150	What do you mean?
150	
150	Let us suppose that philosophical minds always love knowledge of a sort
150	which shows them the eternal nature not varying from generation and
150	corruption.
150	
150	Agreed.
150	
150	And further, I said, let us agree that they are lovers of all true
150	being; there is no part whether greater or less, or more or less
150	honourable, which they are willing to renounce; as we said before of
150	the lover and the man of ambition.
150	
150	True.
150	
150	And if they are to be what we were describing, is there not another
150	quality which they should also possess?
150	
150	What quality?
150	
150	Truthfulness:  they will never intentionally receive into their mind
150	falsehood, which is their detestation, and they will love the truth.
150	
150	Yes, that may be safely affirmed of them.
150	
150	'May be,' my friend, I replied, is not the word; say rather 'must be
150	affirmed:'  for he whose nature is amorous of anything cannot help
150	loving all that belongs or is akin to the object of his affections.
150	
150	Right, he said.
150	
150	And is there anything more akin to wisdom than truth?
150	
150	How can there be?
150	
150	Can the same nature be a lover of wisdom and a lover of falsehood?
150	
150	Never.
150	
150	The true lover of learning then must from his earliest youth, as far as
150	in him lies, desire all truth?
150	
150	Assuredly.
150	
150	But then again, as we know by experience, he whose desires are strong
150	in one direction will have them weaker in others; they will be like a
150	stream which has been drawn off into another channel.
150	
150	True.
150	
150	He whose desires are drawn towards knowledge in every form will be
150	absorbed in the pleasures of the soul, and will hardly feel bodily
150	pleasure--I mean, if he be a true philosopher and not a sham one.
150	
150	That is most certain.
150	
150	Such an one is sure to be temperate and the reverse of covetous; for
150	the motives which make another man desirous of having and spending,
150	have no place in his character.
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	Another criterion of the philosophical nature has also to be considered.
150	
150	What is that?
150	
150	There should be no secret corner of illiberality; nothing can more
150	antagonistic than meanness to a soul which is ever longing after the
150	whole of things both divine and human.
150	
150	Most true, he replied.
150	
150	Then how can he who has magnificence of mind and is the spectator of
150	all time and all existence, think much of human life?
150	
150	He cannot.
150	
150	Or can such an one account death fearful?
150	
150	No indeed.
150	
150	Then the cowardly and mean nature has no part in true philosophy?
150	
150	Certainly not.
150	
150	Or again:  can he who is harmoniously constituted, who is not covetous
150	or mean, or a boaster, or a coward-can he, I say, ever be unjust or
150	hard in his dealings?
150	
150	Impossible.
150	
150	Then you will soon observe whether a man is just and gentle, or rude
150	and unsociable; these are the signs which distinguish even in youth the
150	philosophical nature from the unphilosophical.
150	
150	True.
150	
150	There is another point which should be remarked.
150	
150	What point?
150	
150	Whether he has or has not a pleasure in learning; for no one will love
150	that which gives him pain, and in which after much toil he makes little
150	progress.
150	
150	Certainly not.
150	
150	And again, if he is forgetful and retains nothing of what he learns,
150	will he not be an empty vessel?
150	
150	That is certain.
150	
150	Labouring in vain, he must end in hating himself and his fruitless
150	occupation?  Yes.
150	
150	Then a soul which forgets cannot be ranked among genuine philosophic
150	natures; we must insist that the philosopher should have a good memory?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	And once more, the inharmonious and unseemly nature can only tend to
150	disproportion?
150	
150	Undoubtedly.
150	
150	And do you consider truth to be akin to proportion or to disproportion?
150	
150	To proportion.
150	
150	Then, besides other qualities, we must try to find a naturally
150	well-proportioned and gracious mind, which will move spontaneously
150	towards the true being of everything.
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	Well, and do not all these qualities, which we have been enumerating,
150	go together, and are they not, in a manner, necessary to a soul, which
150	is to have a full and perfect participation of being?
150	
150	They are absolutely necessary, he replied.
150	
150	And must not that be a blameless study which he only can pursue who has
150	the gift of a good memory, and is quick to learn,--noble, gracious, the
150	friend of truth, justice, courage, temperance, who are his kindred?
150	
150	The god of jealousy himself, he said, could find no fault with such a
150	study.
150	
150	And to men like him, I said, when perfected by years and education, and
150	to these only you will entrust the State.
150	
150	SOCRATES - ADEIMANTUS
150	
150	Here Adeimantus interposed and said:  To these statements, Socrates, no
150	one can offer a reply; but when you talk in this way, a strange feeling
150	passes over the minds of your hearers:  They fancy that they are led
150	astray a little at each step in the argument, owing to their own want
150	of skill in asking and answering questions; these littles accumulate,
150	and at the end of the discussion they are found to have sustained a
150	mighty overthrow and all their former notions appear to be turned
150	upside down.  And as unskilful players of draughts are at last shut up
150	by their more skilful adversaries and have no piece to move, so they
150	too find themselves shut up at last; for they have nothing to say in
150	this new game of which words are the counters; and yet all the time
150	they are in the right.  The observation is suggested to me by what is
150	now occurring.  For any one of us might say, that although in words he
150	is not able to meet you at each step of the argument, he sees as a fact
150	that the votaries of philosophy, when they carry on the study, not only
150	in youth as a part of education, but as the pursuit of their maturer
150	years, most of them become strange monsters, not to say utter rogues,
150	and that those who may be considered the best of them are made useless
150	to the world by the very study which you extol.
150	
150	Well, and do you think that those who say so are wrong?
150	
150	I cannot tell, he replied; but I should like to know what is your
150	opinion.
150	
150	Hear my answer; I am of opinion that they are quite right.
150	
150	Then how can you be justified in saying that cities will not cease from
150	evil until philosophers rule in them, when philosophers are
150	acknowledged by us to be of no use to them?
150	
150	You ask a question, I said, to which a reply can only be given in a
150	parable.
150	
150	Yes, Socrates; and that is a way of speaking to which you are not at
150	all accustomed, I suppose.
150	
150	I perceive, I said, that you are vastly amused at having plunged me
150	into such a hopeless discussion; but now hear the parable, and then you
150	will be still more amused at the meagreness of my imagination: for the
150	manner in which the best men are treated in their own States is so
150	grievous that no single thing on earth is comparable to it; and
150	therefore, if I am to plead their cause, I must have recourse to
150	fiction, and put together a figure made up of many things, like the
150	fabulous unions of goats and stags which are found in pictures.
150	Imagine then a fleet or a ship in which there is a captain who is
150	taller and stronger than any of the crew, but he is a little deaf and
150	has a similar infirmity in sight, and his knowledge of navigation is
150	not much better.  The sailors are quarrelling with one another about
150	the steering--every one is of opinion that he has a right to steer,
150	though he has never learned the art of navigation and cannot tell who
150	taught him or when he learned, and will further assert that it cannot
150	be taught, and they are ready to cut in pieces any one who says the
150	contrary.  They throng about the captain, begging and praying him to
150	commit the helm to them; and if at any time they do not prevail, but
150	others are preferred to them, they kill the others or throw them
150	overboard, and having first chained up the noble captain's senses with
150	drink or some narcotic drug, they mutiny and take possession of the
150	ship and make free with the stores; thus, eating and drinking, they
150	proceed on their voyage in such a manner as might be expected of them.
150	Him who is their partisan and cleverly aids them in their plot for
150	getting the ship out of the captain's hands into their own whether by
150	force or persuasion, they compliment with the name of sailor, pilot,
150	able seaman, and abuse the other sort of man, whom they call a
150	good-for-nothing; but that the true pilot must pay attention to the
150	year and seasons and sky and stars and winds, and whatever else belongs
150	to his art, if he intends to be really qualified for the command of a
150	ship, and that he must and will be the steerer, whether other people
150	like or not-the possibility of this union of authority with the
150	steerer's art has never seriously entered into their thoughts or been
150	made part of their calling.  Now in vessels which are in a state of
150	mutiny and by sailors who are mutineers, how will the true pilot be
150	regarded?  Will he not be called by them a prater, a star-gazer, a
150	good-for-nothing?
150	
150	Of course, said Adeimantus.
150	
150	Then you will hardly need, I said, to hear the interpretation of the
150	figure, which describes the true philosopher in his relation to the
150	State; for you understand already.
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	Then suppose you now take this parable to the gentleman who is
150	surprised at finding that philosophers have no honour in their cities;
150	explain it to him and try to convince him that their having honour
150	would be far more extraordinary.
150	
150	I will.
150	
150	Say to him, that, in deeming the best votaries of philosophy to be
150	useless to the rest of the world, he is right; but also tell him to
150	attribute their uselessness to the fault of those who will not use
150	them, and not to themselves.  The pilot should not humbly beg the
150	sailors to be commanded by him--that is not the order of nature;
150	neither are 'the wise to go to the doors of the rich'--the ingenious
150	author of this saying told a lie--but the truth is, that, when a man is
150	ill, whether he be rich or poor, to the physician he must go, and he
150	who wants to be governed, to him who is able to govern.  The ruler who
150	is good for anything ought not to beg his subjects to be ruled by him;
150	although the present governors of mankind are of a different stamp;
150	they may be justly compared to the mutinous sailors, and the true
150	helmsmen to those who are called by them good-for-nothings and
150	star-gazers.
150	
150	Precisely so, he said.
150	
150	For these reasons, and among men like these, philosophy, the noblest
150	pursuit of all, is not likely to be much esteemed by those of the
150	opposite faction; not that the greatest and most lasting injury is done
150	to her by her opponents, but by her own professing followers, the same
150	of whom you suppose the accuser to say, that the greater number of them
150	are arrant rogues, and the best are useless; in which opinion I agreed.
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And the reason why the good are useless has now been explained?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	Then shall we proceed to show that the corruption of the majority is
150	also unavoidable, and that this is not to be laid to the charge of
150	philosophy any more than the other?
150	
150	By all means.
150	
150	And let us ask and answer in turn, first going back to the description
150	of the gentle and noble nature.  Truth, as you will remember, was his
150	leader, whom he followed always and in all things; failing in this, he
150	was an impostor, and had no part or lot in true philosophy.
150	
150	Yes, that was said.
150	
150	Well, and is not this one quality, to mention no others, greatly at
150	variance with present notions of him?
150	
150	Certainly, he said.
150	
150	And have we not a right to say in his defence, that the true lover of
150	knowledge is always striving after being--that is his nature; he will
150	not rest in the multiplicity of individuals which is an appearance
150	only, but will go on--the keen edge will not be blunted, nor the force
150	of his desire abate until he have attained the knowledge of the true
150	nature of every essence by a sympathetic and kindred power in the soul,
150	and by that power drawing near and mingling and becoming incorporate
150	with very being, having begotten mind and truth, he will have knowledge
150	and will live and grow truly, and then, and not till then, will he
150	cease from his travail.
150	
150	Nothing, he said, can be more just than such a description of him.
150	
150	And will the love of a lie be any part of a philosopher's nature?  Will
150	he not utterly hate a lie?
150	
150	He will.
150	
150	And when truth is the captain, we cannot suspect any evil of the band
150	which he leads?
150	
150	Impossible.
150	
150	Justice and health of mind will be of the company, and temperance will
150	follow after?
150	
150	True, he replied.
150	
150	Neither is there any reason why I should again set in array the
150	philosopher's virtues, as you will doubtless remember that courage,
150	magnificence, apprehension, memory, were his natural gifts.  And you
150	objected that, although no one could deny what I then said, still, if
150	you leave words and look at facts, the persons who are thus described
150	are some of them manifestly useless, and the greater number utterly
150	depraved; we were then led to enquire into the grounds of these
150	accusations, and have now arrived at the point of asking why are the
150	majority bad, which question of necessity brought us back to the
150	examination and definition of the true philosopher.
150	
150	Exactly.
150	
150	And we have next to consider the corruptions of the philosophic nature,
150	why so many are spoiled and so few escape spoiling--I am speaking of
150	those who were said to be useless but not wicked--and, when we have
150	done with them, we will speak of the imitators of philosophy, what
150	manner of men are they who aspire after a profession which is above
150	them and of which they are unworthy, and then, by their manifold
150	inconsistencies, bring upon philosophy, and upon all philosophers, that
150	universal reprobation of which we speak.
150	
150	What are these corruptions? he said.
150	
150	I will see if I can explain them to you.  Every one will admit that a
150	nature having in perfection all the qualities which we required in a
150	philosopher, is a rare plant which is seldom seen among men.
150	
150	Rare indeed.
150	
150	And what numberless and powerful causes tend to destroy these rare
150	natures!
150	
150	What causes?
150	
150	In the first place there are their own virtues, their courage,
150	temperance, and the rest of them, every one of which praise worthy
150	qualities (and this is a most singular circumstance) destroys and
150	distracts from philosophy the soul which is the possessor of them.
150	
150	That is very singular, he replied.
150	
150	Then there are all the ordinary goods of life--beauty, wealth,
150	strength, rank, and great connections in the State--you understand the
150	sort of things--these also have a corrupting and distracting effect.
150	
150	I understand; but I should like to know more precisely what you mean
150	about them.
150	
150	Grasp the truth as a whole, I said, and in the right way; you will then
150	have no difficulty in apprehending the preceding remarks, and they will
150	no longer appear strange to you.
150	
150	And how am I to do so? he asked.
150	
150	Why, I said, we know that all germs or seeds, whether vegetable or
150	animal, when they fail to meet with proper nutriment or climate or
150	soil, in proportion to their vigour, are all the more sensitive to the
150	want of a suitable environment, for evil is a greater enemy to what is
150	good than what is not.
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	There is reason in supposing that the finest natures, when under alien
150	conditions, receive more injury than the inferior, because the contrast
150	is greater.
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	And may we not say, Adeimantus, that the most gifted minds, when they
150	are ill-educated, become pre-eminently bad?  Do not great crimes and
150	the spirit of pure evil spring out of a fulness of nature ruined by
150	education rather than from any inferiority, whereas weak natures are
150	scarcely capable of any very great good or very great evil?
150	
150	There I think that you are right.
150	
150	And our philosopher follows the same analogy-he is like a plant which,
150	having proper nurture, must necessarily grow and mature into all
150	virtue, but, if sown and planted in an alien soil, becomes the most
150	noxious of all weeds, unless he be preserved by some divine power.  Do
150	you really think, as people so often say, that our youth are corrupted
150	by Sophists, or that private teachers of the art corrupt them in any
150	degree worth speaking of?  Are not the public who say these things the
150	greatest of all Sophists?  And do they not educate to perfection young
150	and old, men and women alike, and fashion them after their own hearts?
150	
150	When is this accomplished? he said.
150	
150	When they meet together, and the world sits down at an assembly, or in
150	a court of law, or a theatre, or a camp, or in any other popular
150	resort, and there is a great uproar, and they praise some things which
150	are being said or done, and blame other things, equally exaggerating
150	both, shouting and clapping their hands, and the echo of the rocks and
150	the place in which they are assembled redoubles the sound of the praise
150	or blame--at such a time will not a young man's heart, as they say,
150	leap within him?  Will any private training enable him to stand firm
150	against the overwhelming flood of popular opinion? or will he be
150	carried away by the stream?  Will he not have the notions of good and
150	evil which the public in general have--he will do as they do, and as
150	they are, such will he be?
150	
150	Yes, Socrates; necessity will compel him.
150	
150	And yet, I said, there is a still greater necessity, which has not been
150	mentioned.
150	
150	What is that?
150	
150	The gentle force of attainder or confiscation or death which, as you
150	are aware, these new Sophists and educators who are the public, apply
150	when their words are powerless.
150	
150	Indeed they do; and in right good earnest.
150	
150	Now what opinion of any other Sophist, or of any private person, can be
150	expected to overcome in such an unequal contest?
150	
150	None, he replied.
150	
150	No, indeed, I said, even to make the attempt is a great piece of folly;
150	there neither is, nor has been, nor is ever likely to be, any different
150	type of character which has had no other training in virtue but that
150	which is supplied by public opinion--I speak, my friend, of human
150	virtue only; what is more than human, as the proverb says, is not
150	included:  for I would not have you ignorant that, in the present evil
150	state of governments, whatever is saved and comes to good is saved by
150	the power of God, as we may truly say.
150	
150	I quite assent, he replied.
150	
150	Then let me crave your assent also to a further observation.
150	
150	What are you going to say?
150	
150	Why, that all those mercenary individuals, whom the many call Sophists
150	and whom they deem to be their adversaries, do, in fact, teach nothing
150	but the opinion of the many, that is to say, the opinions of their
150	assemblies; and this is their wisdom.  I might compare them to a man
150	who should study the tempers and desires of a mighty strong beast who
150	is fed by him-he would learn how to approach and handle him, also at
150	what times and from what causes he is dangerous or the reverse, and
150	what is the meaning of his several cries, and by what sounds, when
150	another utters them, he is soothed or infuriated; and you may suppose
150	further, that when, by continually attending upon him, he has become
150	perfect in all this, he calls his knowledge wisdom, and makes of it a
150	system or art, which he proceeds to teach, although he has no real
150	notion of what he means by the principles or passions of which he is
150	speaking, but calls this honourable and that dishonourable, or good or
150	evil, or just or unjust, all in accordance with the tastes and tempers
150	of the great brute.  Good he pronounces to be that in which the beast
150	delights and evil to be that which he dislikes; and he can give no
150	other account of them except that the just and noble are the necessary,
150	having never himself seen, and having no power of explaining to others
150	the nature of either, or the difference between them, which is immense.
150	By heaven, would not such an one be a rare educator?
150	
150	Indeed, he would.
150	
150	And in what way does he who thinks that wisdom is the discernment of
150	the tempers and tastes of the motley multitude, whether in painting or
150	music, or, finally, in politics, differ from him whom I have been
150	describing?  For when a man consorts with the many, and exhibits to
150	them his poem or other work of art or the service which he has done the
150	State, making them his judges when he is not obliged, the so-called
150	necessity of Diomede will oblige him to produce whatever they praise.
150	And yet the reasons are utterly ludicrous which they give in
150	confirmation of their own notions about the honourable and good.  Did
150	you ever hear any of them which were not?
150	
150	No, nor am I likely to hear.
150	
150	You recognise the truth of what I have been saying?  Then let me ask
150	you to consider further whether the world will ever be induced to
150	believe in the existence of absolute beauty rather than of the many
150	beautiful, or of the absolute in each kind rather than of the many in
150	each kind?
150	
150	Certainly not.
150	
150	Then the world cannot possibly be a philosopher?
150	
150	Impossible.
150	
150	And therefore philosophers must inevitably fall under the censure of
150	the world?
150	
150	They must.
150	
150	And of individuals who consort with the mob and seek to please them?
150	
150	That is evident.
150	
150	Then, do you see any way in which the philosopher can be preserved in
150	his calling to the end? and remember what we were saying of him, that
150	he was to have quickness and memory and courage and magnificence--these
150	were admitted by us to be the true philosopher's gifts.
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	Will not such an one from his early childhood be in all things first
150	among all, especially if his bodily endowments are like his mental ones?
150	
150	Certainly, he said.
150	
150	And his friends and fellow-citizens will want to use him as he gets
150	older for their own purposes?
150	
150	No question.
150	
150	Falling at his feet, they will make requests to him and do him honour
150	and flatter him, because they want to get into their hands now, the
150	power which he will one day possess.
150	
150	That often happens, he said.
150	
150	And what will a man such as he be likely to do under such
150	circumstances, especially if he be a citizen of a great city, rich and
150	noble, and a tall proper youth?  Will he not be full of boundless
150	aspirations, and fancy himself able to manage the affairs of Hellenes
150	and of barbarians, and having got such notions into his head will he
150	not dilate and elevate himself in the fulness of vain pomp and
150	senseless pride?
150	
150	To be sure he will.
150	
150	Now, when he is in this state of mind, if some one gently comes to him
150	and tells him that he is a fool and must get understanding, which can
150	only be got by slaving for it, do you think that, under such adverse
150	circumstances, he will be easily induced to listen?
150	
150	Far otherwise.
150	
150	And even if there be some one who through inherent goodness or natural
150	reasonableness has had his eyes opened a little and is humbled and
150	taken captive by philosophy, how will his friends behave when they
150	think that they are likely to lose the advantage which they were hoping
150	to reap from his companionship?  Will they not do and say anything to
150	prevent him from yielding to his better nature and to render his
150	teacher powerless, using to this end private intrigues as well as
150	public prosecutions?
150	
150	There can be no doubt of it.
150	
150	And how can one who is thus circumstanced ever become a philosopher?
150	
150	Impossible.
150	
150	Then were we not right in saying that even the very qualities which
150	make a man a philosopher may, if he be ill-educated, divert him from
150	philosophy, no less than riches and their accompaniments and the other
150	so-called goods of life?
150	
150	We were quite right.
150	
150	Thus, my excellent friend, is brought about all that ruin and failure
150	which I have been describing of the natures best adapted to the best of
150	all pursuits; they are natures which we maintain to be rare at any
150	time; this being the class out of which come the men who are the
150	authors of the greatest evil to States and individuals; and also of the
150	greatest good when the tide carries them in that direction; but a small
150	man never was the doer of any great thing either to individuals or to
150	States.
150	
150	That is most true, he said.
150	
150	And so philosophy is left desolate, with her marriage rite incomplete:
150	for her own have fallen away and forsaken her, and while they are
150	leading a false and unbecoming life, other unworthy persons, seeing
150	that she has no kinsmen to be her protectors, enter in and dishonour
150	her; and fasten upon her the reproaches which, as you say, her
150	reprovers utter, who affirm of her votaries that some are good for
150	nothing, and that the greater number deserve the severest punishment.
150	
150	That is certainly what people say.
150	
150	Yes; and what else would you expect, I said, when you think of the puny
150	creatures who, seeing this land open to them--a land well stocked with
150	fair names and showy titles--like prisoners running out of prison into
150	a sanctuary, take a leap out of their trades into philosophy; those who
150	do so being probably the cleverest hands at their own miserable crafts?
150	For, although philosophy be in this evil case, still there remains a
150	dignity about her which is not to be found in the arts.  And many are
150	thus attracted by her whose natures are imperfect and whose souls are
150	maimed and disfigured by their meannesses, as their bodies are by their
150	trades and crafts.  Is not this unavoidable?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	Are they not exactly like a bald little tinker who has just got out of
150	durance and come into a fortune; he takes a bath and puts on a new
150	coat, and is decked out as a bridegroom going to marry his master's
150	daughter, who is left poor and desolate?
150	
150	A most exact parallel.
150	
150	What will be the issue of such marriages?  Will they not be vile and
150	bastard?
150	
150	There can be no question of it.
150	
150	And when persons who are unworthy of education approach philosophy and
150	make an alliance with her who is a rank above them what sort of ideas
150	and opinions are likely to be generated?  Will they not be sophisms
150	captivating to the ear, having nothing in them genuine, or worthy of or
150	akin to true wisdom?
150	
150	No doubt, he said.
150	
150	Then, Adeimantus, I said, the worthy disciples of philosophy will be
150	but a small remnant:  perchance some noble and well-educated person,
150	detained by exile in her service, who in the absence of corrupting
150	influences remains devoted to her; or some lofty soul born in a mean
150	city, the politics of which he contemns and neglects; and there may be
150	a gifted few who leave the arts, which they justly despise, and come to
150	her;--or peradventure there are some who are restrained by our friend
150	Theages' bridle; for everything in the life of Theages conspired to
150	divert him from philosophy; but ill-health kept him away from politics.
150	My own case of the internal sign is hardly worth mentioning, for
150	rarely, if ever, has such a monitor been given to any other man.  Those
150	who belong to this small class have tasted how sweet and blessed a
150	possession philosophy is, and have also seen enough of the madness of
150	the multitude; and they know that no politician is honest, nor is there
150	any champion of justice at whose side they may fight and be saved.
150	Such an one may be compared to a man who has fallen among wild
150	beasts--he will not join in the wickedness of his fellows, but neither
150	is he able singly to resist all their fierce natures, and therefore
150	seeing that he would be of no use to the State or to his friends, and
150	reflecting that he would have to throw away his life without doing any
150	good either to himself or others, he holds his peace, and goes his own
150	way.  He is like one who, in the storm of dust and sleet which the
150	driving wind hurries along, retires under the shelter of a wall; and
150	seeing the rest of mankind full of wickedness, he is content, if only
150	he can live his own life and be pure from evil or unrighteousness, and
150	depart in peace and good-will, with bright hopes.
150	
150	Yes, he said, and he will have done a great work before he departs.
150	
150	A great work--yes; but not the greatest, unless he find a State
150	suitable to him; for in a State which is suitable to him, he will have
150	a larger growth and be the saviour of his country, as well as of
150	himself.
150	
150	The causes why philosophy is in such an evil name have now been
150	sufficiently explained:  the injustice of the charges against her has
150	been shown-is there anything more which you wish to say?
150	
150	Nothing more on that subject, he replied; but I should like to know
150	which of the governments now existing is in your opinion the one
150	adapted to her.
150	
150	Not any of them, I said; and that is precisely the accusation which I
150	bring against them--not one of them is worthy of the philosophic
150	nature, and hence that nature is warped and estranged;--as the exotic
150	seed which is sown in a foreign land becomes denaturalized, and is wont
150	to be overpowered and to lose itself in the new soil, even so this
150	growth of philosophy, instead of persisting, degenerates and receives
150	another character.  But if philosophy ever finds in the State that
150	perfection which she herself is, then will be seen that she is in truth
150	divine, and that all other things, whether natures of men or
150	institutions, are but human;--and now, I know that you are going to
150	ask, what that State is.
150	
150	No, he said; there you are wrong, for I was going to ask another
150	question--whether it is the State of which we are the founders and
150	inventors, or some other?
150	
150	Yes, I replied, ours in most respects; but you may remember my saying
150	before, that some living authority would always be required in the
150	State having the same idea of the constitution which guided you when as
150	legislator you were laying down the laws.
150	
150	That was said, he replied.
150	
150	Yes, but not in a satisfactory manner; you frightened us by interposing
150	objections, which certainly showed that the discussion would be long
150	and difficult; and what still remains is the reverse of easy.
150	
150	What is there remaining?
150	
150	The question how the study of philosophy may be so ordered as not to be
150	the ruin of the State:  All great attempts are attended with risk;
150	'hard is the good,' as men say.
150	
150	Still, he said, let the point be cleared up, and the enquiry will then
150	be complete.
150	
150	I shall not be hindered, I said, by any want of will, but, if at all,
150	by a want of power:  my zeal you may see for yourselves; and please to
150	remark in what I am about to say how boldly and unhesitatingly I
150	declare that States should pursue philosophy, not as they do now, but
150	in a different spirit.
150	
150	In what manner?
150	
150	At present, I said, the students of philosophy are quite young;
150	beginning when they are hardly past childhood, they devote only the
150	time saved from moneymaking and housekeeping to such pursuits; and even
150	those of them who are reputed to have most of the philosophic spirit,
150	when they come within sight of the great difficulty of the subject, I
150	mean dialectic, take themselves off.  In after life when invited by
150	some one else, they may, perhaps, go and hear a lecture, and about this
150	they make much ado, for philosophy is not considered by them to be
150	their proper business:  at last, when they grow old, in most cases they
150	are extinguished more truly than Heracleitus' sun, inasmuch as they
150	never light up again.
150	
150	But what ought to be their course?
150	
150	Just the opposite.  In childhood and youth their study, and what
150	philosophy they learn, should be suited to their tender years: during
150	this period while they are growing up towards manhood, the chief and
150	special care should be given to their bodies that they may have them to
150	use in the service of philosophy; as life advances and the intellect
150	begins to mature, let them increase the gymnastics of the soul; but
150	when the strength of our citizens fails and is past civil and military
150	duties, then let them range at will and engage in no serious labour, as
150	we intend them to live happily here, and to crown this life with a
150	similar happiness in another.
150	
150	How truly in earnest you are, Socrates! he said; I am sure of that; and
150	yet most of your hearers, if I am not mistaken, are likely to be still
150	more earnest in their opposition to you, and will never be convinced;
150	Thrasymachus least of all.
150	
150	Do not make a quarrel, I said, between Thrasymachus and me, who have
150	recently become friends, although, indeed, we were never enemies; for I
150	shall go on striving to the utmost until I either convert him and other
150	men, or do something which may profit them against the day when they
150	live again, and hold the like discourse in another state of existence.
150	
150	You are speaking of a time which is not very near.
150	
150	Rather, I replied, of a time which is as nothing in comparison with
150	eternity.  Nevertheless, I do not wonder that the many refuse to
150	believe; for they have never seen that of which we are now speaking
150	realised; they have seen only a conventional imitation of philosophy,
150	consisting of words artificially brought together, not like these of
150	ours having a natural unity.  But a human being who in word and work is
150	perfectly moulded, as far as he can be, into the proportion and
150	likeness of virtue--such a man ruling in a city which bears the same
150	image, they have never yet seen, neither one nor many of them--do you
150	think that they ever did?
150	
150	No indeed.
150	
150	No, my friend, and they have seldom, if ever, heard free and noble
150	sentiments; such as men utter when they are earnestly and by every
150	means in their power seeking after truth for the sake of knowledge,
150	while they look coldly on the subtleties of controversy, of which the
150	end is opinion and strife, whether they meet with them in the courts of
150	law or in society.
150	
150	They are strangers, he said, to the words of which you speak.
150	
150	And this was what we foresaw, and this was the reason why truth forced
150	us to admit, not without fear and hesitation, that neither cities nor
150	States nor individuals will ever attain perfection until the small
150	class of philosophers whom we termed useless but not corrupt are
150	providentially compelled, whether they will or not, to take care of the
150	State, and until a like necessity be laid on the State to obey them; or
150	until kings, or if not kings, the sons of kings or princes, are
150	divinely inspired with a true love of true philosophy.  That either or
150	both of these alternatives are impossible, I see no reason to affirm:
150	if they were so, we might indeed be justly ridiculed as dreamers and
150	visionaries.  Am I not right?
150	
150	Quite right.
150	
150	If then, in the countless ages of the past, or at the present hour in
150	some foreign clime which is far away and beyond our ken, the perfected
150	philosopher is or has been or hereafter shall be compelled by a
150	superior power to have the charge of the State, we are ready to assert
150	to the death, that this our constitution has been, and is--yea, and
150	will be whenever the Muse of Philosophy is queen.  There is no
150	impossibility in all this; that there is a difficulty, we acknowledge
150	ourselves.
150	
150	My opinion agrees with yours, he said.
150	
150	But do you mean to say that this is not the opinion of the multitude?
150	
150	I should imagine not, he replied.
150	
150	O my friend, I said, do not attack the multitude:  they will change
150	their minds, if, not in an aggressive spirit, but gently and with the
150	view of soothing them and removing their dislike of over-education, you
150	show them your philosophers as they really are and describe as you were
150	just now doing their character and profession, and then mankind will
150	see that he of whom you are speaking is not such as they supposed--if
150	they view him in this new light, they will surely change their notion
150	of him, and answer in another strain.  Who can be at enmity with one
150	who loves them, who that is himself gentle and free from envy will be
150	jealous of one in whom there is no jealousy?  Nay, let me answer for
150	you, that in a few this harsh temper may be found but not in the
150	majority of mankind.
150	
150	I quite agree with you, he said.
150	
150	And do you not also think, as I do, that the harsh feeling which the
150	many entertain towards philosophy originates in the pretenders, who
150	rush in uninvited, and are always abusing them, and finding fault with
150	them, who make persons instead of things the theme of their
150	conversation? and nothing can be more unbecoming in philosophers than
150	this.
150	
150	It is most unbecoming.
150	
150	For he, Adeimantus, whose mind is fixed upon true being, has surely no
150	time to look down upon the affairs of earth, or to be filled with
150	malice and envy, contending against men; his eye is ever directed
150	towards things fixed and immutable, which he sees neither injuring nor
150	injured by one another, but all in order moving according to reason;
150	these he imitates, and to these he will, as far as he can, conform
150	himself.  Can a man help imitating that with which he holds reverential
150	converse?
150	
150	Impossible.
150	
150	And the philosopher holding converse with the divine order, becomes
150	orderly and divine, as far as the nature of man allows; but like every
150	one else, he will suffer from detraction.
150	
150	Of course.
150	
150	And if a necessity be laid upon him of fashioning, not only himself,
150	but human nature generally, whether in States or individuals, into that
150	which he beholds elsewhere, will he, think you, be an unskilful
150	artificer of justice, temperance, and every civil virtue?
150	
150	Anything but unskilful.
150	
150	And if the world perceives that what we are saying about him is the
150	truth, will they be angry with philosophy?  Will they disbelieve us,
150	when we tell them that no State can be happy which is not designed by
150	artists who imitate the heavenly pattern?
150	
150	They will not be angry if they understand, he said.  But how will they
150	draw out the plan of which you are speaking?
150	
150	They will begin by taking the State and the manners of men, from which,
150	as from a tablet, they will rub out the picture, and leave a clean
150	surface.  This is no easy task.  But whether easy or not, herein will
150	lie the difference between them and every other legislator,--they will
150	have nothing to do either with individual or State, and will inscribe
150	no laws, until they have either found, or themselves made, a clean
150	surface.
150	
150	They will be very right, he said.
150	
150	Having effected this, they will proceed to trace an outline of the
150	constitution?
150	
150	No doubt.
150	
150	And when they are filling in the work, as I conceive, they will often
150	turn their eyes upwards and downwards:  I mean that they will first
150	look at absolute justice and beauty and temperance, and again at the
150	human copy; and will mingle and temper the various elements of life
150	into the image of a man; and thus they will conceive according to that
150	other image, which, when existing among men, Homer calls the form and
150	likeness of God.
150	
150	Very true, he said.
150	
150	And one feature they will erase, and another they will put in, they
150	have made the ways of men, as far as possible, agreeable to the ways of
150	God?
150	
150	Indeed, he said, in no way could they make a fairer picture.
150	
150	And now, I said, are we beginning to persuade those whom you described
150	as rushing at us with might and main, that the painter of constitutions
150	is such an one as we are praising; at whom they were so very indignant
150	because to his hands we committed the State; and are they growing a
150	little calmer at what they have just heard?
150	
150	Much calmer, if there is any sense in them.
150	
150	Why, where can they still find any ground for objection?  Will they
150	doubt that the philosopher is a lover of truth and being?
150	
150	They would not be so unreasonable.
150	
150	Or that his nature, being such as we have delineated, is akin to the
150	highest good?
150	
150	Neither can they doubt this.
150	
150	But again, will they tell us that such a nature, placed under
150	favourable circumstances, will not be perfectly good and wise if any
150	ever was?  Or will they prefer those whom we have rejected?
150	
150	Surely not.
150	
150	Then will they still be angry at our saying, that, until philosophers
150	bear rule, States and individuals will have no rest from evil, nor will
150	this our imaginary State ever be realised?
150	
150	I think that they will be less angry.
150	
150	Shall we assume that they are not only less angry but quite gentle, and
150	that they have been converted and for very shame, if for no other
150	reason, cannot refuse to come to terms?
150	
150	By all means, he said.
150	
150	Then let us suppose that the reconciliation has been effected.  Will
150	any one deny the other point, that there may be sons of kings or
150	princes who are by nature philosophers?
150	
150	Surely no man, he said.
150	
150	And when they have come into being will any one say that they must of
150	necessity be destroyed; that they can hardly be saved is not denied
150	even by us; but that in the whole course of ages no single one of them
150	can escape--who will venture to affirm this?
150	
150	Who indeed!
150	
150	But, said I, one is enough; let there be one man who has a city
150	obedient to his will, and he might bring into existence the ideal
150	polity about which the world is so incredulous.
150	
150	Yes, one is enough.
150	
150	The ruler may impose the laws and institutions which we have been
150	describing, and the citizens may possibly be willing to obey them?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	And that others should approve of what we approve, is no miracle or
150	impossibility?
150	
150	I think not.
150	
150	But we have sufficiently shown, in what has preceded, that all this, if
150	only possible, is assuredly for the best.
150	
150	We have.
150	
150	And now we say not only that our laws, if they could be enacted, would
150	be for the best, but also that the enactment of them, though difficult,
150	is not impossible.
150	
150	Very good.
150	
150	And so with pain and toil we have reached the end of one subject, but
150	more remains to be discussed;--how and by what studies and pursuits
150	will the saviours of the constitution be created, and at what ages are
150	they to apply themselves to their several studies?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	I omitted the troublesome business of the possession of women, and the
150	procreation of children, and the appointment of the rulers, because I
150	knew that the perfect State would be eyed with jealousy and was
150	difficult of attainment; but that piece of cleverness was not of much
150	service to me, for I had to discuss them all the same.  The women and
150	children are now disposed of, but the other question of the rulers must
150	be investigated from the very beginning.  We were saying, as you will
150	remember, that they were to be lovers of their country, tried by the
150	test of pleasures and pains, and neither in hardships, nor in dangers,
150	nor at any other critical moment were to lose their patriotism--he was
150	to be rejected who failed, but he who always came forth pure, like gold
150	tried in the refiner's fire, was to be made a ruler, and to receive
150	honours and rewards in life and after death.  This was the sort of
150	thing which was being said, and then the argument turned aside and
150	veiled her face; not liking to stir the question which has now arisen.
150	
150	I perfectly remember, he said.
150	
150	Yes, my friend, I said, and I then shrank from hazarding the bold word;
150	but now let me dare to say--that the perfect guardian must be a
150	philosopher.
150	
150	Yes, he said, let that be affirmed.
150	
150	And do not suppose that there will be many of them; for the gifts which
150	were deemed by us to be essential rarely grow together; they are mostly
150	found in shreds and patches.
150	
150	What do you mean? he said.
150	
150	You are aware, I replied, that quick intelligence, memory, sagacity,
150	cleverness, and similar qualities, do not often grow together, and that
150	persons who possess them and are at the same time high-spirited and
150	magnanimous are not so constituted by nature as to live orderly and in
150	a peaceful and settled manner; they are driven any way by their
150	impulses, and all solid principle goes out of them.
150	
150	Very true, he said.
150	
150	On the other hand, those steadfast natures which can better be depended
150	upon, which in a battle are impregnable to fear and immovable, are
150	equally immovable when there is anything to be learned; they are always
150	in a torpid state, and are apt to yawn and go to sleep over any
150	intellectual toil.
150	
150	Quite true.
150	
150	And yet we were saying that both qualities were necessary in those to
150	whom the higher education is to be imparted, and who are to share in
150	any office or command.
150	
150	Certainly, he said.
150	
150	And will they be a class which is rarely found?
150	
150	Yes, indeed.
150	
150	Then the aspirant must not only be tested in those labours and dangers
150	and pleasures which we mentioned before, but there is another kind of
150	probation which we did not mention--he must be exercised also in many
150	kinds of knowledge, to see whether the soul will be able to endure the
150	highest of all, will faint under them, as in any other studies and
150	exercises.
150	
150	Yes, he said, you are quite right in testing him.  But what do you mean
150	by the highest of all knowledge?
150	
150	You may remember, I said, that we divided the soul into three parts;
150	and distinguished the several natures of justice, temperance, courage,
150	and wisdom?
150	
150	Indeed, he said, if I had forgotten, I should not deserve to hear more.
150	
150	And do you remember the word of caution which preceded the discussion
150	of them?
150	
150	To what do you refer?
150	
150	We were saying, if I am not mistaken, that he who wanted to see them in
150	their perfect beauty must take a longer and more circuitous way, at the
150	end of which they would appear; but that we could add on a popular
150	exposition of them on a level with the discussion which had preceded.
150	And you replied that such an exposition would be enough for you, and so
150	the enquiry was continued in what to me seemed to be a very inaccurate
150	manner; whether you were satisfied or not, it is for you to say.
150	
150	Yes, he said, I thought and the others thought that you gave us a fair
150	measure of truth.
150	
150	But, my friend, I said, a measure of such things Which in any degree
150	falls short of the whole truth is not fair measure; for nothing
150	imperfect is the measure of anything, although persons are too apt to
150	be contented and think that they need search no further.
150	
150	Not an uncommon case when people are indolent.
150	
150	Yes, I said; and there cannot be any worse fault in a guardian of the
150	State and of the laws.
150	
150	True.
150	
150	The guardian then, I said, must be required to take the longer circuit,
150	and toll at learning as well as at gymnastics, or he will never reach
150	the highest knowledge of all which, as we were just now saying, is his
150	proper calling.
150	
150	What, he said, is there a knowledge still higher than this--higher than
150	justice and the other virtues?
150	
150	Yes, I said, there is.  And of the virtues too we must behold not the
150	outline merely, as at present--nothing short of the most finished
150	picture should satisfy us.  When little things are elaborated with an
150	infinity of pains, in order that they may appear in their full beauty
150	and utmost clearness, how ridiculous that we should not think the
150	highest truths worthy of attaining the highest accuracy!
150	
150	A right noble thought; but do you suppose that we shall refrain from
150	asking you what is this highest knowledge?
150	
150	Nay, I said, ask if you will; but I am certain that you have heard the
150	answer many times, and now you either do not understand me or, as I
150	rather think, you are disposed to be troublesome; for you have of been
150	told that the idea of good is the highest knowledge, and that all other
150	things become useful and advantageous only by their use of this.  You
150	can hardly be ignorant that of this I was about to speak, concerning
150	which, as you have often heard me say, we know so little; and, without
150	which, any other knowledge or possession of any kind will profit us
150	nothing.  Do you think that the possession of all other things is of
150	any value if we do not possess the good? or the knowledge of all other
150	things if we have no knowledge of beauty and goodness?
150	
150	Assuredly not.
150	
150	You are further aware that most people affirm pleasure to be the good,
150	but the finer sort of wits say it is knowledge
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And you are aware too that the latter cannot explain what they mean by
150	knowledge, but are obliged after all to say knowledge of the good?
150	
150	How ridiculous!
150	
150	Yes, I said, that they should begin by reproaching us with our
150	ignorance of the good, and then presume our knowledge of it--for the
150	good they define to be knowledge of the good, just as if we understood
150	them when they use the term 'good'--this is of course ridiculous.
150	
150	Most true, he said.
150	
150	And those who make pleasure their good are in equal perplexity; for
150	they are compelled to admit that there are bad pleasures as well as
150	good.
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	And therefore to acknowledge that bad and good are the same?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	There can be no doubt about the numerous difficulties in which this
150	question is involved.
150	
150	There can be none.
150	
150	Further, do we not see that many are willing to do or to have or to
150	seem to be what is just and honourable without the reality; but no one
150	is satisfied with the appearance of good--the reality is what they
150	seek; in the case of the good, appearance is despised by every one.
150	
150	Very true, he said.
150	
150	Of this then, which every soul of man pursues and makes the end of all
150	his actions, having a presentiment that there is such an end, and yet
150	hesitating because neither knowing the nature nor having the same
150	assurance of this as of other things, and therefore losing whatever
150	good there is in other things,--of a principle such and so great as
150	this ought the best men in our State, to whom everything is entrusted,
150	to be in the darkness of ignorance?
150	
150	Certainly not, he said.
150	
150	I am sure, I said, that he who does not know now the beautiful and the
150	just are likewise good will be but a sorry guardian of them; and I
150	suspect that no one who is ignorant of the good will have a true
150	knowledge of them.
150	
150	That, he said, is a shrewd suspicion of yours.
150	
150	And if we only have a guardian who has this knowledge our State will be
150	perfectly ordered?
150	
150	Of course, he replied; but I wish that you would tell me whether you
150	conceive this supreme principle of the good to be knowledge or
150	pleasure, or different from either.
150	
150	Aye, I said, I knew all along that a fastidious gentleman like you
150	would not be contented with the thoughts of other people about these
150	matters.
150	
150	True, Socrates; but I must say that one who like you has passed a
150	lifetime in the study of philosophy should not be always repeating the
150	opinions of others, and never telling his own.
150	
150	Well, but has any one a right to say positively what he does not know?
150	
150	Not, he said, with the assurance of positive certainty; he has no right
150	to do that:  but he may say what he thinks, as a matter of opinion.
150	
150	And do you not know, I said, that all mere opinions are bad, and the
150	best of them blind?  You would not deny that those who have any true
150	notion without intelligence are only like blind men who feel their way
150	along the road?
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	And do you wish to behold what is blind and crooked and base, when
150	others will tell you of brightness and beauty?
150	
150	GLAUCON - SOCRATES
150	
150	Still, I must implore you, Socrates, said Glaucon, not to turn away
150	just as you are reaching the goal; if you will only give such an
150	explanation of the good as you have already given of justice and
150	temperance and the other virtues, we shall be satisfied.
150	
150	Yes, my friend, and I shall be at least equally satisfied, but I cannot
150	help fearing that I shall fall, and that my indiscreet zeal will bring
150	ridicule upon me.  No, sweet sirs, let us not at present ask what is
150	the actual nature of the good, for to reach what is now in my thoughts
150	would be an effort too great for me.  But of the child of the good who
150	is likest him, I would fain speak, if I could be sure that you wished
150	to hear--otherwise, not.
150	
150	By all means, he said, tell us about the child, and you shall remain in
150	our debt for the account of the parent.
150	
150	I do indeed wish, I replied, that I could pay, and you receive, the
150	account of the parent, and not, as now, of the offspring only; take,
150	however, this latter by way of interest, and at the same time have a
150	care that i do not render a false account, although I have no intention
150	of deceiving you.
150	
150	Yes, we will take all the care that we can:  proceed.
150	
150	Yes, I said, but I must first come to an understanding with you, and
150	remind you of what I have mentioned in the course of this discussion,
150	and at many other times.
150	
150	What?
150	
150	The old story, that there is a many beautiful and a many good, and so
150	of other things which we describe and define; to all of them 'many' is
150	applied.
150	
150	True, he said.
150	
150	And there is an absolute beauty and an absolute good, and of other
150	things to which the term 'many' is applied there is an absolute; for
150	they may be brought under a single idea, which is called the essence of
150	each.
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	The many, as we say, are seen but not known, and the ideas are known
150	but not seen.
150	
150	Exactly.
150	
150	And what is the organ with which we see the visible things?
150	
150	The sight, he said.
150	
150	And with the hearing, I said, we hear, and with the other senses
150	perceive the other objects of sense?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	But have you remarked that sight is by far the most costly and complex
150	piece of workmanship which the artificer of the senses ever contrived?
150	
150	No, I never have, he said.
150	
150	Then reflect; has the ear or voice need of any third or additional
150	nature in order that the one may be able to hear and the other to be
150	heard?
150	
150	Nothing of the sort.
150	
150	No, indeed, I replied; and the same is true of most, if not all, the
150	other senses--you would not say that any of them requires such an
150	addition?
150	
150	Certainly not.
150	
150	But you see that without the addition of some other nature there is no
150	seeing or being seen?
150	
150	How do you mean?
150	
150	Sight being, as I conceive, in the eyes, and he who has eyes wanting to
150	see; colour being also present in them, still unless there be a third
150	nature specially adapted to the purpose, the owner of the eyes will see
150	nothing and the colours will be invisible.
150	
150	Of what nature are you speaking?
150	
150	Of that which you term light, I replied.
150	
150	True, he said.
150	
150	Noble, then, is the bond which links together sight and visibility, and
150	great beyond other bonds by no small difference of nature; for light is
150	their bond, and light is no ignoble thing?
150	
150	Nay, he said, the reverse of ignoble.
150	
150	And which, I said, of the gods in heaven would you say was the lord of
150	this element?  Whose is that light which makes the eye to see perfectly
150	and the visible to appear?
150	
150	You mean the sun, as you and all mankind say.
150	
150	May not the relation of sight to this deity be described as follows?
150	
150	How?
150	
150	Neither sight nor the eye in which sight resides is the sun?
150	
150	No.
150	
150	Yet of all the organs of sense the eye is the most like the sun?
150	
150	By far the most like.
150	
150	And the power which the eye possesses is a sort of effluence which is
150	dispensed from the sun?
150	
150	Exactly.
150	
150	Then the sun is not sight, but the author of sight who is recognised by
150	sight.
150	
150	True, he said.
150	
150	And this is he whom I call the child of the good, whom the good begat
150	in his own likeness, to be in the visible world, in relation to sight
150	and the things of sight, what the good is in the intellectual world in
150	relation to mind and the things of mind.
150	
150	Will you be a little more explicit? he said.
150	
150	Why, you know, I said, that the eyes, when a person directs them
150	towards objects on which the light of day is no longer shining, but the
150	moon and stars only, see dimly, and are nearly blind; they seem to have
150	no clearness of vision in them?
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	But when they are directed towards objects on which the sun shines,
150	they see clearly and there is sight in them?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	And the soul is like the eye:  when resting upon that on which truth
150	and being shine, the soul perceives and understands and is radiant with
150	intelligence; but when turned towards the twilight of becoming and
150	perishing, then she has opinion only, and goes blinking about, and is
150	first of one opinion and then of another, and seems to have no
150	intelligence?
150	
150	Just so.
150	
150	Now, that which imparts truth to the known and the power of knowing to
150	the knower is what I would have you term the idea of good, and this you
150	will deem to be the cause of science, and of truth in so far as the
150	latter becomes the subject of knowledge; beautiful too, as are both
150	truth and knowledge, you will be right in esteeming this other nature
150	as more beautiful than either; and, as in the previous instance, light
150	and sight may be truly said to be like the sun, and yet not to be the
150	sun, so in this other sphere, science and truth may be deemed to be
150	like the good, but not the good; the good has a place of honour yet
150	higher.
150	
150	What a wonder of beauty that must be, he said, which is the author of
150	science and truth, and yet surpasses them in beauty; for you surely
150	cannot mean to say that pleasure is the good?
150	
150	God forbid, I replied; but may I ask you to consider the image in
150	another point of view?
150	
150	In what point of view?
150	
150	You would say, would you not, that the sun is only the author of
150	visibility in all visible things, but of generation and nourishment and
150	growth, though he himself is not generation?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	In like manner the good may be said to be not only the author of
