150	knowledge to all things known, but of their being and essence, and yet
150	the good is not essence, but far exceeds essence in dignity and power.
150	
150	Glaucon said, with a ludicrous earnestness:  By the light of heaven,
150	how amazing!
150	
150	Yes, I said, and the exaggeration may be set down to you; for you made
150	me utter my fancies.
150	
150	And pray continue to utter them; at any rate let us hear if there is
150	anything more to be said about the similitude of the sun.
150	
150	Yes, I said, there is a great deal more.
150	
150	Then omit nothing, however slight.
150	
150	I will do my best, I said; but I should think that a great deal will
150	have to be omitted.
150	
150	You have to imagine, then, that there are two ruling powers, and that
150	one of them is set over the intellectual world, the other over the
150	visible.  I do not say heaven, lest you should fancy that I am playing
150	upon the name ('ourhanoz, orhatoz'). May I suppose that you have this
150	distinction of the visible and intelligible fixed in your mind?
150	
150	I have.
150	
150	Now take a line which has been cut into two unequal parts, and divide
150	each of them again in the same proportion, and suppose the two main
150	divisions to answer, one to the visible and the other to the
150	intelligible, and then compare the subdivisions in respect of their
150	clearness and want of clearness, and you will find that the first
150	section in the sphere of the visible consists of images.  And by images
150	I mean, in the first place, shadows, and in the second place,
150	reflections in water and in solid, smooth and polished bodies and the
150	like:  Do you understand?
150	
150	Yes, I understand.
150	
150	Imagine, now, the other section, of which this is only the resemblance,
150	to include the animals which we see, and everything that grows or is
150	made.
150	
150	Very good.
150	
150	Would you not admit that both the sections of this division have
150	different degrees of truth, and that the copy is to the original as the
150	sphere of opinion is to the sphere of knowledge?
150	
150	Most undoubtedly.
150	
150	Next proceed to consider the manner in which the sphere of the
150	intellectual is to be divided.
150	
150	In what manner?
150	
150	Thus:--There are two subdivisions, in the lower or which the soul uses
150	the figures given by the former division as images; the enquiry can
150	only be hypothetical, and instead of going upwards to a principle
150	descends to the other end; in the higher of the two, the soul passes
150	out of hypotheses, and goes up to a principle which is above
150	hypotheses, making no use of images as in the former case, but
150	proceeding only in and through the ideas themselves.
150	
150	I do not quite understand your meaning, he said.
150	
150	Then I will try again; you will understand me better when I have made
150	some preliminary remarks.  You are aware that students of geometry,
150	arithmetic, and the kindred sciences assume the odd and the even and
150	the figures and three kinds of angles and the like in their several
150	branches of science; these are their hypotheses, which they and
150	everybody are supposed to know, and therefore they do not deign to give
150	any account of them either to themselves or others; but they begin with
150	them, and go on until they arrive at last, and in a consistent manner,
150	at their conclusion?
150	
150	Yes, he said, I know.
150	
150	And do you not know also that although they make use of the visible
150	forms and reason about them, they are thinking not of these, but of the
150	ideals which they resemble; not of the figures which they draw, but of
150	the absolute square and the absolute diameter, and so on--the forms
150	which they draw or make, and which have shadows and reflections in
150	water of their own, are converted by them into images, but they are
150	really seeking to behold the things themselves, which can only be seen
150	with the eye of the mind?
150	
150	That is true.
150	
150	And of this kind I spoke as the intelligible, although in the search
150	after it the soul is compelled to use hypotheses; not ascending to a
150	first principle, because she is unable to rise above the region of
150	hypothesis, but employing the objects of which the shadows below are
150	resemblances in their turn as images, they having in relation to the
150	shadows and reflections of them a greater distinctness, and therefore a
150	higher value.
150	
150	I understand, he said, that you are speaking of the province of
150	geometry and the sister arts.
150	
150	And when I speak of the other division of the intelligible, you will
150	understand me to speak of that other sort of knowledge which reason
150	herself attains by the power of dialectic, using the hypotheses not as
150	first principles, but only as hypotheses--that is to say, as steps and
150	points of departure into a world which is above hypotheses, in order
150	that she may soar beyond them to the first principle of the whole; and
150	clinging to this and then to that which depends on this, by successive
150	steps she descends again without the aid of any sensible object, from
150	ideas, through ideas, and in ideas she ends.
150	
150	I understand you, he replied; not perfectly, for you seem to me to be
150	describing a task which is really tremendous; but, at any rate, I
150	understand you to say that knowledge and being, which the science of
150	dialectic contemplates, are clearer than the notions of the arts, as
150	they are termed, which proceed from hypotheses only: these are also
150	contemplated by the understanding, and not by the senses:  yet, because
150	they start from hypotheses and do not ascend to a principle, those who
150	contemplate them appear to you not to exercise the higher reason upon
150	them, although when a first principle is added to them they are
150	cognizable by the higher reason.  And the habit which is concerned with
150	geometry and the cognate sciences I suppose that you would term
150	understanding and not reason, as being intermediate between opinion and
150	reason.
150	
150	You have quite conceived my meaning, I said; and now, corresponding to
150	these four divisions, let there be four faculties in the soul-reason
150	answering to the highest, understanding to the second, faith (or
150	conviction) to the third, and perception of shadows to the last-and let
150	there be a scale of them, and let us suppose that the several faculties
150	have clearness in the same degree that their objects have truth.
150	
150	I understand, he replied, and give my assent, and accept your
150	arrangement.
150	
150	
150	
150	
150	BOOK VII
150	
150	
150	SOCRATES - GLAUCON
150	
150	AND now, I said, let me show in a figure how far our nature is
150	enlightened or unenlightened:--Behold! human beings living in a
150	underground den, which has a mouth open towards the light and reaching
150	all along the den; here they have been from their childhood, and have
150	their legs and necks chained so that they cannot move, and can only see
150	before them, being prevented by the chains from turning round their
150	heads.  Above and behind them a fire is blazing at a distance, and
150	between the fire and the prisoners there is a raised way; and you will
150	see, if you look, a low wall built along the way, like the screen which
150	marionette players have in front of them, over which they show the
150	puppets.
150	
150	I see.
150	
150	And do you see, I said, men passing along the wall carrying all sorts
150	of vessels, and statues and figures of animals made of wood and stone
150	and various materials, which appear over the wall?  Some of them are
150	talking, others silent.
150	
150	You have shown me a strange image, and they are strange prisoners.
150	
150	Like ourselves, I replied; and they see only their own shadows, or the
150	shadows of one another, which the fire throws on the opposite wall of
150	the cave?
150	
150	True, he said; how could they see anything but the shadows if they were
150	never allowed to move their heads?
150	
150	And of the objects which are being carried in like manner they would
150	only see the shadows?
150	
150	Yes, he said.
150	
150	And if they were able to converse with one another, would they not
150	suppose that they were naming what was actually before them?
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	And suppose further that the prison had an echo which came from the
150	other side, would they not be sure to fancy when one of the passers-by
150	spoke that the voice which they heard came from the passing shadow?
150	
150	No question, he replied.
150	
150	To them, I said, the truth would be literally nothing but the shadows
150	of the images.
150	
150	That is certain.
150	
150	And now look again, and see what will naturally follow it' the
150	prisoners are released and disabused of their error.  At first, when
150	any of them is liberated and compelled suddenly to stand up and turn
150	his neck round and walk and look towards the light, he will suffer
150	sharp pains; the glare will distress him, and he will be unable to see
150	the realities of which in his former state he had seen the shadows; and
150	then conceive some one saying to him, that what he saw before was an
150	illusion, but that now, when he is approaching nearer to being and his
150	eye is turned towards more real existence, he has a clearer vision,
150	what will be his reply?  And you may further imagine that his
150	instructor is pointing to the objects as they pass and requiring him to
150	name them,--will he not be perplexed?  Will he not fancy that the
150	shadows which he formerly saw are truer than the objects which are now
150	shown to him?
150	
150	Far truer.
150	
150	And if he is compelled to look straight at the light, will he not have
150	a pain in his eyes which will make him turn away to take and take in
150	the objects of vision which he can see, and which he will conceive to
150	be in reality clearer than the things which are now being shown to him?
150	
150	True, he now
150	
150	And suppose once more, that he is reluctantly dragged up a steep and
150	rugged ascent, and held fast until he's forced into the presence of the
150	sun himself, is he not likely to be pained and irritated?  When he
150	approaches the light his eyes will be dazzled, and he will not be able
150	to see anything at all of what are now called realities.
150	
150	Not all in a moment, he said.
150	
150	He will require to grow accustomed to the sight of the upper world.
150	And first he will see the shadows best, next the reflections of men and
150	other objects in the water, and then the objects themselves; then he
150	will gaze upon the light of the moon and the stars and the spangled
150	heaven; and he will see the sky and the stars by night better than the
150	sun or the light of the sun by day?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	Last of he will be able to see the sun, and not mere reflections of him
150	in the water, but he will see him in his own proper place, and not in
150	another; and he will contemplate him as he is.
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	He will then proceed to argue that this is he who gives the season and
150	the years, and is the guardian of all that is in the visible world, and
150	in a certain way the cause of all things which he and his fellows have
150	been accustomed to behold?
150	
150	Clearly, he said, he would first see the sun and then reason about him.
150	
150	And when he remembered his old habitation, and the wisdom of the den
150	and his fellow-prisoners, do you not suppose that he would felicitate
150	himself on the change, and pity them?
150	
150	Certainly, he would.
150	
150	And if they were in the habit of conferring honours among themselves on
150	those who were quickest to observe the passing shadows and to remark
150	which of them went before, and which followed after, and which were
150	together; and who were therefore best able to draw conclusions as to
150	the future, do you think that he would care for such honours and
150	glories, or envy the possessors of them?  Would he not say with Homer,
150	
150	    Better to be the poor servant of a poor master,
150	
150	and to endure anything, rather than think as they do and live after
150	their manner?
150	
150	Yes, he said, I think that he would rather suffer anything than
150	entertain these false notions and live in this miserable manner.
150	
150	Imagine once more, I said, such an one coming suddenly out of the sun
150	to be replaced in his old situation; would he not be certain to have
150	his eyes full of darkness?
150	
150	To be sure, he said.
150	
150	And if there were a contest, and he had to compete in measuring the
150	shadows with the prisoners who had never moved out of the den, while
150	his sight was still weak, and before his eyes had become steady (and
150	the time which would be needed to acquire this new habit of sight might
150	be very considerable) would he not be ridiculous?  Men would say of him
150	that up he went and down he came without his eyes; and that it was
150	better not even to think of ascending; and if any one tried to loose
150	another and lead him up to the light, let them only catch the offender,
150	and they would put him to death.
150	
150	No question, he said.
150	
150	This entire allegory, I said, you may now append, dear Glaucon, to the
150	previous argument; the prison-house is the world of sight, the light of
150	the fire is the sun, and you will not misapprehend me if you interpret
150	the journey upwards to be the ascent of the soul into the intellectual
150	world according to my poor belief, which, at your desire, I have
150	expressed whether rightly or wrongly God knows.  But, whether true or
150	false, my opinion is that in the world of knowledge the idea of good
150	appears last of all, and is seen only with an effort; and, when seen,
150	is also inferred to be the universal author of all things beautiful and
150	right, parent of light and of the lord of light in this visible world,
150	and the immediate source of reason and truth in the intellectual; and
150	that this is the power upon which he who would act rationally, either
150	in public or private life must have his eye fixed.
150	
150	I agree, he said, as far as I am able to understand you.
150	
150	Moreover, I said, you must not wonder that those who attain to this
150	beatific vision are unwilling to descend to human affairs; for their
150	souls are ever hastening into the upper world where they desire to
150	dwell; which desire of theirs is very natural, if our allegory may be
150	trusted.
150	
150	Yes, very natural.
150	
150	And is there anything surprising in one who passes from divine
150	contemplations to the evil state of man, misbehaving himself in a
150	ridiculous manner; if, while his eyes are blinking and before he has
150	become accustomed to the surrounding darkness, he is compelled to fight
150	in courts of law, or in other places, about the images or the shadows
150	of images of justice, and is endeavouring to meet the conceptions of
150	those who have never yet seen absolute justice?
150	
150	Anything but surprising, he replied.
150	
150	Any one who has common sense will remember that the bewilderments of
150	the eyes are of two kinds, and arise from two causes, either from
150	coming out of the light or from going into the light, which is true of
150	the mind's eye, quite as much as of the bodily eye; and he who
150	remembers this when he sees any one whose vision is perplexed and weak,
150	will not be too ready to laugh; he will first ask whether that soul of
150	man has come out of the brighter light, and is unable to see because
150	unaccustomed to the dark, or having turned from darkness to the day is
150	dazzled by excess of light.  And he will count the one happy in his
150	condition and state of being, and he will pity the other; or, if he
150	have a mind to laugh at the soul which comes from below into the light,
150	there will be more reason in this than in the laugh which greets him
150	who returns from above out of the light into the den.
150	
150	That, he said, is a very just distinction.
150	
150	But then, if I am right, certain professors of education must be wrong
150	when they say that they can put a knowledge into the soul which was not
150	there before, like sight into blind eyes.
150	
150	They undoubtedly say this, he replied.
150	
150	Whereas, our argument shows that the power and capacity of learning
150	exists in the soul already; and that just as the eye was unable to turn
150	from darkness to light without the whole body, so too the instrument of
150	knowledge can only by the movement of the whole soul be turned from the
150	world of becoming into that of being, and learn by degrees to endure
150	the sight of being, and of the brightest and best of being, or in other
150	words, of the good.
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	And must there not be some art which will effect conversion in the
150	easiest and quickest manner; not implanting the faculty of sight, for
150	that exists already, but has been turned in the wrong direction, and is
150	looking away from the truth?
150	
150	Yes, he said, such an art may be presumed.
150	
150	And whereas the other so-called virtues of the soul seem to be akin to
150	bodily qualities, for even when they are not originally innate they can
150	be implanted later by habit and exercise, the of wisdom more than
150	anything else contains a divine element which always remains, and by
150	this conversion is rendered useful and profitable; or, on the other
150	hand, hurtful and useless.  Did you never observe the narrow
150	intelligence flashing from the keen eye of a clever rogue--how eager he
150	is, how clearly his paltry soul sees the way to his end; he is the
150	reverse of blind, but his keen eyesight is forced into the service of
150	evil, and he is mischievous in proportion to his cleverness.
150	
150	Very true, he said.
150	
150	But what if there had been a circumcision of such natures in the days
150	of their youth; and they had been severed from those sensual pleasures,
150	such as eating and drinking, which, like leaden weights, were attached
150	to them at their birth, and which drag them down and turn the vision of
150	their souls upon the things that are below--if, I say, they had been
150	released from these impediments and turned in the opposite direction,
150	the very same faculty in them would have seen the truth as keenly as
150	they see what their eyes are turned to now.
150	
150	Very likely.
150	
150	Yes, I said; and there is another thing which is likely, or rather a
150	necessary inference from what has preceded, that neither the uneducated
150	and uninformed of the truth, nor yet those who never make an end of
150	their education, will be able ministers of State; not the former,
150	because they have no single aim of duty which is the rule of all their
150	actions, private as well as public; nor the latter, because they will
150	not act at all except upon compulsion, fancying that they are already
150	dwelling apart in the islands of the blest.
150	
150	Very true, he replied.
150	
150	Then, I said, the business of us who are the founders of the State will
150	be to compel the best minds to attain that knowledge which we have
150	already shown to be the greatest of all-they must continue to ascend
150	until they arrive at the good; but when they have ascended and seen
150	enough we must not allow them to do as they do now.
150	
150	What do you mean?
150	
150	I mean that they remain in the upper world:  but this must not be
150	allowed; they must be made to descend again among the prisoners in the
150	den, and partake of their labours and honours, whether they are worth
150	having or not.
150	
150	But is not this unjust? he said; ought we to give them a worse life,
150	when they might have a better?
150	
150	You have again forgotten, my friend, I said, the intention of the
150	legislator, who did not aim at making any one class in the State happy
150	above the rest; the happiness was to be in the whole State, and he held
150	the citizens together by persuasion and necessity, making them
150	benefactors of the State, and therefore benefactors of one another; to
150	this end he created them, not to please themselves, but to be his
150	instruments in binding up the State.
150	
150	True, he said, I had forgotten.
150	
150	Observe, Glaucon, that there will be no injustice in compelling our
150	philosophers to have a care and providence of others; we shall explain
150	to them that in other States, men of their class are not obliged to
150	share in the toils of politics:  and this is reasonable, for they grow
150	up at their own sweet will, and the government would rather not have
150	them.  Being self-taught, they cannot be expected to show any gratitude
150	for a culture which they have never received.  But we have brought you
150	into the world to be rulers of the hive, kings of yourselves and of the
150	other citizens, and have educated you far better and more perfectly
150	than they have been educated, and you are better able to share in the
150	double duty.  Wherefore each of you, when his turn comes, must go down
150	to the general underground abode, and get the habit of seeing in the
150	dark.  When you have acquired the habit, you will see ten thousand
150	times better than the inhabitants of the den, and you will know what
150	the several images are, and what they represent, because you have seen
150	the beautiful and just and good in their truth.  And thus our State
150	which is also yours will be a reality, and not a dream only, and will
150	be administered in a spirit unlike that of other States, in which men
150	fight with one another about shadows only and are distracted in the
150	struggle for power, which in their eyes is a great good.  Whereas the
150	truth is that the State in which the rulers are most reluctant to
150	govern is always the best and most quietly governed, and the State in
150	which they are most eager, the worst.
150	
150	Quite true, he replied.
150	
150	And will our pupils, when they hear this, refuse to take their turn at
150	the toils of State, when they are allowed to spend the greater part of
150	their time with one another in the heavenly light?
150	
150	Impossible, he answered; for they are just men, and the commands which
150	we impose upon them are just; there can be no doubt that every one of
150	them will take office as a stern necessity, and not after the fashion
150	of our present rulers of State.
150	
150	Yes, my friend, I said; and there lies the point.  You must contrive
150	for your future rulers another and a better life than that of a ruler,
150	and then you may have a well-ordered State; for only in the State which
150	offers this, will they rule who are truly rich, not in silver and gold,
150	but in virtue and wisdom, which are the true blessings of life.
150	Whereas if they go to the administration of public affairs, poor and
150	hungering after the' own private advantage, thinking that hence they
150	are to snatch the chief good, order there can never be; for they will
150	be fighting about office, and the civil and domestic broils which thus
150	arise will be the ruin of the rulers themselves and of the whole State.
150	
150	Most true, he replied.
150	
150	And the only life which looks down upon the life of political ambition
150	is that of true philosophy.  Do you know of any other?
150	
150	Indeed, I do not, he said.
150	
150	And those who govern ought not to be lovers of the task?  For, if they
150	are, there will be rival lovers, and they will fight.
150	
150	No question.
150	
150	Who then are those whom we shall compel to be guardians?  Surely they
150	will be the men who are wisest about affairs of State, and by whom the
150	State is best administered, and who at the same time have other honours
150	and another and a better life than that of politics?
150	
150	They are the men, and I will choose them, he replied.
150	
150	And now shall we consider in what way such guardians will be produced,
150	and how they are to be brought from darkness to light,--as some are
150	said to have ascended from the world below to the gods?
150	
150	By all means, he replied.
150	
150	The process, I said, is not the turning over of an oyster-shell, but
150	the turning round of a soul passing from a day which is little better
150	than night to the true day of being, that is, the ascent from below,
150	which we affirm to be true philosophy?
150	
150	Quite so.
150	
150	And should we not enquire what sort of knowledge has the power of
150	effecting such a change?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	What sort of knowledge is there which would draw the soul from becoming
150	to being?  And another consideration has just occurred to me: You will
150	remember that our young men are to be warrior athletes
150	
150	Yes, that was said.
150	
150	Then this new kind of knowledge must have an additional quality?
150	
150	What quality?
150	
150	Usefulness in war.
150	
150	Yes, if possible.
150	
150	There were two parts in our former scheme of education, were there not?
150	
150	Just so.
150	
150	There was gymnastic which presided over the growth and decay of the
150	body, and may therefore be regarded as having to do with generation and
150	corruption?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	Then that is not the knowledge which we are seeking to discover?  No.
150	
150	But what do you say of music, which also entered to a certain extent
150	into our former scheme?
150	
150	Music, he said, as you will remember, was the counterpart of gymnastic,
150	and trained the guardians by the influences of habit, by harmony making
150	them harmonious, by rhythm rhythmical, but not giving them science; and
150	the words, whether fabulous or possibly true, had kindred elements of
150	rhythm and harmony in them.  But in music there was nothing which
150	tended to that good which you are now seeking.
150	
150	You are most accurate, I said, in your recollection; in music there
150	certainly was nothing of the kind.  But what branch of knowledge is
150	there, my dear Glaucon, which is of the desired nature; since all the
150	useful arts were reckoned mean by us?
150	
150	Undoubtedly; and yet if music and gymnastic are excluded, and the arts
150	are also excluded, what remains?
150	
150	Well, I said, there may be nothing left of our special subjects; and
150	then we shall have to take something which is not special, but of
150	universal application.
150	
150	What may that be?
150	
150	A something which all arts and sciences and intelligences use in
150	common, and which every one first has to learn among the elements of
150	education.
150	
150	What is that?
150	
150	The little matter of distinguishing one, two, and three--in a word,
150	number and calculation:--do not all arts and sciences necessarily
150	partake of them?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	Then the art of war partakes of them?
150	
150	To the sure.
150	
150	Then Palamedes, whenever he appears in tragedy, proves Agamemnon
150	ridiculously unfit to be a general.  Did you never remark how he
150	declares that he had invented number, and had numbered the ships and
150	set in array the ranks of the army at Troy; which implies that they had
150	never been numbered before, and Agamemnon must be supposed literally to
150	have been incapable of counting his own feet--how could he if he was
150	ignorant of number?  And if that is true, what sort of general must he
150	have been?
150	
150	I should say a very strange one, if this was as you say.
150	
150	Can we deny that a warrior should have a knowledge of arithmetic?
150	
150	Certainly he should, if he is to have the smallest understanding of
150	military tactics, or indeed, I should rather say, if he is to be a man
150	at all.
150	
150	I should like to know whether you have the same notion which I have of
150	this study?
150	
150	What is your notion?
150	
150	It appears to me to be a study of the kind which we are seeking, and
150	which leads naturally to reflection, but never to have been rightly
150	used; for the true use of it is simply to draw the soul towards being.
150	
150	Will you explain your meaning? he said.
150	
150	I will try, I said; and I wish you would share the enquiry with me, and
150	say 'yes' or 'no' when I attempt to distinguish in my own mind what
150	branches of knowledge have this attracting power, in order that we may
150	have clearer proof that arithmetic is, as I suspect, one of them.
150	
150	Explain, he said.
150	
150	I mean to say that objects of sense are of two kinds; some of them do
150	not invite thought because the sense is an adequate judge of them;
150	while in the case of other objects sense is so untrustworthy that
150	further enquiry is imperatively demanded.
150	
150	You are clearly referring, he said, to the manner in which the senses
150	are imposed upon by distance, and by painting in light and shade.
150	
150	No, I said, that is not at all my meaning.
150	
150	Then what is your meaning?
150	
150	When speaking of uninviting objects, I mean those which do not pass
150	from one sensation to the opposite; inviting objects are those which
150	do; in this latter case the sense coming upon the object, whether at a
150	distance or near, gives no more vivid idea of anything in particular
150	than of its opposite.  An illustration will make my meaning
150	clearer:--here are three fingers--a little finger, a second finger, and
150	a middle finger.
150	
150	Very good.
150	
150	You may suppose that they are seen quite close:  And here comes the
150	point.
150	
150	What is it?
150	
150	Each of them equally appears a finger, whether seen in the middle or at
150	the extremity, whether white or black, or thick or thin--it makes no
150	difference; a finger is a finger all the same.  In these cases a man is
150	not compelled to ask of thought the question, what is a finger? for the
150	sight never intimates to the mind that a finger is other than a finger.
150	
150	True.
150	
150	And therefore, I said, as we might expect, there is nothing here which
150	invites or excites intelligence.
150	
150	There is not, he said.
150	
150	But is this equally true of the greatness and smallness of the fingers?
150	Can sight adequately perceive them? and is no difference made by the
150	circumstance that one of the fingers is in the middle and another at
150	the extremity?  And in like manner does the touch adequately perceive
150	the qualities of thickness or thinness, or softness or hardness?  And
150	so of the other senses; do they give perfect intimations of such
150	matters?  Is not their mode of operation on this wise--the sense which
150	is concerned with the quality of hardness is necessarily concerned also
150	with the quality of softness, and only intimates to the soul that the
150	same thing is felt to be both hard and soft?
150	
150	You are quite right, he said.
150	
150	And must not the soul be perplexed at this intimation which the sense
150	gives of a hard which is also soft?  What, again, is the meaning of
150	light and heavy, if that which is light is also heavy, and that which
150	is heavy, light?
150	
150	Yes, he said, these intimations which the soul receives are very
150	curious and require to be explained.
150	
150	Yes, I said, and in these perplexities the soul naturally summons to
150	her aid calculation and intelligence, that she may see whether the
150	several objects announced to her are one or two.
150	
150	True.
150	
150	And if they turn out to be two, is not each of them one and different?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	And if each is one, and both are two, she will conceive the two as in a
150	state of division, for if there were undivided they could only be
150	conceived of as one?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	The eye certainly did see both small and great, but only in a confused
150	manner; they were not distinguished.
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	Whereas the thinking mind, intending to light up the chaos, was
150	compelled to reverse the process, and look at small and great as
150	separate and not confused.
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	Was not this the beginning of the enquiry 'What is great?' and 'What is
150	small?'
150	
150	Exactly so.
150	
150	And thus arose the distinction of the visible and the intelligible.
150	
150	Most true.
150	
150	This was what I meant when I spoke of impressions which invited the
150	intellect, or the reverse--those which are simultaneous with opposite
150	impressions, invite thought; those which are not simultaneous do not.
150	
150	I understand, he said, and agree with you.
150	
150	And to which class do unity and number belong?
150	
150	I do not know, he replied.
150	
150	Think a little and you will see that what has preceded will supply the
150	answer; for if simple unity could be adequately perceived by the sight
150	or by any other sense, then, as we were saying in the case of the
150	finger, there would be nothing to attract towards being; but when there
150	is some contradiction always present, and one is the reverse of one and
150	involves the conception of plurality, then thought begins to be aroused
150	within us, and the soul perplexed and wanting to arrive at a decision
150	asks 'What is absolute unity?' This is the way in which the study of
150	the one has a power of drawing and converting the mind to the
150	contemplation of true being.
150	
150	And surely, he said, this occurs notably in the case of one; for we see
150	the same thing to be both one and infinite in multitude?
150	
150	Yes, I said; and this being true of one must be equally true of all
150	number?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	And all arithmetic and calculation have to do with number?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And they appear to lead the mind towards truth?
150	
150	Yes, in a very remarkable manner.
150	
150	Then this is knowledge of the kind for which we are seeking, having a
150	double use, military and philosophical; for the man of war must learn
150	the art of number or he will not know how to array his troops, and the
150	philosopher also, because he has to rise out of the sea of change and
150	lay hold of true being, and therefore he must be an arithmetician.
150	
150	That is true.
150	
150	And our guardian is both warrior and philosopher?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	Then this is a kind of knowledge which legislation may fitly prescribe;
150	and we must endeavour to persuade those who are prescribe to be the
150	principal men of our State to go and learn arithmetic, not as amateurs,
150	but they must carry on the study until they see the nature of numbers
150	with the mind only; nor again, like merchants or retail-traders, with a
150	view to buying or selling, but for the sake of their military use, and
150	of the soul herself; and because this will be the easiest way for her
150	to pass from becoming to truth and being.
150	
150	That is excellent, he said.
150	
150	Yes, I said, and now having spoken of it, I must add how charming the
150	science is! and in how many ways it conduces to our desired end, if
150	pursued in the spirit of a philosopher, and not of a shopkeeper!
150	
150	How do you mean?
150	
150	I mean, as I was saying, that arithmetic has a very great and elevating
150	effect, compelling the soul to reason about abstract number, and
150	rebelling against the introduction of visible or tangible objects into
150	the argument.  You know how steadily the masters of the art repel and
150	ridicule any one who attempts to divide absolute unity when he is
150	calculating, and if you divide, they multiply, taking care that one
150	shall continue one and not become lost in fractions.
150	
150	That is very true.
150	
150	Now, suppose a person were to say to them:  O my friends, what are
150	these wonderful numbers about which you are reasoning, in which, as you
150	say, there is a unity such as you demand, and each unit is equal,
150	invariable, indivisible,--what would they answer?
150	
150	They would answer, as I should conceive, that they were speaking of
150	those numbers which can only be realised in thought.
150	
150	Then you see that this knowledge may be truly called necessary,
150	necessitating as it clearly does the use of the pure intelligence in
150	the attainment of pure truth?
150	
150	Yes; that is a marked characteristic of it.
150	
150	And have you further observed, that those who have a natural talent for
150	calculation are generally quick at every other kind of knowledge; and
150	even the dull if they have had an arithmetical training, although they
150	may derive no other advantage from it, always become much quicker than
150	they would otherwise have been.
150	
150	Very true, he said.
150	
150	And indeed, you will not easily find a more difficult study, and not
150	many as difficult.
150	
150	You will not.
150	
150	And, for all these reasons, arithmetic is a kind of knowledge in which
150	the best natures should be trained, and which must not be given up.
150	
150	I agree.
150	
150	Let this then be made one of our subjects of education.  And next,
150	shall we enquire whether the kindred science also concerns us?
150	
150	You mean geometry?
150	
150	Exactly so.
150	
150	Clearly, he said, we are concerned with that part of geometry which
150	relates to war; for in pitching a camp, or taking up a position, or
150	closing or extending the lines of an army, or any other military
150	manoeuvre, whether in actual battle or on a march, it will make all the
150	difference whether a general is or is not a geometrician.
150	
150	Yes, I said, but for that purpose a very little of either geometry or
150	calculation will be enough; the question relates rather to the greater
150	and more advanced part of geometry--whether that tends in any degree to
150	make more easy the vision of the idea of good; and thither, as I was
150	saying, all things tend which compel the soul to turn her gaze towards
150	that place, where is the full perfection of being, which she ought, by
150	all means, to behold.
150	
150	True, he said.
150	
150	Then if geometry compels us to view being, it concerns us; if becoming
150	only, it does not concern us?
150	
150	Yes, that is what we assert.
150	
150	Yet anybody who has the least acquaintance with geometry will not deny
150	that such a conception of the science is in flat contradiction to the
150	ordinary language of geometricians.
150	
150	How so?
150	
150	They have in view practice only, and are always speaking? in a narrow
150	and ridiculous manner, of squaring and extending and applying and the
150	like--they confuse the necessities of geometry with those of daily
150	life; whereas knowledge is the real object of the whole science.
150	
150	Certainly, he said.
150	
150	Then must not a further admission be made?
150	
150	What admission?
150	
150	That the knowledge at which geometry aims is knowledge of the eternal,
150	and not of aught perishing and transient.
150	
150	That, he replied, may be readily allowed, and is true.
150	
150	Then, my noble friend, geometry will draw the soul towards truth, and
150	create the spirit of philosophy, and raise up that which is now
150	unhappily allowed to fall down.
150	
150	Nothing will be more likely to have such an effect.
150	
150	Then nothing should be more sternly laid down than that the inhabitants
150	of your fair city should by all means learn geometry.  Moreover the
150	science has indirect effects, which are not small.
150	
150	Of what kind? he said.
150	
150	There are the military advantages of which you spoke, I said; and in
150	all departments of knowledge, as experience proves, any one who has
150	studied geometry is infinitely quicker of apprehension than one who has
150	not.
150	
150	Yes indeed, he said, there is an infinite difference between them.
150	
150	Then shall we propose this as a second branch of knowledge which our
150	youth will study?
150	
150	Let us do so, he replied.
150	
150	And suppose we make astronomy the third--what do you say?
150	
150	I am strongly inclined to it, he said; the observation of the seasons
150	and of months and years is as essential to the general as it is to the
150	farmer or sailor.
150	
150	I am amused, I said, at your fear of the world, which makes you guard
150	against the appearance of insisting upon useless studies; and I quite
150	admit the difficulty of believing that in every man there is an eye of
150	the soul which, when by other pursuits lost and dimmed, is by these
150	purified and re-illumined; and is more precious far than ten thousand
150	bodily eyes, for by it alone is truth seen.  Now there are two classes
150	of persons:  one class of those who will agree with you and will take
150	your words as a revelation; another class to whom they will be utterly
150	unmeaning, and who will naturally deem them to be idle tales, for they
150	see no sort of profit which is to be obtained from them.  And therefore
150	you had better decide at once with which of the two you are proposing
150	to argue.  You will very likely say with neither, and that your chief
150	aim in carrying on the argument is your own improvement; at the same
150	time you do not grudge to others any benefit which they may receive.
150	
150	I think that I should prefer to carry on the argument mainly on my own
150	behalf.
150	
150	Then take a step backward, for we have gone wrong in the order of the
150	sciences.
150	
150	What was the mistake? he said.
150	
150	After plane geometry, I said, we proceeded at once to solids in
150	revolution, instead of taking solids in themselves; whereas after the
150	second dimension the third, which is concerned with cubes and
150	dimensions of depth, ought to have followed.
150	
150	That is true, Socrates; but so little seems to be known as yet about
150	these subjects.
150	
150	Why, yes, I said, and for two reasons:--in the first place, no
150	government patronises them; this leads to a want of energy in the
150	pursuit of them, and they are difficult; in the second place, students
150	cannot learn them unless they have a director.  But then a director can
150	hardly be found, and even if he could, as matters now stand, the
150	students, who are very conceited, would not attend to him.  That,
150	however, would be otherwise if the whole State became the director of
150	these studies and gave honour to them; then disciples would want to
150	come, and there would be continuous and earnest search, and discoveries
150	would be made; since even now, disregarded as they are by the world,
150	and maimed of their fair proportions, and although none of their
150	votaries can tell the use of them, still these studies force their way
150	by their natural charm, and very likely, if they had the help of the
150	State, they would some day emerge into light.
150	
150	Yes, he said, there is a remarkable charm in them.  But I do not
150	clearly understand the change in the order.  First you began with a
150	geometry of plane surfaces?
150	
150	Yes, I said.
150	
150	And you placed astronomy next, and then you made a step backward?
150	
150	Yes, and I have delayed you by my hurry; the ludicrous state of solid
150	geometry, which, in natural order, should have followed, made me pass
150	over this branch and go on to astronomy, or motion of solids.
150	
150	True, he said.
150	
150	Then assuming that the science now omitted would come into existence if
150	encouraged by the State, let us go on to astronomy, which will be
150	fourth.
150	
150	The right order, he replied.  And now, Socrates, as you rebuked the
150	vulgar manner in which I praised astronomy before, my praise shall be
150	given in your own spirit.  For every one, as I think, must see that
150	astronomy compels the soul to look upwards and leads us from this world
150	to another.
150	
150	Every one but myself, I said; to every one else this may be clear, but
150	not to me.
150	
150	And what then would you say?
150	
150	I should rather say that those who elevate astronomy into philosophy
150	appear to me to make us look downwards and not upwards.
150	
150	What do you mean? he asked.
150	
150	You, I replied, have in your mind a truly sublime conception of our
150	knowledge of the things above.  And I dare say that if a person were to
150	throw his head back and study the fretted ceiling, you would still
150	think that his mind was the percipient, and not his eyes.  And you are
150	very likely right, and I may be a simpleton: but, in my opinion, that
150	knowledge only which is of being and of the unseen can make the soul
150	look upwards, and whether a man gapes at the heavens or blinks on the
150	ground, seeking to learn some particular of sense, I would deny that he
150	can learn, for nothing of that sort is matter of science; his soul is
150	looking downwards, not upwards, whether his way to knowledge is by
150	water or by land, whether he floats, or only lies on his back.
150	
150	I acknowledge, he said, the justice of your rebuke.  Still, I should
150	like to ascertain how astronomy can be learned in any manner more
150	conducive to that knowledge of which we are speaking?
150	
150	I will tell you, I said:  The starry heaven which we behold is wrought
150	upon a visible ground, and therefore, although the fairest and most
150	perfect of visible things, must necessarily be deemed inferior far to
150	the true motions of absolute swiftness and absolute slowness, which are
150	relative to each other, and carry with them that which is contained in
150	them, in the true number and in every true figure.  Now, these are to
150	be apprehended by reason and intelligence, but not by sight.
150	
150	True, he replied.
150	
150	The spangled heavens should be used as a pattern and with a view to
150	that higher knowledge; their beauty is like the beauty of figures or
150	pictures excellently wrought by the hand of Daedalus, or some other
150	great artist, which we may chance to behold; any geometrician who saw
150	them would appreciate the exquisiteness of their workmanship, but he
150	would never dream of thinking that in them he could find the true equal
150	or the true double, or the truth of any other proportion.
150	
150	No, he replied, such an idea would be ridiculous.
150	
150	And will not a true astronomer have the same feeling when he looks at
150	the movements of the stars?  Will he not think that heaven and the
150	things in heaven are framed by the Creator of them in the most perfect
150	manner?  But he will never imagine that the proportions of night and
150	day, or of both to the month, or of the month to the year, or of the
150	stars to these and to one another, and any other things that are
150	material and visible can also be eternal and subject to no
150	deviation--that would be absurd; and it is equally absurd to take so
150	much pains in investigating their exact truth.
150	
150	I quite agree, though I never thought of this before.
150	
150	Then, I said, in astronomy, as in geometry, we should employ problems,
150	and let the heavens alone if we would approach the subject in the right
150	way and so make the natural gift of reason to be of any real use.
150	
150	That, he said, is a work infinitely beyond our present astronomers.
150	
150	Yes, I said; and there are many other things which must also have a
150	similar extension given to them, if our legislation is to be of any
150	value.  But can you tell me of any other suitable study?
150	
150	No, he said, not without thinking.
150	
150	Motion, I said, has many forms, and not one only; two of them are
150	obvious enough even to wits no better than ours; and there are others,
150	as I imagine, which may be left to wiser persons.
150	
150	But where are the two?
150	
150	There is a second, I said, which is the counterpart of the one already
150	named.
150	
150	And what may that be?
150	
150	The second, I said, would seem relatively to the ears to be what the
150	first is to the eyes; for I conceive that as the eyes are designed to
150	look up at the stars, so are the ears to hear harmonious motions; and
150	these are sister sciences--as the Pythagoreans say, and we, Glaucon,
150	agree with them?
150	
150	Yes, he replied.
150	
150	But this, I said, is a laborious study, and therefore we had better go
150	and learn of them; and they will tell us whether there are any other
150	applications of these sciences.  At the same time, we must not lose
150	sight of our own higher object.
150	
150	What is that?
150	
150	There is a perfection which all knowledge ought to reach, and which our
150	pupils ought also to attain, and not to fall short of, as I was saying
150	that they did in astronomy.  For in the science of harmony, as you
150	probably know, the same thing happens.  The teachers of harmony compare
150	the sounds and consonances which are heard only, and their labour, like
150	that of the astronomers, is in vain.
150	
150	Yes, by heaven! he said; and 'tis as good as a play to hear them
150	talking about their condensed notes, as they call them; they put their
150	ears close alongside of the strings like persons catching a sound from
150	their neighbour's wall--one set of them declaring that they distinguish
150	an intermediate note and have found the least interval which should be
150	the unit of measurement; the others insisting that the two sounds have
150	passed into the same--either party setting their ears before their
150	understanding.
150	
150	You mean, I said, those gentlemen who tease and torture the strings and
150	rack them on the pegs of the instrument:  might carry on the metaphor
150	and speak after their manner of the blows which the plectrum gives, and
150	make accusations against the strings, both of backwardness and
150	forwardness to sound; but this would be tedious, and therefore I will
150	only say that these are not the men, and that I am referring to the
150	Pythagoreans, of whom I was just now proposing to enquire about
150	harmony.  For they too are in error, like the astronomers; they
150	investigate the numbers of the harmonies which are heard, but they
150	never attain to problems-that is to say, they never reach the natural
150	harmonies of number, or reflect why some numbers are harmonious and
150	others not.
150	
150	That, he said, is a thing of more than mortal knowledge.
150	
150	A thing, I replied, which I would rather call useful; that is, if
150	sought after with a view to the beautiful and good; but if pursued in
150	any other spirit, useless.  Very true, he said.
150	
150	Now, when all these studies reach the point of inter-communion and
150	connection with one another, and come to be considered in their mutual
150	affinities, then, I think, but not till then, will the pursuit of them
150	have a value for our objects; otherwise there is no profit in them.
150	
150	I suspect so; but you are speaking, Socrates, of a vast work.
150	
150	What do you mean?  I said; the prelude or what?  Do you not know that
150	all this is but the prelude to the actual strain which we have to
150	learn?  For you surely would not regard the skilled mathematician as a
150	dialectician?
150	
150	Assuredly not, he said; I have hardly ever known a mathematician who
150	was capable of reasoning.
150	
150	But do you imagine that men who are unable to give and take a reason
150	will have the knowledge which we require of them?
150	
150	Neither can this be supposed.
150	
150	And so, Glaucon, I said, we have at last arrived at the hymn of
150	dialectic.  This is that strain which is of the intellect only, but
150	which the faculty of sight will nevertheless be found to imitate; for
150	sight, as you may remember, was imagined by us after a while to behold
150	the real animals and stars, and last of all the sun himself.  And so
150	with dialectic; when a person starts on the discovery of the absolute
150	by the light of reason only, and without any assistance of sense, and
150	perseveres until by pure intelligence he arrives at the perception of
150	the absolute good, he at last finds himself at the end of the
150	intellectual world, as in the case of sight at the end of the visible.
150	
150	Exactly, he said.
150	
150	Then this is the progress which you call dialectic?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	But the release of the prisoners from chains, and their translation
150	from the shadows to the images and to the light, and the ascent from
150	the underground den to the sun, while in his presence they are vainly
150	trying to look on animals and plants and the light of the sun, but are
150	able to perceive even with their weak eyes the images in the water
150	(which are divine), and are the shadows of true existence (not shadows
150	of images cast by a light of fire, which compared with the sun is only
150	an image)--this power of elevating the highest principle in the soul to
150	the contemplation of that which is best in existence, with which we may
150	compare the raising of that faculty which is the very light of the body
150	to the sight of that which is brightest in the material and visible
150	world--this power is given, as I was saying, by all that study and
150	pursuit of the arts which has been described.
150	
150	I agree in what you are saying, he replied, which may be hard to
150	believe, yet, from another point of view, is harder still to deny.
150	This, however, is not a theme to be treated of in passing only, but
150	will have to be discussed again and again.  And so, whether our
150	conclusion be true or false, let us assume all this, and proceed at
150	once from the prelude or preamble to the chief strain, and describe
150	that in like manner.  Say, then, what is the nature and what are the
150	divisions of dialectic, and what are the paths which lead thither; for
150	these paths will also lead to our final rest?
150	
150	Dear Glaucon, I said, you will not be able to follow me here, though I
150	would do my best, and you should behold not an image only but the
150	absolute truth, according to my notion.  Whether what I told you would
150	or would not have been a reality I cannot venture to say; but you would
150	have seen something like reality; of that I am confident.
150	
150	Doubtless, he replied.
150	
150	But I must also remind you, that the power of dialectic alone can
150	reveal this, and only to one who is a disciple of the previous sciences.
150	
150	Of that assertion you may be as confident as of the last.
150	
150	And assuredly no one will argue that there is any other method of
150	comprehending by any regular process all true existence or of
150	ascertaining what each thing is in its own nature; for the arts in
150	general are concerned with the desires or opinions of men, or are
150	cultivated with a view to production and construction, or for the
150	preservation of such productions and constructions; and as to the
150	mathematical sciences which, as we were saying, have some apprehension
150	of true being--geometry and the like--they only dream about being, but
150	never can they behold the waking reality so long as they leave the
150	hypotheses which they use unexamined, and are unable to give an account
150	of them.  For when a man knows not his own first principle, and when
150	the conclusion and intermediate steps are also constructed out of he
150	knows not what, how can he imagine that such a fabric of convention can
150	ever become science?
150	
150	Impossible, he said.
150	
150	Then dialectic, and dialectic alone, goes directly to the first
150	principle and is the only science which does away with hypotheses in
150	order to make her ground secure; the eye of the soul, which is
150	literally buried in an outlandish slough, is by her gentle aid lifted
150	upwards; and she uses as handmaids and helpers in the work of
150	conversion, the sciences which we have been discussing.  Custom terms
150	them sciences, but they ought to have some other name, implying greater
150	clearness than opinion and less clearness than science: and this, in
150	our previous sketch, was called understanding.  But why should we
150	dispute about names when we have realities of such importance to
150	consider?
150	
150	Why indeed, he said, when any name will do which expresses the thought
150	of the mind with clearness?
150	
150	At any rate, we are satisfied, as before, to have four divisions; two
150	for intellect and two for opinion, and to call the first division
150	science, the second understanding, the third belief, and the fourth
150	perception of shadows, opinion being concerned with becoming, and
150	intellect with being; and so to make a proportion:--
150	
150	    As being is to becoming, so is pure intellect to opinion.
150	    And as intellect is to opinion, so is science to belief, and
150	    understanding to the perception of shadows.
150	
150	But let us defer the further correlation and subdivision of the
150	subjects of opinion and of intellect, for it will be a long enquiry,
150	many times longer than this has been.
150	
150	As far as I understand, he said, I agree.
150	
150	And do you also agree, I said, in describing the dialectician as one
150	who attains a conception of the essence of each thing?  And he who does
150	not possess and is therefore unable to impart this conception, in
150	whatever degree he fails, may in that degree also be said to fail in
150	intelligence?  Will you admit so much?
150	
150	Yes, he said; how can I deny it?
150	
150	And you would say the same of the conception of the good?
150	
150	Until the person is able to abstract and define rationally the idea of
150	good, and unless he can run the gauntlet of all objections, and is
150	ready to disprove them, not by appeals to opinion, but to absolute
150	truth, never faltering at any step of the argument--unless he can do
150	all this, you would say that he knows neither the idea of good nor any
150	other good; he apprehends only a shadow, if anything at all, which is
150	given by opinion and not by science;--dreaming and slumbering in this
150	life, before he is well awake here, he arrives at the world below, and
150	has his final quietus.
150	
150	In all that I should most certainly agree with you.
150	
150	And surely you would not have the children of your ideal State, whom
150	you are nurturing and educating--if the ideal ever becomes a
150	reality--you would not allow the future rulers to be like posts, having
150	no reason in them, and yet to be set in authority over the highest
150	matters?
150	
150	Certainly not.
150	
150	Then you will make a law that they shall have such an education as will
150	enable them to attain the greatest skill in asking and answering
150	questions?
150	
150	Yes, he said, you and I together will make it.
150	
150	Dialectic, then, as you will agree, is the coping-stone of the
150	sciences, and is set over them; no other science can be placed
150	higher--the nature of knowledge can no further go?
150	
150	I agree, he said.
150	
150	But to whom we are to assign these studies, and in what way they are to
150	be assigned, are questions which remain to be considered?
150	
150	Yes, clearly.
150	
150	You remember, I said, how the rulers were chosen before?
150	
150	Certainly, he said.
150	
150	The same natures must still be chosen, and the preference again given
150	to the surest and the bravest, and, if possible, to the fairest; and,
150	having noble and generous tempers, they should also have the natural
150	gifts which will facilitate their education.
150	
150	And what are these?
150	
150	Such gifts as keenness and ready powers of acquisition; for the mind
150	more often faints from the severity of study than from the severity of
150	gymnastics:  the toil is more entirely the mind's own, and is not
150	shared with the body.
150	
150	Very true, he replied.
150	
150	Further, he of whom we are in search should have a good memory, and be
150	an unwearied solid man who is a lover of labour in any line; or he will
150	never be able to endure the great amount of bodily exercise and to go
150	through all the intellectual discipline and study which we require of
150	him.
150	
150	Certainly, he said; he must have natural gifts.
150	
150	The mistake at present is, that those who study philosophy have no
150	vocation, and this, as I was before saying, is the reason why she has
150	fallen into disrepute:  her true sons should take her by the hand and
150	not bastards.
150	
150	What do you mean?
150	
150	In the first place, her votary should not have a lame or halting
150	industry--I mean, that he should not be half industrious and half idle:
150	as, for example, when a man is a lover of gymnastic and hunting, and
150	all other bodily exercises, but a hater rather than a lover of the
150	labour of learning or listening or enquiring.  Or the occupation to
150	which he devotes himself may be of an opposite kind, and he may have
150	the other sort of lameness.
150	
150	Certainly, he said.
150	
150	And as to truth, I said, is not a soul equally to be deemed halt and
150	lame which hates voluntary falsehood and is extremely indignant at
150	herself and others when they tell lies, but is patient of involuntary
150	falsehood, and does not mind wallowing like a swinish beast in the mire
150	of ignorance, and has no shame at being detected?
150	
150	To be sure.
150	
150	And, again, in respect of temperance, courage, magnificence, and every
150	other virtue, should we not carefully distinguish between the true son
150	and the bastard? for where there is no discernment of such qualities
150	States and individuals unconsciously err and the State makes a ruler,
150	and the individual a friend, of one who, being defective in some part
150	of virtue, is in a figure lame or a bastard.
150	
150	That is very true, he said.
150	
150	All these things, then, will have to be carefully considered by us; and
150	if only those whom we introduce to this vast system of education and
150	training are sound in body and mind, justice herself will have nothing
150	to say against us, and we shall be the saviours of the constitution and
150	of the State; but, if our pupils are men of another stamp, the reverse
150	will happen, and we shall pour a still greater flood of ridicule on
150	philosophy than she has to endure at present.
150	
150	That would not be creditable.
150	
150	Certainly not, I said; and yet perhaps, in thus turning jest into
150	earnest I am equally ridiculous.
150	
150	In what respect?
150	
150	I had forgotten, I said, that we were not serious, and spoke with too
150	much excitement.  For when I saw philosophy so undeservedly trampled
150	under foot of men I could not help feeling a sort of indignation at the
150	authors of her disgrace:  and my anger made me too vehement.
150	
150	Indeed!  I was listening, and did not think so.
150	
150	But I, who am the speaker, felt that I was.  And now let me remind you
150	that, although in our former selection we chose old men, we must not do
150	so in this.  Solon was under a delusion when he said that a man when he
150	grows old may learn many things--for he can no more learn much than he
150	can run much; youth is the time for any extraordinary toil.
150	
150	Of course.
150	
150	And, therefore, calculation and geometry and all the other elements of
150	instruction, which are a preparation for dialectic, should be presented
150	to the mind in childhood; not, however, under any notion of forcing our
150	system of education.
150	
150	Why not?
150	
150	Because a freeman ought not to be a slave in the acquisition of
150	knowledge of any kind.  Bodily exercise, when compulsory, does no harm
150	to the body; but knowledge which is acquired under compulsion obtains
150	no hold on the mind.
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	Then, my good friend, I said, do not use compulsion, but let early
150	education be a sort of amusement; you will then be better able to find
150	out the natural bent.
150	
150	That is a very rational notion, he said.
150	
150	Do you remember that the children, too, were to be taken to see the
150	battle on horseback; and that if there were no danger they were to be
150	brought close up and, like young hounds, have a taste of blood given
150	them?
150	
150	Yes, I remember.
150	
150	The same practice may be followed, I said, in all these
150	things--labours, lessons, dangers--and he who is most at home in all of
150	them ought to be enrolled in a select number.
150	
150	At what age?
150	
150	At the age when the necessary gymnastics are over:  the period whether
150	of two or three years which passes in this sort of training is useless
150	for any other purpose; for sleep and exercise are unpropitious to
150	learning; and the trial of who is first in gymnastic exercises is one
150	of the most important tests to which our youth are subjected.
150	
150	Certainly, he replied.
150	
150	After that time those who are selected from the class of twenty years
150	old will be promoted to higher honour, and the sciences which they
150	learned without any order in their early education will now be brought
150	together, and they will be able to see the natural relationship of them
150	to one another and to true being.
150	
150	Yes, he said, that is the only kind of knowledge which takes lasting
150	root.
150	
150	Yes, I said; and the capacity for such knowledge is the great criterion
150	of dialectical talent:  the comprehensive mind is always the
150	dialectical.
150	
150	I agree with you, he said.
150	
150	These, I said, are the points which you must consider; and those who
150	have most of this comprehension, and who are more steadfast in their
150	learning, and in their military and other appointed duties, when they
150	have arrived at the age of thirty have to be chosen by you out of the
150	select class, and elevated to higher honour; and you will have to prove
150	them by the help of dialectic, in order to learn which of them is able
150	to give up the use of sight and the other senses, and in company with
150	truth to attain absolute being: And here, my friend, great caution is
150	required.
150	
150	Why great caution?
150	
150	Do you not remark, I said, how great is the evil which dialectic has
150	introduced?
150	
150	What evil? he said.
150	
150	The students of the art are filled with lawlessness.
150	
150	Quite true, he said.
150	
150	Do you think that there is anything so very unnatural or inexcusable in
150	their case? or will you make allowance for them?
150	
150	In what way make allowance?
150	
150	I want you, I said, by way of parallel, to imagine a supposititious son
150	who is brought up in great wealth; he is one of a great and numerous
150	family, and has many flatterers.  When he grows up to manhood, he
150	learns that his alleged are not his real parents; but who the real are
150	he is unable to discover.  Can you guess how he will be likely to
150	behave towards his flatterers and his supposed parents, first of all
150	during the period when he is ignorant of the false relation, and then
150	again when he knows?  Or shall I guess for you?
150	
150	If you please.
150	
150	Then I should say, that while he is ignorant of the truth he will be
150	likely to honour his father and his mother and his supposed relations
150	more than the flatterers; he will be less inclined to neglect them when
150	in need, or to do or say anything against them; and he will be less
150	willing to disobey them in any important matter.
150	
150	He will.
150	
150	But when he has made the discovery, I should imagine that he would
150	diminish his honour and regard for them, and would become more devoted
150	to the flatterers; their influence over him would greatly increase; he
150	would now live after their ways, and openly associate with them, and,
150	unless he were of an unusually good disposition, he would trouble
150	himself no more about his supposed parents or other relations.
150	
150	Well, all that is very probable.  But how is the image applicable to
150	the disciples of philosophy?
150	
150	In this way:  you know that there are certain principles about justice
150	and honour, which were taught us in childhood, and under their parental
150	authority we have been brought up, obeying and honouring them.
150	
150	That is true.
150	
150	There are also opposite maxims and habits of pleasure which flatter and
150	attract the soul, but do not influence those of us who have any sense
150	of right, and they continue to obey and honour the maxims of their
150	fathers.
150	
150	True.
150	
150	Now, when a man is in this state, and the questioning spirit asks what
150	is fair or honourable, and he answers as the legislator has taught him,
150	and then arguments many and diverse refute his words, until he is
150	driven into believing that nothing is honourable any more than
150	dishonourable, or just and good any more than the reverse, and so of
150	all the notions which he most valued, do you think that he will still
150	honour and obey them as before?
150	
150	Impossible.
150	
150	And when he ceases to think them honourable and natural as heretofore,
150	and he fails to discover the true, can he be expected to pursue any
150	life other than that which flatters his desires?
150	
150	He cannot.
150	
150	And from being a keeper of the law he is converted into a breaker of it?
150	
150	Unquestionably.
150	
150	Now all this is very natural in students of philosophy such as I have
150	described, and also, as I was just now saying, most excusable.
150	
150	Yes, he said; and, I may add, pitiable.
150	
150	Therefore, that your feelings may not be moved to pity about our
150	citizens who are now thirty years of age, every care must be taken in
150	introducing them to dialectic.
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	There is a danger lest they should taste the dear delight too early;
150	for youngsters, as you may have observed, when they first get the taste
150	in their mouths, argue for amusement, and are always contradicting and
150	refuting others in imitation of those who refute them; like puppy-dogs,
150	they rejoice in pulling and tearing at all who come near them.
150	
150	Yes, he said, there is nothing which they like better.
150	
150	And when they have made many conquests and received defeats at the
150	hands of many, they violently and speedily get into a way of not
150	believing anything which they believed before, and hence, not only
150	they, but philosophy and all that relates to it is apt to have a bad
150	name with the rest of the world.
150	
150	Too true, he said.
150	
150	But when a man begins to get older, he will no longer be guilty of such
150	insanity; he will imitate the dialectician who is seeking for truth,
150	and not the eristic, who is contradicting for the sake of amusement;
150	and the greater moderation of his character will increase instead of
150	diminishing the honour of the pursuit.
150	
150	Very true, he said.
150	
150	And did we not make special provision for this, when we said that the
150	disciples of philosophy were to be orderly and steadfast, not, as now,
150	any chance aspirant or intruder?
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	Suppose, I said, the study of philosophy to take the place of
150	gymnastics and to be continued diligently and earnestly and exclusively
150	for twice the number of years which were passed in bodily
150	exercise--will that be enough?
150	
150	Would you say six or four years? he asked.
150	
150	Say five years, I replied; at the end of the time they must be sent
150	down again into the den and compelled to hold any military or other
150	office which young men are qualified to hold: in this way they will get
150	their experience of life, and there will be an opportunity of trying
150	whether, when they are drawn all manner of ways by temptation, they
150	will stand firm or flinch.
150	
150	And how long is this stage of their lives to last?
150	
150	Fifteen years, I answered; and when they have reached fifty years of
150	age, then let those who still survive and have distinguished themselves
150	in every action of their lives and in every branch of knowledge come at
150	last to their consummation; the time has now arrived at which they must
150	raise the eye of the soul to the universal light which lightens all
150	things, and behold the absolute good; for that is the pattern according
150	to which they are to order the State and the lives of individuals, and
150	the remainder of their own lives also; making philosophy their chief
150	pursuit, but, when their turn comes, toiling also at politics and
150	ruling for the public good, not as though they were performing some
150	heroic action, but simply as a matter of duty; and when they have
150	brought up in each generation others like themselves and left them in
150	their place to be governors of the State, then they will depart to the
150	Islands of the Blest and dwell there; and the city will give them
150	public memorials and sacrifices and honour them, if the Pythian oracle
150	consent, as demi-gods, but if not, as in any case blessed and divine.
150	
150	You are a sculptor, Socrates, and have made statues of our governors
150	faultless in beauty.
150	
150	Yes, I said, Glaucon, and of our governesses too; for you must not
150	suppose that what I have been saying applies to men only and not to
150	women as far as their natures can go.
150	
150	There you are right, he said, since we have made them to share in all
150	things like the men.
150	
150	Well, I said, and you would agree (would you not?) that what has been
150	said about the State and the government is not a mere dream, and
150	although difficult not impossible, but only possible in the way which
150	has been supposed; that is to say, when the true philosopher kings are
150	born in a State, one or more of them, despising the honours of this
150	present world which they deem mean and worthless, esteeming above all
150	things right and the honour that springs from right, and regarding
150	justice as the greatest and most necessary of all things, whose
150	ministers they are, and whose principles will be exalted by them when
150	they set in order their own city?
150	
150	How will they proceed?
150	
150	They will begin by sending out into the country all the inhabitants of
150	the city who are more than ten years old, and will take possession of
150	their children, who will be unaffected by the habits of their parents;
150	these they will train in their own habits and laws, I mean in the laws
150	which we have given them:  and in this way the State and constitution
150	of which we were speaking will soonest and most easily attain
150	happiness, and the nation which has such a constitution will gain most.
150	
150	Yes, that will be the best way.  And I think, Socrates, that you have
150	very well described how, if ever, such a constitution might come into
150	being.
150	
150	Enough then of the perfect State, and of the man who bears its
150	image--there is no difficulty in seeing how we shall describe him.
150	
150	There is no difficulty, he replied; and I agree with you in thinking
150	that nothing more need be said.
150	
150	
150	
150	
150	BOOK VIII
150	
150	
150	SOCRATES - GLAUCON
150	
150	AND so, Glaucon, we have arrived at the conclusion that in the perfect
150	State wives and children are to be in common; and that all education
150	and the pursuits of war and peace are also to be common, and the best
150	philosophers and the bravest warriors are to be their kings?
150	
150	That, replied Glaucon, has been acknowledged.
150	
150	Yes, I said; and we have further acknowledged that the governors, when
150	appointed themselves, will take their soldiers and place them in houses
150	such as we were describing, which are common to all, and contain
150	nothing private, or individual; and about their property, you remember
150	what we agreed?
150	
150	Yes, I remember that no one was to have any of the ordinary possessions
150	of mankind; they were to be warrior athletes and guardians, receiving
150	from the other citizens, in lieu of annual payment, only their
150	maintenance, and they were to take care of themselves and of the whole
150	State.
150	
150	True, I said; and now that this division of our task is concluded, let
150	us find the point at which we digressed, that we may return into the
150	old path.
150	
150	There is no difficulty in returning; you implied, then as now, that you
150	had finished the description of the State:  you said that such a State
150	was good, and that the man was good who answered to it, although, as
150	now appears, you had more excellent things to relate both of State and
150	man.  And you said further, that if this was the true form, then the
150	others were false; and of the false forms, you said, as I remember,
150	that there were four principal ones, and that their defects, and the
150	defects of the individuals corresponding to them, were worth examining.
150	When we had seen all the individuals, and finally agreed as to who was
150	the best and who was the worst of them, we were to consider whether the
150	best was not also the happiest, and the worst the most miserable.  I
150	asked you what were the four forms of government of which you spoke,
150	and then Polemarchus and Adeimantus put in their word; and you began
150	again, and have found your way to the point at which we have now
150	arrived.
150	
150	Your recollection, I said, is most exact.
150	
150	Then, like a wrestler, he replied, you must put yourself again in the
150	same position; and let me ask the same questions, and do you give me
150	the same answer which you were about to give me then.
150	
150	Yes, if I can, I will, I said.
150	
150	I shall particularly wish to hear what were the four constitutions of
150	which you were speaking.
150	
150	That question, I said, is easily answered:  the four governments of
150	which I spoke, so far as they have distinct names, are, first, those of
150	Crete and Sparta, which are generally applauded; what is termed
150	oligarchy comes next; this is not equally approved, and is a form of
150	government which teems with evils:  thirdly, democracy, which naturally
150	follows oligarchy, although very different: and lastly comes tyranny,
150	great and famous, which differs from them all, and is the fourth and
150	worst disorder of a State.  I do not know, do you? of any other
150	constitution which can be said to have a distinct character.  There are
150	lordships and principalities which are bought and sold, and some other
150	intermediate forms of government.  But these are nondescripts and may
150	be found equally among Hellenes and among barbarians.
150	
150	Yes, he replied, we certainly hear of many curious forms of government
150	which exist among them.
150	
150	Do you know, I said, that governments vary as the dispositions of men
150	vary, and that there must be as many of the one as there are of the
150	other?  For we cannot suppose that States are made of 'oak and rock,'
150	and not out of the human natures which are in them, and which in a
150	figure turn the scale and draw other things after them?
150	
150	Yes, he said, the States are as the men are; they grow out of human
150	characters.
150	
150	Then if the constitutions of States are five, the dispositions of
150	individual minds will also be five?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	Him who answers to aristocracy, and whom we rightly call just and good,
150	we have already described.
150	
150	We have.
150	
150	Then let us now proceed to describe the inferior sort of natures, being
150	the contentious and ambitious, who answer to the Spartan polity; also
150	the oligarchical, democratical, and tyrannical.  Let us place the most
150	just by the side of the most unjust, and when we see them we shall be
150	able to compare the relative happiness or unhappiness of him who leads
150	a life of pure justice or pure injustice.  The enquiry will then be
