150	completed.  And we shall know whether we ought to pursue injustice, as
150	Thrasymachus advises, or in accordance with the conclusions of the
150	argument to prefer justice.
150	
150	Certainly, he replied, we must do as you say.
150	
150	Shall we follow our old plan, which we adopted with a view to
150	clearness, of taking the State first and then proceeding to the
150	individual, and begin with the government of honour?--I know of no name
150	for such a government other than timocracy, or perhaps timarchy.  We
150	will compare with this the like character in the individual; and, after
150	that, consider oligarchical man; and then again we will turn our
150	attention to democracy and the democratical man; and lastly, we will go
150	and view the city of tyranny, and once more take a look into the
150	tyrant's soul, and try to arrive at a satisfactory decision.
150	
150	That way of viewing and judging of the matter will be very suitable.
150	
150	First, then, I said, let us enquire how timocracy (the government of
150	honour) arises out of aristocracy (the government of the best).
150	Clearly, all political changes originate in divisions of the actual
150	governing power; a government which is united, however small, cannot be
150	moved.
150	
150	Very true, he said.
150	
150	In what way, then, will our city be moved, and in what manner the two
150	classes of auxiliaries and rulers disagree among themselves or with one
150	another?  Shall we, after the manner of Homer, pray the Muses to tell
150	us 'how discord first arose'? Shall we imagine them in solemn mockery,
150	to play and jest with us as if we were children, and to address us in a
150	lofty tragic vein, making believe to be in earnest?
150	
150	How would they address us?
150	
150	After this manner:--A city which is thus constituted can hardly be
150	shaken; but, seeing that everything which has a beginning has also an
150	end, even a constitution such as yours will not last for ever, but will
150	in time be dissolved.  And this is the dissolution:--In plants that
150	grow in the earth, as well as in animals that move on the earth's
150	surface, fertility and sterility of soul and body occur when the
150	circumferences of the circles of each are completed, which in
150	short-lived existences pass over a short space, and in long-lived ones
150	over a long space.  But to the knowledge of human fecundity and
150	sterility all the wisdom and education of your rulers will not attain;
150	the laws which regulate them will not be discovered by an intelligence
150	which is alloyed with sense, but will escape them, and they will bring
150	children into the world when they ought not.  Now that which is of
150	divine birth has a period which is contained in a perfect number, but
150	the period of human birth is comprehended in a number in which first
150	increments by involution and evolution (or squared and cubed) obtaining
150	three intervals and four terms of like and unlike, waxing and waning
150	numbers, make all the terms commensurable and agreeable to one another.
150	The base of these (3) with a third added (4) when combined with five
150	(20) and raised to the third power furnishes two harmonies; the first a
150	square which is a hundred times as great (400 = 4 X 100), and the other
150	a figure having one side equal to the former, but oblong, consisting of
150	a hundred numbers squared upon rational diameters of a square (i. e.
150	omitting fractions), the side of which is five (7 X 7 = 49 X 100 =
150	4900), each of them being less by one (than the perfect square which
150	includes the fractions, sc. 50) or less by two perfect squares of
150	irrational diameters (of a square the side of which is five = 50 + 50 =
150	100); and a hundred cubes of three (27 X 100 = 2700 + 4900 + 400 =
150	8000). Now this number represents a geometrical figure which has
150	control over the good and evil of births.  For when your guardians are
150	ignorant of the law of births, and unite bride and bridegroom out of
150	season, the children will not be goodly or fortunate.  And though only
150	the best of them will be appointed by their predecessors, still they
150	will be unworthy to hold their fathers' places, and when they come into
150	power as guardians, they will soon be found to fall in taking care of
150	us, the Muses, first by under-valuing music; which neglect will soon
150	extend to gymnastic; and hence the young men of your State will be less
150	cultivated.  In the succeeding generation rulers will be appointed who
150	have lost the guardian power of testing the metal of your different
150	races, which, like Hesiod's, are of gold and silver and brass and iron.
150	And so iron will be mingled with silver, and brass with gold, and hence
150	there will arise dissimilarity and inequality and irregularity, which
150	always and in all places are causes of hatred and war.  This the Muses
150	affirm to be the stock from which discord has sprung, wherever arising;
150	and this is their answer to us.
150	
150	Yes, and we may assume that they answer truly.
150	
150	Why, yes, I said, of course they answer truly; how can the Muses speak
150	falsely?
150	
150	And what do the Muses say next?
150	
150	When discord arose, then the two races were drawn different ways: the
150	iron and brass fell to acquiring money and land and houses and gold and
150	silver; but the gold and silver races, not wanting money but having the
150	true riches in their own nature, inclined towards virtue and the
150	ancient order of things.  There was a battle between them, and at last
150	they agreed to distribute their land and houses among individual
150	owners; and they enslaved their friends and maintainers, whom they had
150	formerly protected in the condition of freemen, and made of them
150	subjects and servants; and they themselves were engaged in war and in
150	keeping a watch against them.
150	
150	I believe that you have rightly conceived the origin of the change.
150	
150	And the new government which thus arises will be of a form intermediate
150	between oligarchy and aristocracy?
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	Such will be the change, and after the change has been made, how will
150	they proceed?  Clearly, the new State, being in a mean between
150	oligarchy and the perfect State, will partly follow one and partly the
150	other, and will also have some peculiarities.
150	
150	True, he said.
150	
150	In the honour given to rulers, in the abstinence of the warrior class
150	from agriculture, handicrafts, and trade in general, in the institution
150	of common meals, and in the attention paid to gymnastics and military
150	training--in all these respects this State will resemble the former.
150	
150	True.
150	
150	But in the fear of admitting philosophers to power, because they are no
150	longer to be had simple and earnest, but are made up of mixed elements;
150	and in turning from them to passionate and less complex characters, who
150	are by nature fitted for war rather than peace; and in the value set by
150	them upon military stratagems and contrivances, and in the waging of
150	everlasting wars--this State will be for the most part peculiar.
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	Yes, I said; and men of this stamp will be covetous of money, like
150	those who live in oligarchies; they will have, a fierce secret longing
150	after gold and silver, which they will hoard in dark places, having
150	magazines and treasuries of their own for the deposit and concealment
150	of them; also castles which are just nests for their eggs, and in which
150	they will spend large sums on their wives, or on any others whom they
150	please.
150	
150	That is most true, he said.
150	
150	And they are miserly because they have no means of openly acquiring the
150	money which they prize; they will spend that which is another man's on
150	the gratification of their desires, stealing their pleasures and
150	running away like children from the law, their father: they have been
150	schooled not by gentle influences but by force, for they have neglected
150	her who is the true Muse, the companion of reason and philosophy, and
150	have honoured gymnastic more than music.
150	
150	Undoubtedly, he said, the form of government which you describe is a
150	mixture of good and evil.
150	
150	Why, there is a mixture, I said; but one thing, and one thing only, is
150	predominantly seen,--the spirit of contention and ambition; and these
150	are due to the prevalence of the passionate or spirited element.
150	
150	Assuredly, he said.
150	
150	Such is the origin and such the character of this State, which has been
150	described in outline only; the more perfect execution was not required,
150	for a sketch is enough to show the type of the most perfectly just and
150	most perfectly unjust; and to go through all the States and all the
150	characters of men, omitting none of them, would be an interminable
150	labour.
150	
150	Very true, he replied.
150	
150	Now what man answers to this form of government-how did he come into
150	being, and what is he like?
150	
150	SOCRATES - ADEIMANTUS
150	
150	I think, said Adeimantus, that in the spirit of contention which
150	characterises him, he is not unlike our friend Glaucon.
150	
150	Perhaps, I said, he may be like him in that one point; but there are
150	other respects in which he is very different.
150	
150	In what respects?
150	
150	He should have more of self-assertion and be less cultivated, and yet a
150	friend of culture; and he should be a good listener, but no speaker.
150	Such a person is apt to be rough with slaves, unlike the educated man,
150	who is too proud for that; and he will also be courteous to freemen,
150	and remarkably obedient to authority; he is a lover of power and a
150	lover of honour; claiming to be a ruler, not because he is eloquent, or
150	on any ground of that sort, but because he is a soldier and has
150	performed feats of arms; he is also a lover of gymnastic exercises and
150	of the chase.
150	
150	Yes, that is the type of character which answers to timocracy.
150	
150	Such an one will despise riches only when he is young; but as he gets
150	older he will be more and more attracted to them, because he has a
150	piece of the avaricious nature in him, and is not singleminded towards
150	virtue, having lost his best guardian.
150	
150	Who was that? said Adeimantus.
150	
150	Philosophy, I said, tempered with music, who comes and takes her abode
150	in a man, and is the only saviour of his virtue throughout life.
150	
150	Good, he said.
150	
150	Such, I said, is the timocratical youth, and he is like the
150	timocratical State.
150	
150	Exactly.
150	
150	His origin is as follows:--He is often the young son of a grave father,
150	who dwells in an ill-governed city, of which he declines the honours
150	and offices, and will not go to law, or exert himself in any way, but
150	is ready to waive his rights in order that he may escape trouble.
150	
150	And how does the son come into being?
150	
150	The character of the son begins to develop when he hears his mother
150	complaining that her husband has no place in the government, of which
150	the consequence is that she has no precedence among other women.
150	Further, when she sees her husband not very eager about money, and
150	instead of battling and railing in the law courts or assembly, taking
150	whatever happens to him quietly; and when she observes that his
150	thoughts always centre in himself, while he treats her with very
150	considerable indifference, she is annoyed, and says to her son that his
150	father is only half a man and far too easy-going: adding all the other
150	complaints about her own ill-treatment which women are so fond of
150	rehearsing.
150	
150	Yes, said Adeimantus, they give us plenty of them, and their complaints
150	are so like themselves.
150	
150	And you know, I said, that the old servants also, who are supposed to
150	be attached to the family, from time to time talk privately in the same
150	strain to the son; and if they see any one who owes money to his
150	father, or is wronging him in any way, and he falls to prosecute them,
150	they tell the youth that when he grows up he must retaliate upon people
150	of this sort, and be more of a man than his father.  He has only to
150	walk abroad and he hears and sees the same sort of thing: those who do
150	their own business in the city are called simpletons, and held in no
150	esteem, while the busy-bodies are honoured and applauded.  The result
150	is that the young man, hearing and seeing all these thing--hearing too,
150	the words of his father, and having a nearer view of his way of life,
150	and making comparisons of him and others--is drawn opposite ways: while
150	his father is watering and nourishing the rational principle in his
150	soul, the others are encouraging the passionate and appetitive; and he
150	being not originally of a bad nature, but having kept bad company, is
150	at last brought by their joint influence to a middle point, and gives
150	up the kingdom which is within him to the middle principle of
150	contentiousness and passion, and becomes arrogant and ambitious.
150	
150	You seem to me to have described his origin perfectly.
150	
150	Then we have now, I said, the second form of government and the second
150	type of character?
150	
150	We have.
150	
150	Next, let us look at another man who, as Aeschylus says,
150	
150	    Is set over against another State;
150	
150	or rather, as our plan requires, begin with the State.
150	
150	By all means.
150	
150	I believe that oligarchy follows next in order.
150	
150	And what manner of government do you term oligarchy?
150	
150	A government resting on a valuation of property, in which the rich have
150	power and the poor man is deprived of it.
150	
150	I understand, he replied.
150	
150	Ought I not to begin by describing how the change from timocracy to
150	oligarchy arises?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	Well, I said, no eyes are required in order to see how the one passes
150	into the other.
150	
150	How?
150	
150	The accumulation of gold in the treasury of private individuals is ruin
150	the of timocracy; they invent illegal modes of expenditure; for what do
150	they or their wives care about the law?
150	
150	Yes, indeed.
150	
150	And then one, seeing another grow rich, seeks to rival him, and thus
150	the great mass of the citizens become lovers of money.
150	
150	Likely enough.
150	
150	And so they grow richer and richer, and the more they think of making a
150	fortune the less they think of virtue; for when riches and virtue are
150	placed together in the scales of the balance, the one always rises as
150	the other falls.
150	
150	True.
150	
150	And in proportion as riches and rich men are honoured in the State,
150	virtue and the virtuous are dishonoured.
150	
150	Clearly.
150	
150	And what is honoured is cultivated, and that which has no honour is
150	neglected.
150	
150	That is obvious.
150	
150	And so at last, instead of loving contention and glory, men become
150	lovers of trade and money; they honour and look up to the rich man, and
150	make a ruler of him, and dishonour the poor man.
150	
150	They do so.
150	
150	They next proceed to make a law which fixes a sum of money as the
150	qualification of citizenship; the sum is higher in one place and lower
150	in another, as the oligarchy is more or less exclusive; and they allow
150	no one whose property falls below the amount fixed to have any share in
150	the government.  These changes in the constitution they effect by force
150	of arms, if intimidation has not already done their work.
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	And this, speaking generally, is the way in which oligarchy is
150	established.
150	
150	Yes, he said; but what are the characteristics of this form of
150	government, and what are the defects of which we were speaking?
150	
150	First of all, I said, consider the nature of the qualification just
150	think what would happen if pilots were to be chosen according to their
150	property, and a poor man were refused permission to steer, even though
150	he were a better pilot?
150	
150	You mean that they would shipwreck?
150	
150	Yes; and is not this true of the government of anything?
150	
150	I should imagine so.
150	
150	Except a city?--or would you include a city?
150	
150	Nay, he said, the case of a city is the strongest of all, inasmuch as
150	the rule of a city is the greatest and most difficult of all.
150	
150	This, then, will be the first great defect of oligarchy?
150	
150	Clearly.
150	
150	And here is another defect which is quite as bad.
150	
150	What defect?
150	
150	The inevitable division:  such a State is not one, but two States, the
150	one of poor, the other of rich men; and they are living on the same
150	spot and always conspiring against one another.
150	
150	That, surely, is at least as bad.
150	
150	Another discreditable feature is, that, for a like reason, they are
150	incapable of carrying on any war.  Either they arm the multitude, and
150	then they are more afraid of them than of the enemy; or, if they do not
150	call them out in the hour of battle, they are oligarchs indeed, few to
150	fight as they are few to rule.  And at the same time their fondness for
150	money makes them unwilling to pay taxes.
150	
150	How discreditable!
150	
150	And, as we said before, under such a constitution the same persons have
150	too many callings--they are husbandmen, tradesmen, warriors, all in
150	one.  Does that look well?
150	
150	Anything but well.
150	
150	There is another evil which is, perhaps, the greatest of all, and to
150	which this State first begins to be liable.
150	
150	What evil?
150	
150	A man may sell all that he has, and another may acquire his property;
150	yet after the sale he may dwell in the city of which he is no longer a
150	part, being neither trader, nor artisan, nor horseman, nor hoplite, but
150	only a poor, helpless creature.
150	
150	Yes, that is an evil which also first begins in this State.
150	
150	The evil is certainly not prevented there; for oligarchies have both
150	the extremes of great wealth and utter poverty.
150	
150	True.
150	
150	But think again:  In his wealthy days, while he was spending his money,
150	was a man of this sort a whit more good to the State for the purposes
150	of citizenship?  Or did he only seem to be a member of the ruling body,
150	although in truth he was neither ruler nor subject, but just a
150	spendthrift?
150	
150	As you say, he seemed to be a ruler, but was only a spendthrift.
150	
150	May we not say that this is the drone in the house who is like the
150	drone in the honeycomb, and that the one is the plague of the city as
150	the other is of the hive?
150	
150	Just so, Socrates.
150	
150	And God has made the flying drones, Adeimantus, all without stings,
150	whereas of the walking drones he has made some without stings but
150	others have dreadful stings; of the stingless class are those who in
150	their old age end as paupers; of the stingers come all the criminal
150	class, as they are termed.
150	
150	Most true, he said.
150	
150	Clearly then, whenever you see paupers in a State, somewhere in that
150	neighborhood there are hidden away thieves, and cutpurses and robbers
150	of temples, and all sorts of malefactors.
150	
150	Clearly.
150	
150	Well, I said, and in oligarchical States do you not find paupers?
150	
150	Yes, he said; nearly everybody is a pauper who is not a ruler.
150	
150	And may we be so bold as to affirm that there are also many criminals
150	to be found in them, rogues who have stings, and whom the authorities
150	are careful to restrain by force?
150	
150	Certainly, we may be so bold.
150	
150	The existence of such persons is to be attributed to want of education,
150	ill-training, and an evil constitution of the State?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	Such, then, is the form and such are the evils of oligarchy; and there
150	may be many other evils.
150	
150	Very likely.
150	
150	Then oligarchy, or the form of government in which the rulers are
150	elected for their wealth, may now be dismissed.  Let us next proceed to
150	consider the nature and origin of the individual who answers to this
150	State.
150	
150	By all means.
150	
150	Does not the timocratical man change into the oligarchical on this wise?
150	
150	How?
150	
150	A time arrives when the representative of timocracy has a son: at first
150	he begins by emulating his father and walking in his footsteps, but
150	presently he sees him of a sudden foundering against the State as upon
150	a sunken reef, and he and all that he has is lost; he may have been a
150	general or some other high officer who is brought to trial under a
150	prejudice raised by informers, and either put to death, or exiled, or
150	deprived of the privileges of a citizen, and all his property taken
150	from him.
150	
150	Nothing more likely.
150	
150	And the son has seen and known all this--he is a ruined man, and his
150	fear has taught him to knock ambition and passion head-foremost from
150	his bosom's throne; humbled by poverty he takes to money-making and by
150	mean and miserly savings and hard work gets a fortune together.  Is not
150	such an one likely to seat the concupiscent and covetous element on the
150	vacant throne and to suffer it to play the great king within him, girt
150	with tiara and chain and scimitar?
150	
150	Most true, he replied.
150	
150	And when he has made reason and spirit sit down on the ground
150	obediently on either side of their sovereign, and taught them to know
150	their place, he compels the one to think only of how lesser sums may be
150	turned into larger ones, and will not allow the other to worship and
150	admire anything but riches and rich men, or to be ambitious of anything
150	so much as the acquisition of wealth and the means of acquiring it.
150	
150	Of all changes, he said, there is none so speedy or so sure as the
150	conversion of the ambitious youth into the avaricious one.
150	
150	And the avaricious, I said, is the oligarchical youth?
150	
150	Yes, he said; at any rate the individual out of whom he came is like
150	the State out of which oligarchy came.
150	
150	Let us then consider whether there is any likeness between them.
150	
150	Very good.
150	
150	First, then, they resemble one another in the value which they set upon
150	wealth?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	Also in their penurious, laborious character; the individual only
150	satisfies his necessary appetites, and confines his expenditure to
150	them; his other desires he subdues, under the idea that they are
150	unprofitable.
150	
150	True.
150	
150	He is a shabby fellow, who saves something out of everything and makes
150	a purse for himself; and this is the sort of man whom the vulgar
150	applaud.  Is he not a true image of the State which he represents?
150	
150	He appears to me to be so; at any rate money is highly valued by him as
150	well as by the State.
150	
150	You see that he is not a man of cultivation, I said.
150	
150	I imagine not, he said; had he been educated he would never have made a
150	blind god director of his chorus, or given him chief honour.
150	
150	Excellent!  I said.  Yet consider:  Must we not further admit that
150	owing to this want of cultivation there will be found in him dronelike
150	desires as of pauper and rogue, which are forcibly kept down by his
150	general habit of life?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	Do you know where you will have to look if you want to discover his
150	rogueries?
150	
150	Where must I look?
150	
150	You should see him where he has some great opportunity of acting
150	dishonestly, as in the guardianship of an orphan.
150	
150	Aye.
150	
150	It will be clear enough then that in his ordinary dealings which give
150	him a reputation for honesty he coerces his bad passions by an enforced
150	virtue; not making them see that they are wrong, or taming them by
150	reason, but by necessity and fear constraining them, and because he
150	trembles for his possessions.
150	
150	To be sure.
150	
150	Yes, indeed, my dear friend, but you will find that the natural desires
150	of the drone commonly exist in him all the same whenever he has to
150	spend what is not his own.
150	
150	Yes, and they will be strong in him too.
150	
150	The man, then, will be at war with himself; he will be two men, and not
150	one; but, in general, his better desires will be found to prevail over
150	his inferior ones.
150	
150	True.
150	
150	For these reasons such an one will be more respectable than most
150	people; yet the true virtue of a unanimous and harmonious soul will
150	flee far away and never come near him.
150	
150	I should expect so.
150	
150	And surely, the miser individually will be an ignoble competitor in a
150	State for any prize of victory, or other object of honourable ambition;
150	he will not spend his money in the contest for glory; so afraid is he
150	of awakening his expensive appetites and inviting them to help and join
150	in the struggle; in true oligarchical fashion he fights with a small
150	part only of his resources, and the result commonly is that he loses
150	the prize and saves his money.
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	Can we any longer doubt, then, that the miser and money-maker answers
150	to the oligarchical State?
150	
150	There can be no doubt.
150	
150	Next comes democracy; of this the origin and nature have still to be
150	considered by us; and then we will enquire into the ways of the
150	democratic man, and bring him up for judgement.
150	
150	That, he said, is our method.
150	
150	Well, I said, and how does the change from oligarchy into democracy
150	arise?  Is it not on this wise?--The good at which such a State alms is
150	to become as rich as possible, a desire which is insatiable?
150	
150	What then?
150	
150	The rulers, being aware that their power rests upon their wealth,
150	refuse to curtail by law the extravagance of the spendthrift youth
150	because they gain by their ruin; they take interest from them and buy
150	up their estates and thus increase their own wealth and importance?
150	
150	To be sure.
150	
150	There can be no doubt that the love of wealth and the spirit of
150	moderation cannot exist together in citizens of the same State to any
150	considerable extent; one or the other will be disregarded.
150	
150	That is tolerably clear.
150	
150	And in oligarchical States, from the general spread of carelessness and
150	extravagance, men of good family have often been reduced to beggary?
150	
150	Yes, often.
150	
150	And still they remain in the city; there they are, ready to sting and
150	fully armed, and some of them owe money, some have forfeited their
150	citizenship; a third class are in both predicaments; and they hate and
150	conspire against those who have got their property, and against
150	everybody else, and are eager for revolution.
150	
150	That is true.
150	
150	On the other hand, the men of business, stooping as they walk, and
150	pretending not even to see those whom they have already ruined, insert
150	their sting--that is, their money--into some one else who is not on his
150	guard against them, and recover the parent sum many times over
150	multiplied into a family of children:  and so they make drone and
150	pauper to abound in the State.
150	
150	Yes, he said, there are plenty of them--that is certain.
150	
150	The evil blazes up like a fire; and they will not extinguish it, either
150	by restricting a man's use of his own property, or by another remedy:
150	
150	What other?
150	
150	One which is the next best, and has the advantage of compelling the
150	citizens to look to their characters:--Let there be a general rule that
150	every one shall enter into voluntary contracts at his own risk, and
150	there will be less of this scandalous money-making, and the evils of
150	which we were speaking will be greatly lessened in the State.
150	
150	Yes, they will be greatly lessened.
150	
150	At present the governors, induced by the motives which I have named,
150	treat their subjects badly; while they and their adherents, especially
150	the young men of the governing class, are habituated to lead a life of
150	luxury and idleness both of body and mind; they do nothing, and are
150	incapable of resisting either pleasure or pain.
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	They themselves care only for making money, and are as indifferent as
150	the pauper to the cultivation of virtue.
150	
150	Yes, quite as indifferent.
150	
150	Such is the state of affairs which prevails among them.  And often
150	rulers and their subjects may come in one another's way, whether on a
150	pilgrimage or a march, as fellow-soldiers or fellow-sailors; aye, and
150	they may observe the behaviour of each other in the very moment of
150	danger--for where danger is, there is no fear that the poor will be
150	despised by the rich--and very likely the wiry sunburnt poor man may be
150	placed in battle at the side of a wealthy one who has never spoilt his
150	complexion and has plenty of superfluous flesh--when he sees such an
150	one puffing and at his wit's end, how can he avoid drawing the
150	conclusion that men like him are only rich because no one has the
150	courage to despoil them?  And when they meet in private will not people
150	be saying to one another 'Our warriors are not good for much'?
150	
150	Yes, he said, I am quite aware that this is their way of talking.
150	
150	And, as in a body which is diseased the addition of a touch from
150	without may bring on illness, and sometimes even when there is no
150	external provocation a commotion may arise within-in the same way
150	wherever there is weakness in the State there is also likely to be
150	illness, of which the occasions may be very slight, the one party
150	introducing from without their oligarchical, the other their
150	democratical allies, and then the State falls sick, and is at war with
150	herself; and may be at times distracted, even when there is no external
150	cause.
150	
150	Yes, surely.
150	
150	And then democracy comes into being after the poor have conquered their
150	opponents, slaughtering some and banishing some, while to the remainder
150	they give an equal share of freedom and power; and this is the form of
150	government in which the magistrates are commonly elected by lot.
150	
150	Yes, he said, that is the nature of democracy, whether the revolution
150	has been effected by arms, or whether fear has caused the opposite
150	party to withdraw.
150	
150	And now what is their manner of life, and what sort of a government
150	have they? for as the government is, such will be the man.
150	
150	Clearly, he said.
150	
150	In the first place, are they not free; and is not the city full of
150	freedom and frankness--a man may say and do what he likes?
150	
150	'Tis said so, he replied.
150	
150	And where freedom is, the individual is clearly able to order for
150	himself his own life as he pleases?
150	
150	Clearly.
150	
150	Then in this kind of State there will be the greatest variety of human
150	natures?
150	
150	There will.
150	
150	This, then, seems likely to be the fairest of States, being an
150	embroidered robe which is spangled with every sort of flower.  And just
150	as women and children think a variety of colours to be of all things
150	most charming, so there are many men to whom this State, which is
150	spangled with the manners and characters of mankind, will appear to be
150	the fairest of States.
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	Yes, my good Sir, and there will be no better in which to look for a
150	government.
150	
150	Why?
150	
150	Because of the liberty which reigns there--they have a complete
150	assortment of constitutions; and he who has a mind to establish a
150	State, as we have been doing, must go to a democracy as he would to a
150	bazaar at which they sell them, and pick out the one that suits him;
150	then, when he has made his choice, he may found his State.
150	
150	He will be sure to have patterns enough.
150	
150	And there being no necessity, I said, for you to govern in this State,
150	even if you have the capacity, or to be governed, unless you like, or
150	go to war when the rest go to war, or to be at peace when others are at
150	peace, unless you are so disposed--there being no necessity also,
150	because some law forbids you to hold office or be a dicast, that you
150	should not hold office or be a dicast, if you have a fancy--is not this
150	a way of life which for the moment is supremely delightful
150	
150	For the moment, yes.
150	
150	And is not their humanity to the condemned in some cases quite
150	charming?  Have you not observed how, in a democracy, many persons,
150	although they have been sentenced to death or exile, just stay where
150	they are and walk about the world--the gentleman parades like a hero,
150	and nobody sees or cares?
150	
150	Yes, he replied, many and many a one.
150	
150	See too, I said, the forgiving spirit of democracy, and the 'don't
150	care' about trifles, and the disregard which she shows of all the fine
150	principles which we solemnly laid down at the foundation of the
150	city--as when we said that, except in the case of some rarely gifted
150	nature, there never will be a good man who has not from his childhood
150	been used to play amid things of beauty and make of them a joy and a
150	study--how grandly does she trample all these fine notions of ours
150	under her feet, never giving a thought to the pursuits which make a
150	statesman, and promoting to honour any one who professes to be the
150	people's friend.
150	
150	Yes, she is of a noble spirit.
150	
150	These and other kindred characteristics are proper to democracy, which
150	is a charming form of government, full of variety and disorder, and
150	dispensing a sort of equality to equals and unequals alike.
150	
150	We know her well.
150	
150	Consider now, I said, what manner of man the individual is, or rather
150	consider, as in the case of the State, how he comes into being.
150	
150	Very good, he said.
150	
150	Is not this the way--he is the son of the miserly and oligarchical
150	father who has trained him in his own habits?
150	
150	Exactly.
150	
150	And, like his father, he keeps under by force the pleasures which are
150	of the spending and not of the getting sort, being those which are
150	called unnecessary?
150	
150	Obviously.
150	
150	Would you like, for the sake of clearness, to distinguish which are the
150	necessary and which are the unnecessary pleasures?
150	
150	I should.
150	
150	Are not necessary pleasures those of which we cannot get rid, and of
150	which the satisfaction is a benefit to us?  And they are rightly so,
150	because we are framed by nature to desire both what is beneficial and
150	what is necessary, and cannot help it.
150	
150	True.
150	
150	We are not wrong therefore in calling them necessary?
150	
150	We are not.
150	
150	And the desires of which a man may get rid, if he takes pains from his
150	youth upwards--of which the presence, moreover, does no good, and in
150	some cases the reverse of good--shall we not be right in saying that
150	all these are unnecessary?
150	
150	Yes, certainly.
150	
150	Suppose we select an example of either kind, in order that we may have
150	a general notion of them?
150	
150	Very good.
150	
150	Will not the desire of eating, that is, of simple food and condiments,
150	in so far as they are required for health and strength, be of the
150	necessary class?
150	
150	That is what I should suppose.
150	
150	The pleasure of eating is necessary in two ways; it does us good and it
150	is essential to the continuance of life?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	But the condiments are only necessary in so far as they are good for
150	health?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	And the desire which goes beyond this, or more delicate food, or other
150	luxuries, which might generally be got rid of, if controlled and
150	trained in youth, and is hurtful to the body, and hurtful to the soul
150	in the pursuit of wisdom and virtue, may be rightly called unnecessary?
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	May we not say that these desires spend, and that the others make money
150	because they conduce to production?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	And of the pleasures of love, and all other pleasures, the same holds
150	good?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	And the drone of whom we spoke was he who was surfeited in pleasures
150	and desires of this sort, and was the slave of the unnecessary desires,
150	whereas he who was subject o the necessary only was miserly and
150	oligarchical?
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	Again, let us see how the democratical man grows out of the
150	oligarchical: the following, as I suspect, is commonly the process.
150	
150	What is the process?
150	
150	When a young man who has been brought up as we were just now
150	describing, in a vulgar and miserly way, has tasted drones' honey and
150	has come to associate with fierce and crafty natures who are able to
150	provide for him all sorts of refinements and varieties of
150	pleasure--then, as you may imagine, the change will begin of the
150	oligarchical principle within him into the democratical?
150	
150	Inevitably.
150	
150	And as in the city like was helping like, and the change was effected
150	by an alliance from without assisting one division of the citizens, so
150	too the young man is changed by a class of desires coming from without
150	to assist the desires within him, that which is and alike again helping
150	that which is akin and alike?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	And if there be any ally which aids the oligarchical principle within
150	him, whether the influence of a father or of kindred, advising or
150	rebuking him, then there arises in his soul a faction and an opposite
150	faction, and he goes to war with himself.
150	
150	It must be so.
150	
150	And there are times when the democratical principle gives way to the
150	oligarchical, and some of his desires die, and others are banished; a
150	spirit of reverence enters into the young man's soul and order is
150	restored.
150	
150	Yes, he said, that sometimes happens.
150	
150	And then, again, after the old desires have been driven out, fresh ones
150	spring up, which are akin to them, and because he, their father, does
150	not know how to educate them, wax fierce and numerous.
150	
150	Yes, he said, that is apt to be the way.
150	
150	They draw him to his old associates, and holding secret intercourse
150	with them, breed and multiply in him.
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	At length they seize upon the citadel of the young man's soul, which
150	they perceive to be void of all accomplishments and fair pursuits and
150	true words, which make their abode in the minds of men who are dear to
150	the gods, and are their best guardians and sentinels.
150	
150	None better.
150	
150	False and boastful conceits and phrases mount upwards and take their
150	place.
150	
150	They are certain to do so.
150	
150	And so the young man returns into the country of the lotus-eaters, and
150	takes up his dwelling there in the face of all men; and if any help be
150	sent by his friends to the oligarchical part of him, the aforesaid vain
150	conceits shut the gate of the king's fastness; and they will neither
150	allow the embassy itself to enter, private if private advisers offer
150	the fatherly counsel of the aged will they listen to them or receive
150	them.  There is a battle and they gain the day, and then modesty, which
150	they call silliness, is ignominiously thrust into exile by them, and
150	temperance, which they nickname unmanliness, is trampled in the mire
150	and cast forth; they persuade men that moderation and orderly
150	expenditure are vulgarity and meanness, and so, by the help of a rabble
150	of evil appetites, they drive them beyond the border.
150	
150	Yes, with a will.
150	
150	And when they have emptied and swept clean the soul of him who is now
150	in their power and who is being initiated by them in great mysteries,
150	the next thing is to bring back to their house insolence and anarchy
150	and waste and impudence in bright array having garlands on their heads,
150	and a great company with them, hymning their praises and calling them
150	by sweet names; insolence they term breeding, and anarchy liberty, and
150	waste magnificence, and impudence courage.  And so the young man passes
150	out of his original nature, which was trained in the school of
150	necessity, into the freedom and libertinism of useless and unnecessary
150	pleasures.
150	
150	Yes, he said, the change in him is visible enough.
150	
150	After this he lives on, spending his money and labour and time on
150	unnecessary pleasures quite as much as on necessary ones; but if he be
150	fortunate, and is not too much disordered in his wits, when years have
150	elapsed, and the heyday of passion is over--supposing that he then
150	re-admits into the city some part of the exiled virtues, and does not
150	wholly give himself up to their successors--in that case he balances
150	his pleasures and lives in a sort of equilibrium, putting the
150	government of himself into the hands of the one which comes first and
150	wins the turn; and when he has had enough of that, then into the hands
150	of another; he despises none of them but encourages them all equally.
150	
150	Very true, he said.
150	
150	Neither does he receive or let pass into the fortress any true word of
150	advice; if any one says to him that some pleasures are the
150	satisfactions of good and noble desires, and others of evil desires,
150	and that he ought to use and honour some and chastise and master the
150	others--whenever this is repeated to him he shakes his head and says
150	that they are all alike, and that one is as good as another.
150	
150	Yes, he said; that is the way with him.
150	
150	Yes, I said, he lives from day to day indulging the appetite of the
150	hour; and sometimes he is lapped in drink and strains of the flute;
150	then he becomes a water-drinker, and tries to get thin; then he takes a
150	turn at gymnastics; sometimes idling and neglecting everything, then
150	once more living the life of a philosopher; often he-is busy with
150	politics, and starts to his feet and says and does whatever comes into
150	his head; and, if he is emulous of any one who is a warrior, off he is
150	in that direction, or of men of business, once more in that.  His life
150	has neither law nor order; and this distracted existence he terms joy
150	and bliss and freedom; and so he goes on.
150	
150	Yes, he replied, he is all liberty and equality.
150	
150	Yes, I said; his life is motley and manifold and an epitome of the
150	lives of many;--he answers to the State which we described as fair and
150	spangled.  And many a man and many a woman will take him for their
150	pattern, and many a constitution and many an example of manners is
150	contained in him.
150	
150	Just so.
150	
150	Let him then be set over against democracy; he may truly be called the
150	democratic man.
150	
150	Let that be his place, he said.
150	
150	Last of all comes the most beautiful of all, man and State alike,
150	tyranny and the tyrant; these we have now to consider.
150	
150	Quite true, he said.
150	
150	Say then, my friend, in what manner does tyranny arise?--that it has a
150	democratic origin is evident.
150	
150	Clearly.
150	
150	And does not tyranny spring from democracy in the same manner as
150	democracy from oligarchy--I mean, after a sort?
150	
150	How?
150	
150	The good which oligarchy proposed to itself and the means by which it
150	was maintained was excess of wealth--am I not right?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And the insatiable desire of wealth and the neglect of all other things
150	for the sake of money-getting was also the ruin of oligarchy?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	And democracy has her own good, of which the insatiable desire brings
150	her to dissolution?
150	
150	What good?
150	
150	Freedom, I replied; which, as they tell you in a democracy, is the
150	glory of the State--and that therefore in a democracy alone will the
150	freeman of nature deign to dwell.
150	
150	Yes; the saying is in everybody's mouth.
150	
150	I was going to observe, that the insatiable desire of this and the
150	neglect of other things introduces the change in democracy, which
150	occasions a demand for tyranny.
150	
150	How so?
150	
150	When a democracy which is thirsting for freedom has evil cupbearers
150	presiding over the feast, and has drunk too deeply of the strong wine
150	of freedom, then, unless her rulers are very amenable and give a
150	plentiful draught, she calls them to account and punishes them, and
150	says that they are cursed oligarchs.
150	
150	Yes, he replied, a very common occurrence.
150	
150	Yes, I said; and loyal citizens are insultingly termed by her slaves
150	who hug their chains and men of naught; she would have subjects who are
150	like rulers, and rulers who are like subjects: these are men after her
150	own heart, whom she praises and honours both in private and public.
150	Now, in such a State, can liberty have any limit?
150	
150	Certainly not.
150	
150	By degrees the anarchy finds a way into private houses, and ends by
150	getting among the animals and infecting them.
150	
150	How do you mean?
150	
150	I mean that the father grows accustomed to descend to the level of his
150	sons and to fear them, and the son is on a level with his father, he
150	having no respect or reverence for either of his parents; and this is
150	his freedom, and metic is equal with the citizen and the citizen with
150	the metic, and the stranger is quite as good as either.
150	
150	Yes, he said, that is the way.
150	
150	And these are not the only evils, I said--there are several lesser
150	ones: In such a state of society the master fears and flatters his
150	scholars, and the scholars despise their masters and tutors; young and
150	old are all alike; and the young man is on a level with the old, and is
150	ready to compete with him in word or deed; and old men condescend to
150	the young and are full of pleasantry and gaiety; they are loth to be
150	thought morose and authoritative, and therefore they adopt the manners
150	of the young.
150	
150	Quite true, he said.
150	
150	The last extreme of popular liberty is when the slave bought with
150	money, whether male or female, is just as free as his or her purchaser;
150	nor must I forget to tell of the liberty and equality of the two sexes
150	in relation to each other.
150	
150	Why not, as Aeschylus says, utter the word which rises to our lips?
150	
150	That is what I am doing, I replied; and I must add that no one who does
150	not know would believe, how much greater is the liberty which the
150	animals who are under the dominion of man have in a democracy than in
150	any other State:  for truly, the she-dogs, as the proverb says, are as
150	good as their she-mistresses, and the horses and asses have a way of
150	marching along with all the rights and dignities of freemen; and they
150	will run at anybody who comes in their way if he does not leave the
150	road clear for them:  and all things are just ready to burst with
150	liberty.
150	
150	When I take a country walk, he said, I often experience what you
150	describe.  You and I have dreamed the same thing.
150	
150	And above all, I said, and as the result of all, see how sensitive the
150	citizens become; they chafe impatiently at the least touch of authority
150	and at length, as you know, they cease to care even for the laws,
150	written or unwritten; they will have no one over them.
150	
150	Yes, he said, I know it too well.
150	
150	Such, my friend, I said, is the fair and glorious beginning out of
150	which springs tyranny.
150	
150	Glorious indeed, he said.  But what is the next step?
150	
150	The ruin of oligarchy is the ruin of democracy; the same disease
150	magnified and intensified by liberty overmasters democracy--the truth
150	being that the excessive increase of anything often causes a reaction
150	in the opposite direction; and this is the case not only in the seasons
150	and in vegetable and animal life, but above all in forms of government.
150	
150	True.
150	
150	The excess of liberty, whether in States or individuals, seems only to
150	pass into excess of slavery.
150	
150	Yes, the natural order.
150	
150	And so tyranny naturally arises out of democracy, and the most
150	aggravated form of tyranny and slavery out of the most extreme form of
150	liberty?
150	
150	As we might expect.
150	
150	That, however, was not, as I believe, your question-you rather desired
150	to know what is that disorder which is generated alike in oligarchy and
150	democracy, and is the ruin of both?
150	
150	Just so, he replied.
150	
150	Well, I said, I meant to refer to the class of idle spendthrifts, of
150	whom the more courageous are the-leaders and the more timid the
150	followers, the same whom we were comparing to drones, some stingless,
150	and others having stings.
150	
150	A very just comparison.
150	
150	These two classes are the plagues of every city in which they are
150	generated, being what phlegm and bile are to the body.  And the good
150	physician and lawgiver of the State ought, like the wise bee-master, to
150	keep them at a distance and prevent, if possible, their ever coming in;
150	and if they have anyhow found a way in, then he should have them and
150	their cells cut out as speedily as possible.
150	
150	Yes, by all means, he said.
150	
150	Then, in order that we may see clearly what we are doing, let us
150	imagine democracy to be divided, as indeed it is, into three classes;
150	for in the first place freedom creates rather more drones in the
150	democratic than there were in the oligarchical State.
150	
150	That is true.
150	
150	And in the democracy they are certainly more intensified.
150	
150	How so?
150	
150	Because in the oligarchical State they are disqualified and driven from
150	office, and therefore they cannot train or gather strength; whereas in
150	a democracy they are almost the entire ruling power, and while the
150	keener sort speak and act, the rest keep buzzing about the bema and do
150	not suffer a word to be said on the other side; hence in democracies
150	almost everything is managed by the drones.
150	
150	Very true, he said.
150	
150	Then there is another class which is always being severed from the mass.
150	
150	What is that?
150	
150	They are the orderly class, which in a nation of traders sure to be the
150	richest.
150	
150	Naturally so.
150	
150	They are the most squeezable persons and yield the largest amount of
150	honey to the drones.
150	
150	Why, he said, there is little to be squeezed out of people who have
150	little.
150	
150	And this is called the wealthy class, and the drones feed upon them.
150	
150	That is pretty much the case, he said.
150	
150	The people are a third class, consisting of those who work with their
150	own hands; they are not politicians, and have not much to live upon.
150	This, when assembled, is the largest and most powerful class in a
150	democracy.
150	
150	True, he said; but then the multitude is seldom willing to congregate
150	unless they get a little honey.
150	
150	And do they not share?  I said.  Do not their leaders deprive the rich
150	of their estates and distribute them among the people; at the same time
150	taking care to reserve the larger part for themselves?
150	
150	Why, yes, he said, to that extent the people do share.
150	
150	And the persons whose property is taken from them are compelled to
150	defend themselves before the people as they best can?
150	
150	What else can they do?
150	
150	And then, although they may have no desire of change, the others charge
150	them with plotting against the people and being friends of oligarchy?
150	True.
150	
150	And the end is that when they see the people, not of their own accord,
150	but through ignorance, and because they are deceived by informers,
150	seeking to do them wrong, then at last they are forced to become
150	oligarchs in reality; they do not wish to be, but the sting of the
150	drones torments them and breeds revolution in them.
150	
150	That is exactly the truth.
150	
150	Then come impeachments and judgments and trials of one another.
150	
150	True.
150	
150	The people have always some champion whom they set over them and nurse
150	into greatness.
150	
150	Yes, that is their way.
150	
150	This and no other is the root from which a tyrant springs; when he
150	first appears above ground he is a protector.
150	
150	Yes, that is quite clear.
150	
150	How then does a protector begin to change into a tyrant?  Clearly when
150	he does what the man is said to do in the tale of the Arcadian temple
150	of Lycaean Zeus.
150	
150	What tale?
150	
150	The tale is that he who has tasted the entrails of a single human
150	victim minced up with the entrails of other victims is destined to
150	become a wolf.  Did you never hear it?
150	
150	Oh, yes.
150	
150	And the protector of the people is like him; having a mob entirely at
150	his disposal, he is not restrained from shedding the blood of kinsmen;
150	by the favourite method of false accusation he brings them into court
150	and murders them, making the life of man to disappear, and with unholy
150	tongue and lips tasting the blood of his fellow citizen; some he kills
150	and others he banishes, at the same time hinting at the abolition of
150	debts and partition of lands:  and after this, what will be his
150	destiny?  Must he not either perish at the hands of his enemies, or
150	from being a man become a wolf--that is, a tyrant?
150	
150	Inevitably.
150	
150	This, I said, is he who begins to make a party against the rich?
150	
150	The same.
150	
150	After a while he is driven out, but comes back, in spite of his
150	enemies, a tyrant full grown.
150	
150	That is clear.
150	
150	And if they are unable to expel him, or to get him condemned to death
150	by a public accusation, they conspire to assassinate him.
150	
150	Yes, he said, that is their usual way.
150	
150	Then comes the famous request for a bodyguard, which is the device of
150	all those who have got thus far in their tyrannical career--'Let not
150	the people's friend,' as they say, 'be lost to them.'
150	
150	Exactly.
150	
150	The people readily assent; all their fears are for him--they have none
150	for themselves.
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	And when a man who is wealthy and is also accused of being an enemy of
150	the people sees this, then, my friend, as the oracle said to Croesus,
150	
150	    By pebbly Hermus' shore he flees and rests not and is not
150	    ashamed to be a coward.
150	
150	And quite right too, said he, for if he were, he would never be ashamed
150	again.
150	
150	But if he is caught he dies.
150	
150	Of course.
150	
150	And he, the protector of whom we spoke, is to be seen, not 'larding the
150	plain' with his bulk, but himself the overthrower of many, standing up
150	in the chariot of State with the reins in his hand, no longer
150	protector, but tyrant absolute.
150	
150	No doubt, he said.
150	
150	And now let us consider the happiness of the man, and also of the State
150	in which a creature like him is generated.
150	
150	Yes, he said, let us consider that.
150	
150	At first, in the early days of his power, he is full of smiles, and he
150	salutes every one whom he meets;--he to be called a tyrant, who is
150	making promises in public and also in private! liberating debtors, and
150	distributing land to the people and his followers, and wanting to be so
150	kind and good to every one!
150	
150	Of course, he said.
150	
150	But when he has disposed of foreign enemies by conquest or treaty, and
150	there is nothing to fear from them, then he is always stirring up some
150	war or other, in order that the people may require a leader.
150	
150	To be sure.
150	
150	Has he not also another object, which is that they may be impoverished
150	by payment of taxes, and thus compelled to devote themselves to their
150	daily wants and therefore less likely to conspire against him?  Clearly.
150	
150	And if any of them are suspected by him of having notions of freedom,
150	and of resistance to his authority, he will have a good pretext for
150	destroying them by placing them at the mercy of the enemy; and for all
150	these reasons the tyrant must be always getting up a war.
150	
150	He must.
150	
150	Now he begins to grow unpopular.
150	
150	A necessary result.
150	
150	Then some of those who joined in setting him up, and who are in power,
150	speak their minds to him and to one another, and the more courageous of
150	them cast in his teeth what is being done.
150	
150	Yes, that may be expected.
150	
150	And the tyrant, if he means to rule, must get rid of them; he cannot
150	stop while he has a friend or an enemy who is good for anything.
150	
150	He cannot.
150	
150	And therefore he must look about him and see who is valiant, who is
150	high-minded, who is wise, who is wealthy; happy man, he is the enemy of
150	them all, and must seek occasion against them whether he will or no,
150	until he has made a purgation of the State.
150	
150	Yes, he said, and a rare purgation.
150	
150	Yes, I said, not the sort of purgation which the physicians make of the
150	body; for they take away the worse and leave the better part, but he
150	does the reverse.
150	
150	If he is to rule, I suppose that he cannot help himself.
150	
150	What a blessed alternative, I said:--to be compelled to dwell only with
150	the many bad, and to be by them hated, or not to live at all!
150	
150	Yes, that is the alternative.
150	
150	And the more detestable his actions are to the citizens the more
150	satellites and the greater devotion in them will he require?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	And who are the devoted band, and where will he procure them?
150	
150	They will flock to him, he said, of their own accord, if lie pays them.
150	
150	By the dog!  I said, here are more drones, of every sort and from every
150	land.
150	
150	Yes, he said, there are.
150	
150	But will he not desire to get them on the spot?
150	
150	How do you mean?
150	
150	He will rob the citizens of their slaves; he will then set them free
150	and enrol them in his bodyguard.
150	
150	To be sure, he said; and he will be able to trust them best of all.
150	
150	What a blessed creature, I said, must this tyrant be; he has put to
150	death the others and has these for his trusted friends.
150	
150	Yes, he said; they are quite of his sort.
150	
150	Yes, I said, and these are the new citizens whom he has called into
150	existence, who admire him and are his companions, while the good hate
150	and avoid him.
150	
150	Of course.
150	
150	Verily, then, tragedy is a wise thing and Euripides a great tragedian.
150	
150	Why so?
150	
150	Why, because he is the author of the pregnant saying,
150	
150	    Tyrants are wise by living with the wise;
150	
150	and he clearly meant to say that they are the wise whom the tyrant
150	makes his companions.
150	
150	Yes, he said, and he also praises tyranny as godlike; and many other
150	things of the same kind are said by him and by the other poets.
150	
150	And therefore, I said, the tragic poets being wise men will forgive us
150	and any others who live after our manner if we do not receive them into
150	our State, because they are the eulogists of tyranny.
150	
150	Yes, he said, those who have the wit will doubtless forgive us.
150	
150	But they will continue to go to other cities and attract mobs, and hire
150	voices fair and loud and persuasive, and draw the cities over to
150	tyrannies and democracies.
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	Moreover, they are paid for this and receive honour--the greatest
150	honour, as might be expected, from tyrants, and the next greatest from
150	democracies; but the higher they ascend our constitution hill, the more
150	their reputation fails, and seems unable from shortness of breath to
150	proceed further.
150	
150	True.
150	
150	But we are wandering from the subject:  Let us therefore return and
150	enquire how the tyrant will maintain that fair and numerous and various
150	and ever-changing army of his.
150	
150	If, he said, there are sacred treasures in the city, he will confiscate
150	and spend them; and in so far as the fortunes of attainted persons may
150	suffice, he will be able to diminish the taxes which he would otherwise
150	have to impose upon the people.
150	
150	And when these fail?
150	
150	Why, clearly, he said, then he and his boon companions, whether male or
150	female, will be maintained out of his father's estate.
150	
150	You mean to say that the people, from whom he has derived his being,
150	will maintain him and his companions?
150	
150	Yes, he said; they cannot help themselves.
150	
150	But what if the people fly into a passion, and aver that a grown-up son
150	ought not to be supported by his father, but that the father should be
150	supported by the son?  The father did not bring him into being, or
150	settle him in life, in order that when his son became a man he should
150	himself be the servant of his own servants and should support him and
150	his rabble of slaves and companions; but that his son should protect
150	him, and that by his help he might be emancipated from the government
150	of the rich and aristocratic, as they are termed.  And so he bids him
150	and his companions depart, just as any other father might drive out of
150	the house a riotous son and his undesirable associates.
150	
150	By heaven, he said, then the parent will discover what a monster he has
150	been fostering in his bosom; and, when he wants to drive him out, he
150	will find that he is weak and his son strong.
150	
150	Why, you do not mean to say that the tyrant will use violence?  What!
150	beat his father if he opposes him?
150	
150	Yes, he will, having first disarmed him.
150	
150	Then he is a parricide, and a cruel guardian of an aged parent; and
150	this is real tyranny, about which there can be no longer a mistake: as
150	the saying is, the people who would escape the smoke which is the
150	slavery of freemen, has fallen into the fire which is the tyranny of
150	slaves.  Thus liberty, getting out of all order and reason, passes into
150	the harshest and bitterest form of slavery.
150	
150	True, he said.
150	
150	Very well; and may we not rightly say that we have sufficiently
150	discussed the nature of tyranny, and the manner of the transition from
150	democracy to tyranny?
150	
150	Yes, quite enough, he said.
150	
150	
150	
150	
150	BOOK IX
150	
150	
150	SOCRATES - ADEIMANTUS
150	
150	LAST of all comes the tyrannical man; about whom we have once more to
150	ask, how is he formed out of the democratical? and how does he live, in
150	happiness or in misery?
150	
150	Yes, he said, he is the only one remaining.
150	
150	There is, however, I said, a previous question which remains unanswered.
150	
150	What question?
150	
150	I do not think that we have adequately determined the nature and number
150	of the appetites, and until this is accomplished the enquiry will
150	always be confused.
150	
150	Well, he said, it is not too late to supply the omission.
150	
150	Very true, I said; and observe the point which I want to understand:
150	Certain of the unnecessary pleasures and appetites I conceive to be
150	unlawful; every one appears to have them, but in some persons they are
150	controlled by the laws and by reason, and the better desires prevail
150	over them-either they are wholly banished or they become few and weak;
150	while in the case of others they are stronger, and there are more of
150	them.
150	
150	Which appetites do you mean?
150	
150	I mean those which are awake when the reasoning and human and ruling
150	power is asleep; then the wild beast within us, gorged with meat or
150	drink, starts up and having shaken off sleep, goes forth to satisfy his
150	desires; and there is no conceivable folly or crime--not excepting
150	incest or any other unnatural union, or parricide, or the eating of
150	forbidden food--which at such a time, when he has parted company with
150	all shame and sense, a man may not be ready to commit.
150	
150	Most true, he said.
150	
150	But when a man's pulse is healthy and temperate, and when before going
150	to sleep he has awakened his rational powers, and fed them on noble
150	thoughts and enquiries, collecting himself in meditation; after having
150	first indulged his appetites neither too much nor too little, but just
150	enough to lay them to sleep, and prevent them and their enjoyments and
150	pains from interfering with the higher principle--which he leaves in
150	the solitude of pure abstraction, free to contemplate and aspire to the
150	knowledge of the unknown, whether in past, present, or future:  when
150	again he has allayed the passionate element, if he has a quarrel
150	against any one--I say, when, after pacifying the two irrational
150	principles, he rouses up the third, which is reason, before he takes
150	his rest, then, as you know, he attains truth most nearly, and is least
150	likely to be the sport of fantastic and lawless visions.
150	
150	I quite agree.
150	
150	In saying this I have been running into a digression; but the point
150	which I desire to note is that in all of us, even in good men, there is
150	a lawless wild-beast nature, which peers out in sleep.  Pray, consider
150	whether I am right, and you agree with me.
150	
150	Yes, I agree.
150	
150	And now remember the character which we attributed to the democratic
150	man.  He was supposed from his youth upwards to have been trained under
150	a miserly parent, who encouraged the saving appetites in him, but
150	discountenanced the unnecessary, which aim only at amusement and
150	ornament?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	And then he got into the company of a more refined, licentious sort of
150	people, and taking to all their wanton ways rushed into the opposite
150	extreme from an abhorrence of his father's meanness.  At last, being a
150	better man than his corruptors, he was drawn in both directions until
150	he halted midway and led a life, not of vulgar and slavish passion, but
150	of what he deemed moderate indulgence in various pleasures.  After this
150	manner the democrat was generated out of the oligarch?
150	
150	Yes, he said; that was our view of him, and is so still.
150	
150	And now, I said, years will have passed away, and you must conceive
150	this man, such as he is, to have a son, who is brought up in his
150	father's principles.
150	
150	I can imagine him.
150	
150	Then you must further imagine the same thing to happen to the son which
150	has already happened to the father:--he is drawn into a perfectly
150	lawless life, which by his seducers is termed perfect liberty; and his
150	father and friends take part with his moderate desires, and the
150	opposite party assist the opposite ones.  As soon as these dire
150	magicians and tyrant-makers find that they are losing their hold on
150	him, they contrive to implant in him a master passion, to be lord over
150	his idle and spendthrift lusts--a sort of monstrous winged drone--that
150	is the only image which will adequately describe him.
150	
150	Yes, he said, that is the only adequate image of him.
150	
150	And when his other lusts, amid clouds of incense and perfumes and
150	garlands and wines, and all the pleasures of a dissolute life, now let
150	loose, come buzzing around him, nourishing to the utmost the sting of
150	desire which they implant in his drone-like nature, then at last this
150	lord of the soul, having Madness for the captain of his guard, breaks
150	out into a frenzy:  and if he finds in himself any good opinions or
150	appetites in process of formation, and there is in him any sense of
150	shame remaining, to these better principles he puts an end, and casts
150	them forth until he has purged away temperance and brought in madness
150	to the full.
150	
150	Yes, he said, that is the way in which the tyrannical man is generated.
150	
150	And is not this the reason why of old love has been called a tyrant?
150	
150	I should not wonder.
150	
150	Further, I said, has not a drunken man also the spirit of a tyrant?
150	
150	He has.
150	
150	And you know that a man who is deranged and not right in his mind, will
150	fancy that he is able to rule, not only over men, but also over the
150	gods?
150	
150	That he will.
150	
150	And the tyrannical man in the true sense of the word comes into being
150	when, either under the influence of nature, or habit, or both, he
150	becomes drunken, lustful, passionate?  O my friend, is not that so?
150	
150	Assuredly.
150	
150	Such is the man and such is his origin.  And next, how does he live?
150	
150	Suppose, as people facetiously say, you were to tell me.
150	
150	I imagine, I said, at the next step in his progress, that there will be
150	feasts and carousals and revellings and courtezans, and all that sort
150	of thing; Love is the lord of the house within him, and orders all the
150	concerns of his soul.
150	
150	That is certain.
150	
150	Yes; and every day and every night desires grow up many and formidable,
150	and their demands are many.
150	
150	They are indeed, he said.
150	
150	His revenues, if he has any, are soon spent.
150	
150	True.
150	
150	Then comes debt and the cutting down of his property.
150	
150	Of course.
150	
150	When he has nothing left, must not his desires, crowding in the nest
150	like young ravens, be crying aloud for food; and he, goaded on by them,
150	and especially by love himself, who is in a manner the captain of them,
150	is in a frenzy, and would fain discover whom he can defraud or despoil
150	of his property, in order that he may gratify them?
150	
150	Yes, that is sure to be the case.
150	
150	He must have money, no matter how, if he is to escape horrid pains and
150	pangs.
150	
150	He must.
150	
150	And as in himself there was a succession of pleasures, and the new got
150	the better of the old and took away their rights, so he being younger
150	will claim to have more than his father and his mother, and if he has
150	spent his own share of the property, he will take a slice of theirs.
150	
150	No doubt he will.
150	
150	And if his parents will not give way, then he will try first of all to
150	cheat and deceive them.
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	And if he fails, then he will use force and plunder them.
150	
150	Yes, probably.
150	
150	And if the old man and woman fight for their own, what then, my friend?
150	Will the creature feel any compunction at tyrannizing over them?
150	
150	Nay, he said, I should not feel at all comfortable about his parents.
150	
150	But, O heavens!  Adeimantus, on account of some newfangled love of a
150	harlot, who is anything but a necessary connection, can you believe
150	that he would strike the mother who is his ancient friend and necessary
150	to his very existence, and would place her under the authority of the
150	other, when she is brought under the same roof with her; or that, under
150	like circumstances, he would do the same to his withered old father,
150	first and most indispensable of friends, for the sake of some newly
150	found blooming youth who is the reverse of indispensable?
150	
150	Yes, indeed, he said; I believe that he would.
150	
150	Truly, then, I said, a tyrannical son is a blessing to his father and
150	mother.
150	
150	He is indeed, he replied.
150	
150	He first takes their property, and when that falls, and pleasures are
150	beginning to swarm in the hive of his soul, then he breaks into a
150	house, or steals the garments of some nightly wayfarer; next he
150	proceeds to clear a temple.  Meanwhile the old opinions which he had
150	when a child, and which gave judgment about good and evil, are
150	overthrown by those others which have just been emancipated, and are
150	now the bodyguard of love and share his empire.  These in his
150	democratic days, when he was still subject to the laws and to his
150	father, were only let loose in the dreams of sleep.  But now that he is
150	under the dominion of love, he becomes always and in waking reality
150	what he was then very rarely and in a dream only; he will commit the
150	foulest murder, or eat forbidden food, or be guilty of any other horrid
150	act.  Love is his tyrant, and lives lordly in him and lawlessly, and
150	being himself a king, leads him on, as a tyrant leads a State, to the
150	performance of any reckless deed by which he can maintain himself and
150	the rabble of his associates, whether those whom evil communications
150	have brought in from without, or those whom he himself has allowed to
150	break loose within him by reason of a similar evil nature in himself.
150	Have we not here a picture of his way of life?
150	
150	Yes, indeed, he said.
150	
150	And if there are only a few of them in the State, the rest of the
150	people are well disposed, they go away and become the bodyguard or
150	mercenary soldiers of some other tyrant who may probably want them for
150	a war; and if there is no war, they stay at home and do many little
150	pieces of mischief in the city.
150	
150	What sort of mischief?
150	
150	For example, they are the thieves, burglars, cutpurses, footpads,
150	robbers of temples, man-stealers of the community; or if they are able
150	to speak they turn informers, and bear false witness, and take bribes.
150	
150	A small catalogue of evils, even if the perpetrators of them are few in
150	number.
150	
150	Yes, I said; but small and great are comparative terms, and all these
150	things, in the misery and evil which they inflict upon a State, do not
150	come within a thousand miles of the tyrant; when this noxious class and
150	their followers grow numerous and become conscious of their strength,
150	assisted by the infatuation of the people, they choose from among
150	themselves the one who has most of the tyrant in his own soul, and him
150	they create their tyrant.
150	
150	Yes, he said, and he will be the most fit to be a tyrant.
150	
150	If the people yield, well and good; but if they resist him, as he began
150	by beating his own father and mother, so now, if he has the power, he
150	beats them, and will keep his dear old fatherland or motherland, as the
150	Cretans say, in subjection to his young retainers whom he has
150	introduced to be their rulers and masters.  This is the end of his
150	passions and desires.
150	
150	Exactly.
150	
150	When such men are only private individuals and before they get power,
150	this is their character; they associate entirely with their own
150	flatterers or ready tools; or if they want anything from anybody, they
150	in their turn are equally ready to bow down before them: they profess
150	every sort of affection for them; but when they have gained their point
150	they know them no more.
150	
150	Yes, truly.
150	
150	They are always either the masters or servants and never the friends of
150	anybody; the tyrant never tastes of true freedom or friendship.
150	
150	Certainly not.
150	
150	And may we not rightly call such men treacherous?
150	
150	No question.
150	
150	Also they are utterly unjust, if we were right in our notion of justice?
150	
150	Yes, he said, and we were perfectly right.
150	
150	Let us then sum up in a word, I said, the character of the worst man:
150	he is the waking reality of what we dreamed.
150	
150	Most true.
150	
150	And this is he who being by nature most of a tyrant bears rule, and the
150	longer he lives the more of a tyrant he becomes.
150	
150	SOCRATES - GLAUCON
150	
150	That is certain, said Glaucon, taking his turn to answer.
150	
150	And will not he who has been shown to be the wickedest, be also the
150	most miserable? and he who has tyrannized longest and most, most
150	continually and truly miserable; although this may not be the opinion
150	of men in general?
150	
150	Yes, he said, inevitably.
150	
150	And must not the tyrannical man be like the tyrannical, State, and the
150	democratical man like the democratical State; and the same of the
150	others?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	And as State is to State in virtue and happiness, so is man in relation
150	to man?
150	
150	To be sure.
150	
150	Then comparing our original city, which was under a king, and the city
150	which is under a tyrant, how do they stand as to virtue?
150	
150	They are the opposite extremes, he said, for one is the very best and
150	the other is the very worst.
150	
150	There can be no mistake, I said, as to which is which, and therefore I
150	will at once enquire whether you would arrive at a similar decision
150	about their relative happiness and misery.  And here we must not allow
150	ourselves to be panic-stricken at the apparition of the tyrant, who is
150	only a unit and may perhaps have a few retainers about him; but let us
150	go as we ought into every corner of the city and look all about, and
150	then we will give our opinion.
150	
150	A fair invitation, he replied; and I see, as every one must, that a
150	tyranny is the wretchedest form of government, and the rule of a king
150	the happiest.
150	
150	And in estimating the men too, may I not fairly make a like request,
150	that I should have a judge whose mind can enter into and see through
150	human nature?  He must not be like a child who looks at the outside and
150	is dazzled at the pompous aspect which the tyrannical nature assumes to
150	the beholder, but let him be one who has a clear insight.  May I
150	suppose that the judgment is given in the hearing of us all by one who
150	is able to judge, and has dwelt in the same place with him, and been
150	present at his dally life and known him in his family relations, where
150	he may be seen stripped of his tragedy attire, and again in the hour of
150	public danger--he shall tell us about the happiness and misery of the
150	tyrant when compared with other men?
150	
150	That again, he said, is a very fair proposal.
150	
150	Shall I assume that we ourselves are able and experienced judges and
150	have before now met with such a person?  We shall then have some one
150	who will answer our enquiries.
150	
150	By all means.
150	
150	Let me ask you not to forget the parallel of the individual and the
150	State; bearing this in mind, and glancing in turn from one to the other
150	of them, will you tell me their respective conditions?
150	
150	What do you mean? he asked.
150	
150	Beginning with the State, I replied, would you say that a city which is
150	governed by a tyrant is free or enslaved?
150	
150	No city, he said, can be more completely enslaved.
150	
150	And yet, as you see, there are freemen as well as masters in such a
150	State?
150	
150	Yes, he said, I see that there are--a few; but the people, speaking
150	generally, and the best of them, are miserably degraded and enslaved.
150	
150	Then if the man is like the State, I said, must not the same rule
150	prevail? his soul is full of meanness and vulgarity--the best elements
150	in him are enslaved; and there is a small ruling part, which is also
150	the worst and maddest.
150	
150	Inevitably.
150	
150	And would you say that the soul of such an one is the soul of a
150	freeman, or of a slave?
150	
150	He has the soul of a slave, in my opinion.
150	
150	And the State which is enslaved under a tyrant is utterly incapable of
150	acting voluntarily?
150	
150	Utterly incapable.
150	
150	And also the soul which is under a tyrant (I am speaking of the soul
150	taken as a whole) is least capable of doing what she desires; there is
150	a gadfly which goads her, and she is full of trouble and remorse?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	And is the city which is under a tyrant rich or poor?
150	
150	Poor.
150
