150	And the tyrannical soul must be always poor and insatiable?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	And must not such a State and such a man be always full of fear?
150	
150	Yes, indeed.
150	
150	Is there any State in which you will find more of lamentation and
150	sorrow and groaning and pain?
150	
150	Certainly not.
150	
150	And is there any man in whom you will find more of this sort of misery
150	than in the tyrannical man, who is in a fury of passions and desires?
150	
150	Impossible.
150	
150	Reflecting upon these and similar evils, you held the tyrannical State
150	to be the most miserable of States?
150	
150	And I was right, he said.
150	
150	Certainly, I said.  And when you see the same evils in the tyrannical
150	man, what do you say of him?
150	
150	I say that he is by far the most miserable of all men.
150	
150	There, I said, I think that you are beginning to go wrong.
150	
150	What do you mean?
150	
150	I do not think that he has as yet reached the utmost extreme of misery.
150	
150	Then who is more miserable?
150	
150	One of whom I am about to speak.
150	
150	Who is that?
150	
150	He who is of a tyrannical nature, and instead of leading a private life
150	has been cursed with the further misfortune of being a public tyrant.
150	
150	From what has been said, I gather that you are right.
150	
150	Yes, I replied, but in this high argument you should be a little more
150	certain, and should not conjecture only; for of all questions, this
150	respecting good and evil is the greatest.
150	
150	Very true, he said.
150	
150	Let me then offer you an illustration, which may, I think, throw a
150	light upon this subject.
150	
150	What is your illustration?
150	
150	The case of rich individuals in cities who possess many slaves: from
150	them you may form an idea of the tyrant's condition, for they both have
150	slaves; the only difference is that he has more slaves.
150	
150	Yes, that is the difference.
150	
150	You know that they live securely and have nothing to apprehend from
150	their servants?
150	
150	What should they fear?
150	
150	Nothing.  But do you observe the reason of this?
150	
150	Yes; the reason is, that the whole city is leagued together for the
150	protection of each individual.
150	
150	Very true, I said.  But imagine one of these owners, the master say of
150	some fifty slaves, together with his family and property and slaves,
150	carried off by a god into the wilderness, where there are no freemen to
150	help him--will he not be in an agony of fear lest he and his wife and
150	children should be put to death by his slaves?
150	
150	Yes, he said, he will be in the utmost fear.
150	
150	The time has arrived when he will be compelled to flatter divers of his
150	slaves, and make many promises to them of freedom and other things,
150	much against his will--he will have to cajole his own servants.
150	
150	Yes, he said, that will be the only way of saving himself.
150	
150	And suppose the same god, who carried him away, to surround him with
150	neighbours who will not suffer one man to be the master of another, and
150	who, if they could catch the offender, would take his life?
150	
150	His case will be still worse, if you suppose him to be everywhere
150	surrounded and watched by enemies.
150	
150	And is not this the sort of prison in which the tyrant will be
150	bound--he who being by nature such as we have described, is full of all
150	sorts of fears and lusts?  His soul is dainty and greedy, and yet
150	alone, of all men in the city, he is never allowed to go on a journey,
150	or to see the things which other freemen desire to see, but he lives in
150	his hole like a woman hidden in the house, and is jealous of any other
150	citizen who goes into foreign parts and sees anything of interest.
150	
150	Very true, he said.
150	
150	And amid evils such as these will not he who is ill-governed in his own
150	person--the tyrannical man, I mean--whom you just now decided to be the
150	most miserable of all--will not he be yet more miserable when, instead
150	of leading a private life, he is constrained by fortune to be a public
150	tyrant?  He has to be master of others when he is not master of
150	himself: he is like a diseased or paralytic man who is compelled to
150	pass his life, not in retirement, but fighting and combating with other
150	men.
150	
150	Yes, he said, the similitude is most exact.
150	
150	Is not his case utterly miserable? and does not the actual tyrant lead
150	a worse life than he whose life you determined to be the worst?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	He who is the real tyrant, whatever men may think, is the real slave,
150	and is obliged to practise the greatest adulation and servility, and to
150	be the flatterer of the vilest of mankind.  He has desires which he is
150	utterly unable to satisfy, and has more wants than any one, and is
150	truly poor, if you know how to inspect the whole soul of him: all his
150	life long he is beset with fear and is full of convulsions, and
150	distractions, even as the State which he resembles: and surely the
150	resemblance holds?
150	
150	Very true, he said.
150	
150	Moreover, as we were saying before, he grows worse from having power:
150	he becomes and is of necessity more jealous, more faithless, more
150	unjust, more friendless, more impious, than he was at first; he is the
150	purveyor and cherisher of every sort of vice, and the consequence is
150	that he is supremely miserable, and that he makes everybody else as
150	miserable as himself.
150	
150	No man of any sense will dispute your words.
150	
150	Come then, I said, and as the general umpire in theatrical contests
150	proclaims the result, do you also decide who in your opinion is first
150	in the scale of happiness, and who second, and in what order the others
150	follow:  there are five of them in all--they are the royal,
150	timocratical, oligarchical, democratical, tyrannical.
150	
150	The decision will be easily given, he replied; they shall be choruses
150	coming on the stage, and I must judge them in the order in which they
150	enter, by the criterion of virtue and vice, happiness and misery.
150	
150	Need we hire a herald, or shall I announce, that the son of Ariston
150	(the best) has decided that the best and justest is also the happiest,
150	and that this is he who is the most royal man and king over himself;
150	and that the worst and most unjust man is also the most miserable, and
150	that this is he who being the greatest tyrant of himself is also the
150	greatest tyrant of his State?
150	
150	Make the proclamation yourself, he said.
150	
150	And shall I add, 'whether seen or unseen by gods and men'?
150	
150	Let the words be added.
150	
150	Then this, I said, will be our first proof; and there is another, which
150	may also have some weight.
150	
150	What is that?
150	
150	The second proof is derived from the nature of the soul: seeing that
150	the individual soul, like the State, has been divided by us into three
150	principles, the division may, I think, furnish a new demonstration.
150	
150	Of what nature?
150	
150	It seems to me that to these three principles three pleasures
150	correspond; also three desires and governing powers.
150	
150	How do you mean? he said.
150	
150	There is one principle with which, as we were saying, a man learns,
150	another with which he is angry; the third, having many forms, has no
150	special name, but is denoted by the general term appetitive, from the
150	extraordinary strength and vehemence of the desires of eating and
150	drinking and the other sensual appetites which are the main elements of
150	it; also money-loving, because such desires are generally satisfied by
150	the help of money.
150	
150	That is true, he said.
150	
150	If we were to say that the loves and pleasures of this third part were
150	concerned with gain, we should then be able to fall back on a single
150	notion; and might truly and intelligibly describe this part of the soul
150	as loving gain or money.
150	
150	I agree with you.
150	
150	Again, is not the passionate element wholly set on ruling and
150	conquering and getting fame?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	Suppose we call it the contentious or ambitious--would the term be
150	suitable?
150	
150	Extremely suitable.
150	
150	On the other hand, every one sees that the principle of knowledge is
150	wholly directed to the truth, and cares less than either of the others
150	for gain or fame.
150	
150	Far less.
150	
150	'Lover of wisdom,' 'lover of knowledge,' are titles which we may fitly
150	apply to that part of the soul?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	One principle prevails in the souls of one class of men, another in
150	others, as may happen?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	Then we may begin by assuming that there are three classes of
150	men--lovers of wisdom, lovers of honour, lovers of gain?
150	
150	Exactly.
150	
150	And there are three kinds of pleasure, which are their several objects?
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	Now, if you examine the three classes of men, and ask of them in turn
150	which of their lives is pleasantest, each will be found praising his
150	own and depreciating that of others:  the money-maker will contrast the
150	vanity of honour or of learning if they bring no money with the solid
150	advantages of gold and silver?
150	
150	True, he said.
150	
150	And the lover of honour--what will be his opinion?  Will he not think
150	that the pleasure of riches is vulgar, while the pleasure of learning,
150	if it brings no distinction, is all smoke and nonsense to him?
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	And are we to suppose, I said, that the philosopher sets any value on
150	other pleasures in comparison with the pleasure of knowing the truth,
150	and in that pursuit abiding, ever learning, not so far indeed from the
150	heaven of pleasure?  Does he not call the other pleasures necessary,
150	under the idea that if there were no necessity for them, he would
150	rather not have them?
150	
150	There can be no doubt of that, he replied.
150	
150	Since, then, the pleasures of each class and the life of each are in
150	dispute, and the question is not which life is more or less honourable,
150	or better or worse, but which is the more pleasant or painless--how
150	shall we know who speaks truly?
150	
150	I cannot myself tell, he said.
150	
150	Well, but what ought to be the criterion?  Is any better than
150	experience and wisdom and reason?
150	
150	There cannot be a better, he said.
150	
150	Then, I said, reflect.  Of the three individuals, which has the
150	greatest experience of all the pleasures which we enumerated?  Has the
150	lover of gain, in learning the nature of essential truth, greater
150	experience of the pleasure of knowledge than the philosopher has of the
150	pleasure of gain?
150	
150	The philosopher, he replied, has greatly the advantage; for he has of
150	necessity always known the taste of the other pleasures from his
150	childhood upwards:  but the lover of gain in all his experience has not
150	of necessity tasted--or, I should rather say, even had he desired,
150	could hardly have tasted--the sweetness of learning and knowing truth.
150	
150	Then the lover of wisdom has a great advantage over the lover of gain,
150	for he has a double experience?
150	
150	Yes, very great.
150	
150	Again, has he greater experience of the pleasures of honour, or the
150	lover of honour of the pleasures of wisdom?
150	
150	Nay, he said, all three are honoured in proportion as they attain their
150	object; for the rich man and the brave man and the wise man alike have
150	their crowd of admirers, and as they all receive honour they all have
150	experience of the pleasures of honour; but the delight which is to be
150	found in the knowledge of true being is known to the philosopher only.
150	
150	His experience, then, will enable him to judge better than any one?
150	
150	Far better.
150	
150	And he is the only one who has wisdom as well as experience?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	Further, the very faculty which is the instrument of judgment is not
150	possessed by the covetous or ambitious man, but only by the philosopher?
150	
150	What faculty?
150	
150	Reason, with whom, as we were saying, the decision ought to rest.
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And reasoning is peculiarly his instrument?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	If wealth and gain were the criterion, then the praise or blame of the
150	lover of gain would surely be the most trustworthy?
150	
150	Assuredly.
150	
150	Or if honour or victory or courage, in that case the judgement of the
150	ambitious or pugnacious would be the truest?
150	
150	Clearly.
150	
150	But since experience and wisdom and reason are the judges--
150	
150	The only inference possible, he replied, is that pleasures which are
150	approved by the lover of wisdom and reason are the truest.
150	
150	And so we arrive at the result, that the pleasure of the intelligent
150	part of the soul is the pleasantest of the three, and that he of us in
150	whom this is the ruling principle has the pleasantest life.
150	
150	Unquestionably, he said, the wise man speaks with authority when he
150	approves of his own life.
150	
150	And what does the judge affirm to be the life which is next, and the
150	pleasure which is next?
150	
150	Clearly that of the soldier and lover of honour; who is nearer to
150	himself than the money-maker.
150	
150	Last comes the lover of gain?
150	
150	Very true, he said.
150	
150	Twice in succession, then, has the just man overthrown the unjust in
150	this conflict; and now comes the third trial, which is dedicated to
150	Olympian Zeus the saviour:  a sage whispers in my ear that no pleasure
150	except that of the wise is quite true and pure--all others are a shadow
150	only; and surely this will prove the greatest and most decisive of
150	falls?
150	
150	Yes, the greatest; but will you explain yourself?
150	
150	I will work out the subject and you shall answer my questions.
150	
150	Proceed.
150	
150	Say, then, is not pleasure opposed to pain?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	And there is a neutral state which is neither pleasure nor pain?
150	
150	There is.
150	
150	A state which is intermediate, and a sort of repose of the soul about
150	either--that is what you mean?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	You remember what people say when they are sick?
150	
150	What do they say?
150	
150	That after all nothing is pleasanter than health.  But then they never
150	knew this to be the greatest of pleasures until they were ill.
150	
150	Yes, I know, he said.
150	
150	And when persons are suffering from acute pain, you must.  have heard
150	them say that there is nothing pleasanter than to get rid of their pain?
150	
150	I have.
150	
150	And there are many other cases of suffering in which the mere rest and
150	cessation of pain, and not any positive enjoyment, is extolled by them
150	as the greatest pleasure?
150	
150	Yes, he said; at the time they are pleased and well content to be at
150	rest.
150	
150	Again, when pleasure ceases, that sort of rest or cessation will be
150	painful?
150	
150	Doubtless, he said.
150	
150	Then the intermediate state of rest will be pleasure and will also be
150	pain?
150	
150	So it would seem.
150	
150	But can that which is neither become both?
150	
150	I should say not.
150	
150	And both pleasure and pain are motions of the soul, are they not?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	But that which is neither was just now shown to be rest and not motion,
150	and in a mean between them?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	How, then, can we be right in supposing that the absence of pain is
150	pleasure, or that the absence of pleasure is pain?
150	
150	Impossible.
150	
150	This then is an appearance only and not a reality; that is to say, the
150	rest is pleasure at the moment and in comparison of what is painful,
150	and painful in comparison of what is pleasant; but all these
150	representations, when tried by the test of true pleasure, are not real
150	but a sort of imposition?
150	
150	That is the inference.
150	
150	Look at the other class of pleasures which have no antecedent pains and
150	you will no longer suppose, as you perhaps may at present, that
150	pleasure is only the cessation of pain, or pain of pleasure.
150	
150	What are they, he said, and where shall I find them?
150	
150	There are many of them:  take as an example the pleasures, of smell,
150	which are very great and have no antecedent pains; they come in a
150	moment, and when they depart leave no pain behind them.
150	
150	Most true, he said.
150	
150	Let us not, then, be induced to believe that pure pleasure is the
150	cessation of pain, or pain of pleasure.
150	
150	No.
150	
150	Still, the more numerous and violent pleasures which reach the soul
150	through the body are generally of this sort--they are reliefs of pain.
150	
150	That is true.
150	
150	And the anticipations of future pleasures and pains are of a like
150	nature?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	Shall I give you an illustration of them?
150	
150	Let me hear.
150	
150	You would allow, I said, that there is in nature an upper and lower and
150	middle region?
150	
150	I should.
150	
150	And if a person were to go from the lower to the middle region, would
150	he not imagine that he is going up; and he who is standing in the
150	middle and sees whence he has come, would imagine that he is already in
150	the upper region, if he has never seen the true upper world?
150	
150	To be sure, he said; how can he think otherwise?
150	
150	But if he were taken back again he would imagine, and truly imagine,
150	that he was descending?
150	
150	No doubt.
150	
150	All that would arise out of his ignorance of the true upper and middle
150	and lower regions?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	Then can you wonder that persons who are inexperienced in the truth, as
150	they have wrong ideas about many other things, should also have wrong
150	ideas about pleasure and pain and the intermediate state; so that when
150	they are only being drawn towards the painful they feel pain and think
150	the pain which they experience to be real, and in like manner, when
150	drawn away from pain to the neutral or intermediate state, they firmly
150	believe that they have reached the goal of satiety and pleasure; they,
150	not knowing pleasure, err in contrasting pain with the absence of pain,
150	which is like contrasting black with grey instead of white--can you
150	wonder, I say, at this?
150	
150	No, indeed; I should be much more disposed to wonder at the opposite.
150	
150	Look at the matter thus:--Hunger, thirst, and the like, are inanitions
150	of the bodily state?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And ignorance and folly are inanitions of the soul?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	And food and wisdom are the corresponding satisfactions of either?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	And is the satisfaction derived from that which has less or from that
150	which has more existence the truer?
150	
150	Clearly, from that which has more.
150	
150	What classes of things have a greater share of pure existence in your
150	judgment--those of which food and drink and condiments and all kinds of
150	sustenance are examples, or the class which contains true opinion and
150	knowledge and mind and all the different kinds of virtue?  Put the
150	question in this way:--Which has a more pure being--that which is
150	concerned with the invariable, the immortal, and the true, and is of
150	such a nature, and is found in such natures; or that which is concerned
150	with and found in the variable and mortal, and is itself variable and
150	mortal?
150	
150	Far purer, he replied, is the being of that which is concerned with the
150	invariable.
150	
150	And does the essence of the invariable partake of knowledge in the same
150	degree as of essence?
150	
150	Yes, of knowledge in the same degree.
150	
150	And of truth in the same degree?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And, conversely, that which has less of truth will also have less of
150	essence?
150	
150	Necessarily.
150	
150	Then, in general, those kinds of things which are in the service of the
150	body have less of truth and essence than those which are in the service
150	of the soul?
150	
150	Far less.
150	
150	And has not the body itself less of truth and essence than the soul?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	What is filled with more real existence, and actually has a more real
150	existence, is more really filled than that which is filled with less
150	real existence and is less real?
150	
150	Of course.
150	
150	And if there be a pleasure in being filled with that which is according
150	to nature, that which is more really filled with more real being will
150	more really and truly enjoy true pleasure; whereas that which
150	participates in less real being will be less truly and surely
150	satisfied, and will participate in an illusory and less real pleasure?
150	
150	Unquestionably.
150	
150	Those then who know not wisdom and virtue, and are always busy with
150	gluttony and sensuality, go down and up again as far as the mean; and
150	in this region they move at random throughout life, but they never pass
150	into the true upper world; thither they neither look, nor do they ever
150	find their way, neither are they truly filled with true being, nor do
150	they taste of pure and abiding pleasure.  Like cattle, with their eyes
150	always looking down and their heads stooping to the earth, that is, to
150	the dining-table, they fatten and feed and breed, and, in their
150	excessive love of these delights, they kick and butt at one another
150	with horns and hoofs which are made of iron; and they kill one another
150	by reason of their insatiable lust.  For they fill themselves with that
150	which is not substantial, and the part of themselves which they fill is
150	also unsubstantial and incontinent.
150	
150	Verily, Socrates, said Glaucon, you describe the life of the many like
150	an oracle.
150	
150	Their pleasures are mixed with pains--how can they be otherwise?  For
150	they are mere shadows and pictures of the true, and are coloured by
150	contrast, which exaggerates both light and shade, and so they implant
150	in the minds of fools insane desires of themselves; and they are fought
150	about as Stesichorus says that the Greeks fought about the shadow of
150	Helen at Troy in ignorance of the truth.
150	
150	Something of that sort must inevitably happen.
150	
150	And must not the like happen with the spirited or passionate element of
150	the soul?  Will not the passionate man who carries his passion into
150	action, be in the like case, whether he is envious and ambitious, or
150	violent and contentious, or angry and discontented, if he be seeking to
150	attain honour and victory and the satisfaction of his anger without
150	reason or sense?
150	
150	Yes, he said, the same will happen with the spirited element also.
150	
150	Then may we not confidently assert that the lovers of money and honour,
150	when they seek their pleasures under the guidance and in the company of
150	reason and knowledge, and pursue after and win the pleasures which
150	wisdom shows them, will also have the truest pleasures in the highest
150	degree which is attainable to them, inasmuch as they follow truth; and
150	they will have the pleasures which are natural to them, if that which
150	is best for each one is also most natural to him?
150	
150	Yes, certainly; the best is the most natural.
150	
150	And when the whole soul follows the philosophical principle, and there
150	is no division, the several parts are just, and do each of them their
150	own business, and enjoy severally the best and truest pleasures of
150	which they are capable?
150	
150	Exactly.
150	
150	But when either of the two other principles prevails, it fails in
150	attaining its own pleasure, and compels the rest to pursue after a
150	pleasure which is a shadow only and which is not their own?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	And the greater the interval which separates them from philosophy and
150	reason, the more strange and illusive will be the pleasure?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And is not that farthest from reason which is at the greatest distance
150	from law and order?
150	
150	Clearly.
150	
150	And the lustful and tyrannical desires are, as we saw, at the greatest
150	distance?  Yes.
150	
150	And the royal and orderly desires are nearest?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	Then the tyrant will live at the greatest distance from true or natural
150	pleasure, and the king at the least?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	But if so, the tyrant will live most unpleasantly, and the king most
150	pleasantly?
150	
150	Inevitably.
150	
150	Would you know the measure of the interval which separates them?
150	
150	Will you tell me?
150	
150	There appear to be three pleasures, one genuine and two spurious: now
150	the transgression of the tyrant reaches a point beyond the spurious; he
150	has run away from the region of law and reason, and taken up his abode
150	with certain slave pleasures which are his satellites, and the measure
150	of his inferiority can only be expressed in a figure.
150	
150	How do you mean?
150	
150	I assume, I said, that the tyrant is in the third place from the
150	oligarch; the democrat was in the middle?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And if there is truth in what has preceded, he will be wedded to an
150	image of pleasure which is thrice removed as to truth from the pleasure
150	of the oligarch?
150	
150	He will.
150	
150	And the oligarch is third from the royal; since we count as one royal
150	and aristocratical?
150	
150	Yes, he is third.
150	
150	Then the tyrant is removed from true pleasure by the space of a number
150	which is three times three?
150	
150	Manifestly.
150	
150	The shadow then of tyrannical pleasure determined by the number of
150	length will be a plane figure.
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	And if you raise the power and make the plane a solid, there is no
150	difficulty in seeing how vast is the interval by which the tyrant is
150	parted from the king.
150	
150	Yes; the arithmetician will easily do the sum.
150	
150	Or if some person begins at the other end and measures the interval by
150	which the king is parted from the tyrant in truth of pleasure, he will
150	find him, when the multiplication is complete, living 729 times more
150	pleasantly, and the tyrant more painfully by this same interval.
150	
150	What a wonderful calculation!  And how enormous is the distance which
150	separates the just from the unjust in regard to pleasure and pain!
150	
150	Yet a true calculation, I said, and a number which nearly concerns
150	human life, if human beings are concerned with days and nights and
150	months and years.
150	
150	Yes, he said, human life is certainly concerned with them.
150	
150	Then if the good and just man be thus superior in pleasure to the evil
150	and unjust, his superiority will be infinitely greater in propriety of
150	life and in beauty and virtue?
150	
150	Immeasurably greater.
150	
150	Well, I said, and now having arrived at this stage of the argument, we
150	may revert to the words which brought us hither:  Was not some one
150	saying that injustice was a gain to the perfectly unjust who was
150	reputed to be just?
150	
150	Yes, that was said.
150	
150	Now then, having determined the power and quality of justice and
150	injustice, let us have a little conversation with him.
150	
150	What shall we say to him?
150	
150	Let us make an image of the soul, that he may have his own words
150	presented before his eyes.
150	
150	Of what sort?
150	
150	An ideal image of the soul, like the composite creations of ancient
150	mythology, such as the Chimera or Scylla or Cerberus, and there are
150	many others in which two or more different natures are said to grow
150	into one.
150	
150	There are said of have been such unions.
150	
150	Then do you now model the form of a multitudinous, many-headed monster,
150	having a ring of heads of all manner of beasts, tame and wild, which he
150	is able to generate and metamorphose at will.
150	
150	You suppose marvellous powers in the artist; but, as language is more
150	pliable than wax or any similar substance, let there be such a model as
150	you propose.
150	
150	Suppose now that you make a second form as of a lion, and a third of a
150	man, the second smaller than the first, and the third smaller than the
150	second.
150	
150	That, he said, is an easier task; and I have made them as you say.
150	
150	And now join them, and let the three grow into one.
150	
150	That has been accomplished.
150	
150	Next fashion the outside of them into a single image, as of a man, so
150	that he who is not able to look within, and sees only the outer hull,
150	may believe the beast to be a single human creature.  I have done so,
150	he said.
150	
150	And now, to him who maintains that it is profitable for the human
150	creature to be unjust, and unprofitable to be just, let us reply that,
150	if he be right, it is profitable for this creature to feast the
150	multitudinous monster and strengthen the lion and the lion-like
150	qualities, but to starve and weaken the man, who is consequently liable
150	to be dragged about at the mercy of either of the other two; and he is
150	not to attempt to familiarize or harmonize them with one another--he
150	ought rather to suffer them to fight and bite and devour one another.
150	
150	Certainly, he said; that is what the approver of injustice says.
150	
150	To him the supporter of justice makes answer that he should ever so
150	speak and act as to give the man within him in some way or other the
150	most complete mastery over the entire human creature.
150	
150	He should watch over the many-headed monster like a good husbandman,
150	fostering and cultivating the gentle qualities, and preventing the wild
150	ones from growing; he should be making the lion-heart his ally, and in
150	common care of them all should be uniting the several parts with one
150	another and with himself.
150	
150	Yes, he said, that is quite what the maintainer of justice say.
150	
150	And so from every point of view, whether of pleasure, honour, or
150	advantage, the approver of justice is right and speaks the truth, and
150	the disapprover is wrong and false and ignorant.
150	
150	Yes, from every point of view.
150	
150	Come, now, and let us gently reason with the unjust, who is not
150	intentionally in error.  'Sweet Sir,' we will say to him, what think
150	you of things esteemed noble and ignoble?  Is not the noble that which
150	subjects the beast to the man, or rather to the god in man; and the
150	ignoble that which subjects the man to the beast?' He can hardly avoid
150	saying yes--can he now?
150	
150	Not if he has any regard for my opinion.
150	
150	But, if he agree so far, we may ask him to answer another question:
150	'Then how would a man profit if he received gold and silver on the
150	condition that he was to enslave the noblest part of him to the worst?
150	Who can imagine that a man who sold his son or daughter into slavery
150	for money, especially if he sold them into the hands of fierce and evil
150	men, would be the gainer, however large might be the sum which he
150	received?  And will any one say that he is not a miserable caitiff who
150	remorselessly sells his own divine being to that which is most godless
150	and detestable?  Eriphyle took the necklace as the price of her
150	husband's life, but he is taking a bribe in order to compass a worse
150	ruin.'
150	
150	Yes, said Glaucon, far worse--I will answer for him.
150	
150	Has not the intemperate been censured of old, because in him the huge
150	multiform monster is allowed to be too much at large?
150	
150	Clearly.
150	
150	And men are blamed for pride and bad temper when the lion and serpent
150	element in them disproportionately grows and gains strength?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And luxury and softness are blamed, because they relax and weaken this
150	same creature, and make a coward of him?
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	And is not a man reproached for flattery and meanness who subordinates
150	the spirited animal to the unruly monster, and, for the sake of money,
150	of which he can never have enough, habituates him in the days of his
150	youth to be trampled in the mire, and from being a lion to become a
150	monkey?
150	
150	True, he said.
150	
150	And why are mean employments and manual arts a reproach Only because
150	they imply a natural weakness of the higher principle; the individual
150	is unable to control the creatures within him, but has to court them,
150	and his great study is how to flatter them.
150	
150	Such appears to be the reason.
150	
150	And therefore, being desirous of placing him under a rule like that of
150	the best, we say that he ought to be the servant of the best, in whom
150	the Divine rules; not, as Thrasymachus supposed, to the injury of the
150	servant, but because every one had better be ruled by divine wisdom
150	dwelling within him; or, if this be impossible, then by an external
150	authority, in order that we may be all, as far as possible, under the
150	same government, friends and equals.
150	
150	True, he said.
150	
150	And this is clearly seen to be the intention of the law, which is the
150	ally of the whole city; and is seen also in the authority which we
150	exercise over children, and the refusal to let them be free until we
150	have established in them a principle analogous to the constitution of a
150	state, and by cultivation of this higher element have set up in their
150	hearts a guardian and ruler like our own, and when this is done they
150	may go their ways.
150	
150	Yes, he said, the purpose of the law is manifest.
150	
150	From what point of view, then, and on what ground can we say that a man
150	is profited by injustice or intemperance or other baseness, which will
150	make him a worse man, even though he acquire money or power by his
150	wickedness?
150	
150	From no point of view at all.
150	
150	What shall he profit, if his injustice be undetected and unpunished?
150	He who is undetected only gets worse, whereas he who is detected and
150	punished has the brutal part of his nature silenced and humanized; the
150	gentler element in him is liberated, and his whole soul is perfected
150	and ennobled by the acquirement of justice and temperance and wisdom,
150	more than the body ever is by receiving gifts of beauty, strength and
150	health, in proportion as the soul is more honourable than the body.
150	
150	Certainly, he said.
150	
150	To this nobler purpose the man of understanding will devote the
150	energies of his life.  And in the first place, he will honour studies
150	which impress these qualities on his soul and disregard others?
150	
150	Clearly, he said.
150	
150	In the next place, he will regulate his bodily habit and training, and
150	so far will he be from yielding to brutal and irrational pleasures,
150	that he will regard even health as quite a secondary matter; his first
150	object will be not that he may be fair or strong or well, unless he is
150	likely thereby to gain temperance, but he will always desire so to
150	attemper the body as to preserve the harmony of the soul?
150	
150	Certainly he will, if he has true music in him.
150	
150	And in the acquisition of wealth there is a principle of order and
150	harmony which he will also observe; he will not allow himself to be
150	dazzled by the foolish applause of the world, and heap up riches to his
150	own infinite harm?
150	
150	Certainly not, he said.
150	
150	He will look at the city which is within him, and take heed that no
150	disorder occur in it, such as might arise either from superfluity or
150	from want; and upon this principle he will regulate his property and
150	gain or spend according to his means.
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	And, for the same reason, he will gladly accept and enjoy such honours
150	as he deems likely to make him a better man; but those, whether private
150	or public, which are likely to disorder his life, he will avoid?
150	
150	Then, if that is his motive, he will not be a statesman.
150	
150	By the dog of Egypt, he will! in the city which is his own he certainly
150	will, though in the land of his birth perhaps not, unless he have a
150	divine call.
150	
150	I understand; you mean that he will be a ruler in the city of which we
150	are the founders, and which exists in idea only; for I do not believe
150	that there is such an one anywhere on earth?
150	
150	In heaven, I replied, there is laid up a pattern of it, methinks, which
150	he who desires may behold, and beholding, may set his own house in
150	order.  But whether such an one exists, or ever will exist in fact, is
150	no matter; for he will live after the manner of that city, having
150	nothing to do with any other.
150	
150	I think so, he said.
150	
150	
150	
150	
150	BOOK X
150	
150	
150	SOCRATES - GLAUCON
150	
150	OF THE many excellences which I perceive in the order of our State,
150	there is none which upon reflection pleases me better than the rule
150	about poetry.
150	
150	To what do you refer?
150	
150	To the rejection of imitative poetry, which certainly ought not to be
150	received; as I see far more clearly now that the parts of the soul have
150	been distinguished.
150	
150	What do you mean?
150	
150	Speaking in confidence, for I should not like to have my words repeated
150	to the tragedians and the rest of the imitative tribe--but I do not
150	mind saying to you, that all poetical imitations are ruinous to the
150	understanding of the hearers, and that the knowledge of their true
150	nature is the only antidote to them.
150	
150	Explain the purport of your remark.
150	
150	Well, I will tell you, although I have always from my earliest youth
150	had an awe and love of Homer, which even now makes the words falter on
150	my lips, for he is the great captain and teacher of the whole of that
150	charming tragic company; but a man is not to be reverenced more than
150	the truth, and therefore I will speak out.
150	
150	Very good, he said.
150	
150	Listen to me then, or rather, answer me.
150	
150	Put your question.
150	
150	Can you tell me what imitation is? for I really do not know.
150	
150	A likely thing, then, that I should know.
150	
150	Why not? for the duller eye may often see a thing sooner than the
150	keener.
150	
150	Very true, he said; but in your presence, even if I had any faint
150	notion, I could not muster courage to utter it.  Will you enquire
150	yourself?
150	
150	Well then, shall we begin the enquiry in our usual manner: Whenever a
150	number of individuals have a common name, we assume them to have also a
150	corresponding idea or form.  Do you understand me?
150	
150	I do.
150	
150	Let us take any common instance; there are beds and tables in the
150	world--plenty of them, are there not?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	But there are only two ideas or forms of them--one the idea of a bed,
150	the other of a table.
150	
150	True.
150	
150	And the maker of either of them makes a bed or he makes a table for our
150	use, in accordance with the idea--that is our way of speaking in this
150	and similar instances--but no artificer makes the ideas themselves: how
150	could he?
150	
150	Impossible.
150	
150	And there is another artist,--I should like to know what you would say
150	of him.
150	
150	Who is he?
150	
150	One who is the maker of all the works of all other workmen.
150	
150	What an extraordinary man!
150	
150	Wait a little, and there will be more reason for your saying so.  For
150	this is he who is able to make not only vessels of every kind, but
150	plants and animals, himself and all other things--the earth and heaven,
150	and the things which are in heaven or under the earth; he makes the
150	gods also.
150	
150	He must be a wizard and no mistake.
150	
150	Oh! you are incredulous, are you?  Do you mean that there is no such
150	maker or creator, or that in one sense there might be a maker of all
150	these things but in another not?  Do you see that there is a way in
150	which you could make them all yourself?
150	
150	What way?
150	
150	An easy way enough; or rather, there are many ways in which the feat
150	might be quickly and easily accomplished, none quicker than that of
150	turning a mirror round and round--you would soon enough make the sun
150	and the heavens, and the earth and yourself, and other animals and
150	plants, and all the other things of which we were just now speaking, in
150	the mirror.
150	
150	Yes, he said; but they would be appearances only.
150	
150	Very good, I said, you are coming to the point now.  And the painter
150	too is, as I conceive, just such another--a creator of appearances, is
150	he not?
150	
150	Of course.
150	
150	But then I suppose you will say that what he creates is untrue.  And
150	yet there is a sense in which the painter also creates a bed?
150	
150	Yes, he said, but not a real bed.
150	
150	And what of the maker of the bed?  Were you not saying that he too
150	makes, not the idea which, according to our view, is the essence of the
150	bed, but only a particular bed?
150	
150	Yes, I did.
150	
150	Then if he does not make that which exists he cannot make true
150	existence, but only some semblance of existence; and if any one were to
150	say that the work of the maker of the bed, or of any other workman, has
150	real existence, he could hardly be supposed to be speaking the truth.
150	
150	At any rate, he replied, philosophers would say that he was not
150	speaking the truth.
150	
150	No wonder, then, that his work too is an indistinct expression of truth.
150	
150	No wonder.
150	
150	Suppose now that by the light of the examples just offered we enquire
150	who this imitator is?
150	
150	If you please.
150	
150	Well then, here are three beds:  one existing in nature, which is made
150	by God, as I think that we may say--for no one else can be the maker?
150	
150	No.
150	
150	There is another which is the work of the carpenter?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And the work of the painter is a third?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	Beds, then, are of three kinds, and there are three artists who
150	superintend them:  God, the maker of the bed, and the painter?
150	
150	Yes, there are three of them.
150	
150	God, whether from choice or from necessity, made one bed in nature and
150	one only; two or more such ideal beds neither ever have been nor ever
150	will be made by God.
150	
150	Why is that?
150	
150	Because even if He had made but two, a third would still appear behind
150	them which both of them would have for their idea, and that would be
150	the ideal bed and the two others.
150	
150	Very true, he said.
150	
150	God knew this, and He desired to be the real maker of a real bed, not a
150	particular maker of a particular bed, and therefore He created a bed
150	which is essentially and by nature one only.
150	
150	So we believe.
150	
150	Shall we, then, speak of Him as the natural author or maker of the bed?
150	
150	Yes, he replied; inasmuch as by the natural process of creation He is
150	the author of this and of all other things.
150	
150	And what shall we say of the carpenter--is not he also the maker of the
150	bed?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	But would you call the painter a creator and maker?
150	
150	Certainly not.
150	
150	Yet if he is not the maker, what is he in relation to the bed?
150	
150	I think, he said, that we may fairly designate him as the imitator of
150	that which the others make.
150	
150	Good, I said; then you call him who is third in the descent from nature
150	an imitator?
150	
150	Certainly, he said.
150	
150	And the tragic poet is an imitator, and therefore, like all other
150	imitators, he is thrice removed from the king and from the truth?
150	
150	That appears to be so.
150	
150	Then about the imitator we are agreed.  And what about the painter?-- I
150	would like to know whether he may be thought to imitate that which
150	originally exists in nature, or only the creations of artists?
150	
150	The latter.
150	
150	As they are or as they appear?  You have still to determine this.
150	
150	What do you mean?
150	
150	I mean, that you may look at a bed from different points of view,
150	obliquely or directly or from any other point of view, and the bed will
150	appear different, but there is no difference in reality.  And the same
150	of all things.
150	
150	Yes, he said, the difference is only apparent.
150	
150	Now let me ask you another question:  Which is the art of painting
150	designed to be--an imitation of things as they are, or as they
150	appear--of appearance or of reality?
150	
150	Of appearance.
150	
150	Then the imitator, I said, is a long way off the truth, and can do all
150	things because he lightly touches on a small part of them, and that
150	part an image.  For example:  A painter will paint a cobbler,
150	carpenter, or any other artist, though he knows nothing of their arts;
150	and, if he is a good artist, he may deceive children or simple persons,
150	when he shows them his picture of a carpenter from a distance, and they
150	will fancy that they are looking at a real carpenter.
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	And whenever any one informs us that he has found a man knows all the
150	arts, and all things else that anybody knows, and every single thing
150	with a higher degree of accuracy than any other man--whoever tells us
150	this, I think that we can only imagine to be a simple creature who is
150	likely to have been deceived by some wizard or actor whom he met, and
150	whom he thought all-knowing, because he himself was unable to analyse
150	the nature of knowledge and ignorance and imitation.
150	
150	Most true.
150	
150	And so, when we hear persons saying that the tragedians, and Homer, who
150	is at their head, know all the arts and all things human, virtue as
150	well as vice, and divine things too, for that the good poet cannot
150	compose well unless he knows his subject, and that he who has not this
150	knowledge can never be a poet, we ought to consider whether here also
150	there may not be a similar illusion.  Perhaps they may have come across
150	imitators and been deceived by them; they may not have remembered when
150	they saw their works that these were but imitations thrice removed from
150	the truth, and could easily be made without any knowledge of the truth,
150	because they are appearances only and not realities?  Or, after all,
150	they may be in the right, and poets do really know the things about
150	which they seem to the many to speak so well?
150	
150	The question, he said, should by all means be considered.
150	
150	Now do you suppose that if a person were able to make the original as
150	well as the image, he would seriously devote himself to the
150	image-making branch?  Would he allow imitation to be the ruling
150	principle of his life, as if he had nothing higher in him?
150	
150	I should say not.
150	
150	The real artist, who knew what he was imitating, would be interested in
150	realities and not in imitations; and would desire to leave as memorials
150	of himself works many and fair; and, instead of being the author of
150	encomiums, he would prefer to be the theme of them.
150	
150	Yes, he said, that would be to him a source of much greater honour and
150	profit.
150	
150	Then, I said, we must put a question to Homer; not about medicine, or
150	any of the arts to which his poems only incidentally refer: we are not
150	going to ask him, or any other poet, whether he has cured patients like
150	Asclepius, or left behind him a school of medicine such as the
150	Asclepiads were, or whether he only talks about medicine and other arts
150	at second hand; but we have a right to know respecting military
150	tactics, politics, education, which are the chiefest and noblest
150	subjects of his poems, and we may fairly ask him about them.  'Friend
150	Homer,' then we say to him, 'if you are only in the second remove from
150	truth in what you say of virtue, and not in the third--not an image
150	maker or imitator--and if you are able to discern what pursuits make
150	men better or worse in private or public life, tell us what State was
150	ever better governed by your help?  The good order of Lacedaemon is due
150	to Lycurgus, and many other cities great and small have been similarly
150	benefited by others; but who says that you have been a good legislator
150	to them and have done them any good?  Italy and Sicily boast of
150	Charondas, and there is Solon who is renowned among us; but what city
150	has anything to say about you?'  Is there any city which he might name?
150	
150	I think not, said Glaucon; not even the Homerids themselves pretend
150	that he was a legislator.
150	
150	Well, but is there any war on record which was carried on successfully
150	by him, or aided by his counsels, when he was alive?
150	
150	There is not.
150	
150	Or is there any invention of his, applicable to the arts or to human
150	life, such as Thales the Milesian or Anacharsis the Scythian, and other
150	ingenious men have conceived, which is attributed to him?
150	
150	There is absolutely nothing of the kind.
150	
150	But, if Homer never did any public service, was he privately a guide or
150	teacher of any?  Had he in his lifetime friends who loved to associate
150	with him, and who handed down to posterity an Homeric way of life, such
150	as was established by Pythagoras who was so greatly beloved for his
150	wisdom, and whose followers are to this day quite celebrated for the
150	order which was named after him?
150	
150	Nothing of the kind is recorded of him.  For surely, Socrates,
150	Creophylus, the companion of Homer, that child of flesh, whose name
150	always makes us laugh, might be more justly ridiculed for his
150	stupidity, if, as is said, Homer was greatly neglected by him and
150	others in his own day when he was alive?
150	
150	Yes, I replied, that is the tradition.  But can you imagine, Glaucon,
150	that if Homer had really been able to educate and improve mankind--if
150	he had possessed knowledge and not been a mere imitator--can you
150	imagine, I say, that he would not have had many followers, and been
150	honoured and loved by them?  Protagoras of Abdera, and Prodicus of
150	Ceos, and a host of others, have only to whisper to their
150	contemporaries: 'You will never be able to manage either your own house
150	or your own State until you appoint us to be your ministers of
150	education'--and this ingenious device of theirs has such an effect in
150	making them love them that their companions all but carry them about on
150	their shoulders.  And is it conceivable that the contemporaries of
150	Homer, or again of Hesiod, would have allowed either of them to go
150	about as rhapsodists, if they had really been able to make mankind
150	virtuous?  Would they not have been as unwilling to part with them as
150	with gold, and have compelled them to stay at home with them?  Or, if
150	the master would not stay, then the disciples would have followed him
150	about everywhere, until they had got education enough?
150	
150	Yes, Socrates, that, I think, is quite true.
150	
150	Then must we not infer that all these poetical individuals, beginning
150	with Homer, are only imitators; they copy images of virtue and the
150	like, but the truth they never reach?  The poet is like a painter who,
150	as we have already observed, will make a likeness of a cobbler though
150	he understands nothing of cobbling; and his picture is good enough for
150	those who know no more than he does, and judge only by colours and
150	figures.
150	
150	Quite so.
150	
150	In like manner the poet with his words and phrases may be said to lay
150	on the colours of the several arts, himself understanding their nature
150	only enough to imitate them; and other people, who are as ignorant as
150	he is, and judge only from his words, imagine that if he speaks of
150	cobbling, or of military tactics, or of anything else, in metre and
150	harmony and rhythm, he speaks very well--such is the sweet influence
150	which melody and rhythm by nature have.  And I think that you must have
150	observed again and again what a poor appearance the tales of poets make
150	when stripped of the colours which music puts upon them, and recited in
150	simple prose.
150	
150	Yes, he said.
150	
150	They are like faces which were never really beautiful, but only
150	blooming; and now the bloom of youth has passed away from them?
150	
150	Exactly.
150	
150	Here is another point:  The imitator or maker of the image knows
150	nothing of true existence; he knows appearances only.  Am I not right?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	Then let us have a clear understanding, and not be satisfied with half
150	an explanation.
150	
150	Proceed.
150	
150	Of the painter we say that he will paint reins, and he will paint a bit?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And the worker in leather and brass will make them?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	But does the painter know the right form of the bit and reins?  Nay,
150	hardly even the workers in brass and leather who make them; only the
150	horseman who knows how to use them--he knows their right form.
150	
150	Most true.
150	
150	And may we not say the same of all things?
150	
150	What?
150	
150	That there are three arts which are concerned with all things: one
150	which uses, another which makes, a third which imitates them?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And the excellence or beauty or truth of every structure, animate or
150	inanimate, and of every action of man, is relative to the use for which
150	nature or the artist has intended them.
150	
150	True.
150	
150	Then the user of them must have the greatest experience of them, and he
150	must indicate to the maker the good or bad qualities which develop
150	themselves in use; for example, the flute-player will tell the
150	flute-maker which of his flutes is satisfactory to the performer; he
150	will tell him how he ought to make them, and the other will attend to
150	his instructions?
150	
150	Of course.
150	
150	The one knows and therefore speaks with authority about the goodness
150	and badness of flutes, while the other, confiding in him, will do what
150	he is told by him?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	The instrument is the same, but about the excellence or badness of it
150	the maker will only attain to a correct belief; and this he will gain
150	from him who knows, by talking to him and being compelled to hear what
150	he has to say, whereas the user will have knowledge?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	But will the imitator have either?  Will he know from use whether or no
150	his drawing is correct or beautiful?  Or will he have right opinion
150	from being compelled to associate with another who knows and gives him
150	instructions about what he should draw?
150	
150	Neither.
150	
150	Then he will no more have true opinion than he will have knowledge
150	about the goodness or badness of his imitations?
150	
150	I suppose not.
150	
150	The imitative artist will be in a brilliant state of intelligence about
150	his own creations?
150	
150	Nay, very much the reverse.
150	
150	And still he will go on imitating without knowing what makes a thing
150	good or bad, and may be expected therefore to imitate only that which
150	appears to be good to the ignorant multitude?
150	
150	Just so.
150	
150	Thus far then we are pretty well agreed that the imitator has no
150	knowledge worth mentioning of what he imitates.  Imitation is only a
150	kind of play or sport, and the tragic poets, whether they write in
150	iambic or in Heroic verse, are imitators in the highest degree?
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	And now tell me, I conjure you, has not imitation been shown by us to
150	be concerned with that which is thrice removed from the truth?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	And what is the faculty in man to which imitation is addressed?
150	
150	What do you mean?
150	
150	I will explain:  The body which is large when seen near, appears small
150	when seen at a distance?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	And the same object appears straight when looked at out of the water,
150	and crooked when in the water; and the concave becomes convex, owing to
150	the illusion about colours to which the sight is liable.  Thus every
150	sort of confusion is revealed within us; and this is that weakness of
150	the human mind on which the art of conjuring and of deceiving by light
150	and shadow and other ingenious devices imposes, having an effect upon
150	us like magic.
150	
150	True.
150	
150	And the arts of measuring and numbering and weighing come to the rescue
150	of the human understanding-there is the beauty of them--and the
150	apparent greater or less, or more or heavier, no longer have the
150	mastery over us, but give way before calculation and measure and weight?
150	
150	Most true.
150	
150	And this, surely, must be the work of the calculating and rational
150	principle in the soul
150	
150	To be sure.
150	
150	And when this principle measures and certifies that some things are
150	equal, or that some are greater or less than others, there occurs an
150	apparent contradiction?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	But were we not saying that such a contradiction is the same faculty
150	cannot have contrary opinions at the same time about the same thing?
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	Then that part of the soul which has an opinion contrary to measure is
150	not the same with that which has an opinion in accordance with measure?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	And the better part of the soul is likely to be that which trusts to
150	measure and calculation?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	And that which is opposed to them is one of the inferior principles of
150	the soul?
150	
150	No doubt.
150	
150	This was the conclusion at which I was seeking to arrive when I said
150	that painting or drawing, and imitation in general, when doing their
150	own proper work, are far removed from truth, and the companions and
150	friends and associates of a principle within us which is equally
150	removed from reason, and that they have no true or healthy aim.
150	
150	Exactly.
150	
150	The imitative art is an inferior who marries an inferior, and has
150	inferior offspring.
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	And is this confined to the sight only, or does it extend to the
150	hearing also, relating in fact to what we term poetry?
150	
150	Probably the same would be true of poetry.
150	
150	Do not rely, I said, on a probability derived from the analogy of
150	painting; but let us examine further and see whether the faculty with
150	which poetical imitation is concerned is good or bad.
150	
150	By all means.
150	
150	We may state the question thus:--Imitation imitates the actions of men,
150	whether voluntary or involuntary, on which, as they imagine, a good or
150	bad result has ensued, and they rejoice or sorrow accordingly.  Is
150	there anything more?
150	
150	No, there is nothing else.
150	
150	But in all this variety of circumstances is the man at unity with
150	himself--or rather, as in the instance of sight there was confusion and
150	opposition in his opinions about the same things, so here also is there
150	not strife and inconsistency in his life?  Though I need hardly raise
150	the question again, for I remember that all this has been already
150	admitted; and the soul has been acknowledged by us to be full of these
150	and ten thousand similar oppositions occurring at the same moment?
150	
150	And we were right, he said.
150	
150	Yes, I said, thus far we were right; but there was an omission which
150	must now be supplied.
150	
150	What was the omission?
150	
150	Were we not saying that a good man, who has the misfortune to lose his
150	son or anything else which is most dear to him, will bear the loss with
150	more equanimity than another?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	But will he have no sorrow, or shall we say that although he cannot
150	help sorrowing, he will moderate his sorrow?
150	
150	The latter, he said, is the truer statement.
150	
150	Tell me:  will he be more likely to struggle and hold out against his
150	sorrow when he is seen by his equals, or when he is alone?
150	
150	It will make a great difference whether he is seen or not.
150	
150	When he is by himself he will not mind saying or doing many things
150	which he would be ashamed of any one hearing or seeing him do?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	There is a principle of law and reason in him which bids him resist, as
150	well as a feeling of his misfortune which is forcing him to indulge his
150	sorrow?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	But when a man is drawn in two opposite directions, to and from the
150	same object, this, as we affirm, necessarily implies two distinct
150	principles in him?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	One of them is ready to follow the guidance of the law?
150	
150	How do you mean?
150	
150	The law would say that to be patient under suffering is best, and that
150	we should not give way to impatience, as there is no knowing whether
150	such things are good or evil; and nothing is gained by impatience;
150	also, because no human thing is of serious importance, and grief stands
150	in the way of that which at the moment is most required.
150	
150	What is most required? he asked.
150	
150	That we should take counsel about what has happened, and when the dice
150	have been thrown order our affairs in the way which reason deems best;
150	not, like children who have had a fall, keeping hold of the part struck
150	and wasting time in setting up a howl, but always accustoming the soul
150	forthwith to apply a remedy, raising up that which is sickly and
150	fallen, banishing the cry of sorrow by the healing art.
150	
150	Yes, he said, that is the true way of meeting the attacks of fortune.
150	
150	Yes, I said; and the higher principle is ready to follow this
150	suggestion of reason?
150	
150	Clearly.
150	
150	And the other principle, which inclines us to recollection of our
150	troubles and to lamentation, and can never have enough of them, we may
150	call irrational, useless, and cowardly?
150	
150	Indeed, we may.
150	
150	And does not the latter--I mean the rebellious principle--furnish a
150	great variety of materials for imitation?  Whereas the wise and calm
150	temperament, being always nearly equable, is not easy to imitate or to
150	appreciate when imitated, especially at a public festival when a
150	promiscuous crowd is assembled in a theatre.  For the feeling
150	represented is one to which they are strangers.
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	Then the imitative poet who aims at being popular is not by nature
150	made, nor is his art intended, to please or to affect the principle in
150	the soul; but he will prefer the passionate and fitful temper, which is
150	easily imitated?
150	
150	Clearly.
150	
150	And now we may fairly take him and place him by the side of the
150	painter, for he is like him in two ways:  first, inasmuch as his
150	creations have an inferior degree of truth--in this, I say, he is like
150	him; and he is also like him in being concerned with an inferior part
150	of the soul; and therefore we shall be right in refusing to admit him
150	into a well-ordered State, because he awakens and nourishes and
150	strengthens the feelings and impairs the reason.  As in a city when the
150	evil are permitted to have authority and the good are put out of the
150	way, so in the soul of man, as we maintain, the imitative poet implants
150	an evil constitution, for he indulges the irrational nature which has
150	no discernment of greater and less, but thinks the same thing at one
150	time great and at another small-he is a manufacturer of images and is
150	very far removed from the truth.
150	
150	Exactly.
150	
150	But we have not yet brought forward the heaviest count in our
150	accusation:--the power which poetry has of harming even the good (and
150	there are very few who are not harmed), is surely an awful thing?
150	
150	Yes, certainly, if the effect is what you say.
150	
150	Hear and judge:  The best of us, as I conceive, when we listen to a
150	passage of Homer, or one of the tragedians, in which he represents some
150	pitiful hero who is drawling out his sorrows in a long oration, or
150	weeping, and smiting his breast--the best of us, you know, delight in
150	giving way to sympathy, and are in raptures at the excellence of the
150	poet who stirs our feelings most.
150	
150	Yes, of course I know.
150	
150	But when any sorrow of our own happens to us, then you may observe that
150	we pride ourselves on the opposite quality--we would fain be quiet and
150	patient; this is the manly part, and the other which delighted us in
150	the recitation is now deemed to be the part of a woman.
150	
150	Very true, he said.
150	
150	Now can we be right in praising and admiring another who is doing that
150	which any one of us would abominate and be ashamed of in his own person?
150	
150	No, he said, that is certainly not reasonable.
150	
150	Nay, I said, quite reasonable from one point of view.
150	
150	What point of view?
150	
150	If you consider, I said, that when in misfortune we feel a natural
150	hunger and desire to relieve our sorrow by weeping and lamentation, and
150	that this feeling which is kept under control in our own calamities is
150	satisfied and delighted by the poets;-the better nature in each of us,
150	not having been sufficiently trained by reason or habit, allows the
150	sympathetic element to break loose because the sorrow is another's; and
150	the spectator fancies that there can be no disgrace to himself in
150	praising and pitying any one who comes telling him what a good man he
150	is, and making a fuss about his troubles; he thinks that the pleasure
150	is a gain, and why should he be supercilious and lose this and the poem
150	too?  Few persons ever reflect, as I should imagine, that from the evil
150	of other men something of evil is communicated to themselves.  And so
150	the feeling of sorrow which has gathered strength at the sight of the
150	misfortunes of others is with difficulty repressed in our own.
150	
150	How very true!
150	
150	And does not the same hold also of the ridiculous?  There are jests
150	which you would be ashamed to make yourself, and yet on the comic
150	stage, or indeed in private, when you hear them, you are greatly amused
150	by them, and are not at all disgusted at their unseemliness;--the case
150	of pity is repeated;--there is a principle in human nature which is
150	disposed to raise a laugh, and this which you once restrained by
150	reason, because you were afraid of being thought a buffoon, is now let
150	out again; and having stimulated the risible faculty at the theatre,
150	you are betrayed unconsciously to yourself into playing the comic poet
150	at home.
150	
150	Quite true, he said.
150	
150	And the same may be said of lust and anger and all the other
150	affections, of desire and pain and pleasure, which are held to be
150	inseparable from every action--in all of them poetry feeds and waters
150	the passions instead of drying them up; she lets them rule, although
150	they ought to be controlled, if mankind are ever to increase in
150	happiness and virtue.
150	
150	I cannot deny it.
150	
150	Therefore, Glaucon, I said, whenever you meet with any of the eulogists
150	of Homer declaring that he has been the educator of Hellas, and that he
150	is profitable for education and for the ordering of human things, and
150	that you should take him up again and again and get to know him and
150	regulate your whole life according to him, we may love and honour those
150	who say these things--they are excellent people, as far as their lights
150	extend; and we are ready to acknowledge that Homer is the greatest of
150	poets and first of tragedy writers; but we must remain firm in our
150	conviction that hymns to the gods and praises of famous men are the
150	only poetry which ought to be admitted into our State.  For if you go
150	beyond this and allow the honeyed muse to enter, either in epic or
150	lyric verse, not law and the reason of mankind, which by common consent
150	have ever been deemed best, but pleasure and pain will be the rulers in
150	our State.
150	
150	That is most true, he said.
150	
150	And now since we have reverted to the subject of poetry, let this our
150	defence serve to show the reasonableness of our former judgment in
150	sending away out of our State an art having the tendencies which we
150	have described; for reason constrained us.  But that she may impute to
150	us any harshness or want of politeness, let us tell her that there is
150	an ancient quarrel between philosophy and poetry; of which there are
150	many proofs, such as the saying of 'the yelping hound howling at her
150	lord,' or of one 'mighty in the vain talk of fools,' and 'the mob of
150	sages circumventing Zeus,' and the 'subtle thinkers who are beggars
150	after all'; and there are innumerable other signs of ancient enmity
150	between them.  Notwithstanding this, let us assure our sweet friend and
150	the sister arts of imitation that if she will only prove her title to
150	exist in a well-ordered State we shall be delighted to receive her--we
150	are very conscious of her charms; but we may not on that account betray
150	the truth.  I dare say, Glaucon, that you are as much charmed by her as
150	I am, especially when she appears in Homer?
150	
150	Yes, indeed, I am greatly charmed.
150	
150	Shall I propose, then, that she be allowed to return from exile, but
150	upon this condition only--that she make a defence of herself in lyrical
150	or some other metre?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	And we may further grant to those of her defenders who are lovers of
150	poetry and yet not poets the permission to speak in prose on her
150	behalf: let them show not only that she is pleasant but also useful to
150	States and to human life, and we will listen in a kindly spirit; for if
150	this can be proved we shall surely be the gainers--I mean, if there is
150	a use in poetry as well as a delight?
150	
150	Certainly, he said, we shall the gainers.
150	
150	If her defence fails, then, my dear friend, like other persons who are
150	enamoured of something, but put a restraint upon themselves when they
150	think their desires are opposed to their interests, so too must we
150	after the manner of lovers give her up, though not without a struggle.
150	We too are inspired by that love of poetry which the education of noble
150	States has implanted in us, and therefore we would have her appear at
150	her best and truest; but so long as she is unable to make good her
150	defence, this argument of ours shall be a charm to us, which we will
150	repeat to ourselves while we listen to her strains; that we may not
150	fall away into the childish love of her which captivates the many.  At
150	all events we are well aware that poetry being such as we have
150	described is not to be regarded seriously as attaining to the truth;
150	and he who listens to her, fearing for the safety of the city which is
150	within him, should be on his guard against her seductions and make our
150	words his law.
150	
150	Yes, he said, I quite agree with you.
150	
150	Yes, I said, my dear Glaucon, for great is the issue at stake, greater
150	than appears, whether a man is to be good or bad.  And what will any
150	one be profited if under the influence of honour or money or power,
150	aye, or under the excitement of poetry, he neglect justice and virtue?
150	
150	Yes, he said; I have been convinced by the argument, as I believe that
150	any one else would have been.
150	
150	And yet no mention has been made of the greatest prizes and rewards
150	which await virtue.
150	
150	What, are there any greater still?  If there are, they must be of an
150	inconceivable greatness.
150	
150	Why, I said, what was ever great in a short time?  The whole period of
150	threescore years and ten is surely but a little thing in comparison
150	with eternity?
150	
150	Say rather 'nothing,' he replied.
150	
150	And should an immortal being seriously think of this little space
150	rather than of the whole?
150	
150	Of the whole, certainly.  But why do you ask?
150	
150	Are you not aware, I said, that the soul of man is immortal and
150	imperishable?
150	
150	He looked at me in astonishment, and said:  No, by heaven: And are you
150	really prepared to maintain this?
150	
150	Yes, I said, I ought to be, and you too--there is no difficulty in
150	proving it.
150	
150	I see a great difficulty; but I should like to hear you state this
150	argument of which you make so light.
150	
150	Listen then.
150	
150	I am attending.
150	
150	There is a thing which you call good and another which you call evil?
150	
150	Yes, he replied.
150	
150	Would you agree with me in thinking that the corrupting and destroying
150	element is the evil, and the saving and improving element the good?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	And you admit that every thing has a good and also an evil; as
150	ophthalmia is the evil of the eyes and disease of the whole body; as
150	mildew is of corn, and rot of timber, or rust of copper and iron: in
150	everything, or in almost everything, there is an inherent evil and
150	disease?
150	
150	Yes, he said.
150	
150	And anything which is infected by any of these evils is made evil, and
150	at last wholly dissolves and dies?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	The vice and evil which is inherent in each is the destruction of each;
150	and if this does not destroy them there is nothing else that will; for
150	good certainly will not destroy them, nor again, that which is neither
150	good nor evil.
150	
150	Certainly not.
150	
150	If, then, we find any nature which having this inherent corruption
150	cannot be dissolved or destroyed, we may be certain that of such a
150	nature there is no destruction?
150	
150	That may be assumed.
150	
150	Well, I said, and is there no evil which corrupts the soul?
150	
150	Yes, he said, there are all the evils which we were just now passing in
150	review:  unrighteousness, intemperance, cowardice, ignorance.
150	
150	But does any of these dissolve or destroy her?--and here do not let us
150	fall into the error of supposing that the unjust and foolish man, when
150	he is detected, perishes through his own injustice, which is an evil of
150	the soul.  Take the analogy of the body: The evil of the body is a
150	disease which wastes and reduces and annihilates the body; and all the
150	things of which we were just now speaking come to annihilation through
150	their own corruption attaching to them and inhering in them and so
150	destroying them.  Is not this true?
150	
150	Yes.
150	
150	Consider the soul in like manner.  Does the injustice or other evil
150	which exists in the soul waste and consume her?  Do they by attaching
150	to the soul and inhering in her at last bring her to death, and so
150	separate her from the body?
150	
150	Certainly not.
150	
150	And yet, I said, it is unreasonable to suppose that anything can perish
150	from without through affection of external evil which could not be
150	destroyed from within by a corruption of its own?
150	
150	It is, he replied.
150	
150	Consider, I said, Glaucon, that even the badness of food, whether
150	staleness, decomposition, or any other bad quality, when confined to
150	the actual food, is not supposed to destroy the body; although, if the
150	badness of food communicates corruption to the body, then we should say
150	that the body has been destroyed by a corruption of itself, which is
150	disease, brought on by this; but that the body, being one thing, can be
150	destroyed by the badness of food, which is another, and which does not
150	engender any natural infection--this we shall absolutely deny?
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	And, on the same principle, unless some bodily evil can produce an evil
150	of the soul, we must not suppose that the soul, which is one thing, can
150	be dissolved by any merely external evil which belongs to another?
150	
150	Yes, he said, there is reason in that.
150	
150	Either then, let us refute this conclusion, or, while it remains
150	unrefuted, let us never say that fever, or any other disease, or the
150	knife put to the throat, or even the cutting up of the whole body into
150	the minutest pieces, can destroy the soul, until she herself is proved
150	to become more unholy or unrighteous in consequence of these things
150	being done to the body; but that the soul, or anything else if not
150	destroyed by an internal evil, can be destroyed by an external one, is
150	not to be affirmed by any man.
150	
150	And surely, he replied, no one will ever prove that the souls of men
150	become more unjust in consequence of death.
150	
150	But if some one who would rather not admit the immortality of the soul
150	boldly denies this, and says that the dying do really become more evil
150	and unrighteous, then, if the speaker is right, I suppose that
150	injustice, like disease, must be assumed to be fatal to the unjust, and
150	that those who take this disorder die by the natural inherent power of
150	destruction which evil has, and which kills them sooner or later, but
150	in quite another way from that in which, at present, the wicked receive
150	death at the hands of others as the penalty of their deeds?
150	
150	Nay, he said, in that case injustice, if fatal to the unjust, will not
150	be so very terrible to him, for he will be delivered from evil.  But I
150	rather suspect the opposite to be the truth, and that injustice which,
150	if it have the power, will murder others, keeps the murderer
150	alive--aye, and well awake too; so far removed is her dwelling-place
150	from being a house of death.
150	
150	True, I said; if the inherent natural vice or evil of the soul is
150	unable to kill or destroy her, hardly will that which is appointed to
150	be the destruction of some other body, destroy a soul or anything else
150	except that of which it was appointed to be the destruction.
150	
150	Yes, that can hardly be.
150	
150	But the soul which cannot be destroyed by an evil, whether inherent or
150	external, must exist for ever, and if existing for ever, must be
150	immortal?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	That is the conclusion, I said; and, if a true conclusion, then the
150	souls must always be the same, for if none be destroyed they will not
150	diminish in number.  Neither will they increase, for the increase of
150	the immortal natures must come from something mortal, and all things
150	would thus end in immortality.
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	But this we cannot believe--reason will not allow us--any more than we
150	can believe the soul, in her truest nature, to be full of variety and
150	difference and dissimilarity.
150	
150	What do you mean? he said.
150	
150	The soul, I said, being, as is now proven, immortal, must be the
150	fairest of compositions and cannot be compounded of many elements?
150	
150	Certainly not.
150	
150	Her immortality is demonstrated by the previous argument, and there are
150	many other proofs; but to see her as she really is, not as we now
150	behold her, marred by communion with the body and other miseries, you
150	must contemplate her with the eye of reason, in her original purity;
150	and then her beauty will be revealed, and justice and injustice and all
150	the things which we have described will be manifested more clearly.
150	Thus far, we have spoken the truth concerning her as she appears at
150	present, but we must remember also that we have seen her only in a
150	condition which may be compared to that of the sea-god Glaucus, whose
150	original image can hardly be discerned because his natural members are
150	broken off and crushed and damaged by the waves in all sorts of ways,
150	and incrustations have grown over them of seaweed and shells and
150	stones, so that he is more like some monster than he is to his own
