150	natural form.  And the soul which we behold is in a similar condition,
150	disfigured by ten thousand ills.  But not there, Glaucon, not there
150	must we look.
150	
150	Where then?
150	
150	At her love of wisdom.  Let us see whom she affects, and what society
150	and converse she seeks in virtue of her near kindred with the immortal
150	and eternal and divine; also how different she would become if wholly
150	following this superior principle, and borne by a divine impulse out of
150	the ocean in which she now is, and disengaged from the stones and
150	shells and things of earth and rock which in wild variety spring up
150	around her because she feeds upon earth, and is overgrown by the good
150	things of this life as they are termed:  then you would see her as she
150	is, and know whether she has one shape only or many, or what her nature
150	is.  Of her affections and of the forms which she takes in this present
150	life I think that we have now said enough.
150	
150	True, he replied.
150	
150	And thus, I said, we have fulfilled the conditions of the argument; we
150	have not introduced the rewards and glories of justice, which, as you
150	were saying, are to be found in Homer and Hesiod; but justice in her
150	own nature has been shown to be best for the soul in her own nature.
150	Let a man do what is just, whether he have the ring of Gyges or not,
150	and even if in addition to the ring of Gyges he put on the helmet of
150	Hades.
150	
150	Very true.
150	
150	And now, Glaucon, there will be no harm in further enumerating how many
150	and how great are the rewards which justice and the other virtues
150	procure to the soul from gods and men, both in life and after death.
150	
150	Certainly not, he said.
150	
150	Will you repay me, then, what you borrowed in the argument?
150	
150	What did I borrow?
150	
150	The assumption that the just man should appear unjust and the unjust
150	just:  for you were of opinion that even if the true state of the case
150	could not possibly escape the eyes of gods and men, still this
150	admission ought to be made for the sake of the argument, in order that
150	pure justice might be weighed against pure injustice.  Do you remember?
150	
150	I should be much to blame if I had forgotten.
150	
150	Then, as the cause is decided, I demand on behalf of justice that the
150	estimation in which she is held by gods and men and which we
150	acknowledge to be her due should now be restored to her by us; since
150	she has been shown to confer reality, and not to deceive those who
150	truly possess her, let what has been taken from her be given back, that
150	so she may win that palm of appearance which is hers also, and which
150	she gives to her own.
150	
150	The demand, he said, is just.
150	
150	In the first place, I said--and this is the first thing which you will
150	have to give back--the nature both of the just and unjust is truly
150	known to the gods.
150	
150	Granted.
150	
150	And if they are both known to them, one must be the friend and the
150	other the enemy of the gods, as we admitted from the beginning?
150	
150	True.
150	
150	And the friend of the gods may be supposed to receive from them all
150	things at their best, excepting only such evil as is the necessary
150	consequence of former sins?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	Then this must be our notion of the just man, that even when he is in
150	poverty or sickness, or any other seeming misfortune, all things will
150	in the end work together for good to him in life and death: for the
150	gods have a care of any one whose desire is to become just and to be
150	like God, as far as man can attain the divine likeness, by the pursuit
150	of virtue?
150	
150	Yes, he said; if he is like God he will surely not be neglected by him.
150	
150	And of the unjust may not the opposite be supposed?
150	
150	Certainly.
150	
150	Such, then, are the palms of victory which the gods give the just?
150	
150	That is my conviction.
150	
150	And what do they receive of men?  Look at things as they really are,
150	and you will see that the clever unjust are in the case of runners, who
150	run well from the starting-place to the goal but not back again from
150	the goal:  they go off at a great pace, but in the end only look
150	foolish, slinking away with their ears draggling on their shoulders,
150	and without a crown; but the true runner comes to the finish and
150	receives the prize and is crowned.  And this is the way with the just;
150	he who endures to the end of every action and occasion of his entire
150	life has a good report and carries off the prize which men have to
150	bestow.
150	
150	True.
150	
150	And now you must allow me to repeat of the just the blessings which you
150	were attributing to the fortunate unjust.  I shall say of them, what
150	you were saying of the others, that as they grow older, they become
150	rulers in their own city if they care to be; they marry whom they like
150	and give in marriage to whom they will; all that you said of the others
150	I now say of these.  And, on the other hand, of the unjust I say that
150	the greater number, even though they escape in their youth, are found
150	out at last and look foolish at the end of their course, and when they
150	come to be old and miserable are flouted alike by stranger and citizen;
150	they are beaten and then come those things unfit for ears polite, as
150	you truly term them; they will be racked and have their eyes burned
150	out, as you were saying.  And you may suppose that I have repeated the
150	remainder of your tale of horrors.  But will you let me assume, without
150	reciting them, that these things are true?
150	
150	Certainly, he said, what you say is true.
150	
150	These, then, are the prizes and rewards and gifts which are bestowed
150	upon the just by gods and men in this present life, in addition to the
150	other good things which justice of herself provides.
150	
150	Yes, he said; and they are fair and lasting.
150	
150	And yet, I said, all these are as nothing, either in number or
150	greatness in comparison with those other recompenses which await both
150	just and unjust after death.  And you ought to hear them, and then both
150	just and unjust will have received from us a full payment of the debt
150	which the argument owes to them.
150	
150	Speak, he said; there are few things which I would more gladly hear.
150	
150	SOCRATES
150	
150	Well, I said, I will tell you a tale; not one of the tales which
150	Odysseus tells to the hero Alcinous, yet this too is a tale of a hero,
150	Er the son of Armenius, a Pamphylian by birth.  He was slain in battle,
150	and ten days afterwards, when the bodies of the dead were taken up
150	already in a state of corruption, his body was found unaffected by
150	decay, and carried away home to be buried.  And on the twelfth day, as
150	he was lying on the funeral pile, he returned to life and told them
150	what he had seen in the other world.  He said that when his soul left
150	the body he went on a journey with a great company, and that they came
150	to a mysterious place at which there were two openings in the earth;
150	they were near together, and over against them were two other openings
150	in the heaven above.  In the intermediate space there were judges
150	seated, who commanded the just, after they had given judgment on them
150	and had bound their sentences in front of them, to ascend by the
150	heavenly way on the right hand; and in like manner the unjust were
150	bidden by them to descend by the lower way on the left hand; these also
150	bore the symbols of their deeds, but fastened on their backs.  He drew
150	near, and they told him that he was to be the messenger who would carry
150	the report of the other world to men, and they bade him hear and see
150	all that was to be heard and seen in that place.  Then he beheld and
150	saw on one side the souls departing at either opening of heaven and
150	earth when sentence had been given on them; and at the two other
150	openings other souls, some ascending out of the earth dusty and worn
150	with travel, some descending out of heaven clean and bright.  And
150	arriving ever and anon they seemed to have come from a long journey,
150	and they went forth with gladness into the meadow, where they encamped
150	as at a festival; and those who knew one another embraced and
150	conversed, the souls which came from earth curiously enquiring about
150	the things above, and the souls which came from heaven about the things
150	beneath.  And they told one another of what had happened by the way,
150	those from below weeping and sorrowing at the remembrance of the things
150	which they had endured and seen in their journey beneath the earth (now
150	the journey lasted a thousand years), while those from above were
150	describing heavenly delights and visions of inconceivable beauty.  The
150	Story, Glaucon, would take too long to tell; but the sum was this:--He
150	said that for every wrong which they had done to any one they suffered
150	tenfold; or once in a hundred years--such being reckoned to be the
150	length of man's life, and the penalty being thus paid ten times in a
150	thousand years.  If, for example, there were any who had been the cause
150	of many deaths, or had betrayed or enslaved cities or armies, or been
150	guilty of any other evil behaviour, for each and all of their offences
150	they received punishment ten times over, and the rewards of beneficence
150	and justice and holiness were in the same proportion.  I need hardly
150	repeat what he said concerning young children dying almost as soon as
150	they were born.  Of piety and impiety to gods and parents, and of
150	murderers, there were retributions other and greater far which he
150	described.  He mentioned that he was present when one of the spirits
150	asked another, 'Where is Ardiaeus the Great?' (Now this Ardiaeus lived
150	a thousand years before the time of Er: he had been the tyrant of some
150	city of Pamphylia, and had murdered his aged father and his elder
150	brother, and was said to have committed many other abominable crimes.)
150	The answer of the other spirit was: 'He comes not hither and will never
150	come.  And this,' said he, 'was one of the dreadful sights which we
150	ourselves witnessed.  We were at the mouth of the cavern, and, having
150	completed all our experiences, were about to reascend, when of a sudden
150	Ardiaeus appeared and several others, most of whom were tyrants; and
150	there were also besides the tyrants private individuals who had been
150	great criminals:  they were just, as they fancied, about to return into
150	the upper world, but the mouth, instead of admitting them, gave a roar,
150	whenever any of these incurable sinners or some one who had not been
150	sufficiently punished tried to ascend; and then wild men of fiery
150	aspect, who were standing by and heard the sound, seized and carried
150	them off; and Ardiaeus and others they bound head and foot and hand,
150	and threw them down and flayed them with scourges, and dragged them
150	along the road at the side, carding them on thorns like wool, and
150	declaring to the passers-by what were their crimes, and that they were
150	being taken away to be cast into hell.' And of all the many terrors
150	which they had endured, he said that there was none like the terror
150	which each of them felt at that moment, lest they should hear the
150	voice; and when there was silence, one by one they ascended with
150	exceeding joy.  These, said Er, were the penalties and retributions,
150	and there were blessings as great.
150	
150	Now when the spirits which were in the meadow had tarried seven days,
150	on the eighth they were obliged to proceed on their journey, and, on
150	the fourth day after, he said that they came to a place where they
150	could see from above a line of light, straight as a column, extending
150	right through the whole heaven and through the earth, in colour
150	resembling the rainbow, only brighter and purer; another day's journey
150	brought them to the place, and there, in the midst of the light, they
150	saw the ends of the chains of heaven let down from above:  for this
150	light is the belt of heaven, and holds together the circle of the
150	universe, like the under-girders of a trireme.  From these ends is
150	extended the spindle of Necessity, on which all the revolutions turn.
150	The shaft and hook of this spindle are made of steel, and the whorl is
150	made partly of steel and also partly of other materials.  Now the whorl
150	is in form like the whorl used on earth; and the description of it
150	implied that there is one large hollow whorl which is quite scooped
150	out, and into this is fitted another lesser one, and another, and
150	another, and four others, making eight in all, like vessels which fit
150	into one another; the whorls show their edges on the upper side, and on
150	their lower side all together form one continuous whorl.  This is
150	pierced by the spindle, which is driven home through the centre of the
150	eighth.  The first and outermost whorl has the rim broadest, and the
150	seven inner whorls are narrower, in the following proportions--the
150	sixth is next to the first in size, the fourth next to the sixth; then
150	comes the eighth; the seventh is fifth, the fifth is sixth, the third
150	is seventh, last and eighth comes the second.  The largest (of fixed
150	stars) is spangled, and the seventh (or sun) is brightest; the eighth
150	(or moon) coloured by the reflected light of the seventh; the second
150	and fifth (Saturn and Mercury) are in colour like one another, and
150	yellower than the preceding; the third (Venus) has the whitest light;
150	the fourth (Mars) is reddish; the sixth (Jupiter) is in whiteness
150	second.  Now the whole spindle has the same motion; but, as the whole
150	revolves in one direction, the seven inner circles move slowly in the
150	other, and of these the swiftest is the eighth; next in swiftness are
150	the seventh, sixth, and fifth, which move together; third in swiftness
150	appeared to move according to the law of this reversed motion the
150	fourth; the third appeared fourth and the second fifth.  The spindle
150	turns on the knees of Necessity; and on the upper surface of each
150	circle is a siren, who goes round with them, hymning a single tone or
150	note.  The eight together form one harmony; and round about, at equal
150	intervals, there is another band, three in number, each sitting upon
150	her throne: these are the Fates, daughters of Necessity, who are
150	clothed in white robes and have chaplets upon their heads, Lachesis and
150	Clotho and Atropos, who accompany with their voices the harmony of the
150	sirens--Lachesis singing of the past, Clotho of the present, Atropos of
150	the future; Clotho from time to time assisting with a touch of her
150	right hand the revolution of the outer circle of the whorl or spindle,
150	and Atropos with her left hand touching and guiding the inner ones, and
150	Lachesis laying hold of either in turn, first with one hand and then
150	with the other.
150	
150	When Er and the spirits arrived, their duty was to go at once to
150	Lachesis; but first of all there came a prophet who arranged them in
150	order; then he took from the knees of Lachesis lots and samples of
150	lives, and having mounted a high pulpit, spoke as follows: 'Hear the
150	word of Lachesis, the daughter of Necessity.  Mortal souls, behold a
150	new cycle of life and mortality.  Your genius will not be allotted to
150	you, but you choose your genius; and let him who draws the first lot
150	have the first choice, and the life which he chooses shall be his
150	destiny.  Virtue is free, and as a man honours or dishonours her he
150	will have more or less of her; the responsibility is with the
150	chooser--God is justified.' When the Interpreter had thus spoken he
150	scattered lots indifferently among them all, and each of them took up
150	the lot which fell near him, all but Er himself (he was not allowed),
150	and each as he took his lot perceived the number which he had obtained.
150	Then the Interpreter placed on the ground before them the samples of
150	lives; and there were many more lives than the souls present, and they
150	were of all sorts.  There were lives of every animal and of man in
150	every condition.  And there were tyrannies among them, some lasting out
150	the tyrant's life, others which broke off in the middle and came to an
150	end in poverty and exile and beggary; and there were lives of famous
150	men, some who were famous for their form and beauty as well as for
150	their strength and success in games, or, again, for their birth and the
150	qualities of their ancestors; and some who were the reverse of famous
150	for the opposite qualities.  And of women likewise; there was not,
150	however, any definite character them, because the soul, when choosing a
150	new life, must of necessity become different.  But there was every
150	other quality, and the all mingled with one another, and also with
150	elements of wealth and poverty, and disease and health; and there were
150	mean states also.  And here, my dear Glaucon, is the supreme peril of
150	our human state; and therefore the utmost care should be taken.  Let
150	each one of us leave every other kind of knowledge and seek and follow
150	one thing only, if peradventure he may be able to learn and may find
150	some one who will make him able to learn and discern between good and
150	evil, and so to choose always and everywhere the better life as he has
150	opportunity.  He should consider the bearing of all these things which
150	have been mentioned severally and collectively upon virtue; he should
150	know what the effect of beauty is when combined with poverty or wealth
150	in a particular soul, and what are the good and evil consequences of
150	noble and humble birth, of private and public station, of strength and
150	weakness, of cleverness and dullness, and of all the soul, and the
150	operation of them when conjoined; he will then look at the nature of
150	the soul, and from the consideration of all these qualities he will be
150	able to determine which is the better and which is the worse; and so he
150	will choose, giving the name of evil to the life which will make his
150	soul more unjust, and good to the life which will make his soul more
150	just; all else he will disregard.  For we have seen and know that this
150	is the best choice both in life and after death.  A man must take with
150	him into the world below an adamantine faith in truth and right, that
150	there too he may be undazzled by the desire of wealth or the other
150	allurements of evil, lest, coming upon tyrannies and similar
150	villainies, he do irremediable wrongs to others and suffer yet worse
150	himself; but let him know how to choose the mean and avoid the extremes
150	on either side, as far as possible, not only in this life but in all
150	that which is to come.  For this is the way of happiness.
150	
150	And according to the report of the messenger from the other world this
150	was what the prophet said at the time:  'Even for the last comer, if he
150	chooses wisely and will live diligently, there is appointed a happy and
150	not undesirable existence.  Let not him who chooses first be careless,
150	and let not the last despair.'  And when he had spoken, he who had the
150	first choice came forward and in a moment chose the greatest tyranny;
150	his mind having been darkened by folly and sensuality, he had not
150	thought out the whole matter before he chose, and did not at first
150	sight perceive that he was fated, among other evils, to devour his own
150	children.  But when he had time to reflect, and saw what was in the
150	lot, he began to beat his breast and lament over his choice, forgetting
150	the proclamation of the prophet; for, instead of throwing the blame of
150	his misfortune on himself, he accused chance and the gods, and
150	everything rather than himself.  Now he was one of those who came from
150	heaven, and in a former life had dwelt in a well-ordered State, but his
150	virtue was a matter of habit only, and he had no philosophy.  And it
150	was true of others who were similarly overtaken, that the greater
150	number of them came from heaven and therefore they had never been
150	schooled by trial, whereas the pilgrims who came from earth, having
150	themselves suffered and seen others suffer, were not in a hurry to
150	choose.  And owing to this inexperience of theirs, and also because the
150	lot was a chance, many of the souls exchanged a good destiny for an
150	evil or an evil for a good.  For if a man had always on his arrival in
150	this world dedicated himself from the first to sound philosophy, and
150	had been moderately fortunate in the number of the lot, he might, as
150	the messenger reported, be happy here, and also his journey to another
150	life and return to this, instead of being rough and underground, would
150	be smooth and heavenly.  Most curious, he said, was the spectacle--sad
150	and laughable and strange; for the choice of the souls was in most
150	cases based on their experience of a previous life.  There he saw the
150	soul which had once been Orpheus choosing the life of a swan out of
150	enmity to the race of women, hating to be born of a woman because they
150	had been his murderers; he beheld also the soul of Thamyras choosing
150	the life of a nightingale; birds, on the other hand, like the swan and
150	other musicians, wanting to be men.  The soul which obtained the
150	twentieth lot chose the life of a lion, and this was the soul of Ajax
150	the son of Telamon, who would not be a man, remembering the injustice
150	which was done him the judgment about the arms.  The next was
150	Agamemnon, who took the life of an eagle, because, like Ajax, he hated
150	human nature by reason of his sufferings.  About the middle came the
150	lot of Atalanta; she, seeing the great fame of an athlete, was unable
150	to resist the temptation:  and after her there followed the soul of
150	Epeus the son of Panopeus passing into the nature of a woman cunning in
150	the arts; and far away among the last who chose, the soul of the jester
150	Thersites was putting on the form of a monkey.  There came also the
150	soul of Odysseus having yet to make a choice, and his lot happened to
150	be the last of them all.  Now the recollection of former tolls had
150	disenchanted him of ambition, and he went about for a considerable time
150	in search of the life of a private man who had no cares; he had some
150	difficulty in finding this, which was lying about and had been
150	neglected by everybody else; and when he saw it, he said that he would
150	have done the had his lot been first instead of last, and that he was
150	delighted to have it.  And not only did men pass into animals, but I
150	must also mention that there were animals tame and wild who changed
150	into one another and into corresponding human natures--the good into
150	the gentle and the evil into the savage, in all sorts of combinations.
150	
150	All the souls had now chosen their lives, and they went in the order of
150	their choice to Lachesis, who sent with them the genius whom they had
150	severally chosen, to be the guardian of their lives and the fulfiller
150	of the choice:  this genius led the souls first to Clotho, and drew
150	them within the revolution of the spindle impelled by her hand, thus
150	ratifying the destiny of each; and then, when they were fastened to
150	this, carried them to Atropos, who spun the threads and made them
150	irreversible, whence without turning round they passed beneath the
150	throne of Necessity; and when they had all passed, they marched on in a
150	scorching heat to the plain of Forgetfulness, which was a barren waste
150	destitute of trees and verdure; and then towards evening they encamped
150	by the river of Unmindfulness, whose water no vessel can hold; of this
150	they were all obliged to drink a certain quantity, and those who were
150	not saved by wisdom drank more than was necessary; and each one as he
150	drank forgot all things.  Now after they had gone to rest, about the
150	middle of the night there was a thunderstorm and earthquake, and then
150	in an instant they were driven upwards in all manner of ways to their
150	birth, like stars shooting.  He himself was hindered from drinking the
150	water.  But in what manner or by what means he returned to the body he
150	could not say; only, in the morning, awaking suddenly, he found himself
150	lying on the pyre.
150	
150	And thus, Glaucon, the tale has been saved and has not perished, and
150	will save us if we are obedient to the word spoken; and we shall pass
150	safely over the river of Forgetfulness and our soul will not be
150	defiled.  Wherefore my counsel is that we hold fast ever to the
150	heavenly way and follow after justice and virtue always, considering
150	that the soul is immortal and able to endure every sort of good and
150	every sort of evil.  Thus shall we live dear to one another and to the
150	gods, both while remaining here and when, like conquerors in the games
150	who go round to gather gifts, we receive our reward.  And it shall be
150	well with us both in this life and in the pilgrimage of a thousand
150	years which we have been describing.
